Port Jefferson v. Consolidated Petroleum Terminal, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


71 Misc. 2d 948; 337 N.Y.S.2d 636; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1447



October 20, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff village alleged that defendant, a large transporter of fuel and heating oils, was in violation of certain fire prevention ordinances enacted by the village. The village asked the court to enjoin the transporter from receiving petroleum products at its pier until it complies with the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The transporter maintained a facility for docking oil tanker vessels in the harbor and pumping the petroleum through a pipeline to a tank farm approximately two miles south of the harbor. The transporter's upland and a portion of its pier were located within the boundaries of the village but the remainder of the pier, including the "T" shaped dock for receiving the vessels was outside the village border and within the territorial limits of another town. The town ordinance stated that certain fire prevention measure had to be in place at the harbor where vessels were loaded and unloaded. The transporter argued that the village had no jurisdiction over the subject matter. The court held that the very activities sought to be regulated that were the object of the ordinance were conducted beyond the village's jurisdiction because none of the various boundary indicia for showing the perimeter of the village included the site of the dock or platform where the tankers tied up and where the petroleum products were unladen. Furthermore, the waters beneath the pier and the harbor bottom were owned and controlled by the other town and not the village.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- fire prevention ordinances -- paragraph (2) of subdivision 63 of section 89 of Village Law dealing with boating and sewage regulations is inapplicable to village's fire prevention ordinance -- village's fire prevention ordinance does not apply to defendant's pier located outside village boundaries; complaint is dismissed.

1. Insofar as the validity of certain fire prevention ordinances of plaintiff village is concerned, paragraph (2) of subdivision 63 of section 89 of the Village Law, dealing with boating and sewage regulations is not in point.

2. Subdivision 34 of said section limits a village's jurisdiction to the actual village boundaries. Since defendant's pier, where oil tankers dock for discharging petroleum, is outside the village's territory, the ordinances are inapplicable. The complaint is dismissed.

 

1622 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re County of Nassau


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


71 Misc. 2d 554; 336 N.Y.S.2d 566; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1465



October 13, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county condemned for public use claimant property owner's land consisting of 29.4 acres of land, with frontage on the Atlantic Ocean and with one-half the property improved with a cabana and beach club complex. The issue before the court was the determining of the land value for property used as a beach and cabana club.


OVERVIEW: The county condemned for public use the property owner's land, which consisted of land with frontage on the Atlantic Ocean. Half of the land was improved with a beach club complex and the rest was vacant. The difficulty was determining the land value for the property used as a beach and cabana club. The parties stipulated that the value of the beach club improvements was $ 711,000. The court found there existed a duality of use for the entire tract. The 9.4 acres not used in the beach club operation could have been granted a change of zone to residential, as land waterfront land was highly desirable especially when close to New York City. The county's appraiser said the best use of the east 9.4 acres was residential but did not value them as such. He also failed to prove economic obsolescence. Thus, the property owner's appraiser's approach was more realistic and the 20 acres of subject property devoted to beach club use had a value of $ 928,000 and the east 9 acres had a land value of $ 55,000 per acre. Because the county condemned the property and then continued to run a successful beach club, the going concern value of the intangible assets condemned by the county was $ 77,500.


OUTCOME: The court found there existed a duality of use for the entire tract of land, in the matter where the county condemned for public use the property owner's land.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Eminent domain -- compensation -- beach and cabana club constituted business specialty; county appropriated it for purpose of continuing to operate it for benefit of residents of county; court awards value of land, plus sound value of improvements, plus value as going concern, plus higher value of remaining land which is best suited for residential development -- though three employees of county worked in business office before vesting date, county did not de facto oust owner until after vesting date.

1. The County of Nassau appropriated on December 18, 1967 (a) claimant's beach and cabana club situated on 20 acres which have a frontage of 750 feet on the Atlantic ocean, and (b) claimant's adjoining land, which was mostly unimproved and which comprised 9.4 acres. This beach and cabana club was a specialty. The court values the 20 acres at $ 46,400 an acre, or $ 928,000, and also awards $ 711,000 as the sound value of the improvements.

2. Furthermore, since this beach and cabana club was a profitable business enterprise, and since the county's purpose in appropriating it was and is to continue to operate it as a beach and cabana club for residents of the county, the owner [***2] is justly entitled to compensation for the value of the business as a going concern. For this value, the court awards $ 77,500.

3. The remaining 9.4 acres are best suited for residential development and are valued by the court at $ 55,000 an acre, or $ 517,000. Thus, the total award to claimant is $ 2,233,500.

4. No de facto taking occurred prior to the de jure taking; that is, although three employees of the county were working in the club office for several months prior to the vesting date, the county did not actually oust the owner from possession until three months after the vesting date.

 

1623 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People of Smithtown v. Poveromo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Fourth District, Suffolk County


71 Misc. 2d 524; 336 N.Y.S.2d 764; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1483



October 10, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the foregoing, the information is dismissed, as it would appear to be defective for the reason that it is based upon an ordinance that is unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town filed an action against defendant landowner, charging the landowner with a violation of Smithtown, N.Y., Township Code ch. 25B-3 (ordinance) for filling in property on the foreshore of a river. After trial, the landowner filed a motion to dismiss the information, arguing, among other things, that the ordinance was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The landowner operated a landfill on his property and dumped fresh fill into an area where the fill intruded upon a part of the shore of the river. The town alleged that such action violated the ordinance, which provided that no person could remove from or deposit any material in any waterway or wetland without a permit. A violation of the ordinance was a misdemeanor. The landowner argued that the definition of "wetland" was so ambiguous and vague that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court granted the landowner's motion to dismiss the information because the ordinance was unconstitutional, but found that it was unconstitutional for other reasons. The court held that the lands bounded by the river were held subject to the public's right to undiminished utility for public purposes and that the town had no statutory power to regulate the use of the river. The court further held that the state in adopting N.Y. Conserv. Law § 400-a effectively declared a proscription to the regulation and control of the water resources of New York State by municipalities within the state. As such, the court found that the town lacked the power to grant a permit under the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court granted the landowner's motion to dismiss the information filed against him by the town and held that the ordinance was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Waters and watercourses -- wetlands -- water resources, including wetlands, are governed by Environmental Conservation Law (art. 15); even if municipality owns bed of river, it has no constitutional power to authorize filling in of any part of it.

Even if the Town of Smithtown owns the land under the waters of the Nissequogue River (which flows into Long Island Sound), its ownership is in trust for the public. The water resources of the State, including wetlands, are governed by the Environmental Conservation Law (art. 15). Wetlands cannot be filled in, nor can soil be removed from the ecological systems of rivers, unless such filling or removing is done with strict adherence to the provisions of article 15 of the Environmental Conservation Law. Local Law No. 1 of 1970 of the Town of Smithtown, insofar as it may purport to give the town the power to permit earth fill or to permit dredging within the wetlands of the Nissequogue River in the Town of Smithtown, is unconstitutional and void (N. Y. Const.,  [***2] art. IX, §§ 2, 3).

 

1624 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sternshuss v. Sternshuss


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


71 Misc. 2d 552; 336 N.Y.S.2d 586; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1507



October 3, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife filed an action against defendant husband for divorce, support, and a ruling that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 230 was unconstitutional. In turn, the husband filed a motion to dismiss the divorce and support claims due to the wife's failure to satisfy § 230(5)'s residency requirement. The husband also sought leave to file a defense claiming lack of in personam jurisdiction, while the New York attorney-general sought leave to intervene.


OVERVIEW: The parties were married in New Jersey and both resided there until their separation, when the wife moved to New York. Because she had not resided in New York for a continuous period of at least two years prior to filing the action, the husband argued that she did not meet the residency requirement of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 230(5). After granting the attorney general leave to intervene, the court ruled that statutes could only be declared invalid in the clearest cases. Finding § 230 constitutional, the court rejected the wife's comparison of it to statutes setting forth residency requirements for welfare eligibility and the exercise of voting rights, noting that denial of the latter during an initial statutory waiting period meant that one subject thereto had irretrievably lost such rights, whereas a divorce action was only postponed by the residency requirements of § 230. Because the jurisdiction of New York courts in matrimonial actions was purely statutory, the general domiciliary law contained in N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 61 was irrelevant. By not arguing that the court lacked in personam jurisdiction over him in his motion to dismiss, the husband waived the issue.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the New York statute setting forth the residency requirements in divorce actions was constitutional. Thus, it dismissed the wife's action for divorce; her action for support was transferred to the county's family court. The court denied the husband's motion for leave to file a defense of lack of in personam jurisdiction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and wife -- matrimonial actions -- Domestic Relations Law ( § 230) validly and constitutionally requires that, if parties were married in another State and cause of action arose there, plaintiff must have resided here for one year; accordingly, her cause of action for divorce is dismissed.

Husband and wife were married in New Jersey in 1969 and they separated there in 1971 and the wife established a residence and domicile in New York in May, 1972 and she commenced this action against him in the New York Supreme Court in 1972 for divorce and support. She has not resided here long enough (Domestic Relations Law, § 230) and hence her action for divorce is dismissed. The statute's residence requirements are constitutional. However, the court transfers her claim for support to the Family Court (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 19, subd. a).

 

1625 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Johnson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Queens County


71 Misc. 2d 423; 336 N.Y.S.2d 179; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1521



September 29, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a charge against her for unauthorized use of a vehicle in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 165.05.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, with her uncle's consent, borrowed his lease car. Defendant was stopped and cited for unauthorized operation of a vehicle. Defendant sought review of the charge levied against her and contended that it was inappropriate because she did have the lessee's consent to use the vehicle and did not deprive the owner of possession. The court agreed with defendant and dismissed the charge against her. The court held that an essential element of the charge included the usage of the vehicle absent the owner's consent and where defendant had the lessee's consent, she could not be guilty of the offense of unauthorized operation of a vehicle. The court reasoned that although the vehicle was leased and the lease agreement forbade anyone other than the lessee to operate the vehicle, a criminal act did not result by defendant's operation of the vehicle. Moreover, the court reasoned that the vehicle was not yet due back to the lessor, therefore no deprivation to the owner of the vehicle occurred. Lastly, the court held that dismissal of the charge was proper in the interest of justice.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the charge of unauthorized use of a motor vehicle against defendant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor vehicles -- unauthorized use of vehicle -- person who drives automobile with consent of lessee, but not of lessor, is not guilty of unauthorized use of vehicle (Penal Law, § 165.05, subd. 1).

An agreement whereby a person rents an automobile from a car rental corporation usually provides that he shall not let anyone else use the automobile. Nevertheless, if he expressly or impliedly does let someone else drive the automobile, such other person is not guilty of the misdemeanor of operating the automobile "without the consent of the owner" (Penal Law, § 165.05, subd. 1).

 

1626 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Creighton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Suffolk County


71 Misc. 2d 370; 336 N.Y.S.2d 73; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1528



September 27, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Submit order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant requested habeas corpus relief under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20, pursuant to his conviction on eight counts of promoting gambling in the second degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 225.05, and his sentence of six months in jail on each count to run concurrently, a fine of $ 1,000 on the first six counts with an alternative of four months' incarceration for failure to pay, to run consecutively to each other and to the six months.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the sheriff had improperly calculated the term of imprisonment as 30 months, but the State maintained that the authorized period of incarceration for the sentences was eight years and the trial court's imposition of an additional 24 month period for defendant's failure to pay the fines was an authorized disposition as it did not exceed the authorized period. In denying defendant the relief sought, the court held that defendant's failure to allege any indigency prevented it from finding that the six four-month incarcerations for the failure to pay the fine were illegal, as the United States Supreme Court had ruled. Thus, the sentence presented no constitutional issues. Further, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 420.10, limiting aggregate sentences to a term less than the maximum imprisonment, and N.Y. Penal Law § 70.30, limiting such sentences to two years, could be reconciled so as to permit the imposition of the 30-month aggregate sentence imposed on defendant. However, in light of the clear intent of the statute that the aggregate sentence of a fine and imprisonment should not exceed the maximum authorized term, the two year limitation of two years also applied.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion for habeas corpus relief, but allowed defendant to include a point urging that his sentence was invalid in his appeals brief.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- on conviction of eight counts of class A misdemeanor, defendant was lawfully sentenced to one term of six months plus six fines, and, in case of nonpayment of fines, to six additional and consecutive terms of four months each -- he is not indigent -- though this sentence lawfully aggregates 30 months, Sheriff shall release him after 24 months (Penal Law, § 70.30, subd. 2, par. [b]) -- writ of habeas corpus transferred to sentencing court (22 NYCRR 683 [a] [1]) -- no resentence needed.

1. The maximum sentence for one class A misdemeanor is one year (Penal Law, § 70.15). The County Court convicted defendant of eight counts of a class A misdemeanor (gambling) and sentenced him to serve eight concurrent terms (Penal Law, § 70.25) of six months each, and to pay, on the first six counts, fines of $ 1,000. The County Court further directed that, if he did not pay the fines, he should be imprisoned in the county jail for not exceeding four months for each of those six counts, and that such four-month sentences be served consecutively to each other and to the six-month jail sentence. Defendant does not allege that he is indigent.  [***2] Though those sentences aggregate a maximum of 30 months, they are proper ( CPL 420.10, subd. 3, pars. [b], [d]); however, they shall be "satisfied * * * by service of two years imprisonment" (Penal Law, § 70.30, subd. 2, par. [b]) -- meaning that the Sheriff shall release the prisoner after two years. No resentencing is needed.

2. The prisoner's writ of habeas corpus was transferred by the Supreme Court to the sentencing court -- the County Court -- as a motion under CPL 440.20 (22 NYCRR 683 [a] [1]).

 

1627 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Metesky


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


71 Misc. 2d 519; 336 N.Y.S.2d 581; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1535



September 26, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion will be considered as one made under section 74 of the Mental Hygiene Law to review by a jury trial the prior order of a Justice of this court dated June 22, 1972 and it is in all respects granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought an order directing a jury trial of his condition of insanity to determine whether he was a "dangerous incapacitated person" whose detention at a state hospital should be continued.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who 15 years earlier had been found to be a "dangerous incapacitated person" and was confined at a state hospital following his indictment for attempted murder, sought a jury trial to determine whether he should continue to be confined. The court agreed, noting that a hearing had been ordered, but had been held without a jury, to hear, and grant, the hospital director's application for an order to continue defendant's custody as a dangerous person. Supporting the established policy of upholding the constitutionality of state statutes whenever possible, especially when all classes of mentally ill were not accorded a jury trial, the court pointed to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 730.60, a provision by which the legislature intended to bar transfer of incapacitated persons to an appropriate institution without the benefit of a jury trial. By also reading into this provision "the retention of" such persons, the court granted mentally ill persons not previously granted a review by jury trial that specific right allowed under N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 74 for orders of retention. Consequently, defendant's motion would be considered as having been made under § 74, the court concluded.


OUTCOME: Order granted directing a jury trial of defendant's condition of insanity to determine whether he was a "dangerous incapacitated person" whose detention at a state mental hospital should be continued, as state statute was construed in a manner that allowed similar treatment of all classes of mentally ill and preserved statute's constitutionality.


HEADNOTES

Mental health -- dangerously mentally ill persons -- jury trial is required to determine whether indicted defendants are dangerously mentally ill, in order that they may be committed to, or retained in, Matteawan State Hospital.

Persons who are under felony indictment, and who are too mentally ill to proceed to a trial thereof, are entitled, before they are committed to Matteawan State Hospital (an institution under the jurisdiction of the Department of Correction), to a jury trial to determine whether they are dangerously mentally ill; and persons who are already in Matteawan State Hospital are entitled to a jury trial to determine whether they are still so dangerously mentally ill as to require their continued retention in such institution under the jurisdiction of the Department of Correction. Their constitutional right to the equal protection of the laws requires that such trial be by jury; and the entire scheme of CPL article 730 and of [***2] sections 74 and 85 of the Mental Hygiene Law indicates that a trial by jury is intended and is required. Thus, the court reads into subdivision 3 of section 730.60 of the CPL, immediately after the words "transfer him to", the words "or retain him at".

 

1628 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Association of Bar of New York v. Lewisohn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


71 Misc. 2d 401; 336 N.Y.S.2d 338; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1564



September 13, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state bar association sought order directing respondents, the city finance administrator and the city tax commission, to remove the bar association's previously exempt real property from the tax rolls.


OVERVIEW: Respondents placed the state bar association's real property on the city's tax rolls. The state bar association sought an order to have the property removed from the tax rolls claiming that the property was used exclusively for educational and charitable purposes and was exempt from taxation pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 1. The state bar association claimed that if N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 421 authorized the city to tax real property used exclusively for educational and charitable purposes, then § 421 was unconstitutional because it conflicted with N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 1. Respondents argued that the categories of dedication for determining whether the use of real property was exempt under § 421 were mutually exclusive. The court ruled that § 421 was constitutionally valid and that there was no incompatibility between simultaneous dedication and use of real property for educational and charitable purposes on the one hand and bar association purposes on the other. Furthermore, the state bar association's property was used exclusively for charitable and educational purposes. Thus, respondents exceeded their statutory authority by placing the property on the city tax rolls.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of the state bar association and ordered respondents to remove the state bar association's previously exempt real property from the city tax rolls as of the date upon which the property was first made subject to taxation.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- exemption -- where petitioner alleges that taxing authority exceeded its jurisdiction in placing petitioner's property on tax rolls, exhaustion of administrative procedures is not required -- petitioner bar association's property prior to 1971 was exempted from taxation as "bar association" in statute granting exemption; by amendment of 1971, 5 categories of users, i.e., religious, charitable, hospital, educational and cemetery, were granted exemption but 15 others' properties could be taxed by municipal corporation by local law; City of New York adopted law subjecting property of 15 categories to taxation; prior exemption in "bar association" group does not bar petitioner from exemption as "charitable" or "educational" corporation -- 5 categories are not mutually exclusive from 15 so that bar association can be charitable or educational; petitioner is charitable or educational corporation and its property is exempt -- if 5 and 15 categories are construed as not mutually exclusive, section 421 of Real Property Tax Law and Local Law No. 46 of 1971 of City of New York are constitutional and valid.

1. In a proceeding alleging that respondent taxing authority has proceeded [***2] in excess of its jurisdiction in placing petitioner's real property on the tax rolls, the failure to exhaust the administrative procedures for review of tax assessments does not bar the seeking of judicial review.

2. In 1938, section 1 of article XVI of the Constitution was adopted and provided that "Exemptions [of real property from taxation] may be altered or repealed except those exempting * * * property used exclusively for religious, educational, or charitable purposes as defined by law and owned by any corporation or association organized or conducted exclusively for one or more such purposes and not operating for profit." By section 421 of the Real Property Tax Law, as amended in 1971, complete exemption from taxation was continued in the three constitutionally protected categories plus two additional categories in the following order: religious, charitable, hospital, educational, cemetery. The next paragraph of said section provided that real property of a corporation or association not organized for these five purposes but for, among others totaling 15, bar association, library, and the enforcement of laws relating to children or animals shall be exempt from taxation;  [***3] "provided, however, that such property shall be taxable by any municipal corporation * * * which * * * adopts a local law * * * so providing." New York City, by Local Law No. 46 of 1971, adopted such law and has placed petitioner's property on the tax rolls. Petitioner's prior exemption in the "bar association" category does not preclude a claim of exemption in the educational and charitable categories at this time.

3. The 5 categories in section 421 are not mutually exclusive from the 15 others. There is no incompatibility between simultaneous dedication and use of real property for "educational" and "charitable" purposes on the one hand and "bar association" purposes on the other. While petitioner's use of the property is for "bar association" purposes, it is also used exclusively for "charitable" and "educational" purposes and therefore its real property is not subject to taxation by the City of New York.

4. As so construed, section 421 of the Real Property Tax Law and Local Law No. 46 of 1971 are constitutional and valid.

 

1629 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Baker v. Schubin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


71 Misc. 2d 262; 335 N.Y.S.2d 671; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1570



September 11, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application is denied and the petition is dismissed accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding (brought in the nature of mandamus), petitioner inmate, whose conditional release had been revoked by parole authorities after eight months and some few days of restricted liberty, sought a judgment directing respondent correctional facility superintendent to credit the inmate with "street time" against his remaining maximum expiration date.


OVERVIEW: The inmate argued that time served as a conditionally released person was part and parcel of his original sentence, since he remained in the legal custody of parole authorities in a "prison without walls." On consideration of the petition, however, the court disagreed and instead dismissed the inmate's claims. Specifically, the court rejected the inmate's arguments that were directed to the constitutionality of a conditional release contract and which argued that the loss of credit for the period the inmate spent conditionally released from a correctional facility was violative of the equal protection clause and a denial of due process. Rather, the court noted that the inmate had to have demonstrated a clear legal right to relief and had to have persuaded the court to exercise its discretion in granting such an order. However, in this situation, the court concluded that the inmate had presented no clear demonstration of unconstitutionality because there were many federal authorities squarely holding that there was no constitutional impediment preventing such treatment. Accordingly, the court denied the inmate's petition.


OUTCOME: The inmate's application was denied and the petition was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- prisoner's conditional release contract, providing that time spent under conditional release would not be credited against his sentence if he were returned to prison for violation of conditions, is constitutional -- mandamus denied.

Petitioner was conditionally released from prison after signing a contract containing the statutory provision (Correction Law, § 827, subd. 5): "I understand and agree that if I am returned to a correctional institution for violation of any of the above conditions, the time spent under conditional release will not be credited against the term of my sentence." Parole authorities revoked his release after he had been at liberty for approximately eight months, and they properly declined to credit the eight-month period against the remainder of his sentence (Penal Law, § 70.40, subd. 3, par. [b]). The cited statutes and contract are constitutional, and petitioner's application for relief in the nature of mandamus is denied.

 

1630 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rosenthal v. Hartnett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


71 Misc. 2d 264; 335 N.Y.S.2d 758; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1581



September 6, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, solely on the ground that the improper quantum of proof was applied, and that section 227 (subd. 1) of the Vehicle and Traffic Law effects an unconstitutional deprivation of due process to those residing in cities of one million or more, the petition is granted, the decision annulled, and the matter remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, petitioner driver sought an order reviewing and annulling a determination by respondents, a hearing officer and the Administrative Adjudication Bureau of the City of New York, reached after a hearing before the hearing officer and affirmed in administrative review proceedings, finding the driver guilty of a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180 (speeding).


OVERVIEW: In his petition, the driver asserted that the determination by the hearing officer and the Bureau was arbitrary and that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 227(1), which prescribed the necessary quantum of proof in the administrative proceedings held, was unconstitutional. On consideration of the petition, the court agreed and held that § 227(1) effected an unconstitutional deprivation of due process to those residing in cities of one million or more. Thus, the court granted the petition and annulled the Bureau's decision. Specifically, the court stated that although like distinctions had been upheld predicated on a recognition of a need to deal with the administrative problems inherent in and unique to heavily populated urban areas, there was a clear distinction between changing procedures or forums to enable a more efficient processing of matters and changes in the quantum of proof necessary for conviction. Accordingly, the court remanded the matter for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court granted the driver's petition, annulled the Bureau's decision, and remanded the matter for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- burden of proof -- traffic infraction must be proved beyond reasonable doubt, even when tried by administrative bureau -- Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 227, subd. 1), providing for merely "clear and convincing evidence" in cities with population of one million or more, is unconstitutional.

Petitioner was found guilty of speeding (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1180), after a hearing before an administrative hearing officer whose determination was affirmed in an administrative review proceeding (Vehicle and Traffic Law, art. 2-A). Section 227 (subd. 1) of said article 2-A of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which provides that, in cities with a population of one million or more, a traffic infraction may be proved "by clear and convincing evidence", is unconstitutional. The proof must [***2] be beyond a reasonable doubt. The administrative decision is annulled and the matter is remanded to the bureau for further proceedings.

 

1631 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Diamond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Long Beach


71 Misc. 2d 311; 335 N.Y.S.2d 711; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1599



August 30, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] I therefore hold that the ordinance as set forth in section 9-101.1 of the Municipal Code is unconstitutional and does deprive defendant of his property and rights without due process of law and, accordingly, the motion by the defendant to dismiss this charge against him is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought a motion to dismiss the charges against him in the State's action for a violation of Long Beach, N.Y., Mun. Code § 9-101.1, which disallowed placement of any "For Sale" or "Sold" signs on any property in a residential district. Defendant sought to have § 9-101.1 declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: While the State argued that a municipality could properly regulate the erection of signs under its police power for the purpose of public safety and welfare and for aesthetic reasons, the court determined that the cases cited involved ordinances which only prohibited the use of signs in specific circumstances, such as where they projected over the streets or sidewalks. Although other decisions indicated that certain zoning ordinances were valid if based solely on aesthetic grounds, the court held that § 9-101.1 could not be considered such an ordinance as it only banned one type of sign. The court found that the same reasoning applied to an argument that the ordinance involved public safety. Based on those determinations, the court held that § 9-101.1 was unconstitutional and deprived defendant of his property and rights without due process of law. Therefore, the court dismissed the charges against defendant.


OUTCOME: The court declared the local ordinance in question was unconstitutional and granted defendant's motion to dismiss the charges against him.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- ordinance prohibiting "For Sale" or "Sold" signs on property in residence district is unconstitutional.

An ordinance of a city which renders it unlawful to place a "For Sale" or "Sold" sign on any property located in a residence district but which does not bar any other sign is unconstitutional. A charge made against defendant of violating the ordinance is dismissed.

 

1632 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Celler v. Larkin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


71 Misc. 2d 17; 335 N.Y.S.2d 791; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1601



August 29, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The constitutional grounds advanced by petitioner in support of his application are also found to be without merit. The petition is therefore dismissed.

Settle order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner losing candidate for the Democratic Party nomination for Congress brought a special proceeding against respondent successful candidate to nullify the results of a primary election and obtain an order directing a new primary election, based on alleged frauds or irregularities and the adoption of certain procedures by a board of elections that allegedly violated statutory requirements and the losing candidate's constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: The successful candidate won by 609 votes. The losing candidate conceded that the successful candidate did not encourage or take part in the alleged frauds and irregularities. Dismissing the petition, the court held that the losing candidate failed to show that the irregularities shown were of such a nature so as to establish the probability that the result of the election would have been changed by a shift in or an invalidation of the votes. The court held, in part, that votes were not void because an election inspector failed to note voting dates or public counter numbers on registration poll records and that registrations did not automatically lapse under N.Y. Elec. Law § 405 when a voter failed to vote in two prior general elections. The court ruled that the losing candidate's contentions that N.Y. Elec. Law § 242-a(7) was unconstitutional and that the board unlawfully delegated to its printer its responsibilities to set up the required alphabetical rotation of candidates on the ballots were not well taken because he could have objected before the election and he took advantage of the fact that his name was at the top of the ballot more often than the successful candidate's.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the losing candidate's petition.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- primary election irregularities -- loser failed to show probability that result was changed by discrepancies between number of public counter votes and number of voters who signed in, or by registration cards attested by only one inspector, or by voters who had not voted in past two years -- rotation of names of candidates in alphabetical order from election district to election district (L. 1972, ch. 146), valid and constitutional.

1. A primary election in New York City in which the winner of the Democratic nomination for Congressman received 15,596 votes as against 14,987 votes, or 609 more than the loser -- will not be set aside on the ground of irregularities, since the loser has not shown the probability, as distinct from a possibility, that the result would be changed if the court were to invalidate or shift irregular votes.

2. The number of public counter votes exceeds, by about 900, the number of voters who signed the registration poll records (the so-called buff cards); and approximately [***2] 50 buff cards are missing; also, petitioner claims that an election inspector failed to note, on perhaps 525 buff cards, the voting dates or the public counter numbers; and some 453 persons voted in the Democratic primary whose enrollments were either blank or void or missing or Liberal or Conservative. The court cannot infer that these human and/or mechanical discrepancies represent any assumed number of persons who voted for either of these Congressional candidates. The court does find that some 50 Democrats who resided in another Congressional district were erroneously permitted to vote in this Congressional district.

3. Some 234 persons who had not voted in a general election during the past two years were permitted to vote in this primary election; however, the Board of Elections had not canceled any of these registrations pursuant to the Election Law ( § 405).

4. The Board of Elections properly deputized volunteer inspectors to register voters at various street locations which it designated as its branch offices, and it properly instructed these volunteers that the registration cards, after being signed by the registrants, must be attested by two inspectors, one from each [***3] major party (cf. Election Law, § 365). In fact, many of the buff cards, though properly signed by the registrants, were then signed by only one inspector. Such irregularity or failure by an employee of the Board of Elections cannot disenfranchise a voter.

5. The Election Law ( § 242-a, subd. 7, as amd. by L. 1972, ch. 146) validly and constitutionally provides that, in primary elections in New York City, the names of candidates shall be rotated in alphabetical order from election district to election district. The Board of Elections did not act unlawfully in leaving it to the printer to set up the required rotation; and in fact, the printer gave the loser the top position on the ballots in 87 election districts as compared with only 82 for the winner.

 

1633 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stievater


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Erie County


71 Misc. 2d 29; 335 N.Y.S.2d 530; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1615



August 24, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion is granted and this indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the indictment against them for conspiracy and bribery.


OVERVIEW: A state grand jury began an investigation into matters related to the county's proposed construction of a domed sports stadium. Federal authorities previously commenced a similar inquiry. The county investigation resulted in the return of an indictment charging defendants with the crimes of conspiracy and bribery. During the course of the simultaneous, but independently conducted investigations, each of defendants were subpoenaed to appear before both the federal and state grand juries. Upon their refusal to testify for federal authorities, defendants were granted immunity from prosecution, pursuant to 18 U.S.C.S. § 2514, and they subsequently testified. The federal investigation resulted in the conviction of two county legislators. Defendants sought dismissal of the state indictment on grounds that they were immune from prosecution under § 2514 because they had received transactional immunity. The court held that § 2514 was a constitutional exercise of power under the Supremacy Clause and, as such, it was capable of precluding state as well as federal prosecution of an immunized witness for any crime to which his testimony referred.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion and dismissed the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- immunity -- indictment of persons who, on account of same "transaction, matter or thing", had been granted full transactional immunity by Federal authorities under United States Code (tit. 18, § 2514), is dismissed.

The United States Code (tit. 18, § 2514), validly and constitutionally provides that, whenever in the judgment of a United States Attorney the testimony of any witness is needed before a Federal grand jury or Federal court, such United States Attorney, upon the approval of the United States Attorney General, shall request the Federal court to instruct the witness to testify despite self incrimination; and further, that, after giving such testimony, "No such witness shall be prosecuted * * * for or on account of any transaction, matter or thing concerning which he is [thus] compelled * * * to testify * * * nor shall testimony so compelled be used as evidence in any criminal proceeding * * * against him in any court." Even though such grant of full transactional immunity may be broader than is constitutionally necessary to eliminate the witness' privilege against self incrimination,  [***2] such full grant of immunity is constitutionally binding upon the State courts, under the supremacy clause of the United States Constitution. Accordingly, the State court dismisses this State indictment of these witnesses, since this indictment covers the same "transaction, matter or thing concerning which" these defendants were compelled to testify before a Federal grand jury.

 

1634 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sanford v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


70 Misc. 2d 833; 335 N.Y.S.2d 502; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1622; 81 L.R.R.M. 2076



August 21, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, so much of respondents' motions as seek to dismiss the petitions on behalf of all others similarly situated are granted, and in all other respects the motions to dismiss are denied, all without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees initiated proceedings, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, on behalf of themselves and all other employees similarly situated, in order to annul the Director of Employee Relations' determination that the employees had engaged in a strike and to declare that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210(1), (2)(b), (d), (e), (f), and (h) was unconstitutional. Respondent governor moved to dismiss the petitions.


OVERVIEW: The Director notified the employees that he had determined that they had engaged in a strike in violation of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210(1). The notice advised that the penalties for such violation included probation for a period of one year following the determination and the loss of two days salary for each day's violation. Each employee contested the Director's determination. The employees were on probation since the date of the Director's initial determination, and each had the monetary penalty deducted from his or her salary. The court held that the system of review by objection under § 210(2), insofar as if failed to provide an opportunity for a hearing before the imposition of the statutory penalties, was inadequate to satisfy the requirements of due process. The provision in § 210(2) permitting the employer to unilaterally dismiss an employee's objection without a hearing was violative of due process. However, the court determined that a class action could not be maintained because the wrongs asserted were individual and the aggrieved persons could determine the remedy for themselves.


OUTCOME: The court granted so much of the motion as sought to dismiss the employees' petitions on behalf of all others similarly situated, but in all other respects the motions to dismiss were denied, all without costs.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- strikes by public employees -- penalties -- provisions of Civil Service Law imposing penalties on public employees who strike unconstitutionally deny due process by failing to accord prior hearing; motions to dismiss proceedings challenging constitutionality of statute are denied -- one petitioner is entitled to judicial review of dismissal of his objection without hearing -- proceedings cannot be maintained as class actions.

1. In these proceedings to annual a determination of the State Director of Employee Relations that petitioners had engaged in a strike and assessing the penalties provided by subdivision 2 of section 210 of the Civil Service Law against them and for judgment declaring subdivision 1 and paragraphs (b), (d), (e), (f), and (h) of subdivision 2 of section 210 of the Civil Service Law unconstitutional, respondents, motions to dismiss the petitions for failing to state grounds for relief are denied. The system for imposing the [***2] penalties for striking against the State fails to provide an opportunity for a hearing before the imposition of the penalties and fails to satisfy the requirements of due process. The provision of subdivision 2 permitting the employer to unilaterally dismiss an employee's objection without a hearing is violative of the requirement of notice and an opportunity to be heard.

2. One petitioner is entitled to judicial review of the dismissal of his objection without a hearing under the statute as it now reads. The motions to dismiss the proceedings as premature are denied.

3. These proceedings may not be maintained as a class action since the wrongs asserted are individual to the different persons involved.

House of Spirits, Inc. v. Doyle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


72 Misc. 2d 1036; 339 N.Y.S.2d 495; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1626



August 18, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff retailer brought an action against defendants, officials and an association, in which the retailer sought a judgment declaring N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 101-bb(2) unconstitutional and enjoining its enforcement. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The statute provided that no licensee authorized to sell liquor at retail for off-premises consumption could sell liquor at a price that was less than cost. The court first stated that the legislative purpose in enacting the statute was to prevent large liquor stores from driving smaller retail stores out of business, and this purpose did not make the statute an invalid exercise of police power. Next, the court rejected the retailer's contention that the legislative determination that 12 percent of the price per bottle was the average minimum overhead necessarily incurred in connection with the sale of a bottle of liquor was an arbitrary and capricious determination violative of due process. Because it had concluded that the purpose of the statute was valid, the court would not substitute its judgment for the legislature to determine whether the statute would accomplish the desired end; moreover, there was a presumption that the legislation was supported by facts known to the legislature. Finally, the court rejected the argument that the statute was discriminatory in that other members of the liquor industry did not have a mandated selling price.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' cross-motion for summary judgment. It declared the statute to be constitutional and valid.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Intoxicating liquors -- minimum prices -- constitutional law -- minimum price of packaged liquor, at 12% above wholesaler's price, valid and constitutional (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 101-bb, subd. 2).

Subdivision 2 (as amd. by L. 1971, ch. 191) of section 101-bb of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law validly and constitutionally requires package liquor stores to sell liquors at no less than 12% above the wholesaler's bottle prices. The statute is a valid exercise of the State's power to police and regulate the liquor business.

 

1636 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jiminez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, Bronx County


71 Misc. 2d 867; 337 N.Y.S.2d 438; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1642



August 11, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, who had pleaded guilty to Class A misdemeanors while they were under the age of 21 years and were sentenced under an alternative reformatory provision, N.Y. Penal Law § 75.20, sought resentencing under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20.


OVERVIEW: After being sentenced under N.Y. Penal Law § 75.20, each defendant began serving his respective sentence. Each defendant reached the age of 21 years during the period of his confinement. In their action for resentencing, defendants alleged that, pursuant to a directive of a city department of corrections, they were transferred from the reformatory to a correctional facility; consequently, defendants contended that they were being deprived of the rehabilitative benefit of a reformatory sentence. Thus, defendants maintained that their sentences had become illegal and that they were being detained without warrant in law. The court noted that although the nature of a sentence was not controlled by the place of confinement, a reformatory-term inmate must be given treatment consistent with his sentence. While the affidavits supporting defendants' applications for resentencing contained merely conclusory allegations that the transfers to adult institutions had frustrated the implementation of defendants' local reformatory sentences, the issue raised was of sufficient importance in the administration of the criminal law to warrant inquiry by the court.


OUTCOME: The court set the matter for a hearing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Prisons and prisoners -- reformatory sentence -- defendants were sentenced to reformatory and during imprisonment reached age 21; thereupon they were transferred to correctional facility; each claims he is deprived of rehabilitative benefit of reformatory sentence; issue is one of treatment and hearing is required to determine facts.

Each defendant, then under 21 years of age, was charged with a felony, but each pleaded guilty to class A misdemeanors for which the maximum sentence was one year. Each was sentenced under the alternative reformatory sentence provision (Penal Law, § 75.20). During imprisonment, each reached 21 of age and each was transferred from a reformatory to a correctional facility and therefore each charges that he is being deprived of the rehabilitative benefit of a reformatory sentence. The issue is one of treatment rather than place of confinement, although the place of confinement may well be one of the factors to be considered in determining whether or not the treatment accorded is appropriate to a reformatory inmate. A hearing must be held to determine the facts.

 

1637 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Graves v. Lombardi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


70 Misc. 2d 1053; 335 N.Y.S.2d 189; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1650



August 9, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers instituted an action for declaratory judgment declaring illegal and invalid a resolution of defendant town board (board) to retain special counsel to oppose the Urban Development Corporation (U.D.C.) and for an injunction restraining the board from retaining a law firm.


OVERVIEW: The matter was before the court on the taxpayers' motion for an injunction pendente lite to enjoin and restrain the board from retaining any law firms and from spending any public funds therefor until the termination of the action. The court denied the motion and found that it could not be determined whether the board would be able to plead a good cause of action in a potential law-suit against the U.D.C. That question would only be resolved if and when a complaint was framed and served upon the U.D.C. and challenged in the courts. The court was loathe to rule that the town was not entitled to retain special counsel to advise it. The court held that a part-time town attorney should not be expected to render single-handedly the legal services necessary to protect fully the interests of the town in a matter as complex and difficult as is the subject of town and U.D.C. relationship.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayer's request for a mandatory injunction in a case to keep the board from retaining counsel.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- retention of special counsel -- town board has standing to maintain action against Urban Development Corporation (U. D. C.) -- retention of special counsel by town to advise it as to its rights and if warranted to commence action against U. D. C. is authorized by law -- even if budget does not provide for payment, town has power to retain special counsel -- use of town money for special counsel is not waste.

1. The town board of a town has standing to maintain an action against the Urban Development Corporation (U. D. C.). The town board must enforce town ordinances and the U. D. C. in constructing buildings in the town must be prevented from violating town zoning laws and other town laws.

2. The town board is authorized to employ counsel and special counsel if needed. The town board has retained special counsel to advise its members of their rights, duties, liabilities and disabilities in respect to the proposed or possible entry of the U. D. C. into their rural or suburban town. The town board intends to bring an action against the U. D. C. if such legal advice warrants [***2] it. The retention of special counsel for that purpose is expressly authorized under section 20 (subd. 2, par. [a]) of the Town Law.

3. Even though the town's budget does not provide for the hiring of special counsel, the town has power to provide appropriations for expenditures for which an insufficient or no provision has been made in the annual budget.

4. The use of town money for retaining special counsel is not a waste of assets.

 

1638 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. De Perez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Ontario County


70 Misc. 2d 982; 335 N.Y.S.2d 526; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1659



August 7, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For all of the foregoing reasons the order appealed from is in all respects affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the City Court of Canandaigua (New York), judgment that denied his motion to set aside a sentence pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20 on the grounds that the sentence of a reformatory term of imprisonment was invalid as a matter of law in violation of the Equal Protection Clause.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted on his plea of guilty to possession of a weapon in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05(3). He was not treated as a youthful offender, but was sentenced as a young adult to a reformatory term of imprisonment. Appellant moved to set aside the sentence pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.20 on the grounds that the sentence of a reformatory term of imprisonment was illegal and invalid as a violation of the Equal Protection Clause. The motion was denied. Appellant contended on appeal that N.Y. Penal Law § 75.00 violated his equal protection rights insofar as a person adjudged a youthful offender for committing acts which would constitute a misdemeanor could not be sentenced to a reformatory term of imprisonment. The court affirmed the order, holding that appellant's claim was not the law on the point and thus failed. The due process guarantees of federal and state constitutions did not prevent a change in the law, and a regulation was not necessarily rendered invalid because, incidental to its prospective operation, it was written to apply only to persons coming within a particular category or class for the first time on or after the effective date.


OUTCOME: Order denying appellant's motion to set aside his sentence as invalid was affirmed because to accept appellant's position would mean no court could on a misdemeanor conviction sentence either a young adult between 16 and 19 years of age or a youthful offender to a reformatory term, but would be limited to sentencing either category to a year in the county jail. Such a view was not logical in light of the purpose of a reformatory sentence.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- reformatory sentence -- section 75.00 of Penal Law authorizing reformatory term lasting four years for persons between 16 and 19 years of age who are convicted of misdemeanors and not treated as youthful offenders is constitutional.

 Section 75.00 of the Penal Law, insofar as it authorizes a reformatory term, which could have a maximum duration of four years, for persons between the ages of 16 and 19 years who are convicted of misdemeanors and not treated as youthful offenders, is constitutional, despite the fact that, if treated as a youthful offender, the term of imprisonment would be one year in the county jail.

In re Estate of Belton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


70 Misc. 2d 814; 335 N.Y.S.2d 177; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1666



August 4, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an accounting proceeding involving an intestate estate in the hands of a public administrator, objectant claimant, who alleged that she was the decedent's out-of-wedlock daughter, contended that she was the sole distributee. The public administrator and the state attorney general resisted her claim.


OVERVIEW: Under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2(2) and (3), an illegitimate child could inherit from his intestate father only if a court had made an order of filiation declaring paternity in a proceeding instituted during the mother's pregnancy or within two years from the birth of the child. The claimant, who produced an affidavit by the decedent admitting paternity, school report cards signed by the decedent as her father, and her birth certificate showing the decedent as her father, acknowledged that no paternity proceeding had ever been brought. She argued that § 4-1.2(2) and (3) were unconstitutional. Finding that the statute did not impose an improper condition and did not violate equal protection, the court stated that in enacting the statute, the legislature had been concerned with guarding against false paternity claims and harassing litigation; thus, there was good reason for requiring a judicial determination of paternity and for prescribing a limitation of time. The court noted that the United States Supreme Court had specifically denied illegitimate children the right to take under state laws of succession.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the claimant's objections.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- inheritance by illegitimate child -- statutory provision that illegitimate child may inherit from father if order of filiation declaring paternity was made in proceeding instituted during pregnancy of mother or within two years from birth is not unconstitutional -- in absence of such order of filiation, claimant who alleges that she is decedent's daughter, born out of wedlock, and his sole distributee, and has offered proof that decedent was her father, has not established her status as his distributee.

1. EPTL 4-1.2 (subd. [a], par. [2]), providing that "An illegitimate child is the legitimate child of his father so that he and his issue inherit from his father if a court of competent jurisdiction has, during the lifetime of the father, made an order of filiation declaring paternity in a proceeding instituted during the pregnancy of the mother or within two years from the birth of the child", is not unconstitutional.

2. In the absence of an order of filiation, a claimant who alleges that she is decedent's daughter, born out of wedlock, his sole distributee, and, as such, entitled to the balance of his intestate estate, and who has offered testimony by her mother [***2] as to her parentage, her birth certificate bearing decedent's name as father, an affidavit, made by him at the time of her birth, admitting paternity, and her school report cards, signed by him as her father, has not established her status as his distributee.

Spatt v. State


Claim No. 54967*


* See, also, Spratt v. State of New York, 361 F. Supp. 1048, affd. 414 U.S. 1058, decided December 3, 1973.


Court of Claims of New York


76 Misc. 2d 114; 350 N.Y.S.2d 280; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1203



August 4, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] By reason of the foregoing, the claim is dismissed. Disposition of Motions No. M-14553 and No. M-14565 is, accordingly, moot and they are dismissed for such reason.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant, who applied for and was refused a state regent's scholarship, brought an action against defendant State, seeking the value of the scholarship he was denied and damages for the denial. The claimant moved for a preference for the trial of the claim and for a change of venue. The State cross-moved to dismiss the claim pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a).


OVERVIEW: The claimant opted to matriculate to an institution of higher learning outside of the State. Thus, the State's education department advised the claimant that the regent's scholarship was unavailable to him pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 602(5)(b). In his subsequent action for damages against the State, the claimant contended that the statute was unconstitutional because it was discriminatorily applied. In granting the State's motion to dismiss, the court held that the claimant was not entitled to damages because the certification of recipients for the scholarship was a governmental function that was not ministerial but quasi-judicial in nature. Moreover, the State had not waived its sovereign immunity with respect to the exercise of the governmental function. Even if the court had jurisdiction to entertain the claimant's action, it would still have dismissed the action because the statute challenged was not discriminatory. The regulations classifying students as residents or nonresidents for tuition purposes were not arbitrary because they bore a rational limitation to the State's objective purpose of operating its publicly financed institutions of higher learning.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the claimant's action.


HEADNOTES

Colleges and universities -- State scholarships -- procedure for review of action of State through its officers and employees with regard to implementation of legislative directives is by way of Supreme Court action and not Court of Claims -- statute (Education Law, § 602, subd. 5, par. b) which provides that recipient of State-funded scholarship must be matriculated in institution situated within State is constitutional.

1. Procedure for review of the action of the State through its officers and employees with regard to the implementation of legislative directives is by way of a Supreme Court action and not a demand for damages in the Court of Claims. Sovereign immunity not having been waived with respect to individual wrongs which may have resulted from the exercise of judgment by an officer of the State in the performance of his duty, the Court of Claims is without jurisdiction and the claim is dismissed.

2. Assuming the Court of Claims did have jurisdiction, section 602 of the Education Law (subd. 5,  [***2] par. b) which provides that a recipient of a State-funded scholarship ("Regents Scholarship") must be matriculated in an institution situated within the State is constitutional. The statute is analogous to regulations classifying students as residents or nonresidents for tuition purposes, which are not arbitrary or unreasonable and bear a rational limitation to the State's objective and purpose of financing, operating and maintaining its publicly-financed institutions of higher learning.

 

1641 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Taylor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Dutchess County


70 Misc. 2d 970; 335 N.Y.S.2d 324; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1673



August 2, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the foregoing reasons, the motion to dismiss the sixth count of the indictment is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 210.20, for an order dismissing the first count of an indictment for failing to state the supposed drug sale was to a ''person" or the name of that person, contrary to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 200.50(7), and dismissing the sixth count for failing to state a crime or, in the alternative, that N.Y. Penal Law § 220.50(2) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant moved for an order dismissing the first and sixth counts of an indictment against him. He argued the first count, charging him with the crime of criminally selling a dangerous drug, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.35, should have been dismissed for failure to state that the supposed sale was to a "person" or the name of that person, contrary to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 200.50(7). He argued the sixth count, accusing him of criminally using drug paraphernalia, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.50(2), should have been dismissed for failure to state a crime or, alternatively, that the section was unconstitutional. The court denied the motion. The charge proscribed in the first count was unmistakably a sale of marihuana. To hold the count defective for not stating that the sale was made to a "person" was ludicrous. The conduct proscribed in the sixth count was not the possession of an innocuous plastic bag, but rather unlawfully packaging and dispensing a narcotic drug contrary to the statute's purposes. That the prosecution might have had a heavy burden was insufficient to declare unconstitutional a statute that was definite, precise, and reasonable.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss the counts was denied because the first count of the indictment met the standards established by decisional and statutory law, even though the name of the person to whom the sale was made was omitted, and the sixth count was not vague and was a reasonable restriction upon a person's activities in the interest of the public good and, accordingly, was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- dangerous drugs -- Penal Law ( § 220.50, subd. 2), prohibiting possession or sale of material intended and suitable for narcotic use, is constitutional -- Penal Law ( § 220.35) prohibits "selling a dangerous drug"; indictment need not name purchaser -- but defendant is entitled to bill of particulars naming purchaser.

1. The Penal Law ( § 220.50) validly and constitutionally provides: "A person is guilty of criminally using drug paraphernalia * * * when he knowingly possesses or sells * * * 2. Gelatin capsules, glassine envelopes or any other material suitable for the packaging of individual quantities of narcotic drugs under circumstances evincing an intent to use * * * the same for the purpose of unlawfully manufacturing, packaging or dispensing of any narcotic drug." The statute is not too vague.

2. The Penal Law ( § 220.35) also validly prohibits "criminally selling a dangerous drug" (cf. Penal Law, § 220.00, subd. 5). The indictment is sufficiently "factual" (cf. CPL 200.50, subd. 7) even though it does not name the person to whom defendant sold the drug. Motion [***2] to dismiss the indictment is denied.

3. However, defendant is entitled to a bill of particulars naming the purchaser (cf. CPL 200.90).

Saratoga Springs Mun. Civil Serv. Comm'n v. New York State Civil Serv. Comm'n


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Saratoga County


70 Misc. 2d 744; 334 N.Y.S.2d 711; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1687



July 27, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Municipal Civil Service Commission filed a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 seeking to force respondent New York State Civil Service Commission to approve a rule establishing the jurisdictional classification of one of its officers, the position of City Engineer, as noncompetitive.


OVERVIEW: The city commission held a meeting at which a resolution was adopted placing the position of city engineer in the noncompetitive classification. The resolution was sent to the state commission for its approval as required by N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 20(2). The state commission declined to approve the resolution upon the ground that it was practicable to fill this position by competitive examination. The court dismissed the city's petition, holding that ample time was provided to the city commission to present its position and therefore a hearing and notice were not required prior to a final determination by the state. The court concluded that it could not find that the state's refusal to approve the resolution was arbitrary, and absent such a showing by the city the court dismissed the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the city's petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- noncompetitive class positions -- city's Civil Service Commission has power to bring proceeding challenging as arbitrary State Civil Service Commission's refusal to approval rule placing position of City Engineer in noncompetitive class -- in absence of showing of special circumstances, State Commission did not act arbitrarily in denying approval -- hearing on notice and representation at meeting of State Commission was not required.

1. The principle that a local agency of the State receives its powers from the State and that it cannot question any diminution in those powers does not apply to the exercise by petitioner of its rule-making power by placing the position of the City Engineer in the noncompetitive class. It therefore has standing to bring this proceeding charging that respondent State Civil Service Commission acted arbitrarily in refusing to approve such rule.

2. No reason for making the position noncompetitive is given other than what is stated in the rule itself. Respondent from its experience states that such position can be filled by competitive examination unless some special circumstances are shown. On this [***2] record respondent has not acted arbitrarily in refusing to approve the rule.

3. Petitioner was given ample time to show that special circumstances exist. A hearing on notice and representation at the meeting at which respondent acted was not required.
