Neil M. v. Gregory M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Bronx County


71 Misc. 2d 396; 336 N.Y.S.2d 304; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1688



July 26, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The warrant is vacated; the order of protection is confirmed; no further action appears warranted by law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner foster parent sought confirmation of a warrant and an order of protection issued against respondent foster child.


OVERVIEW: The foster child was repeatedly abusive to his foster parents. A warrant was issued and the foster child was excluded from the foster home by an order of protection. The foster child was 16 years old. The court held that it had no power to place the child in a facility conducive to his social and psychological development under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 842. The court held that lacking any other alternative, the warrant was vacated and the order of protection was confirmed. The court held that although the child was in need of assistance, no further action was warranted by law.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the warrant and confirmed the order of protection is confirmed.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Family Court -- in family offense proceeding involving boy just over 16 years of age who assaulted his adoptive parents, because of restrictions contained in section 842 of Family Court Act, Family Court lacks ability to deal with boy.

In a family offense proceeding under article 8 of the Family Court Act involving a boy just over 16 years of age who assaulted his adoptive parents, section 842 of the Family Court Act outlines the court's alternatives, one of which is an order of protection, a disposition already made. That leaves the boy outside the home and unsupervised. He refuses placement arranged for him by an agency and no relative is available for the boy's placement. Section 842 prohibits the court from placing any child in an institution or agency in this type of proceeding. Were the boy under 16 years of age, he could have been adjudicated a person in need of supervision and the court would have had jurisdiction to place him in a suitable facility. Since section 842 offers the court no alternative, nothing can be done for the boy. The Legislature [***2] is urged to amend section 842 so as to remove the curbs on the court's ability to deal with a boy over 16 years of age and not yet 18.

 

1644 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Farr v. Mancusi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Wyoming County


70 Misc. 2d 830; 335 N.Y.S.2d 161; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1697



July 24, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who had been convicted of the crime of sodomy second degree, challenged his conviction on grounds that N.Y. Penal Law § 690 was unconstitutional. He contended, in part, that the statute was unconstitutionally vague because it did not clearly advise him of what his crime was. He claimed that the statute did not tell him what "carnal knowledge" was or how one had "carnal knowledge" by the anus or mouth.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected defendant's contention that N.Y. Penal Law § 483-b, which involved abusing the body of a child 10 to 16 years of age, was also unconstitutionally confusing because the acts prohibited in § 483-b were clearly distinguishable from the acts covered in the sodomy statute. There was no evidence that defendant's crime involved a child or acts other than sodomous intercourse. The court held that the statute was not vague. Beginning from the presumption that a statute was constitutional, the court noted that although the term "carnal knowledge" was old fashioned, it was commonly understood to connote sexual intercourse. The statutory term had been used for so long that it had to be considered to have a definite meaning. Therefore, the language sufficiently conveyed a warning as to the proscribed conduct when measured by common understanding and practices.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition challenging his conviction for sodomy.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sodomy -- relator, convicted of sodomy in second degree under section 690 of former Penal Law, as it read in 1963, contends that language contained in that section was so vague as to render it unconstitutional -- his petition is denied.

A relator, who was convicted of the crime of sodomy in the second degree under section 690 of the former Penal Law, as it read in 1963, contends that language contained in that section was so vague as to render it unconstitutional. His petition is denied.

 

1645 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hvizd


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Westchester County


70 Misc. 2d 654; 334 N.Y.S.2d 534; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1724



July 10, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An order was issued with respect to defendant's discriminatory conduct. Defendant filed motions to inspect the minutes of the grand jury, to dismiss the indictment against him based upon insufficient evidence and on the ground that N.Y. Exec. Law § 299 was unconstitutional, and to grant him the names and addresses of all witnesses known to the district attorney.


OVERVIEW: The New York State Division of Human Rights determined that there was probable cause to believe that defendant engaged in unlawful discriminatory practices. The Division of Human Rights filed a petition in an appellate court seeking enforcement of the order. The appellate court affirmed the order. Defendant sought to inspect the grant jury minutes and dismiss the indictment. The court held that the New York Criminal Procedure Law provided no mechanism whereby defendant could inspect the grand jury minutes prior to trial. Defendant's constitutional rights were not abridged by the procedure established or the procedure followed in the case. The enforcement order was valid. N.Y. Exec. Law § 299 established a sanction for failure to comply with the enforcement order. The indictment was sufficient to inform the defendant of the crimes with which he was being charged and to protect him against double jeopardy. The names and addresses of prospective witnesses were not subject to pretrial discovery.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motions to inspect the grand jury minutes, dismiss the indictment, and grant him the names and addresses of all witnesses known to the district attorney.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil rights -- discriminatory conduct -- in penal action to enforce sanctions against unlawful discriminatory conduct, no facts alleged to show that Grand Jury evidence is insufficient -- section 299 of Executive Law is constitutional -- indictment is sufficient -- defendant is not entitled to discovery of names and addresses of witnesses -- statements of witnesses cannot be discovered -- materiality of criminal records of prospective witnesses has not been shown -- defendant is entitled to copy of order of Commissioner of Human Rights -- no ex parte eavesdropping order has been obtained.

1. Since defendant has alleged no facts indicating that there is reasonable cause to believe that the Grand Jury evidence was not legally sufficient to support the indictment, defendant's motion to inspect the minutes of the Grand Jury is denied.

2. A determination of the State Division of Human Rights that defendant had engaged in unlawful discriminatory practices was affirmed by the Appellate Division and an enforcement order was issued. The law against discriminatory behavior is constitutional. Section 299 of the Executive Law which provides penal sanctions for noncompliance with the [***2] enforcement order is constitutional.

3. The indictment is sufficient to inform defendant of the crimes with which he is being charged and to protect him against double jeopardy.

4. The names and addresses of prospective witnesses are not subject to pretrial discovery.

5. Paragraph (b) of subdivision 3 of section 240.10 of the CPL provides that statements of witnesses are exempt property. Defendant is not entitled to copies.

6. Defendant fails to show the materiality and reasonableness of a request for the criminal records of any of the People's witnesses. The People are under a pre-existing duty to disclose exculpatory evidence.

7. A copy of the order of the Commissioner of Human Rights described in the indictment will be served on defendant.

8. The People aver that there has been no ex parte eavesdropping order and therefore defendant is not entitled to a copy thereof.

 

1646 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Norwick v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


70 Misc. 2d 923; 334 N.Y.S.2d 571; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1727



July 10, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motions to dismiss the complaint for lack of standing to sue are denied, but it is declared that the messages of the Governor in the forms used by him are constitutionally sufficient and the related legislation was validly enacted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, state civil liberties union and national welfare organizations, initiated an action in the court (New York) against defendants, governor, secretary of state, and associated individuals, contending that the governor's emergency messages were unconstitutional. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss for lack of standing to sue.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs initiated an action against defendants contending that that governor's emergency messages were unconstitutional. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss for lack of standing to sue. The court determined that the test for standing to challenge the integrity of the legislative process was that of personal aggrievement. The court found that standing required that the party seeking relief have such a personal stake in the outcome of the controversy as to assure that concrete adverseness depended for illumination of difficult constitutional questions. The court held that plaintiffs had standing to challenge the emergency messages. However, the court determined that the emergency messages and the associated legislation was valid and followed the enactment procedures of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 14. The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss but declared that the emergency messages used by the governor were constitutionally sufficient and that the legislation allowing such messages was validly enacted.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss for lack of standing. The court held, however, that the emergency messages used by the governor were constitutionally sufficient and that the legislation allowing such messages was validly enacted.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- consideration of legislative bills -- message of necessity -- plaintiffs have standing to challenge constitutionality of 79 laws enacted with aid of Governor's message of necessity -- Constitution provides that Legislature may consider bills not on desks in final printed form for three days if Governor presents message of necessity with "facts" which necessitate immediate vote; in connection with 79 laws, message recited that it was requested by leaders of both houses; messages sufficiently recite "facts"; legislation was validly enacted.

1. A challenge to 79 laws enacted at the 1971 session of the Legislature following the issuance of messages of necessity by the Governor in purported compliance with section 14 of article III of the Constitution may be maintained by three organizations, two Senators, four Assemblymen, a recipient of welfare, and a journalist. They have standing because of a discernible outlook for [***2] a liberalization of the doctrine of standing, because of the variety of interests represented by the various plaintiffs, some of whom appear to be directly affected by some of the laws and because of the importance of the main issues involved.

2. The Constitution requires that a bill in final printed form be on the desks of the legislators for three days before it be considered for passage but immediate consideration may be given a bill if the Governor issues a message of necessity wherein he sets forth "the facts which in his opinion necessitate an immediate vote". In connection with the 79 measures under attack, the Governor's message stated that because the measure had not been on the legislative desk three days "the Leaders of your Honorable bodies have requested this message to permit immediate consideration of the bill prior to your anticipated final adjournment". Under the holding in Sullivan County Harness Racing Assn. v. New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Comm. (30 N Y 2d 207), and under the doctrine of separation of powers and absent criteria in the Constitution defining "the facts" which in the Governor's opinion require his message of necessity, their [***3] sufficiency is unassailable. It is declared that the messages of the Governor in the forms used by him are constitutionally sufficient and the legislation was validly enacted.

 

1647 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Greenwald v. Frank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


70 Misc. 2d 632; 334 N.Y.S.2d 680; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1759



June 29, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner policeman brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding alleging that Rule 22 of article VII of the Rules and Regulations of the Nassau County Police Department was arbitrary and capricious. The action was brought against respondent commissioner of police and the county police department.


OVERVIEW: The policeman, as president of a policemen's association, challenged Rule 22 of article VII of the Rules and Regulations of the Nassau County Police Department. Rule 22 contained grooming standards regarding the length of hair, sideburns, moustaches, beards, and goatees. The policeman contended that the grooming standards deprived all policemen of their right of freedom of choice of appearance. The police commissioner argued that the police department had an interest in a disciplined and efficient police department and that the limitation on appearance related to the maintenance of discipline. The court held that the policeman's private interest in determining his own personal appearance was offset by the powerful countervailing interest of the police department and of the general public. The maintenance of a disciplined and efficient police department, in which uniformed policemen maintained a standard of appearance that connoted public confidence, acceptance, and respect, constituted a powerful countervailing interest.


OUTCOME: The court held that the commissioner of police and the police department were entitled to summary judgment declaring the regulation in its present form valid.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- grooming standards for policemen -- regulation of county police department containing grooming standards relating to hair on face and head of policemen is constitutionally valid.

A regulation of the Nassau County Police Department containing grooming standards relating to hair on the face and head of policemen employed by the department is constitutionally valid. A policeman while in uniform does not have a constitutional right to determine his own appearance.

Stony Point v. McDonnell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rockland County


70 Misc. 2d 490; 333 N.Y.S.2d 826; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1771



June 26, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town filed an action for a permanent and temporary injunction restraining defendant chairman from continuing to serve as Chairman of the Zoning Board of Appeals of the Town of Stony Point (New York) and directing him to relinquish said position to the chairman as duly appointed by the town board.


OVERVIEW: Both the zoning board and the town board appointed parties to serve as the Chairman of the Zoning Board of Appeals of the Town of Stony Point. The town filed an action for a temporary injunction pendente lite restraining the chairman from continuing to serve as such, and for an order directing the chairman to relinquish his position to the party that was duly appointed by the town board. The court granted the injunction. First, the court noted that the town board was granted the power to appoint a party to aforementioned position under N.Y. Town Law § 267(1). Further, the court held that said power could not be delegated. Therefore, the court held unconstitutional the portion of Stony Point, N.Y., Ordinance § 39-72 that granted said authority to the zoning board.


OUTCOME: The court granted a temporary injunction restraining the chairman from continuing to serve as chairman of the zoning board of appeals and directed him to relinquish said position to the party duly appointed by the town board.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- designation of Chairman of Zoning Board of Appeals -- under Town Law (§ 267, subd. 1) Town Board has power to appoint Chairman of Zoning Board of Appeals and ordinance purporting to empower members of Zoning Board of Appeals to designate chairman is invalid -- injunction pendente lite issued against defendant, chairman designated by Zoning Board of Appeals.

1. Under subdivision 1 of section 267 of the Town Law, a town board is given the power to appoint a Board of Appeals and to designate its chairman and that portion of a town zoning ordinance which established a Board of Appeals and empowered the five members to name one of their number as chairman is invalid.

2. Since plaintiff has alleged that the Board of Appeals has been unable to function and there are many applications pending undetermined, plaintiff has met the tests therefor and is granted an injunction pendente lite restraining defendant from acting as chairman and directing him to relinquish his position to the chairman designated by the town board.

People v. Steele


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


70 Misc. 2d 351; 333 N.Y.S.2d 959; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1773



June 23, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The complaints are dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, seven nuns and one laywoman, caused a disturbance of a religious service by lying in a cathedral's center aisle during high mass without permission or authority, and prosecution charged them with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 240.21, describing a crime denominated as "aggravated disorderly conduct." Defendants moved for dismissal, asserting that statute violated freedom of religion under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: The defendants, seven nuns and one laywoman, were charged with a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.21, which read "Disruption, or disturbance of religious service" and denominated the crime described therein as "aggravated disorderly conduct." Although the church authorities made it plain that they did not wish to press charges, the prosecution alleged that defendants were lying in the center aisle of St. Patrick's Cathedral during a high mass and thereby caused a disturbance of said religious service without permission or authority. There was no allegation of noise or shouting. Based on the complainant's having withdrawn the charge, defendants contended that the complaints should be dismissed. Defendants further argued that the statute violated both the Free Exercise and Establishment Clauses of U.S. Const. amend. I. Because a statute was not to be declared unconstitutional where the decision in the matter might be rested on an alternative ground, the court dismissed the complaints because to reach these questions of religious controversies would require the court to engage in the forbidden process of interpreting and weighing church doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court, refusing to engage in forbidden process of interpreting and weighing church doctrine, dismissed "aggravated disorderly conduct" charges against defendant nuns and laywoman who lay down in cathedral's center aisle during high mass.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- disruption of religious service -- nuns and Catholic lay teacher, charged with lying in center aisle of cathedral during high mass, in violation of Penal Law ( § 240.21, entitled disturbance of religious service), allege that what they did was proper under church law; complaints are dismissed; court will not interpret church doctrine.

The complaints, made by police officers, against seven nuns and one Roman Catholic lay teacher charging them with violation of section 240.21 of the Penal Law, which bears the catchline "Disruption, or disturbance of religious service", in that they were "lying in the center aisle of St. Patrick's Cathedral during a high mass" are dismissed. Defendants allege that what they did, under church law, was proper and within established Catholic tradition and ritual. The court will not engage in the process of interpreting and weighing church doctrine.

 

1650 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Di Lorenzo v. Endicott


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Broome County


70 Misc. 2d 159; 333 N.Y.S.2d 456; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1800



June 16, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant village brought a motion to dismiss the action of plaintiffs, the father and natural guardian of an infant child, for bodily injuries suffered by the child when a metal sewer grating broke in a gutter where the child was walking.


OVERVIEW: An infant child was walking in a gutter in a village roadway. When he stepped on a sewer grating, the grating broke, and caused the child to fall and suffer bodily injuries. The child's father and natural guardian brought an action against the village, claiming that the grating was in such disrepair that the village either knew or should have known of its condition. The court denied the village's motion to dismiss the father's action, and held that the ordinance, Village of Endicott, N.Y., Village Law § 341-a, which required actual notice of a defect in order to sustain a cause of action was limited to roadways and culverts. The ordinance did not include defects in the village sewer system. Therefore, the court ruled that a metal sewer grating in the gutter area was a "special use," and actual notice of the defect was not required to maintain an action against the village.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion of the village to dismiss the action for bodily injuries brought on behalf of the injured child.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- prior written notice under Village Law ( § 341-a) was not required to be served with respect to defective metal sewer grating since it was not part of street.

A metal sewer grating in the gutter area of a street in a village was not a part of the surface of any street, culvert, sidewalk or crosswalk as to which prior written notice of defects therein must be given under section 341-a of the Village Law. Moreover, a metal sewer grating in the gutter area is a "special use" not requiring prior written notice. Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint in an action to recover damages for injuries sustained by infant plaintiff by stepping on a grate which broke under his weight, is denied.

Estate of Schloessinger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


70 Misc. 2d 206; 333 N.Y.S.2d 683; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1821



June 12, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the death of the testatrix, her will was admitted to probate.


OVERVIEW: The testatrix died and her will was admitted to probate. After the testatrix's death, the beneficiary of the trust the testatrix had created in the will, by written instrument, renounced her right to the trust income. The court found that N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 3-3.10 provided that, unless a testator had otherwise provided, the filing of a renunciation had the same effect with respect to the renounced interest as though the renouncing party had predeceased the testator. The court held that because the will created by the testatrix contained no provision governing a possible renunciation of a testamentary interest, § 3-3.10 was applicable even though the section went into effect after the testatrix had executed her will. The court concluded that because the right to dispose of property by will was acquired by statute, legislation applied to a will executed prior to the effective date of a statute. The court further held that the effect of the renunciation was to terminate the life estate as of the date of the death of the testatrix and that the principal sum otherwise allocable to the trust was payable to the persons entitled to the next eventual estate.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the principal sum allocated to the trust beneficiary was payable to the persons entitled to the next eventual estate.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Wills -- renunciation of bequest -- EPTL 3-3.10 which became effective prior to testatrix' death applies to her will -- section provides that renunciation has same effect as though renouncer predeceased testatrix; daughter renounced interest in trust; her children are entitled to principal.

1. Testatrix created a trust for her daughter with remainder to the daughter's three children. Testatrix died on January 2, 1972. EPTL 3-3.10 became effective on November 3, 1971 and provided that the filing of a renunciation has the same effect as to the renounced interest as though the renouncing party had predeceased testator. Testatrix' daughter renounced her right to income which was filed on February 25, 1972. Since testatrix died after the effective date of the statute, it applies to her will.

2. The effect of the renunciation was to terminate the life estate as of the date of death of testatrix and the principal must be paid to the three children of the daughter as the persons entitled to the next eventual estate.

Spencer v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Schenectady County


69 Misc. 2d 1091; 333 N.Y.S.2d 308; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1827



June 9, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minor brought a proceeding to set aide the action of respondent Board of Education of the City of Schenectady, New York. The minor alleged that the board's action in locking the minor's name off the ballot violated her constitutional rights under the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and under the Twenty-sixth Amendment of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The minor filed petitions containing a sufficient number of signatures to be designated as a candidate for election to the Board. The Board opposed the application on the ground that the court was without jurisdiction to hear and determine the issues involved. The Board argued that the dispute had to be referred to the Commissioner of Education, and that the commissioner's determination was exclusive and final. The court found that it had jurisdiction of the subject matter because the questions raised by the pleadings concerned the interpretation of statutes and the constitutional rights of the minor. The court further found that the minor was a qualified candidate and that the action of the Board in locking the minor's name off the ballot was in violation of state law and the United States Constitution. The court directed that a new election be held. The Board's action was without proper authority because N.Y. Elec. Law § 145 was not followed. Additionally, under N.Y. Educ. Law § 2012(2) the minor was a qualified candidate and that statute took precedence over a more general statute on the subject, N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 3(1).


OUTCOME: The court determined that the minor was a qualified candidate and the Board improperly locked the minor's name off the ballot. The court directed that a new election be held.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- candidates for Board of Education -- 18-year-old citizen is entitled to be candidate for member of Board of Education (Education Law, art. 53; U. S. Const., 26th Amdt.) -- new election shall be held.

In a city school district with less than 125,000 inhabitants, a qualified candidate for member of the Board of Education cannot be denied a place on the ballot, merely because she is only 18 years old. It was unlawful and unconstitutional of the Board of Education to block off her name on the ballots and to lock the voting key below her name on the voting machines. The court directs that a new election be held. (U. S. Const., 26th, 14th and 1st Amdts.; Education Law, art. 53, § 2502, subd. 7; § 2608, subds. 1, 2; § 2609, subd. 1; Election Law, §§ 145, 330; cf. Public Officers Law, § 3, subd. 1; Education Law, § 2037; § 2012, subd. 2; § 2603, subd. 2.)

People v. Lewis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


70 Misc. 2d 41; 332 N.Y.S.2d 929; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1851



May 31, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with loitering in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35, subd. 5. Before the court for determination was the issue of defendant's guilt.


OVERVIEW: The stipulated facts were that defendant and another passed out leaflets on a public sidewalk. The leaflets urged participation in a demonstration to call for an end to American involvement in a war. Although the leaflets were given to all pedestrians, defendant's aim was to distribute the leaflets to high school students. Defendant was arrested after he refused to leave when told so by a police officer. Defendant was then charged with loitering. He contended that § 240.35, subd. 5 violated the First Amendment's guarantee of free speech in that § 240.35 prohibited defendant, who was rightfully on a public street for a legitimate purpose, from exercising his rights to express his views in an orderly fashion. The court adjudged defendant guilty. The court held that § 240.35 did allow the expression of First Amendment rights with the written permission of an authorized person in accordance with the rules and regulations. The statute did not lay down a blanket prohibition against all forms of picketing or demonstrating. There was no evidence as to whether defendant even applied for permission to exercise his rights, or that any rules were not proper and reasonable guidelines.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant guilty.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- loitering on school grounds -- person who handed out anti-war leaflets on public sidewalk in front of high school was found guilty of loitering there (Penal Law, § 240.35, subd. 5).

The Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 5) provides that "A person is guilty of loitering when he * * * 5. Loiters or remains in or about a school * * * building or grounds, not having any reason or relationship involving custody of or responsibility for a pupil * * * or any other * * * legitimate reason for being there, and not having written permission from anyone authorized to grant the same". This validly and constitutionally applies to a person who stood peacefully on the public sidewalk in front of a high school and who did not block pedestrians and who handed out anti-Vietnam war leaflets to students and any other pedestrians who would accept the leaflets. This person is found guilty.

 

1654 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Jennifer S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


69 Misc. 2d 951; 333 N.Y.S.2d 79; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1854



May 31, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A previous opinion was made in connection with the motion to dismiss the proceeding on the ground that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384 was unconstitutional in that its provisions violated the procedural and substantive constitutional rights of petitioner natural mother and, that motion having been denied, a hearing on the issue of abandonment of the infant was held.


OVERVIEW: The issues were: (a) whether the mother by her acts or failure to act, had in fact abandoned the child, even though she had never signed a surrender in writing, and even though she later urged that the child be given to her custody for the first time in its more than five years of life; (b) whether the Bureau of Child Welfare and respondent adoption service made diligent efforts to strengthen the parent-child relationship and whether such efforts were necessary or could be dispensed with; and (c) whether uprooting the child from the foster home in which she had spent substantially all her life would have been a denial of her constitutional right to due process of law even assuming that the Bureau and the adopting service failed to make diligent efforts to strengthen the parent-child relationship for approximately one year after the birth of the child. The court ruled that the child had a right to the protection of due process of law, and a combination of parental abandonment and agency failure to promote the parent-child relationship could not override the substantial best interests of the child, under both N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384 and Family Court Act §§ 611, 614 and 622.


OUTCOME: The child was found to be an abandoned child and the custody and guardianship of her person was committed to the adoption service. The adoption service was authorized to consent to an adoption of the child by a suitable person or persons, subject to the customary approval and decree of the surrogate's court, and the consent of the mother to such an adoption was dispensed with.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and child -- abandoned child -- after trial of issues, Surrogate's Court finds that parents have abandoned child without good reason, and that child loves its foster parents; court authorizes child care agency to consent to have child adopted; consent of natural mother is not needed (Social Services Law, § 371, subd. 2; § 384) -- even if child care agency has not always made diligent efforts to encourage parental relationship (Family Ct. Act, §§ 611, 614), child has constitutional right to protection of laws in remaining in only home it has ever known.

1. Respondent is the mother of four children (one of whom died), all born out of wedlock. Shortly after giving birth to the fourth child, a girl, the mother signed an authorization that this baby be placed in foster care. This child is now five and half years old and has lived in her present foster home -- the only home she has ever known -- for almost five years. Her mother never tried to visit her, and the efforts of the child care and adoption agency to contact the mother were thwarted by said mother. The putative father of the child is unknown, and the mother is supported by public assistance. After a trial [***2] of the issues, the Surrogate's Court finds that the parents have abandoned the child without good reason, and that the child has never known her parents but has grown to love her foster parents permanently, and that thus the mother is now unfit to assume custody of this child. Therefore, the Surrogate's Court now commits the custody and guardianship of this child to the child care and adoption agency, and authorizes said agency to consent to have the child adopted by a suitable person or persons, subject to the customary approval and decree of the Surrogate's Court; and the court directs that the consent of the child's natural mother to such adoption shall not be needed. (Social Services Law, § 371, subd. 2; § 384.)

2. Even if it were found that the child care agency and/or the Bureau of Child Welfare have not always made "diligent efforts to encourage and strengthen the parental relationship", and even if it were found that any "such efforts would [not] be detrimental to the moral and temporal welfare of the child" (Family Ct. Act, §§ 611, 614, 622), still the natural parents' rights to these due processes of law cannot override the innocent child's substantial constitutional [***3] rights. The best interests of this child are to remain in the only home she has ever known. The child, too, is entitled to due processes of law and to the protection of the laws.

 

1655 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rivington v. Lowery


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


70 Misc. 2d 155; 333 N.Y.S.2d 211; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1866



May 24, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is granted, respondents' determination is annulled, and respondents are directed to pay petitioners the supplemental retirement allowance provided for in section 207-i of the General Municipal Law, retroactive to the effective date provided for by that section.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, retired city firemen, brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, seeking to annul the determination of defendants, board of trustees of the fire department pension fund, denying the retired firemen "cost of living" supplements pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-i.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-i provided for a supplemental retirement allowance for all fire department retirees throughout the state, based upon the consumer price index published by the United States Bureau of Labor Statistics. The retired firemen already received supplemental pension payments under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code §§ D49-30.0 and D49-31.0 which were greater than the supplemental retirement allowance provided for in N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-i. The trustees had determined that the retired firemen were not entitled to the additional cost of living payments under § 207-i. The retired firemen asserted that the "benefits" referred to in § 207-i(c) meant cost of living payments otherwise provided for, not the "supplemental pension payments" provided for in the New York City, N.Y., Administrative Code. The court held that the supplemental pension payment was a benefit, but it was not the kind of benefit which § 207-i contemplated as a bar to receiving the benefits provided for in that section. Accordingly, the court held that §§ D49-30.0 and D49-31.0 did not confer "benefits" as that term was used in N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-I(c).


OUTCOME: The petition was granted, the trustees' determination was annulled, and the trustees were directed to pay the retired firemen the relevant supplemental retirement allowance.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- pensions -- New York City firemen who retired prior to May 1, 1963 and then received additional supplement to give them pension of half pay based on annual salary on date of retirement, are entitled to cost of living supplement provided by section 207-i of General Municipal Law.

Petitioners are firemen of the City of New York who retired between 1960 and May 1, 1963 under the city's pension plan established in 1940, referred to as the article 1-B plan. That plan provided a pension computed on the basis of the retiree's five-year average salary. In 1963, section 207-e of the General Municipal Law was enacted granting to a retiree who retired after May 1, 1963 a pension of one half of the retiree's annual compensation on the date of retirement. By local law, in order to afford retirees like petitioners the same pension as those retiring after May 1, 1963, a new article 4 was added [***2] to the Administrative Code providing for a "supplemental pension payment to be known as a supplemental retirement allowance", which would be an amount which together with the pension, should be equal to one half of his annual compensation earned at the date of his retirement. Section 207-i of the General Municipal Law was added in 1967 and provides for a cost of living "supplemental retirement allowance" based upon the consumer price index. Subdivision c of section 207-i provides that "The benefits hereinabove provided for shall be in lieu of the benefits presently provided by any other general, special or local law unless such benefits are in excess of those provided by this section, in which latter case such benefits shall be paid by the retirement system pursuant to this section." While the supplemental pension payment of article 4 is a benefit, it is not the kind of benefit which section 207-i contemplates as a bar to receiving the benefits provided for therein. It is a corrective payment forming a permanent, vested component of the pension corpus. The section 207-i benefit is a temporary, transitory measure re-enacted annually. Petitioners are entitled to the cost of living [***3] supplement.

 

1656 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pieri


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Erie County


69 Misc. 2d 1085; 332 N.Y.S.2d 786; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1892



May 16, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to suppress evidence offered by the State that was acquired upon the execution of warrants, primarily on the grounds that an eavesdropping warrant was unlawfully issued. Defendant was charged with gambling and conspiracy violations of the New York Penal Law as a result of evidence seized during a search of his residence pursuant to a search warrant.


OVERVIEW: The search warrant was based upon information and evidence acquired, at least in part, by means of a previously authorized wiretap upon defendant's telephone. The eavesdropping warrant was issued under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law art. 700 and was directed at conversations between defendant and others pertaining to violations of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 225.10, 225.20, and 105.05. The warrant was issued for a period of 30 days. Finding the warrant to be invalid, the court granted defendant's motion. Under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 700.10, an eavesdropping warrant had to be limited to the period necessary to achieve its object and could not be more than 30 days. The section was adopted pursuant to 18 U.S.C.S. § 2510 et seq., which was intended to meet the standards and limitations established by the United States Supreme Court for eavesdropping. Eavesdropping warrants had to minimize the invasion of privacy and had to be strictly limited in duration. The affidavits underlying the warrant failed to establish a proper basis for continuing the interceptions for 30 days. The eavesdropping warrant failed to include the provision regarding termination required by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 700.30(7).


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- wiretapping -- County Court orders suppression of telephone conversations which were intercepted pursuant to order of Supreme Court Justice, and suppression of evidence seized pursuant to City Court search warrant which was based on said wiretap order -- wiretap order directed that interceptions should continue for 30 days because gambling conspiracy was involved; but supporting affidavit did not show any overt act of conspiracy with any one -- hence, wiretap order unconstitutionally violated CPL 700.10 (subd. 2) and CPL 700.30 (subd. 7).

A police officer made affidavit that telephone conversations of gambling activities (Penal Law, §§ 225.10, 225.20) were being repeated many times daily between the defendant and unknown persons; and thereupon a Supreme Court Justice authorized the tapping of defendant's telephone. This warrant further provided that, since a conspiracy was involved (Penal Law, § 105.05), the wiretap should not be disconnected after such conversations had been successfully intercepted but instead should continue for 30 days. However, the supporting affidavit did not set forth any overt act of conspiracy with any one, but only conjecture and suspicion;  [***2] hence this warrant unconstitutionally violated CPL 700.10 (subd. 2) and CPL 700.30 (subd. 7). Upon the basis in part of these illegally intercepted communications, a City Court Judge issued a warrant to search defendant's residence, and there certain evidence was seized; and defendant is now charged with the above-cited crimes of gambling and conspiracy. His motion in County Court, to suppress the wiretapped conversations and the seized evidence, is granted.

 

1657 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Shy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Village of Spring Valley, Rockland County


70 Misc. 2d 92; 332 N.Y.S.2d 561; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1900



May 11, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons stated, the motions are in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landlords moved to dismiss the informations, which alleged that they failed to apply for and obtain certificates of occupancy prior to placing new tenants in residential apartments in violation of Village of Spring Valley, N.Y., Ordinance § 513.05.


OVERVIEW: The landlords failed to obtain certificates of occupancy prior to placing new tenants in residential apartments. The landlords challenged the constitutionality of Village of Spring Valley, N.Y., Ordinance § 513.05 and moved to dismiss the informations against them. The court denied the motions. The court found that the ordinance was a valid exercise of the village's police power and that placing the burden on the landlords to deliver to the tenant a violation-free apartment rather than requiring the state to prove a violation at a later date did not violate due process. Even assuming that the ordinance was enforced only against landlords, the court found that the landlords would not be denied equal protection of the law. The court held that the state was permitted to omit providing 15-day notice of violation in an emergency situation. Village of Spring Valley, N.Y., Housing Ordinance § 9-220, which required repainting of walls and ceilings to maintain them in a "sanitary condition," was not found to be vague. The court set the charges for trial.


OUTCOME: The court denied the landlords' motions to dismiss the informations, which alleged they failed to apply for and obtain certificates of occupancy prior to placing new tenants in residential apartments. The charges were set for trial.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- removal of violations in apartments -- village ordinance provided that upon termination of occupancy of apartment, owner must apply for certificate of occupancy before occupancy by another; such ordinance is valid exercise of police power and constitutional -- no facts alleged to support claim of discrimination against landlords -- ordinance does not conflict with State statutes but is authorized by them -- service of notice of violation is not required before issuance of summons -- standards are not required in ordinance to determine "sanitary conditions".

1. A village ordinance provided that upon termination of the occupancy of any dwelling unit, an application must be made for a certificate of occupancy prior to occupancy by a new tenant. Such ordinance is valid in the village's exercise of the police power to promote the public health. Due process is not violated by putting upon landlord the onus to deliver to tenant a violation-free apartment. The ordinance is not unreasonable, arbitrary or capricious, nor vague nor ambiguous.

2. On this motion to dismiss the informations,  [***2] there are no facts to support a claim of discrimination against landlords or of enforcement of the ordinance only against landlords. Even if enforced only against landlords, the police power will not have been exerted unreasonably when measured against the avowed purpose of the Village Board.

3. The ordinance is not in conflict with subdivision 1 of section 329 of the Multiple Residence Law, subdivision 2 of section 22 of the Municipal Home Rule Law and subdivision 1 of section 175 of the Village Law. Section 175 gives the village specific authority to "regulate * * * use of buildings". The Multiple Residence Law provides for the legality of more restrictive provisions of a local ordinance. The Municipal Home Rule Law allows a State statute to be superseded by a local law where authorized by any other State statute.

4. The service of notice of violation is not required before issuance of a summons under the ordinance and the ordinance is not rendered unconstitutional thereby. Section 9-20 is a procedural authorization for the issuance of a notice to remedy and is only a prerequisite to the filing of an information in a situation where it would be unreasonable not to afford an [***3] opportunity to remedy an alleged violation. The reasonableness of the time afforded depends on the circumstances of each case and is finally determined by the court rather than the inspector.

5. The ordinance need not provide standards to determine "sanitary conditions" nor is the placing of that determination in the opinion of the housing inspector unconstitutional. The ultimate determination will be made by the court.

 

1658 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Delmar Associates v. Peters


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Second District, Nassau County


70 Misc. 2d 58; 331 N.Y.S.2d 681; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1926



May 3, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a summary proceeding for nonpayment of rent, respondent tenant moved to dismiss on the ground that petitioner landlord had not alleged and proven that the rent did not exceed the maximum rent under the Economic Stabilization Act and the executive orders promulgated pursuant to that act.


OVERVIEW: The landlord raised the tenant's rent after the expiration of a rent freeze period. The court, observing that the constitutionality of federal price control regulations was doubtful, held that it would be unnecessary and impractical to routinely require landlords in all nonpayment cases to allege and prove that the rental did not exceed the maximum rent. The general standards used to determine the maximum allowable rent did not have the precision and certitude necessary for the prompt adjudication of summary proceedings for the nonpayment of rent.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment in favor of the landlord.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- maximum rent -- landlord is not required to allege and prove that rent does not exceed maximum under Economic Stabilization Act and executive orders thereunder.

In a summary proceeding for nonpayment of rent, landlord is not required to allege and prove that the rent does not exceed the maximum rent under the Economic Stabilization Act and the executive orders promulgated pursuant to that act.

 

1659 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Torsiello v. State


Claim No. 54800


Court of Claims of New York


70 Misc. 2d 294; 332 N.Y.S.2d 794; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1949



April 26, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion of the defendant is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant, the executrix for a property owner, filed a claim after defendant State of New York appropriated the owner's land. The State filed a motion to dismiss and asserted that the claim had not be timely filed pursuant to the two-year statute of limitations imposed by former N.Y. Ct. Cl. Act § 10(1).


OVERVIEW: The State took proper steps to appropriate lands belonging to the owner. The executrix failed to file the action within the two-year statutory time limit imposed under § 10(1), which was in force when the appropriation took place. Twenty days after the time period had run, however, 1971 N.Y. Laws 234 became effective; it amended former N.Y. Ct. Cl. Act § 10(1) by extending the time period for filing claims from two to three years. The executrix promptly filed the action. The State moved to dismiss the claim, asserting that 1971 N.Y. Laws 234 should not be retroactively applied to claims that had expired before the statute's effective date. In denying the State's motion, the court held that 1971 N.Y. Laws 234 retroactively extended the time limitation applicable to the executrix's claim. Legislatively enacted changes in procedure were generally excepted from the rule that favored prospective construction of statutes. The lapsing of the statute of limitation had not destroyed the executrix's cause of action, it merely barred her from seeking a remedy. The legislature had the power to give the executrix more time to bring her pre-existing cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court denied the State's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

State -- time for filing claim -- law, extending time for filing claim for appropriation of property from two to three years, has retroactive application to claim already barred so as to revive claim and render its filing within three-year period valid.

Chapter 234 of the Laws of 1971, which amended subdivision 1 of section 10 of the Court of Claims Act to extend from two to three years the time for the filing of a claim for the appropriation of land, applies retroactively to a claim which has been barred for approximately 20 days and a claim for such appropriation filed on March 7, 1972 was therefore timely. Defendant's motion to dismiss the claim is denied.

 

1660 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Holder


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Nassau County


69 Misc. 2d 863; 331 N.Y.S.2d 557; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1964



April 25, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Ordered that defendant's motion to suppress is granted and all 60 intercepted telephonic communications, tape recordings, transcripts and evidence obtained, either directly or indirectly, as a result of the intercept authority of August 28, 1970, September 8, 1970 and September 17, 1970, are suppressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Co-defendants were charged with conspiring to cause a felonious death. One defendant filed a motion to suppress eavesdropping evidence obtained by the people in its investigation of the death, or in the alternative, to vacate a certain eavesdropping order and other orders based thereon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the eavesdropping statute, N.Y. Crim. Proc. §§ 813-j to 813-m; §§ 814 to 825, was unconstitutional, that neither the statute's requirements nor those of the Fourth Amendment were satisfied because there was no probable cause, that the application for the warrant was insufficient, and that the eavesdropping order failed to comply with the statute. The court found the statute to be constitutional but granted relief based on the people's failure to include a minimization notice in the eavesdropping order. Such notice was mandatory under the statute. The statute met the requirements of specificity, narrowness, particularity, and judicial supervision required by decisional law and thus was constitutional. The applications were sufficient to justify the issuance of the eavesdropping warrant. Nevertheless, the eavesdropping order failed to include the minimization notice required under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 819(7), designed to prevent a wiretap from turning into a wholesale invasion of privacy. The record was devoid of any attempt to minimize the interception of unauthorized calls. Only a severe penalty would insure future compliance.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion for suppression.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- eavesdropping warrants -- telephonic eavesdropping warrant failed to provide that interceptions "shall be conducted in such a way as to minimize the interception of communications not otherwise subject to eavesdropping" (Code Crim. Pro., § 819, subd. 7); police never cut off their interception; less than 10% of taped conversations could have been constitutionally listened to in full; court ordered all 60 reels of tape recordings suppressed -- however, our eavesdropping statute is constitutional, and application for warrant complied with statute.

1. A telephonic eavesdropping warrant failed to specify that the wiretap "must" be installed "as soon as practicable", and failed to provide that such telephonic interception "shall be conducted in such a way as to minimize the interception of communications not otherwise subject to eavesdropping" or interception (Code [***2] Crim. Pro., § 819, subd. 7; U. S. Code, tit. 18, § 2518, subd. [5]). Pursuant to this warrant, the police never cut off their interception. They made 60 reels of tape recordings of all conversations in full on the tapped telephone, regardless of the persons and the subject matter. Less than 10% of the intercepted communications could have been lawfully and constitutionally listened to in their entirety. Therefore, the court granted defendant's motion ( CPL 710.20) and suppressed all 60 reels of tape recordings.

2. However, our eavesdropping statute (Code Crim. Pro., § 814 to § 825; see, now, CPL art. 700) is constitutional; and the affidavits of application for this warrant sufficiently set forth the required facts and statements and descriptions (Code Crim. Pro., § 817, subd. 2, par. [b], subpars. [i], [iii]; § 817, subd. 2, pars. [d], [e]).

 

1661 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ferraro v. City School Dist.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Schenectady County


69 Misc. 2d 800; 331 N.Y.S.2d 490; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1966



April 24, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitioner's application for an order to place his name on the ballot as a candidate for election to membership on the Board of Education of the city school district of the City of Schenectady is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate filed an application for an order directing respondent school board to place his name on the ballot for election of candidate for member of the local board of education.


OVERVIEW: The candidate applied for an order directing the school district to place his name on the ballot for the election of candidates for member of the local board of education. The school district contended that as a policeman and thus a holder of a city office, he was an ineligible candidate pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 2502. The court denied the application for an order. The candidate was an officer of the city, as opposed to a mere employee. A patrolman was invested with some part of the sovereign functions of the government, and tenure was not transient, occasional or incidental. In carrying out his duties to preserve law and order, protect residents, and enforce the laws, he was exercising some portion of the sovereign power. The position of patrolman contained all the indicia and criteria of public office. He was also not eligible to run for office. If the disqualification applied only to the holding of the office and not to the capacity of the candidate for election, then the electors could never know when voting for the office whether they would succeed in filling it or not. The candidate also failed to demonstrate that the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the candidate's application for an order directing the school board to place his name on the ballot for election to the local board of education.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- elections -- city policeman is not eligible to run for office of member of city board of education (Education Law, art. 51, § 2502, subd. 7).

As to city school districts with less than 125,000 inhabitants, the Education Law (art. 51, § 2502, subd. 7) validly and constitutionally provides that "No person shall be eligible to the office of member of the board of education who is not a qualified voter * * * and * * * a resident of such district for a period of at least three years immediately preceding the date of his election; provided, however, that no person shall hold at the same time the office of member of the board of education and any city office". Accordingly, the Board of Education of the City of Schenectady properly refused to place the name of a patrolman of the Police Department of the City of Schenectady on the ballot for member of said Board of Education ( Education Law, § 2608, subd. 2; Election Law, § 147, subd. [3]).on

 

1662 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Slater v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Madison County


69 Misc. 2d 842; 330 N.Y.S.2d 947; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1969



April 21, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a prior action, the court declared the composition of defendant, the Board of Supervisors of Cortland County, to be unconstitutional and directed the Board to adopt a plan of apportionment in conformity with the "one man, one vote" concept. The Board's first plan was declared invalid, and the Board later adopted a second plan. Plaintiff challenged the constitutional validity of the second plan and sought to impose his own plan on the County.


OVERVIEW: The court declared the composition of the Board to be unconstitutional. The court ordered the Board to adopt a plan of apportionment in conformity with the "one man, one vote" concept. After the Board's first plan was declared invalid, it adopted a second plan combining straight districting with weighted voting. Plaintiff challenged the constitutional validity of the second plan and sought to impose his multi-member district plan on the County. The court held that the Board's plan was constitutionally valid because the two percent deviation between effective voting power and the actual percentage of population between districts was not excessive and the plan complied with the five percent rule of N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 10(1)(a)(13)(ii). The court also held that the Board's plan took precedence over plaintiff's plan.


OUTCOME: The court approved the Board's plan and denied plaintiff's motion to impose his own plan on the County.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- plan of apportionment providing for 19 legislative districts and adjusting difference in population by weighted votes depending on whether majority, two-thirds or three-fifths vote is called for, producing deviation of 2%, is not excessive -- standard of Municipal Home Rule Law that there be 5% full ratio for each representative has been complied with; plan is constitutionally valid -- Board of Supervisors' plan takes precedence over constitutionally valid plan submitted by plaintiff.

1. A plan of apportionment of a county provides for 19 legislative districts, 8 of which are in a city. Rural towns are grouped to form legislative districts, and towns with larger populations are divided to comprise legislative districts. The districts vary in population from 2,140 to 2,716. Each legislator is assigned weighted votes which differ depending upon whether the subject requires for passage a majority vote, two-thirds vote or a three-fifths vote. Analysis of the plan with a computer showed that the disparity of effective voting power and the actual percentage of population between districts is less than 2%, whether [***2] a majority, a two-thirds or a three-fifths vote was needed. Such 2% deviation is not excessive.

2. The Municipal Home Rule Law ( § 10, subd. 1, par. a, cl. [13], subcl. [ii]) provides as a standard that "Adjacent representation areas in the same town or city shall not contain a greater excess in population than five per cent of a full ratio for each representative". "A full ratio for each representative" is a figure arrived at by dividing the population of the county (45,894) by the number of districts (19). Using that definition, the 5% rule has been complied with in the plan. The local law which enacted the plan is constitutionally valid and should be submitted to a referendum.

3. A plan meeting constitutional standards submitted by the representative body of the county takes precedence over plaintiff's multi-member district plan which also meets constitutional standards.

 

1663 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Blomquist v. County of Orange


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


69 Misc. 2d 1077; 332 N.Y.S.2d 546; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1973



April 20, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners filed an action for declaratory judgment declaring the bond resolution of defendant county unconstitutional, illegal, and void, an injunction against further payment of money or acquisition of property authorized by the resolution, and monetary damages. The property owners filed a motion for a order dismissing the county's answer for lack of merit and for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The county adopted a bond resolution to pay for the cost of the acquisition of land for various county purposes. The property owners filed an action by which they sought a declaration that the bond resolution was unconstitutional and in violation of the N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 2, an injunction against further payment of money or acquisition of property, and monetary damages. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment. The court granted summary judgment to the county to dismiss the action seeking a declaration that the bond was illegal because it was clear that the bond resolution was adopted for county purposes. The court enjoined further acquisitions of land for future reservoir purposes because the county did not comply with the requirements of the New York Conservation Law regarding the submission of maps and plans for approval by the water resources commission. The court denied summary judgment dismissing the county's answer because there remained a question of fact as to whether the county's action violated any rights of the property owners.


OUTCOME: The court granted the county's motion for summary judgment dismissal of the property owner's action for declaratory judgment, but granted the property owners' petition for an injunction. The court denied the property owners' motion for summary judgment dismissal of the county's answer.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Counties -- water supply -- county cannot acquire lands for reservoir purposes unless and until it obtains approval of State Water Resources Commission.

A resolution by the Board of Supervisors of a county to issue $ 5,000,000 of bonds and capital notes in order to acquire lands "for various County purposes" is not necessarily unconstitutional for failure to specify the county purposes (cf. N. Y. Const., art. VIII, § 2; Local Finance Law, §§ 32.00, 82.00); but, since the purpose actually is to develop reservoirs for the county's water supply, the county must first submit its maps and plans to, and obtain the approval of, the New York State Water Resources Commission (Conservation Law, § 450, subd. [1], par. [b]). A county is a "public corporation" within the meaning of the Conservation Law (§ 403, subd. [3]; General Corporation Law, § 3, subd. 1). Therefore, the court will enjoin any further land acquisitions pursuant to this general resolution, unless and until the county complies with the Water Resources Law (Conservation Law, art. 5).

 

1664 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Friedler v. University of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


70 Misc. 2d 416; 333 N.Y.S.2d 928; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1976



April 19, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The plaintiff's petition is in all respects denied and the proceeding dismissed, without costs to either party.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff student filed a class action petition against defendants, a state university and others, alleging that N.Y. Educ. Law § 602, subd. 2 was unconstitutional in that it denied aliens the equal protection of the laws guaranteed by the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The student was a British citizen who refused to become a naturalized United States citizen because it would have revoked her British citizenship. She was denied "scholar incentive" benefits because of the citizenship requirements of § 602, subd. 2. The student filed suit on her behalf and on behalf of all others similarly situated and alleged that § 602, subd. 2 violated the Fourteenth Amendment. The court held that § 602, subd. 2 was constitutional because it was relevant and rationally related to the achievement of a permissible state objective. The stricter "compelling state interest" test did not apply because higher education was not a fundamental right.


OUTCOME: The court denied the student's petition and dismissed the action.


HEADNOTES

Colleges and universities -- State scholarships -- Education Law (art. 13, § 602, subd. 2) validly and constitutionally requires that any applicant for State scholarship who is not United States citizen must apply for such citizenship.

The Education Law (art. 13, § 602, subd. 2) validly and constitutionally requires that any applicant for a scholarship or fellowship or other award in higher education must either be a United States citizen, or must have applied for United States citizenship, or must apply for United States citizenship "as soon as eligible". A British citizen who declines to take steps to become naturalized is not eligible.

 

1665 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ewing v. State


Claim No. 54186


Court of Claims of New York


69 Misc. 2d 923; 331 N.Y.S.2d 287; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1988



April 18, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion of the defendant must be granted and the claim is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the State of New York, filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff claimant's complaint, which alleged a breach of contract, unconstitutional impairment of contractual obligations, and an unconstitutional legislative enactment.


OVERVIEW: The claimant applied for and received a graduate fellowship, which had been made available by legislative enactment. After the claimant accepted the fellowship, the legislature repealed the act that had provided for the fellowships and the claimant was notified that due to the repeal, his fellowship was canceled. The claimant then filed suit against the State, claiming breach of contract, unconstitutional impairment of a contractual obligation, and unconstitutional enactment of the repealing legislation. In dismissing the complaint, the court held that the granting of a fellowship was not contractual in nature. Further, because the state comptroller had not approved the fellowship, it could not have been a binding contract upon the State. The court found that the legislature had the power repeal the former act without liability to the claimant who did not have contractual or vested rights. Further, the claimant was notified when he was awarded the fellowship that the award was in anticipation that the law authorizing the scholarship would continue to exist and that payment of the fellowship was dependent upon the continuance of the law.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

State -- statute but no appropriation of moneys therefor -- no moneys were ever appropriated for college fellowships created by statute, and statute was repealed; thus, applicant to whom fellowship had been awarded had no binding contract with State (N. Y. Const., art. VII, § 7) -- even if Governor's message of necessity for repealer did not meet requirements of New York Constitution (art. III, § 14), still, applicant has no binding contract and hence no unconstitutional impairment of contract -- his claim against State is dismissed.

1. The 1969 Legislature created 90 college graduate fellowships but did not appropriate any moneys therefor; and the 1971 Legislature repealed the 1969 act. Meantime the State Education Department had awarded one of those fellowships to an applicant and he had accepted it; but no contract was ever filed with or approved by the State Comptroller (State Finance Law, § 112). Moreover, "No money shall ever be paid out of the state treasury * * * except in pursuance of an appropriation by law" (N. Y. Const., art. VII, § 7). Thus, there was no binding contract and hence [***2] there was no breach of a contract.

2. The repealer was swiftly enacted after a message of necessity from the Governor. Even if it could be adjudged that the Governor's message did not comply with the requirements of the New York Constitution (art. III, § 14), still there never was an appropriation and never a contract; and hence there was no unconstitutional impairment of a contract.

 

1666 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Orlofsky


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Saratoga County


70 Misc. 2d 298; 332 N.Y.S.2d 778; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1990



April 17, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Pursuant to subdivision 2 of CPL 470.20 the information with supporting depositions upon which the defendant was tried is dismissed and the preliminary short form information or complaint under the UTC procedure is also dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Court of Special Sessions of the Town of Ballston (New York) convicted defendant of the misdemeanor crime of reckless driving, in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1190. After her conviction, defendant paid a fine. Procedurally, defendant properly perfected an appeal from each and every part of the judgment of conviction.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to convict because the information was defective. The court rejected the contention and held the information to be legally sufficient. The court noted that the information alleged that the manner of driving by defendant unreasonably interfered with the free use and proper use of the public highway and unreasonably endangered other users of the public highway. The information also stated two factual allegations of the manner of the operation of the vehicle by defendant to equate with the statutory definition of the criminal offense. The court also found and held that the procedures followed by the trial court on the trial date were not defective and that the trial court did not lack jurisdiction of the case. The court, however, held that the trial evidence was legally insufficient to support the conviction of reckless driving. The court noted that defendant's testimony demonstrated that she was deeply concerned with the consequences of the situation which had arisen on that highway and actually drove her vehicle in such a manner as to demonstrate that she was attempting to avoid a collision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of conviction and dismissed the information with supporting depositions upon which defendant was tried. The court also dismissed the preliminary short form information or complaint.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- reckless driving -- long form information sufficiently alleged crime of reckless driving and gave court jurisdiction -- procedures of court on trial date were not defective -- testimony shows that defendant was attempting to avoid collision in face of sudden emergency and was not guilty of reckless driving; conviction reversed and fine remitted.

1. A long form information alleging that the manner in which defendant drove her vehicle "unreasonably interfered with the free and proper use of the public highway and unreasonably endangered other users of the public highway" and reciting other factual allegations sufficiently alleged the crime and gave the court jurisdiction.

2. The procedures followed by the court on the trial date were not defective.

3. Defendant, who was confronted with an emergency when the vehicle in front of her came to a stop, turned to the right to avoid a collision and, when this was not possible, turned [***2] to the left to avoid a collision, crossed into the opposite lane and collided with a vehicle in the opposite lane. Defendant was not thereby driving her vehicle recklessly without regard to the rights of others. The testimony shows that defendant was deeply concerned with the consequences of the situation which arose on the highway and drove her vehicle in a manner to show that she was attempting to avoid a collision. Her conviction is reversed and the fine is remitted.

 

1667 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


First Nat'l Bank v. Felder


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Fifth District, Suffolk County


69 Misc. 2d 812; 331 N.Y.S.2d 306; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1992



April 17, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, adjudicated bankrupts, filed an application for an order directing that a discharge of record be marked on the docket of the judgment in an action by plaintiff judgment assignee.


OVERVIEW: The husband and wife had been adjudicated bankrupts in 1968. A bank, the judgment lienor at the time, participated in the bankruptcy proceeding as a creditor. In 1969 the bank assigned its judgment lien to the assignee, who opposed the adjudicated bankrupts' application for an order directing that a discharge of record be marked upon the docket of the judgment. The court found that the burden was on the creditor to establish that the judgment was not one upon which a discharge in bankruptcy acted automatically, and the debtor had the burden to establish that the judgment was in fact listed and dischargeable in bankruptcy. The court determined that the fact that the adjudicated bankrupts were insolvent when the judgment was entered, which meant that the judgment lien was voidable in bankruptcy. The court found, however, that the lien was not automatically void without some affirmative action by the trustee or anyone else, and hence, survived the bankruptcy, and was currently extant. The court ruled that pursuant to N.Y. Debt. & Cred. Law § 150, upon sufficient proof, a court in which the judgment was docketed could issue an order discharging the judgment and the lien of record.


OUTCOME: The court granted the application of the adjudicated bankrupts for an order directing that a discharge of record be marked upon the docket of the judgment, holding that justice and the underlying tenets of fairness upon which that concept was based dictated this result.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Bankruptcy -- discharge -- judgment obtained when defendants were insolvent was voidable but since trustee in bankruptcy took no affirmative action, judgment survives discharge -- bank's assignment of judgment is valid -- plaintiff, by filing claim in bankruptcy stating that it held no security for debt and by participating in dividends, surrendered lien of judgment -- purchase of judgment by assignee did not constitute champerty -- defendants are entitled to order discharging judgment and lien.

1. Plaintiff bank obtained a judgment against defendants at a time when defendants were insolvent. The judgment lien was, therefore, voidable in the subsequent bankruptcy of defendants, but since neither the trustee nor anyone else took affirmative action to void it, the judgment survived the discharge in bankruptcy. Defendants who purchased the property from the trustee, bought it subject to the lien of such judgment.

2. When the bank assigned the judgment, the assignment was valid and the assignee is a proper party to oppose the present application by defendants for a discharge of the judgment of record.

3. By filing proof of claim in the bankruptcy proceeding stating therein [***2] that it held no security for the debt, by having its claim allowed and by participating in the dividends paid in the bankrupt estate plaintiff bank surrendered its lien.

4. The purchase of the judgment by the assignee does not involve champerty. Transfers of judgments are permitted by the General Obligations Law ( § 13-103). That the assignee buys judgments in the regular course of business as a collection agency is inconsequential.

5. Under section 150 of the Debtor and Creditor Law, this court in which the judgment was docketed has power to and does order that the judgment and lien be discharged of record.
