People v. Dobbs Ferry Medical Pavillion, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


69 Misc. 2d 886; 332 N.Y.S.2d 186; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1994



April 17, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state initiated a case against defendants, medical clinic and administrator, based on the alleged illegal operation of an abortion clinic. The state filed a motion for an injunction pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 2801-a, 2801-b, and N.Y. Bus. Corp. Law § 1115, to restrain defendants from performing abortions or operating the abortion clinic pending determination of the case.


OVERVIEW: The state initiated a case against defendants and asserted that defendants' abortion clinic was a "hospital" within the meaning of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2801-a(1) and that defendants failed to obtain approval from the Public Health Council before opening. The state filed a motion for a preliminary injunction to prohibit defendants from performing abortions or otherwise operating the clinic pending determination of the case. The state argued that the illegal operation of the abortion clinic had resulted in and continued to result in injury to the people of the state. Defendants argued that the preliminary injunction could not be granted because the claim of public harm was not substantiated. The court held that the state was not entitled to a preliminary injunction because the statutory violation was the very issue to be determined in the case. The court ordered that the case be given preference and set for an immediate hearing to determine whether defendants' clinic violated N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 2801-a and 2801-b.


OUTCOME: The court held that the state was not entitled to a preliminary injunction to prohibit defendants from operating the abortion clinic pending resolution of the case. The court ordered that the case be given preference and be set for an immediate hearing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Abortion -- abortion clinics -- approval of Public Health Council is needed for "independent out-of-hospital * * * facility" for lawful abortions (10 NYCRR 700.2 [a] [6]; Public Health Law, § 2801, subd. 1; § 2801-a, subd. 1; § 2803) -- however, preliminary injunction against defendants is denied and early trial is directed.

A lawful abortion clinic, that is, an "independent out-of-hospital health facility * * * with one or more health clinics", cannot be established without the prior written approval of the Public Health Council (10 NYCRR 700.2 [a] [6]; Public Health Law, § 2801, subd. 1; § 2801-a, subd. 1). These cited provisions are valid and constitutional. However, the People's application for a preliminary injunction (Public Health Law, § 2801-b; Business Corporation Law, § 1115) against these defendants is denied, and an early trial is directed to determine whether these defendants are maintaining such an institution. A further issue for trial is whether the applicable provisions (Public Health Law, §§ 2801, 2801-a, 2803) are being applied against these defendants in an intentionally discriminatory manner.

 

1669 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Moreira


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Fourth District, Suffolk County


70 Misc. 2d 68; 333 N.Y.S.2d 215; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1995



April 16, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People charged defendant with appearing in a public place as a topless dancer, thereby violating Town of Smithtown, N.Y., Local Law No. 2 (1970), which was passed pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 245.01.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with appearing in a public place clothed in an illegal manner because the lower portion of her breasts were not covered by a fully opaque covering. She was a topless dancer. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss on grounds that the proof failed to specify that the alleged violation occurred within a certain township and raised constitutional questions. The court held that N.Y. Penal Law § 245.01 was a valid and proper delegation of legislative power to a municipality. The court found that the town law adopted thereunder was neither capricious, arbitrary, nor oppressive, nor did it discriminate or classify. Therefore, it was a proper exercise of the town's police power. The court held that topless appearances were a form of expression explicitly excluded from the broad protection of the First Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant guilty as charged and scheduled sentencing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- local law against exposure of female -- local law prohibiting exposure of topless dancer is valid and constitutional.

The Penal Law (§ 245.01) states: "A female is guilty of exposure when, in a public place * * * the portion of her breast below the top of the areola is not covered * * *. This * * * shall not apply to any female entertaining or performing in a play, exhibition, show or entertainment. * * * Nothing in this section shall prevent the adoption by a city, town or village of a local law prohibiting [such] exposure * * * whether or not such female is entertaining or performing in a play, exhibition, show or entertainment." The Town of Smithtown has enacted such a local law. The local law is valid and constitutional. The District Court of Suffolk County finds a so-called topless dancer guilty of violating the local law.

People v. Braun


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Third District, Nassau County


69 Misc. 2d 682; 330 N.Y.S.2d 937; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2005



April 12, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People prosecuted defendants based on an alleged violation of North Hempstead, NY, Building Zone Ordinance art. XIX, § 19.14(c).


OVERVIEW: An information charged defendants with violating North Hempstead, NY, Building Zone Ordinance art. XIX, § 19.14(c) by maintaining gas price signs in a location other than attached to the gas pump and larger than the maximum permitted measurement. Defendants presented no evidence, but challenged the constitutionality of the portion of the ordinance that limited the signs to a maximum of 12 inches square. The court dismissed the information and discharged defendants, holding that the maximum size of sign provision of the ordinance was unconstitutional in that it was oppressive and imposed an undue burden on defendants' business. The court further held that the People failed to show a violation of the requirement of § 19.14(c) that a sign of certain dimensions be on each pump. There was no testimony that any pump lacked such a sign.


OUTCOME: The court, after trial, dismissed the information and discharged defendants.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- violation of zoning laws -- portion of zoning ordinance of town which states that sign attached to gasoline pump showing selling price shall not exceed 12 inches square is unconstitutional -- moreover, no violation proved against defendants for evidence dealt with larger signs in front of pumps rather than on pumps.

1. A portion of a zoning ordinance of a town which states that a sign attached to the pump showing the selling price of gasoline on a gasoline service station shall be not larger than 12 inches square is unconstitutional. Such provision is oppressive and imposes an undue burden on defendants' business.

2. Moreover, the People failed to show a violation of the ordinance. The section requires a sign on the pump of a specified size. The People failed to prove that any pump lacked such a sign. The information and the testimony referred to a larger sign in front of the pump. Such a sign is not specifically prohibited by the ordinance. Defendants therefore cannot be held for violating the ordinance.

 

1671 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gannett Co. v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


69 Misc. 2d 619; 330 N.Y.S.2d 648; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2013



April 11, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The plaintiff's request for a summary judgment is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff publisher challenged the decision of defendants, city, city council, and council members, which repealed a former provision of the municipal code and enacted Rochester, N.Y., Municipal Code ch. 74. Chapter 74 placed limitations on the ability to maintain newspaper vending machines and similar structures within the city.


OVERVIEW: Chapter 74 required, inter alia, a permit to place or use any table, box, stand, newspaper vending machine, or any other structure for the sale, display, or storage of newspapers or magazines upon any sidewalk. Authority to grant or deny the permits was vested with a city official. The court held that chapter 74 was unconstitutional and invalid as violative of free speech and equal protection rights. Although cities were granted broad authority in the exercise of their police power, there was no compelling reason for chapter 74's enactment. The oppressive requirement of filing for a permit and showing insurance, all at the whim of the public official, constituted a suppression of free speech and the free press. The news and the free exchange of ideas could not be killed or discouraged by fear of criminal sanctions. The vague standards vesting the city official with uncontrollable discretion could not be tolerated. Further, chapter 74's saving clause did not save it because the invalid portion was inseparably connected to the remainder. Finally, chapter 74 denied certain parties equal protection under the law by singling out a particular type of communication to be regulated.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiff's request for summary judgment in its challenge to the city's ordinance and declared the ordinance unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- news vending machines on streets -- public's constitutional rights to free speech and free press are invalidly abridged by city ordinance requiring permit at least 10 days in advance for any news vending machine or any table, box or stand for display or sale of magazines or newspapers on any public sidewalk; entire ordinance is invalid.

1. The public's constitutional rights of freedom of speech and freedom of the press and equal protection of the laws are unreasonably and invalidly abridged by a city ordinance which imposes civil and criminal penalties upon any person who maintains on any public sidewalk a newspaper vending machine or a table, box or stand for the sale or display of magazines or newspapers unless he has applied at least 10 days in advance for a permit to "interfere" in such manner with pedestrians and with vehicular traffic.

2. The ordinance contains a separability saving clause; however, any otherwise valid portions of the ordinance are inseparably connected with this prior restraint on free speech. The entire ordinance is unconstitutional and invalid.

3. Of course, this [***2] adjudication does not deprive the city of its power to enact an ordinance prohibiting the use of the public streets for distributing purely commercial leaflets.
