In re Estate of Perez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


69 Misc. 2d 538; 330 N.Y.S.2d 881; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2016



April 7, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner administrators commenced a proceeding that requested a decree be signed settling their account of the estate of the decedent, father of objectant child. A guardian ad litem was appointed by order of the court to represent the child, who had been born out-of-wedlock. The guardian filed objections to the accounting.


OVERVIEW: The decedent had acknowledged paternity of the child with a social services agency. The decedent was later killed in a car accident. The administrators obtained a wrongful death settlement, the estate's only asset, and applied for a decree to settle their account. The guardian ad litem opposed the account and contended that the child was entitled to the full amount of the recovery. The court found in favor of the child. The court determined that N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 5-4.4, which denied recovery to an out-of-wedlock minor for the wrongful death of his putative father, violated the equal protection laws. The court decided that no legislative intent existed that indicated that the legislature would prefer the statute to be void instead of modifying it. For purposes of the wrongful death statute, the court determined that illegitimate children were to be deemed the children of both their natural parents. The court found that N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2(a)(2) had to be disregarded as well, because it set up an unconstitutional invidious distinction, unequally protecting children depending upon their age at the institution of successful paternity actions.


OUTCOME: The court set attorney's fees and guardian ad litem fees and awarded the entire net amount of the wrongful death settlement to the child after payment of the fees.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Death -- wrongful death -- illegitimate child -- net amount of settlement of cause of action for wrongful death of man belongs, not to his parents who suffered no pecuniary loss, but to his only child though illegitimate -- though no order of filiation was ever made, our statute purporting to disinherit illegitimate child in such situation is discriminatory and unconstitutional -- Department of Social Services has no claim against such proceeds for having supported said child since shortly after his birth -- custody of child will be left temporarily with Department of Social Services, subject to further proceedings in Family Court or other court of competent jurisdiction.

1. Decedent's only assets are the damages recoverable for his wrongful death. Those damages belong, not to his parents, who have not shown that they suffered any pecuniary injury, but to his only child -- an illegitimate. When the child was 4 years old -- he is now 10 years old -- decedent executed an agreement with the city Department of Social Services, acknowledging his paternity and agreeing to pay $ 6.75 a week for the child's support. No order of filiation was ever made; but our statute purporting [***2] to disinherit such a child in such a situation ( EPTL 4-1.2, subd. [a], par. [2]; cf. EPTL 5-4.4, subd. [a]) is invidiously discriminatory and unconstitutional.

2. The Surrogate authorizes the administrators to settle the wrongful death cause of action for $ 12,500. Out of this they shall pay the burial and funeral expenses, and $ 3,566 to the attorney, and $ 1,000 to the guardian ad litem, and $ 300 to themselves for their travel expenses, and the balance (approximately $ 6,000) shall be deposited for the benefit of the out-of-wedlock child.

3. The Department of Social Services properly withdrew its claim for $ 6,600 for having supported said out-of-wedlock child since shortly after its birth. Such a claim cannot be paid out of the proceeds of a wrongful death cause of action.

4. Upon the voluntary consent of the mother of this child, the custody of the child will be left temporarily where it is -- with the city Department of Social Services -- subject to further proceedings in the Family Court or other court of competent jurisdiction.

 

1673 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re "S"


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


69 Misc. 2d 942; 330 N.Y.S.2d 872; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2023



April 7, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For all of the reasons set forth above, the motion to dismiss is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner adoption agency initiated an action seeking a non-consensual adoption of a child of respondent mother based on an alleged abandonment of the child by the mother. The mother filed a motion to dismiss the petition, claiming that she was deprived of due process of law by the non-consensual adoption.


OVERVIEW: The mother gave consent to the placement of one of her children in foster care when the child was one month old. Six years later, the agency filed a petition for the adoption of the child, on the basis that the mother had not visited the child during the intervening six years and thus had abandoned the child according to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 371(2). The mother opposed the adoption and filed a motion to dismiss the petition, claiming that the statutory scheme under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 371 and 384(5), which permitted a finding of parental "abandonment," even where the natural mother was opposing the adoption, amounted to a denial of due process under U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV. The court denied the mother's motion, holding that the mother's inaction in not visiting the child, if proved at trial, could lead to a finding that the infant's statutory and constitutional rights to an adoptive home were infringed by dismissal of the action. The court held that, if necessary for the welfare of the infant, and to free it for adoption, "permanent neglect" as defined in the Family Court Act would have to be construed to approximate "abandonment" under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 371(2).


OUTCOME: The court denied the mother's motion to dismiss the agency's petition for a non-consensual adoption of the mother's child.


HEADNOTES

Parent and child -- abandoned child -- child who has been abandoned "without good reason" is constitutionally entitled to have permanent home with adoptive parents, even if natural mother now objects that she herself is fit -- such natural parent has constitutional right to notice and counsel -- Surrogate will try issues (Social Services Law, § 371, subd. 2; § 384, subd. 5; Family Ct. Act, §§ 611, 614).

A baby was born out of wedlock to a woman who, with her two older infants, was and is supported by the Department of Social Services. When this baby was a month old, the mother executed a consent to its placement for foster care. Six years have elapsed. Allegedly, the mother has not visited the child and has abandoned it. A child adoption agency now petitions the Surrogate's Court for the custody of the child (Social Services Law, § 384, subd. 5). The cited statute is interpreted as requiring that the mother be notified of the petition -- in this case, the natural father does not live with her and is not claimed to be involved in any way -- and as requiring that the mother have counsel of her [***2] own choosing or that counsel be made available to her if she is indigent, and as requiring proof that the abandonment was "without good reason" (Social Services Law, § 371, subd. 2, par. [c]; see, also, Family Ct. Act, §§ 611, 614, 622). Thus interpreted, the statute is constitutional. An abandoned child has a constitutional right to have a permanent home with her adoptive parents, even if the natural parent objects that she herself is fit and that now she wants custody. Nor can the constitutional rights of the child be frustrated by any past lack of diligence on the part of the child care agency and the Department of Social Services (cf. Social Services Law, § 392). The Surrogate will try the issues. Motion to dismiss this petition is denied.

 

1674 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sprague


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Ontario County


69 Misc. 2d 663; 330 N.Y.S.2d 421; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2040



April 4, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons stated herein, defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment on constitutional grounds is denied. Since defendant makes no claim that he served notice on the District Attorney requesting that he be allowed to appear before the Grand Jury, his motion based on CPL 210.35 (subd. 4) is also denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with numerous drug-related crimes. Thereafter, he filed a motion to dismiss the indictment against him, under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 210.35(4) and 190.50(5)(d), on the ground that the district attorney failed to inform him of a grand jury proceeding against him and, thus, he lost his right to appear as a witness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with numerous crimes related to the possession and sale of drugs. Prior to the return of the indictment, defendant had not been arrested, arraigned in a local criminal court, or held for the action of a grand jury in relation to any of the charges in the indictment. Defendant moved to dismiss the indictment, under §§ 210.35(4) and 190.50(5)(d), based on the failure of the district attorney to inform him regarding the proceedings. The court denied the motion and held that the statutes, under which there was no requirement that any defendant be given notification that a grand jury proceeding against him was pending, were constitutional. The fact that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 190.50(5) required notification of grand jury proceedings to person who had been arraigned in a local criminal court upon an undisposed of felony complaint did not make N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 210.35(4) and 190.50(5)(d) unconstitutional on equal protection grounds. The court concluded that the law requiring notification to defendants in one situation and not to others was not capricious or arbitrary; that it had a reasonable, natural, and rational basis, and was based on public policy.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment on constitutional grounds and denied defendant's motion that he be allowed to appear before the grand jury.


HEADNOTES

Grand jury -- defendant's right to testify before Grand Jury -- person who has not been arrested or arraigned has no statutory or constitutional right to be notified that Grand Jury is considering indicting him.

A person who has not been arrested or arraigned is not statutorily or constitutionally entitled to be notified that the Grand Jury is considering indicting him (cf. CPL 190.50, subd. 5, par. [c]; CPL 210.35, subd. 4). His motion to dismiss the indictment for lack of such notice is denied.

 

1675 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wilhelm


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Buffalo


69 Misc. 2d 523; 330 N.Y.S.2d 279; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2052



March 30, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The charge is, therefore, dismissed and the defendant discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was charged with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 245.02, asserted that § 245.02 was unconstitutional per se or as applied. Defendant contended, in the alternative, that the information did not allege a crime and/or was otherwise insufficient.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was a photographer, took pictures of a partially-nude female model in his studio. He then was charged with violating § 245.02, which prohibited promoting the exposure of a woman whose breast was uncovered. The court dismissed the charge, finding that § 245.02 was unconstitutional as applied. The court concluded that it was obligated to construe § 245.02 according to the fair import of its terms, without reading in a proscription where the language did not create one. The court noted that the section barred a female's exposed breast, but did not prohibit the model's nudity in a private photography studio, and held that the information did not allege the crime or offense cited. The court also ruled that § 245.02 was being invoked and applied in a manner violative of the Due Process and Equal Protection Clauses of the United States Constitution and the New York State Constitution. Additionally, the section was enforced in a manner that exceeded the State's police power. The effect of § 245.02, as applied, was to oppress a certain class of individuals and entrepreneurs and unreasonably interfere with their presumptively legitimate activities.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the charge and discharged defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- topless females -- information charging defendant with violating section 245.02 of Penal Law by directing female to pose for picture taking in nude in his public place does not violate statute which renders person guilty of promoting exposure of female where he furnishes premises where female appears with portion of her breast below areola not covered with opaque covering.

An information charging that defendant promoted the exposure of a female in a public place for a fee of $ 20 by selling the services of said female for the purpose of taking pictures of her in the nude fails to charge a violation of section 245.02 of the Penal Law which renders a person guilty of promoting the exposure of a female when he furnishes public premises where a female appears with a portion of her breast below the top of the areola not covered with a fully opaque covering, except that the provision does not apply where the female is entertaining or performing "in a play, exhibition, show or entertainment". To apply the statute to the taking of pictures of nude females results in a violation of the due process and [***2] equal protection clauses of the United States Constitution and of the State Constitution and in a manner which exceeds the police power of the State. The statute as here applied is arbitrary and unreasonable and discriminatory against many legitimate businesses including the operators of photo studios, art studios, and art schools among others.

 

1676 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bonnerwith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Town of Rhinebeck, Dutchess County


69 Misc. 2d 516; 330 N.Y.S.2d 248; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2076



March 26, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, defendant's motion to have this writer disqualify himself is denied since there has not been demonstrated that this court could not render fair judgment based upon the facts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charges against him that he had violated Rhinebeck, N.Y., Local Law No. 1, § 422 (1971) on the ground that the statute was unconstitutional and filed a motion for the judge in the case to disqualify himself.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating Rhinebeck, N.Y., Local Law No. 1, § 422 (1971) in regard to the storage and sale of motor vehicles in an area zoned against such use. Defendant pleaded not guilty and sought to dismiss the charges, alleging that the statute was unconstitutional because it provided for a penalty upon conviction of a sentence of imprisonment for a period of not more then six months but it did not provide for a jury trial. Defendant also requested that the judge disqualify himself, alleging that the judge might be too familiar with the prosecution witnesses. The court denied both motions. Defendant was not entitled to a jury trial. A breach of the zoning law was a quasi-criminal violation rather than a crime. The court held that violations of zoning laws often involved technical questions of fact, local law or ordinance, which were best resolved by a court without jury. Zoning cases were to be heard before a court without a jury. The judge's knowledge of facts about the participants in the proceeding did not deprive him from hearing the action because defendant had not demonstrated that the court could not render fair judgment based upon the facts.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss, holding that the zoning law was constitutional. The court also denied defendant's motion for the judge to disqualify himself.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- in prosecution for violation of zoning law, since Town Law describes violation as "offense" and local law as "misdemeanor", Town Law prevails -- since violation is below grade of misdemeanor, defendant is not entitled to jury trial -- no basis for Town Justice's disqualification of himself because he may be familiar with People's witnesses.

1. In a prosecution against defendant for violation of a town's zoning law, since the Town Law ( § 268, subd. 1; § 135, subds. 1, 2) describes the violation as an "offense" and the zoning law as a "misdemeanor", the Town Law prevails.

2. In 1958 the Town Law was amended to substitute for the word "misdemeanor" the word "offense", and to add a statement that for enforcement purposes only a breach of a zoning law should be deemed a misdemeanor, but for purposes of conviction should be deemed an offense. In doing so, the Legislature made such a violation not a crime, but a quasi-criminal breach of the law now known [***2] as a violation. Since such violation is an offense of a grade less than a misdemeanor defendant is not entitled to a jury trial. Moreover, under Baldwin v. New York (399 U.S. 66), since the jail term provided by the zoning ordinance is six months, defendant would not be entitled to a jury trial.

3. No basis for the Town Justice's disqualification of himself exists because, as alleged by defendant, he "may be too familiar with all the witnesses on the People's side". As a local resident, a Town Justice necessarily is acquainted with many of the local people but that is not sufficient. Defendant has failed to show that said Justice could not render fair judgment based upon the facts.

 

1677 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gewirtz v. Long Beach


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


69 Misc. 2d 763; 330 N.Y.S.2d 495; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2079



March 24, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state residents petitioned the court to find Long Beach, N.Y. Local Law No. 9 illegal, unconstitutional, null and void in a case involving defendant city and a local ordinance dedicating land for use as a public park.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff state residents brought suit against defendant city and challenged the constitutionality of Long Beach, N.Y., Local Law No. 9. The court held that the local law was invalid to the extent that it attempted to restrict the use of a beach. The court found that when defendant created "a public park" out of its municipally-owned ocean front property, it intended to create and to maintain a facility open to use by the public at large. The court held that there was a completed dedication of the ocean beach facilities to the use of the public at large and that such dedication was irrevocable. It was beyond the governmental power of defendant to restrict the use of the beach forming part of the Ocean Beach Park to residents of the city and their invited guests without specific legislative authorization.


OUTCOME: The court held that a local law passed by defendant city and challenged by plaintiff state residents was invalid because it restricted use of the beach to residents of the city and invited guests without specific legislative authorization.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- public beach parks -- City of Long Beach acquired title to its beach front and dedicated it for use by public at large -- city has not been given legislative authority to change its use -- to extent that local law in 1970 attempted to restrict use of beach by residents only, it is invalid.

1. The City of Long Beach acquired title between 1935 and 1937 to its entire beach front by instruments of conveyance which did not contain restrictions. The city's intent to dedicate the beach and boardwalk to public use as a park is manifested by its adoption of a local law in 1936 and by ordinances prescribing a charge for use of the park. The city must be regarded as having accepted the facilities on behalf of the public at large. Once a dedication has become complete, it is irrevocable. When the city dedicated this property to use as a public park and thereafter devoted it to the use of the public at large for upwards of 30 years, it put itself in the position of holding that property subject to a public trust for the benefit of the public at large.

2. The city has not been given legislative authority to discontinue use of the beach park for the general [***2] public or to change its use.

3. The city could not, in 1970, restrict use of the beach park to residents of the city. A local law, adopted in 1970 without legislative authority, insofar as it purports to restrict the use of the park to residents, is invalid.

 

1678 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bambino


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


69 Misc. 2d 387; 329 N.Y.S.2d 922; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2101



March 15, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The third phase of the defendant's motion to suppress from evidence certain property, including, but not limited to, certain checks mentioned in the indictment alleged to have been seized by the police in violation of the defendant's constitutional rights, is hereby granted to the extent that a search and seizure hearing will be conducted pursuant to CPL 710.10, by the Trial Judge immediately prior to the trial herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought dismissal of a count of the indictment that charged him with loitering in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6). Defendant claimed that § 240.35(6) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with loitering under § 240.35(6), which provided that a person was guilty of loitering when he loitered in or about a place without apparent reason and under circumstances which justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in a crime, and refused to identify himself or failed to give a reasonably credible account of his conduct. The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the charge, finding that the statute was unconstitutional. The court reasoned that the prohibition against loitering about a place without apparent reason was an unconstitutional interference with freedom of movement and that portion of the statute was impermissibly vague, inadequate, and uncertain since it was inexplicit as to the prohibited activities. The court also determined that the part of the statute that allowed an arrest under circumstances justifying a suspicion that a person may be engaged or about to engage in a crime also rendered the statute unconstitutional. The court reasoned that the statute allowed an arrest without probable cause based upon an officer's subjective beliefs as opposed to the reasonable suspicion required to satisfy the due process clause.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the loitering count of the indictment against him.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- even if police officer saw defendant remove paper which obscured registration plate of defendant's automobile, and even if defendant refused to identify himself, officer's "suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime" ( Penal Law, § 240.35, subd. 6) was too vague and subjective; cited statute unconstitutional; charge dismissed.

Even if, as alleged, "defendant was observed by a peace officer removing a paper which obscured the license plate of his 1969 Chevrolet automobile bearing registration number 3978MZ", and even if, as alleged, "upon inquiry by a peace officer, the defendant refused to identify himself", these circumstances did not constitutionally justify his arrest upon "suspicion that he may be engaged or about [***2] to engage in crime" ( Penal Law, § 240.35, subd. 6). The cited statute is too vague and subjective to be constitutional. This charge against defendant is dismissed, on motion.

 

1679 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


903 Park Ave. Corp. v. City Rent Agency


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


69 Misc. 2d 604; 330 N.Y.S.2d 434; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2123



March 8, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation filed an application for a preliminary injunction in an action against defendant New York City Rent Agency, which sought a declaration that N.Y. City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 7 (1972) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 7 (1972) continued until June 30, 1972 the exemptions provided for the elderly poor contained in N.Y. City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 31. In May of 1971, the New York legislature passed a vacancy decontrol law, which provided for decontrol of rent controlled apartments vacated after July 1, 1972. A companion statute restricted New York City's powers to control rents on accommodations so decontrolled, or to place more stringent or restrictive controls on presently controlled apartments. The corporation argued that N.Y. City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 7 (1972) contravened the legislative injunction against more restrictive legislation. The court disagreed. The court had little doubt that the new local law was constitutional. The net effect of the new law was not to raise the umbrella of protection but to maintain it for those who were eligible for exemption, under N.Y. City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 30, but failed to take the technical steps necessary to avail themselves of the exemption. The new law did not change any rent control law in effect. Therefore, the court found that the equities weighed in favor of the Agency.


OUTCOME: The court denied the corporation's motion for a preliminary injunction, and it dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- exemption from rent increase -- Local Law No. 7 of 1972 of City of New York which extends applicability of 1970 local law enabling elderly poor to be exempt from rent increase is constitutional; complaint dismissed.

State law enjoins passage of local laws subjecting controlled housing accommodations to "more stringent provisions of regulation and control than those presently in effect". Local Law No. 30 of 1970 of the City of New York provided for a new system of maximum rents. Local Law No. 31 exempted eligible senior citizens from No. 30's rent increases. Local Law No. 7 of 1972 continues the exemptions for eligible elderly poor until June 30, 1972. The effect of Local Law No. 7 is not to protect more people but maintain it for those who were eligible for exemption under Local Law No. 30 but for some reason failed to take the technical steps necessary to avail themselves of it. It changes no rent control law and does not conflict with State law. Such law is constitutional. Plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction is denied and defendant's motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint [***2] is granted.

 

1680 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


McEvoy Dodge West Ridge, Inc. v. Zoning Bd. of Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


69 Misc. 2d 55; 329 N.Y.S.2d 171; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2166



February 26, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is denied and dismissed in accordance with the above decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner filed a proceeding attacking the constitutionality of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 239-m, which provided for respondent planning council.


OVERVIEW: The landowner applied to respondent, zoning board of appeals (board), for approval to erect signs on its premises. The square footage request was beyond what was provided in the ordinance, and the board adjourned pending the decision of the council, which took jurisdiction of the application pursuant to § 239-m because the landowner's property was located near a state highway. The council rejected the applications. Thereafter, the board approved the applications with respect to four of the signs, which received the affirmative vote of a majority plus one of the board members as required by § 239-m. The landowner challenged the denial of one of the signs claiming that § 239-m was unconstitutional. The court denied the petition finding that the landowner was not deprived of any of its constitutional procedural safeguards. The landowner was granted a hearing with notice and had the opportunity to present its evidence. However, because the council's decisions affected matters that had county-wide importance, § 239-m made it harder to overturn a decision of the planning council, which was required to look at the nine criteria set forth in § 239-l in its deliberations.


OUTCOME: The court denied and dismissed the landowner's petition attacking the constitutionality of the statute providing for the council.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- "vote of a majority plus one" -- if county's Planning Board disapproves application for special permit, town's Zoning Board of Appeals cannot override Planning Board, "except by a vote of a majority plus one of all the members" (General Municipal Law, § 239-m); on six-member board, four votes for overriding are not enough -- sections 239-l and 239-m of General Municipal Law are constitutional.

1. An application by a business corporation for special permission to erect, on its property "within a distance of five hundred feet from * * * state * * * highway", a free-standing sign much larger than allowed by the town ordinance, was disapproved by the county's Planning Council, and therefore it could not be approved by the town's Zoning Board of Appeals "except by a vote of a majority plus one of all the members" of the Zoning Board of Appeals (General Municipal Law, § 239-m). The board had six members. Four votes for approval of the application, as against two votes for disapproval of the application, were not enough. The application was denied.

2. Sections 239-l and [***2] 239-m of the General Municipal Law are valid and constitutional.

 

1681 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Application of Anonymous


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


69 Misc. 2d 181; 329 N.Y.S.2d 542; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2170



February 24, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state hospital applied for an order to transfer and commit respondent patient to a state hospital under the jurisdiction of the Department of Correction on the ground that he was "dangerously mentally ill" after he committed several assaults during his involuntary commitment.


OVERVIEW: The patient was involuntarily committed to a state hospital under § 72 of the Mental Hygiene Law. He committed several assaults, and the hospital applied for an order to transfer and commit him to another state hospital that was under the jurisdiction of the Department of Correction because he was "dangerously mentally ill." The patient objected, arguing that he was not dangerously mentally ill and that even if he were, the transfer would violate the equal protection and due process clauses of the federal and state Constitutions. The court denied the application because the transfer and commitment did not have a rational basis and was violative of the patient's constitutional rights. The patient was not charged with any crime, nor was he involved in the criminal process. That a person was "dangerously mentally ill" was neither a crime nor a basis for the denial of constitutional rights. The court held that no legitimate legislative end could be served by incarcerating him with convicted criminals while alternative facilities were available, and it would unnecessarily impose even greater restrictions on his liberty than were reasonably necessary to the legislative end sought.


OUTCOME: The court denied the state hospital's application to transfer and commit a patient it determined was "dangerously mentally ill" to another hospital that was under the jurisdiction of the Department of Corrections after the patient committed several assaults.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Mental health -- dangerously mentally ill persons -- dangerously mentally ill person who is not involved in any criminal process cannot constitutionally be placed in custody of Department of Correction at Matteawan State Hospital, section 85 of Mental Hygiene Law to contrary notwithstanding.

A patient in a civil State hospital of the Department of Mental Hygiene, who is "so dangerously mentally ill that his presence in such a hospital is dangerous to the safety of other patients therein, the officers or employees thereof, or to the community", but who is not involved in any criminal action or proceeding, cannot constitutionally be "placed" in an institution operated by the Department of Correction, such as Matteawan State Hospital, section 85 of the Mental Hygiene Law to the contrary notwithstanding (cf. Correction Law, § 400 et seq. and CPL art. 730).

 

1682 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schneider v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


68 Misc. 2d 869; 328 N.Y.S.2d 996; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2182



February 21, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens sought consolidated review of two proceedings pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 5 of III and N.Y. Laws § 773 (1911), and filed motions pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2215 for an order pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(7)(a), 3211(c), and 3212(a) adjudging N.Y. Laws § 11 (1972) to be constitutional and valid and dismissing the petitions. Respondent officials filed a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The citizens commenced proceedings for a review of the apportionment and districting of the state into senate and assembly districts arguing that the apportionment was unconstitutional and void because it was not in compliance with the state constitution, the statute raised questions as to whether partisan gerrymandering was justiciable, it improperly fixed the number of senate members, and it was not based on final census data. The citizens requested the court direct that a constitutionally valid plan of apportionment be enacted and that the named officials, their agents and employees, be temporarily and permanently directed to treat the law and the apportionment and districting of the senate and assembly districts as void and of no effect. The officials filed a motion for summary judgment. The court granted the officials' motion for summary judgment and denied the petition, dismissing the citizens' case, holding that in its opinion, the statute was in accord with the state constitution and granted summary judgment declaring and acknowledging that it was valid and constitutional apportionment statute.


OUTCOME: The court denied the citizens' motions in both proceedings and dismissed their petitions to declare the statutes unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Legislature -- chapter 11 of Laws of 1972 apportioning Legislature into 150 Assembly Districts and 60 Senate Districts is constitutional.

1. In the apportionment and districting of the State into Senate and Assembly districts by chapter 11 of the Laws of 1972, the Legislature did not unconstitutionally abandon county lines as district boundaries. Petitioners' allegations and conclusions do not make a prima facie showing of failure to comply with subsisting provisions of article III of the Constitution for they make no showing that the historic and traditional significance of counties and towns in the districting process was not continued where and as far as possible.

2. Conclusory allegations that the districts created are neither "compact" nor "convenient" do not make a prima facie showing of failure to comply with the subsisting provisions of article III of the Constitution.

3. An argument that "gerrymandering" is justiciable does not make a prima facie showing of failure to comply with article III of the Constitution. The statement that chapter 11 shows numerous examples of districts drawn to minimize the votes of some and maximize [***2] the votes of others in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution raises no question of fact.

4. Chapter 11 does not unlawfully fix the size of the Senate at 60 members, thereby allegedly violating sections 3 and 4 of article III of the Constitution. The procedure followed by the Legislature in arriving at a Senate of 60 members complies with the ruling in Matter of Fay (291 N. Y. 198).

5. Chapter 11 is constitutional.

 

1683 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Wright v. Town Bd. of Carlton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


70 Misc. 2d 1; 332 N.Y.S.2d 233; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2205



February 15, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers sought review of an order made by respondent town board (New York), which created a town-wide water district and assessed the capital costs thereof against the various parcels of real property in the town on a so-called benefit basis.


OVERVIEW: The town board ordered the creation of a town-wide water district and assessed the capital costs thereof against the various parcels of real property in the town on a so-called benefit basis. Petitioners filed an action challenging the town's order. Upon review, the court conditionally granted a judgment in favor of the town. The court found to the extent that a town law restricted participation in the referendum to owners of taxable real property situated in the proposed district, it was unconstitutional where a significant portion of the ultimate burden of paying property taxes was passed on to tenants and lessees or was recovered by businesses in the prices of goods and services. The court determined the town's fixing of district boundaries so as to encompass the entire township was not arbitrary where it was not shown that the decision was so unjust as to have amounted to a confiscation of land. The court concluded petitioner's complaint regarding the flat rate or unit plan of assessment proposed by the county was premature because no assessment by the town had been made. The court found a further hearing was needed to determine whether persons were denied the right to vote.


OUTCOME: The court held it would confirm an order of the town board that created a water district, unless it was shown that persons were denied the right to vote at said special election even though they were the owners of taxable real property. The court found the issue of notice needed to be cleared up by a hearing at a trial term of the court.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- water districts -- in proceeding to review order of Town Board creating town-wide water district, vote in referendum by voters who were not owners of taxable property was lawful -- different degrees of benefit from improvement will be reflected in assessments made -- since no plan of assessment has been adopted, there is nothing that court can review -- petitioners' private wells could become polluted and so their lands would would still benefit from potable water supply.

1. In a proceeding to review an order of a Town Board creating a town-wide water district and assessing the capital costs against the various parcels of real property in the town on a benefit basis, subdivision 3 of section 209-e of the Town Law, to the extent that it restricts participation in the referendum to "owners" of taxable real property, is unconstitutional and participation in the special election held on April 29, 1971 by qualified voters who were not owners of taxable real property was lawful.

2. The water district was coterminous with the town boundaries. Three classes of benefited properties were created. All petitioners fall into the directly benefited class. A [***2] finding by the Town Board that all lands in the town would be benefited by the improvements is an exercise of legislative power and since it has not been shown to be arbitrary or so unjust as to amount to a confiscation of property, it will not be interfered with. Moreover, petitioners cannot raise the question since they do not own lands in the indirectly benefited category. The imbalance in voting power is inherent when the owners of all benefited lands are entitled to vote upon the installation of a public improvement. The differing degrees of benefit will be reflected in the assessments made upon each.

3. Petitioners' claim that the unit plan of assessment proposed is arbitrary is premature since there is no determination with respect to benefit assessments which this court can review.

4. Even though petitioners have wells from which they receive water, it cannot be said that their lands would not benefit since pollution of their private water supplies would render the provision of an adequate supply of potable water a benefit.

 

1684 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Southampton v. Tekworth


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


69 Misc. 2d 291; 329 N.Y.S.2d 225; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2213



February 14, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's cross motion for summary judgment is granted, and plaintiff is awarded judgment permanently enjoining the defendant from permitting or providing entertainment upon defendant's premises without having obtained a license to do so under Local Law No. 1 of 1970 of the Village of Southampton, subject to the plaintiff's excising therefrom the aforesaid provisions which the court has found to be null and void.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant business owner sought summary judgment against plaintiff village based upon the first, second, third, fourth, fifth and seventh counterclaims and for judgment declaring a certain local law of the village to be unconstitutional. The village cross-moved for summary judgment upon its cause of action for a permanent injunction, enjoining and restraining the owner from operating its business without a license pursuant to local law.


OVERVIEW: The owner applied for a license pursuant to a local law, which required a license for entertainment in places that are open to the public. The owner's application was denied. The owner appealed the decision of the building inspector to the village board, which conducted a hearing and denied the owner's appeal. The parties both sought summary judgment. The court granted the owner's motion for summary judgment as to Southampton, N.Y. Local Law No. 1, §§ 5, 14 and 15(b) (1970) (local law) as they were declared null and void. The court granted the village's cross motion for summary judgment, awarded judgment permanently enjoining the owner from permitting or providing entertainment upon his premises without having obtained a license to do so under the local law, subject to the village excising the null and void provisions therefrom. The court held that § 5, relating to sound, could not reasonably be complied with. The court found that §§ 14 and 15(b), relating to the revocation and suspension of the licenses, violated the principles of due process of law. The court found that the remaining provisions were reasonable and related to the public health, safety, morals and general welfare.


OUTCOME: The court granted the owner's motion for summary judgment and declared certain sections of the local law null and void. The court granted the village's cross motion for summary judgment, awarded judgment permanently enjoining the owner from permitting or providing entertainment upon his premises without having obtained a license to do so under the local law, subject to the village excising the null and void provisions therefrom.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- licensing of places of entertainment -- village may validly require license for any place of live or mechanical entertainment -- but following provisions of local law are invalid: no sound shall be audible after 11:00 p.m. 100 feet away; suspension shall remain in effect pending appeal; license may be revoked if licensee violates any other ordinance of village, even though no charge or conviction therefor -- such provisions contravene due process.

1. A village's local law requiring a license for any place of public entertainment, whether by live performance or by radio or phonograph or "juke box" or mechanical device, is valid. A tavern, whose application for such a license was denied by the Building Inspector and then by the Village [***2] Board, is permanently enjoined from furnishing entertainment.

2. However, the following portions of the local law contravene due process and are invalid: (a) a provision that no sound from such a place shall be plainly audible between 11:00 p.m. and 7:00 a.m. at a distance of 100 feet; (b) a provision that a temporary suspension of a license shall remain in effect during an appeal; and (c) a provision that a license may be suspended or revoked if the licensee violates any other ordinance of the village "even though no charge of such violation is made or conviction therefor had".

 

1685 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. State Div. of Human Rights


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


68 Misc. 2d 1035; 330 N.Y.S.2d 274; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2219; 4 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 752; 4 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P7883



February 11, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the foregoing reasons, plaintiffs' motion for a preliminary injunction is denied and defendants' cross motion to dismiss the complaint is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, Board of Education and associated individuals, commenced a case against defendants, State Division of Human Rights and associated individuals, seeking a permanent injunction to restrain defendants from conducting a hearing concerning allegations of racial discrimination and a declaratory judgment adjudging the Human Rights Law, N.Y. Exec. Law §§ 290-301, as unconstitutional. Defendants filed a cross motion to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: A teacher filed a complaint with defendants that charged plaintiffs with unlawful discriminatory practices in failing to renew her employment contract. Defendants conducted a preliminary investigation into the teacher's complaint and determined that there was probable cause to believe that plaintiffs engaged in discriminatory practices. Plaintiffs initiated a case against defendants seeking a permanent injunction to restrain defendants from conducting a hearing on the discrimination charges. Plaintiffs claimed that they had been damaged in the performance of their professional duties by the allegedly unfounded finding of probable cause and were denied due process because there was no statutory procedure for them to challenge the probable cause finding. The court held that plaintiffs had no standing to challenge the preliminary administrative determination because they were in the middle of due process since they were notified of the hearing and would have an opportunity to be heard at the hearing. The court concluded that there was no valid reason for judicial intervention because a preliminary injunction would thwart the Division's mandated function under the Human Rights Law.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiffs' motion for a preliminary injunction and granted defendants' cross motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Civil rights -- State Division of Human Rights -- preliminary determination that "there is probable cause to believe that the person named in the complaint", (a school board) "has engaged or is engaging in an unlawful discriminatory practice" (Executive Law, § 297, subd. 2) is constitutional.

A determination by a regional director of the State Division of Human Rights that "there is probable cause to believe that the person named in the complaint [here, a school board] has engaged or is engaging in an unlawful discriminatory practice" (Executive Law, § 297, subd. 2) is not final and does not violate the constitutional right of the school board to due process of law. The Human Rights Law (Executive Law, § 290 et seq.) fairly provides for due notice and a plenary hearing on the complaint. The school board's application for a preliminary injunction against any hearing on a black teacher's complaint of racial discrimination is denied, and the school board's action for an injunction and a declaratory judgment of unconstitutionality [***2] is dismissed, on motion.

 

1686 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Cordero v. Thomas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


69 Misc. 2d 28; 329 N.Y.S.2d 131; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2230



February 9, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, upon relator's posting of bail on the charge pending in this court, the writ of habeas corpus is granted, the parole violation warrant shall be vacated and the relator released unless the Parole Board holds a due process parole revocation hearing within 20 days after the date of the oral decision rendered herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator inmate brought a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, which sought relief from imprisonment on an alleged parole violation. The inmate alleged that his detention was illegal and requested that he either be discharged or that bail be fixed on his parole violation warrant. While he was on parole for a robbery conviction, the inmate was arrested for assault, and held on the parole violation pending the outcome of his assault trial.


OVERVIEW: The issues before the court were whether it had jurisdiction over the writ and whether the inmate, in being held without bond on the parole violation, had been deprived of due process of law. The court held that the inmate was entitled to habeas corpus relief, finding that: (1) the writ was properly issued and made returnable in the county in which the parole violation was pending; (2) the arrest of the inmate on the assault charge constituted reasonable cause for the issuance of the parole violation warrant; (3) the legislative intent of N.Y. Correct. Law § 212(7) was that a parolee receive a prompt hearing upon an alleged parole violation; (4) in refusing to allow the inmate bail while the assault charge was pending against him, the Parole Board circumvented the presumption of innocence; and (5) it was offensive to the concept of due process that the inmate was deprived of his liberty, indefinitely, without any opportunity to provide bail to assure his continued presence within the jurisdiction, and without any hearing whatsoever to ascertain whether was any actual basis for so depriving him of his liberty, other than the fact that he had been accused of a crime.


OUTCOME: Upon the inmate's posting of bail on the charge pending before the court, the writ of habeas corpus was granted, the parole violation warrant was vacated, and the inmate was released, unless the Parole Board held a due process parole revocation hearing within 20 days after the date of the court's decision.


HEADNOTES

Parole -- revocation based on charges against parolee -- right of parolee to bail -- since charges against parolee are pending in Supreme Court, Kings County, such court has jurisdiction of habeas corpus proceeding even though parolee is in Queens County prison -- while on parole, charges laid against parolee resulted in indictment; Parole Board filed detainer warrant; although parolee can post bail for pending indictment, detainer warrant prevents release; no hearing has been held for seven months; arrest of parolee for new charges furnished reasonable cause for issuance of detainer warrant; parolee not entitled to prewarrant hearing -- denial of postwarrant hearing at earliest opportunity resulted in illegally depriving parolee of liberty and denied parolee due process -- upon parolee's posting of bail, his release is ordered and parole violation warrant  [***2] is vacated unless hearing within 20 days is had.

1. The Supreme Court of Kings County, in which an indictment against relator is pending and which issued the writ of habeas corpus, has jurisdiction even though relator is detained in a prison in Queens County.

2. While paroled, relator became involved in community affairs and was arrested in the course of street demonstrations for certain crimes, and bail in the sum of $ 2,500 was fixed. A month later the Parole Board filed a detainer warrant with the warden of the prison where relator is being detained charging him with violation of parole. No hearing has been held by the Parole Board even though seven months have elapsed, the Parole Board taking the position that it will await the outcome of the charges against relator. Relator has been unable to obtain his release on bail because of the parole violation detainer. The arrest of relator for said crimes constituted reasonable cause for the issuance of the parole violation warrant. The court lacks the power to go behind the warrant and relator was not entitled as a matter of right to a prewarrant hearing. The filing of the warrant with the warden furnished jurisdiction for [***3] a parole revocation proceeding.

3. The Correction Law (§ 212, subd. 7) requires that a hearing in a parole revocation proceeding be held "at the first available opportunity". There has been a failure to comply with the statute and relator is being illegally deprived of his liberty. The holding of a hearing in advance of the disposition of the crimes would enable relator to determine whether or not he would answer questions relating to the crimes. His privilege against self incrimination is a personal one to be exercised by him at the hearing. The procedure whereby relator, upon arrest, may be deprived of his liberty indefinitely without any opportunity to provide bail, to assure his continued presence within the jurisdiction, and without any hearing whatsoever to ascertain whether there be any actual basis for so depriving him of his liberty, other than the fact that he has been accused of crimes, violates due process.

4. Upon relator's posting of bail on the charge pending in this court, the writ of habeas corpus is granted, the parole violation warrant is vacated and relator will be released unless the Parole Board holds a due process parole violation hearing within 20 days.

 

1687 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


County of Orange v. Newburgh


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


68 Misc. 2d 998; 328 N.Y.S.2d 891; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2238



February 7, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's prayer for relief is therefore denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county filed an action against defendant city seeking, inter alia, an order directing the city to collect state and county taxes and seeking a final judgment declaring that the city must comply with the tax collection provisions of N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 9. The action was brought after the city decided to stop collecting the taxes.


OVERVIEW: The city operated under a charter, and it decided to stop collecting state and county taxes on behalf of those entities, and it repealed Newburgh, N.Y., Ordinance art. 1, § 9-1, relieving itself of the obligation. The county filed an action seeking to force it to continue collecting the taxes, contending, inter alia, that the city was under a direct mandate to collect the taxes by reason of N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law §§ 900 and 904, and that it would be irreparably harmed if it had to collect its own taxes. The city contended it would be inviting bankruptcy if it continued to bear the burden for uncollected county taxes and that the tax law was not a mandate. The court denied the county's prayer for relief, holding that there was no legal basis to impose an obligation on the city to collect the taxes. The tax law did not explicitly impose a duty on cities to collect county taxes, it presupposed the existence of such a duty and set guidelines for the county to issue tax warrants to cities and towns. There was no general law or constitutional requirement imposing such a duty and no obligation imposed on the city to collect county taxes unless it elected to do so.


OUTCOME: The court denied the county's prayer for relief.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- collection of county taxes -- no obligation is imposed on defendant city by its charter to collect taxes for plaintiff county; there is no basis for imposing that obligation on defendant city -- county not irreparably harmed by defendant's action in relieving itself of burden of collecting such taxes.

1. There is no obligation imposed on defendant city (by its charter) to collect county taxes for the County of Orange unless it elects to do so. There is no legal basis to impose that obligation. Plaintiff county is not entitled to a declaration that defendant city collect such taxes.

2. Since section 972 of the Real Property Tax Law provides an optional method whereby the county can collect its own taxes along with a service charge for so doing, plaintiff will not be irreparably harmed by defendant's repeal of an ordinance which had the effect of relieving itself of the obligation to collect county taxes.

 

1688 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Zipser v. Pound


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, White Plains


69 Misc. 2d 152; 329 N.Y.S.2d 494; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2240



February 4, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff owner's action to recover for certain art works that were destroyed on the grounds that the owner did not comply with the 90-day notice provision of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e(1).


OVERVIEW: The owner alleged that the county destroyed its art works without permission and filed an action against the county. The county filed a motion to dismiss on the grounds that the owner did not comply with the 90-day notice provision of § 50-e(1). The court denied the motion and held that the statute was unconstitutional. The court noted that § 50-e effectively created two separate and distinct classes of plaintiffs. The first class was a private plaintiff who sued a private defendant. In such a case, the plaintiff could have three years to assert a claim. The second class involved plaintiffs who sued the county. In such a case, the plaintiffs would only have 90 days to assert a claim. The court held that such classes violated the due process and equal protection laws of the state and federal constitutions. The court held that the law was unfair, arbitrary, and gave only one-sided protection.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- notice of claim -- constitutional law -- section 50-e of General Municipal Law which grants negligence plaintiff 90 days to assert claim against municipality as compared with three years against others is unconstitutional as denying him equal protection of law.

A plaintiff having a negligence claim, if asserted against another, has a limitation period of three years but if asserted against a municipality has, by virtue of section 50-e of the General Municipal Law (subd. 1), 90 days only. Section 50-e thereby denies plaintiff equal protection of the law and is unconstitutional.

 

1689 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sher


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Greene County


68 Misc. 2d 917; 329 N.Y.S.2d 2; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2241



February 4, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons stated above the motions to suppress the evidence acquired by eavesdropping are denied, as are the motions for dismissal of the indictment, on the ground that those minutes indicate a sufficiency of legal evidence and the jurisdiction of this court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed motions to suppress alleged recordings of telephonic communications and for an order to permit inspection of the grand jury minutes or, in the alternative, dismissing the indictments. Defendants were indicted and charged with promoting gambling in the first degree, possession of gambling records in the first degree, and conspiracy in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, indicted on gambling and conspiracy charges, filed the motions to suppress telephone recordings obtained by the police. The court held that the names of persons overheard during telephone surveillance were not required to be named in the warrant, pursuant to N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 825, unless their identities were known and that the warrants contained all elements statutorily required. The court then found that the police obtained the amendment to the warrant "as soon as practicable," for purposes of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 825(4), when 72 days transpired between the original conversation indicating that a crime not mentioned in the warrant was being committed and the issuance of the amended warrant. Defendants failed to sustain their burden of proving that their substantial rights were violated by the warrant or the amending order. Further, police properly obtained authorization to intercept conversations of persons after their crimes were described over the telephone. The overheard information gave rise to the probable cause that another crime was about to be committed. The court denied defendants' motions.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motions to suppress the evidence of telephonic communications and for the order to permit inspection of the grand jury minutes or, in the alternative, to dismiss the indictments.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- eavesdropping warrants -- geographical jurisdiction of conspiracy -- eavesdropping warrants were validly and constitutionally amended nunc pro tunc and "as soon as practicable" ( CPL 700.65, subd. 4; Code Crim. Pro., § 825, subd. 4) -- omission to file ( CPL 700.55) did not invalidate warrants and evidence -- Grand Jury of another county, in which overt act occurred, had jurisdiction to indict all conspirators ( CPL 20.40, subd. 1) -- defendants' motions to suppress evidence and dismiss indictments are denied.

1. Upon probable cause, warrants were issued in Albany County on May 4 and May 26 and June 23 and July 22, 1971 to intercept telephone calls to or from certain stated telephone numbers. The first warrant recited, as bases, gambling and conspiracy, the second warrant added criminal usury and grand larceny by extortion, the third warrant extended the duration of the second warrant, and the fourth [***2] warrant amended "as soon as practicable" the first two warrants nunc pro tunc ( CPL 700.65, subd. 4; Code Crim. Pro., § 825, subd. 4). The intercepted conversations led to further evidence, and the evidence thus obtained showed prima facie that defendant Sher in Albany County communicated with defendant D'Apice in Kings County and that they conspired to compel a certain person to pay a gambling debt, that defendant Sher met said debtor in Greene County and arranged to have said debtor pay, that defendant Amash subsequently collected some of such moneys from said debtor in Greene County, and that, when said debtor failed to pay, defendant Amash assaulted him in Greene County. Thereafter, said three defendants were arrested and a Greene County Grand Jury properly indicted them for conspiracy, criminal usury, and assault ( Penal Law, §§ 105.05, 105.10, 190.40, 120.05). These eavesdropping warrants were valid and constitutional even though the first warrant did not mention these ultimately ascertained defendants, and the second warrant named only one of them. The nunc pro tunc warrant named all of them.

2. The eavesdropping warrants and the evidence thus obtained were not voided [***3] by the subsequent ministerial omission to comply with CPL 700.55 which directs that the warrants and supporting affidavits and transcripts of the recordings be kept in the custody of the court.

3. The Greene County Grand Jury had jurisdiction, and its indictment properly included all three defendants as conspirators. Overt acts in furtherance of the conspiracy occurred in Greene County. ( CPL 1.10, subd. 2, and 20.40, subd. 1; Penal Law, § 105.20; § 105.25, subd. 1.)

4. Therefore, defendants' motions to suppress the evidence ( CPL 710.20) and dismiss the indictment are denied.

 

1690 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Meredith v. Connally


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Rensselaer County


68 Misc. 2d 956; 328 N.Y.S.2d 719; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2251



February 3, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The plaintiffs' request for a preliminary injuction is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers filed an action to enjoin enforcement of Troy, N.Y., Local Law No. 1 (1972), which provided that defendant city council should appoint the corporation counsel instead of the city manager.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers filed an action to block enforcement of a local law providing for the appointment of the city's corporation counsel by the city council rather than the city manager. The taxpayers argued that the law impermissibly amended the local charter; the court disagreed and refused to issue the injunction. The city charter did not provide methods for its own amendment, so the court had to look to New York Municipal Home Rule Law. The city council's change in the appointment method of the corporation counsel did not constitute a new charter. Neither the city manager nor the corporation counsel was an elective office. There was no requirement for a mandatory referendum when the powers of an appointive office were abolished or curtailed. Because the working conditions of the city manager and the corporation counsel as to salary, compensation, and hours were not fixed by state statute, there was no requirement for a mandatory referendum.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayer's motion for a preliminary injunction to block enforcement of the local law providing for the appointment of corporation counsel by the city council rather than the city manager.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- charter amendments -- Troy City Council, by local law, without referendum, validly amended City Charter so as to take away City Manager's power to appoint Corporation Counsel and so as to vest such power in itself (Municipal Home Rule Law, §§ 10, 11, 21, 23, 24) -- in effect, City Council temporarily suspended its own inner rule which provided that it should ask Corporation Counsel to draft proposed local laws.

1. Under the Troy City Charter (Local Laws, 1959, No. 3 of City of Troy; published in Local Laws of 1963), the elected City Council appoints a City Manager and the latter appoints a Corporation Counsel (Charter, § 3.00; § 6.02). The City Council has now (Local Laws, 1972, No. 1 of City of Troy) validly and constitutionally placed in itself the power to appoint the Corporation Counsel. The City Council's power to enact such a charter amendment, without any referendum, is set forth in other provisions of the Troy City Charter (§ 2.12, subd. [b]; § 2.26) and in the Municipal Home Rule Law ( §§ 10, 11, 21, 23, 24).

2. Rules which the City Council adopted in 1964 for its [***2] own inner working are not jurisdictional, and the City Council may, in effect, suspend any such rule temporarily. Thus, one of those rules provides that the City Council shall ask the Corporation Counsel to draft proposed local laws and to indorse his approval on such a draft. It would have been folly to ask the Corporation Counsel to draft and approve a local law which would result in removing him from office; and, in effect, the City Council validly suspended such rule temporarily.

 

1691 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williams


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Bronx County


68 Misc. 2d 1074; 328 N.Y.S.2d 709; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2255



February 3, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the foregoing reasons, this motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to reconsider and reduce two 15-year sentences imposed for drug offenses, alleging that the sentences were excessive and constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that consecutive 15-year sentences for drug offenses were excessive because he was a small-scale street pusher, and alleged that he was punished for pleading not guilty and putting the prosecution to its proof. He also argued that the sentence failed to comply with N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law §§ 207, 208 because an examination to determine whether he was a drug addict was not performed before his sentencing. In denying the motion, the court rejected the claim that the sentence was influenced by the prosecutor's recommendation of a severe sentence because defendant "put the People to their proof." The sentences imposed were within the range established by statute and were not cruel and inhumane. Where defendant had previously been certified as a narcotics addict, §§ 207, 208 were complied with, notwithstanding the suspension of those provisions due to budgetary constraints.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to reduce the sentences imposed on defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- so-called small-fry drug pusher was convicted, by jury, of two Class C felonies and two Class D felonies and two Class A misdemeanors; Class C felonies were for selling narcotics on two separate dates; Probation Department reported that he had previously been certified to Narcotic Addiction Control Commission and had violated parole three times; court now sentences him to two indeterminate terms, to be served consecutively, each with 15-year maximum (Penal Law, §§ 70.00, 70.25; CPL 440.20, subd. 1) -- court rejects joint request of defense and prosecution to make both terms concurrent -- since April 20, 1971, new medical examinations and certifications of drug addicts (Mental Hygiene Law, §§ 208, 210-a) have been suspended, for lack of funds.

1. After trial by jury, defendant was convicted of two Class C felonies and two Class D felonies and two Class A misdemeanors. The Class C felonies -- sales of narcotic drugs -- had occurred on two separate dates, August 24 and August 26, 1971. The Probation Department's report showed that, three years ago, he had been convicted of "mugging"  [***2] a woman and stealing her pocketbook and that he had then been medically examined and found to be a drug addict and had been certified to the custody of the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission; that said commission paroled him three times and three times he violated his parole; that he had been previously arrested several times on drug-related charges; and that, at the time of his arrest in August, 1971, he was wanted on two other drug-related warrants of arrest. This court sentenced him to two indeterminate terms of imprisonment, to be served consecutively, each with a 15-year maximum (Penal Law, § 70.00, subd. 2, par. [c]; § 70.25; CPL 440.20, subd. 1). Defendant's motion to set aside or reduce the sentence is denied.

2. The sentence is not excessive or cruel or unusual. The court rejects the joint request of the defendant's counsel and the District Attorney to make the two terms concurrent. The court believes that the so-called "small-fry drug pushers" are the main agents of the "big-time dope dealers" and that they must be taken out of circulation.

3. Pursuant to the Mental Hygiene Law ( § 210-a), the Chairman of the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission has written [***3] a letter dated April 20, 1971 to the Administrator of the Judicial Conference, which has the effect of suspending, for lack of funds, any new medical examinations and certifications under sections 207 to 210 of the Mental Hygiene Law.

 

1692 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pettigrew


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Suffolk County


69 Misc. 2d 985; 332 N.Y.S.2d 33; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2256



February 2, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant is found guilty as charged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with disorderly conduct in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20. Police authorities asked defendant to move on after a crowd had gathered blocking the sidewalk and causing the blockage of part of the street to the extent that both pedestrian and vehicular traffic were impeded.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, dressed as a turkey, in conjunction with another, dressed as Santa Claus, made speeches and handed out literature criticizing the commercialism of Christmas, the Vietnam War, the Kent State incident, racial inequality, and other matters. About 75 people gathered, and the crowd subsequently increased to about 150. The police asked defendant to move on. Convicting defendant, the court noted that he was aware of and consciously and recklessly disregarded the risk of public inconvenience or annoyance, a risk created by his own conduct. In addition, the court found that defendant caused the obstruction of both vehicular and pedestrian traffic, and that he congregated with other persons in a public place, refusing to comply with a lawful order of the police to disperse. The court emphasized that it made no comment and was in no way influenced by whatever defendant might have said in his speeches. In fact, the record was virtually bare in this regard. The court emphatically reaffirmed the right of defendant to have embrace and vigorously to have propagated his views, but the refusal to obey violated the statute.


OUTCOME: The court convicted defendant of disorderly conduct.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- disorderly conduct -- under information charging disorderly conduct, evidence regarding violation of any subdivision of section 240.20 of Penal Law is sufficient to sustain conviction -- defendant was dressed as turkey and made speeches against Vietnam War, and other subjects; crowd grew from 100 to 150 people; defendant breached statute when he refused police order to move -- his conduct was not protected by right of free speech and free assembly.

1. Even though the information charging defendant with disorderly conduct refers to a violation of subdivision 6 of section 240.20 of the Penal Law (congregating in a public place and refusing to comply with a lawful order to disperse), evidence regarding the violation of any other subdivision is admissible and is sufficient to sustain a conviction of violation of any other subdivision.

2. Defendant was dressed as a turkey and was making speeches criticizing the commercialism of Christmas, the Vietnam War, the Kent State incident and other matters. Even though defendant was accompanied by another person dressed as Santa Claus, there was sufficient evidence to establish that [***2] some of the crowd was attracted by defendant. Defendant was aware of and consciously and recklessly disregarded the risk of public inconvenience or annoyance, a risk created by his own conduct. Moreover, defendant caused the obstruction of vehicular and pedestrian traffic, congregated with others in a public place and refused to comply with a lawful order of the police to disperse. Defendant is therefore guilty as charged.

3. Defendant's conduct is not protected by the constitutional guarantee of the right to free speech and assembly on the facts here present. Defendant refused to move when directed to do so by the police. The crowd grew, from 75 to 100 people when the officer arrived, to 150 when defendant refused the order to move.

Harris v. Jacobs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


69 Misc. 2d 4; 329 N.Y.S.2d 229; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2257



February 1, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Since a motion for summary judgment searches the record ( CPLR 3212, subd. [b]) and since all parties have agreed that no factual issues are in dispute, summary judgment is granted in favor of defendants and the complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer instituted an action against defendant, Treasurer of Suffolk County and Suffolk County (county), to restrain the county from collecting unpaid taxes on real property in Suffolk County. The county filed a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer owed taxes on real property. The taxpayer instituted an action against the county to prevent it from implementation of a change in the procedure of processing the collection of unpaid taxes for real property in Suffolk County, and to restore the previous practice of selling tax certificates at public auction. The county filed a motion for summary judgment. The court granted the motion for summary judgment. The court found that the Suffolk County Tax Act, § 45 was constitutional because a county purpose could include the use of tax moneys to build and operate hospitals, clinics, transit systems, water supply, and other utilities, all in competition with private enterprise. Further, the court found that the notice, advertisement, and mechanics of the sale were not in violation of law.


OUTCOME: The court granted the county's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- tax liens, tax sales and tax titles -- Suffolk County Tax Act ( § 45) has been validly and constitutionally amended so that county may reject any bids or all bids, and may bid in any or all properties at only 3% interest for six months -- in 1971 tax sale announcement, erroneous words "tax liens" were clarified by subsequent words "lands" and "parcels".

1. Section 45 of the Suffolk County Tax Act has been validly and constitutionally amended (by L. 1971, ch. 915 and by appropriate resolutions of the Suffolk County Legislature) so that now, at the annual tax sale, the County Treasurer may reject any bids or all the bids and, on behalf of the county, may bid in and buy all such properties at only 3% interest for six months. This new procedure does not contravene the State Constitution (art. VIII, §§ 1, 2) or the Real Property Tax Law ( §§ 1008, 1606; cf. Suffolk County Tax Act, §§ 32, 43). A taxpayer's action seeking to invalidate [***2] and restrain this new procedure is dismissed.

2. In the 1971 tax sale announcement, the erroneous words "tax liens" are clarified by the subsequent words "lands purchased" and "parcels * * * sold".
