Pitkin Furniture Co. v. Jackson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, Kings County


74 Misc. 2d 77; 344 N.Y.S.2d 464; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2259



February 1, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] With respect to defendant's third counterclaim, defendant's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant buyer brought a motion for summary judgment on her counterclaims in plaintiff seller's action to recover possession of certain chattels that the buyer purchased under retail installment sales contracts.


OVERVIEW: The seller instituted an action against the buyer to recover possession of certain chattels that she had purchased under two retail installment sales contracts. The buyer filed counterclaims against the seller and a motion for summary judgment. The court granted partial summary judgment to the buyer on her counterclaims under 15 U.S.C.S. §§ 1638, 1640 of the Truth in Lending Act, but denied summary judgment on her counterclaim that was based upon the repossession of the items that were the subject matter of the contracts. The court found that neither of the contracts complied with the requirements of 15 U.S.C.S. § 1638(a)(8) in that the number of payments scheduled to repay the indebtedness was not indicated and that one of the contracts did not express the finance charge as an annual percentage rate. The court further found that the seller admitted the existence of the contracts and the absence of a proper statement of the finance charge and payment schedule.


OUTCOME: The court granted partial summary judgment to the buyer on her first and second counterclaims against the seller for violations of the Truth in Lending Act based upon the retail installment sales contracts. The court denied the buyer's motion with respect to her third counterclaim concerning the repossession of the purchased items.


HEADNOTES

Sales -- Truth in Lending Act -- for failing to set forth number of payments and annual percentage rate, furniture store must pay $ 200 to purchaser -- after these replevying furniture, seller is not permitted to correct these "errors" (U. S. Code, tit. 15, § 1640) -- in action by seller for purchase price, purchaser is granted summary judgment on counterclaim for such $ 200.

1. For failing to set forth the number of payments required to pay the balance of the indebtedness and the annual percentage rate of the finance charge, a furniture store in this case must pay $ 200 to the purchaser (U. S. Code, tit. 15, § 1638, subd. [a], pars. [7], [8]; § 1640, subd. [a]).

2. After having replevied the furniture, such seller cannot be permitted to amend its agreements so as to correct these "errors" (cf. U. S. Code, tit. 15, § 1640, subds. [b], [c]).

3. Accordingly in this action by such seller for the unpaid balance of the purchase price, the purchaser is granted summary judgment for $ 200.

 

1695 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hayden


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Suffolk County


68 Misc. 2d 1022; 328 N.Y.S.2d 988; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2266



January 31, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is in all respects denied, without a hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was defendant's motion for a writ of error coram nobis in order to be resentenced under the revised penal law for the crime of sexual abuse in the first degree instead of the indeterminate sentence that he received upon his conviction for carnal abuse of a child.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had received an indeterminate term of imprisonment upon his conviction for carnal abuse of a child. In 1968, he was granted a hearing for resentencing whereupon the sentencing judge had issued a resentence nunc pro tunc of a similar indeterminate term of imprisonment. Defendant then filed a motion for a writ of error coram nobis to be resentenced under the revised penal for the crime of sexual abuse in the first degree. Defendant contended that N.Y. Penal Law § 5.05(3) was unconstitutional because it discriminated against a sex offender who committed a crime prior to September 1, 1967, but was sentenced thereafter to a harsher sentence than a person who committed a like crime after that date. The court did not find any merit in this contention. Further, the court found defendant had misconstrued the meaning and effect of his 1968 resentence nunc pro tunc because the sole purpose of the resentence was correct the original sentence in order to comply with a retroactive mandate. Finally, the court found the errors complained of could be not be reviewed by a coram nobis application when they could have been raised by an appeal.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed defendant's motion for a writ of error coram nobis. The court declined to resentence defendant under the revised penal law for the crime of sexual abuse in the first degree instead of the indeterminate sentence that he received upon his conviction for carnal abuse of a child.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- resentence imposed nunc pro tunc was to comply retroactively with People v. Bailey (21 N Y 2d 588) -- defendant's claim that he should have been resentenced under revised Penal Law was apparent on face of record and should have been reviewed on appeal, not by coram nobis -- on merits, defendant's resentence was proper since new Penal Law does not contain crime of which defendant was convicted -- defendant's resentence did not deny him equal protection of law.

1. The sole purpose of resentencing defendant on December 27, 1968 was to correct the original sentence imposed on November 20, 1964 to comply with the retroactive mandate of People v. Bailey (21 N Y 2d 588) that there be a hearing to controvert a psychiatric report prior to the imposition of sentence. Such sentence was properly imposed nunc pro tunc.

2. Defendant's present claim that he should have been sentenced under the revised Penal Law for the crime of sexual abuse in the first degree which he equates with carnal abuse of a child, the penalty for which is a maximum indeterminate term of seven years was apparent on the [***2] face of the record and should have been raised on appeal. It may not be reviewed by a coram nobis application.

3. Even on the merits, defendant may not be resentenced, pursuant to the revised Penal Law, for the crime of sexual abuse in the first degree. There was no such crime when he committed the underlying criminal act. Moreover, although sexual abuse in the first degree is derived from the carnal abuse offenses, they are different crimes. "Lack of consent" which is an element of each degree of sexual abuse was not an element of the carnal abuse offenses.

4. Defendant's resentence under the former Penal Law was in accordance with the statute and was not in violation of the constitutional requirement of equal protection of the law.

 

1696 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Axelrod


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


69 Misc. 2d 57; 329 N.Y.S.2d 524; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2271



January 27, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was defendant's motion to suppress certain evidence that was seized pursuant to a search warrant upon the ground that the search warrant and the subsequent seizure were unconstitutional in that they were not preceded by an adversary hearing on the issue of obscenity.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had filed a motion to suppress certain evidence that was seized pursuant to a search warrant. Defendant contended that the issuance of the search warrant and the subsequent seizure were unconstitutional in that they were not preceded by an adversary hearing on the issue of obscenity. The court could not agree with defendant's contention that the distribution of any form of expression alleged to be obscene had to be completely undisturbed until an independent determination of obscenity had been made by a judicial officer in an adversary proceeding. The court did not find any case that suggested the necessity for a prior adversary hearing before the issuance of warrants for a reasonable search and seizure of obscene materials. Further, the court noted that warrants involved in this case were preceded by judicial scrutiny and were not left to the judgment of policemen. The court concluded that the warrant, authorizing the seizure of six copies of certain obscene material without any prior adversary hearing, was fully justified.


OUTCOME: The court did not find the search warrant and the subsequent seizure were unconstitutional in that they were not preceded by an adversary hearing on the issue of obscenity. The court denied defendant's motion to suppress certain evidence that was seized pursuant to the search warrant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- Judge who viewed copies of allegedly obscene magazine was not required to conduct evidentiary hearing before issuing warrant to seize six copies of magazine for evidentiary purposes; motion to suppress such seized evidence denied.

A warrant for the seizure, for evidentiary purposes, of six copies of an allegedly obscene magazine was issued on a policeman's affidavit showing probable cause and on the Judge's viewing of copies of the magazine, without any evidentiary hearing as to their obscenity. Motion for suppression of the seized evidence was denied.

Blye v. Globe-Wernicke Realty Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 948; 328 N.Y.S.2d 257; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2284



January 25, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss is accordingly granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant hotel filed a motion to dismiss the complaint of plaintiff former guest, who had been locked out of her room for nonpayment of charges. The guest sought an injunction against the continued holding of her baggage, damages, and a judgment declaring unconstitutional N.Y. Lien Law § 181 and N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law §§ 207, 208.


OVERVIEW: The guest contended that the statutes were unconstitutional insofar as they authorized the hotel to seize and hold her property without providing notice and a prior hearing. She contended that they were also unconstitutional because they allowed the hotel to sell the property at a public auction without providing judicial or administrative intervention. The hotel based its motion to dismiss on prior caselaw that had already upheld the constitutionality of the statutes. In that case, the court of appeals discussed the public policy considerations supporting the compensatory lien to protect hotels as a special consideration in view of their "extraordinary" liability. The court granted the motion to dismiss. The court of appeals expressly upheld the constitutionality of the statutes, and no situation requiring special protection to a state or creditor interest was presented by the facts. There was no element of danger of life or liberty to the guest or of severe consequences or irreparable damage.


OUTCOME: The court granted the hotel's motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Innkeepers -- lien -- unpaid innkeeper may seize baggage without notice and may sell same at auction without any court order (Lien Law, § 181; General Business Law, §§ 207, 208) -- statute which Court of Appeals declared constitutional in 1907 should not now be declared unconstitutional by court of first instance.

1. An innkeeper may, without any notice, seize a nonpaying guest's baggage, and may, without any court order, sell same at public auction (Lien Law, § 181; General Business Law, §§ 207, 208). The cited statutes are valid and constitutional.

2. The constitutionality of the predecessor of section 181 of the Lien Law was upheld by our Court of Appeals in 1907. A court of first instance should adhere to such a decision. It is for our Court of Appeals to decide whether a 1969 decision of the United States Supreme Court, to the effect that a Wisconsin no-notice garnishment statute is unconstitutional, applies to our innkeepers' lien statute.

 

1698 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Beaux Arts Properties, Inc. v. United Nations Dev. Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 785; 328 N.Y.S.2d 16; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2301



January 18, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to dismiss is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff building owner sought a determination to declare a state statute unconstitutional, sought a determination that the implementation of a plan proposed by defendant United Nations and approved by defendants, city and city agencies, was unconstitutional, and sought a declaration that the United Nations and the city had exercised an illegal taking. The United Nations and the city filed a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: A building owner operated a hotel in a development area around the United Nations. The United Nations included the building owner's property in a development plan. The city approved the development plan. The building owner commenced suit to block the plan, sought to invalidate the statute enabling the plan, and claimed that the city and United Nations were engaging in an unconstitutional taking. The United Nations and the city moved to dismiss. The court granted the motion. The court held: (1) the building owner did not have standing to challenge the statute because the cause of action was not ripe; and (2) there was no evidence that the United Nations and the city engaged in a de facto taking of the property.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion of the United Nation and the city to dismiss the complaint of the building owner, and dismissed the building owner's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- standing to challenge constitutionality -- property owner whose property was originally in United Nations development area but by amendment of 1970 was excluded therefrom has no standing to challenge constitutionality of law; facts do not show de facto condemnation of plaintiff's property.

A 1968 law provided for development of an area near the United Nations building to serve the needs of those who daily visit the United Nations. At that time plaintiff's property lay within the development area. In 1971, an amendment excluded plaintiff's property from the development area. In its first cause of action, plaintiff attacks the 1971 amendment as unconstitutional because it allegedly takes private property for nonpublic uses. In its second cause of action, it alleges that defendants exercised control over its property as a result of which tenants vacated, others who stayed refused to pay higher rentals and prospective tenants have been discouraged from seeking space, and thus there was a de facto taking of its property. In the third cause of action, plaintiff attacks the constitutionality on the [***2] ground that the provision which requires the State to keep the debt service funds solvent constitutes a pledge of the State's credit in violation of the Constitution. While plaintiff has no standing to challenge constitutionality on the basis of the first and third causes of action, the second cause of action deals with the alleged infringement of a right belonging specifically to plaintiff. In that second cause, plaintiff is seeking judgment for a purported de facto taking of its property. However, publicity, even though widespread, does not constitute an invasion of property nor does it constitute a legal restraint on the beneficial use and free enjoyment of the realty. The letters written to plaintiff's tenants that condemnation might occur at some future date and emphasizing that there was no intent to interfere with present use and enjoyment do not constitute such an act of dominion as to limit the right of use and enjoyment. These acts furnish no basis for the claim of a de facto appropriation. Since the second cause of action is insufficient, plaintiff lacks standing to attack the constitutionality of the act, nor does it have standing to attack the implementation [***3] thereof.

 

1699 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Spano v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


68 Misc. 2d 804; 328 N.Y.S.2d 229; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2306



January 17, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the several motions by the defendants to dismiss the complaint are granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a taxpayer and parent, filed an action for declaratory judgment against defendants, the state comptroller, the state attorney general, the state tax commissioner, the state education commissioner, the school district and the town. The taxpayer asserted that the state's legislative and constitutional provisions for levying and distributing school taxes were unconstitutional. Defendants filed motions to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer filed an action for declaratory judgment against state and town officials. Citing to precedent from another jurisdiction, the taxpayer argued that the state's legislative and constitutional provisions for levying and distributing school taxes were unconstitutional. The state and town officials filed motions to dismiss. The court discussed neither the challenged statutes nor the state constitution. Instead, the court examined the precedent cited by the taxpayer and found it unpersuasive. The other court had attempted to distinguish its holding from two cases rendered by the United States Supreme Court, which in per curiam opinions sustained two different circuit courts' determinations that the public school funding schemes at issue were constitutional. The court explained that it was demeaning to the Court to construe its holdings lightly when they were issued in per curiam form. The court further stated that it was not within the competence of a state court to presume to express the Supreme Court's unexpressed thinking. Finally, the public schools required financial certainty in their business dealings. The court granted the motions to dismiss the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motions to dismiss filed by the state and town officials and held that the taxpayer's complaint about the constitutionality of public education taxes was legally insufficient in light of the United States Supreme Court's recent jurisprudence.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- school taxes -- court dismisses complaint which seeks to invalidate New York State's method of levying and distributing school taxes.

A taxpayer and parent asks the court to declare that the State of New York's existing constitutional and legislative provisions for levying and distributing school taxes are unconstitutional and invalid under the United States Constitution. The basic issue is a matter for the Legislature, or, perhaps, for the United States Supreme Court. Defendants' motions to dismiss this complaint are granted.

 

1700 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Berkey v. Downing


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Tompkins County


68 Misc. 2d 595; 327 N.Y.S.2d 921; 1972 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2316



January 12, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the complaint is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought both a declaratory judgment and an adjudication from the board (New York) declaring a deed given pursuant to a sale to be null and void, that all proceedings of appellee school district leading up to the delivery of said deed be rescinded, that school district make restitution of school property and respond to the school district in damages.


OVERVIEW: Appellant school district sold school property, which it deemed was no longer needed for school purposes. Appellant taxpayers instituted an action against appellee school district and appellee purchasers of the property under a number of claims. Appellees filed a motion that the complaint be dismissed. The court dismissed the action, finding that appellants lacked standing to bring such a suit because a taxpayer had no right of action against a school district. Appellants contended that they had a right of action where waste was alleged. The court disagreed, finding that the complaint failed to show that appellants were in any way aggrieved in their personal rights in a manner differing from any other taxpayer, nor did appellants have a cause of action against appellee property purchasers. There were no allegations in the complaint that by any interpretation could be construed to charge appellees with wrongdoing on which appellants were entitled to relief. There was no claim of bad faith, collusion or fraud. Finally, the court held that appellee school board had clear statutory authority to sell the school property.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed a complaint instituted by appellant taxpayers that challenged the sale of school property by appellee school district where they lacked standing to bring such an action and appellee purchasers were not charged with wrongdoing entitling appellants to relief.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- sale of school property -- complaint in action by taxpayers against city school district and purchasers of former school property, for declaratory jupdgment that sale was void, dismissed -- plaintiffs lacked legal capacity to sue and complaint failed to state cause of action.

1. Taxpayers in the City of Ithaca seek a judgment declaring void a deed given by defendant city school district pursuant to a sale of a former school building and its site to individual defendants, and requiring the latter to make restitution of the school property and respond in damages. Plaintiffs allege, inter alia, that the sale constituted a gift, had been illegally held without a referendum, and damaged them, as taxpayers, to the extent of a certain amount of the assessed valuation of their respective properties. Their complaint is dismissed.

2. The complaint, as a whole, is clearly framed as a taxpayer's action and a taxpayer has no right of action against a school district. Moreover, plaintiffs have not shown any special right or interest in the matter, other than those common to all taxpayers, which would give rise to such [***2] a legal relationship as would give them standing to sue for declaratory relief. Furthermore, a private citizen may not challenge the validity of a governmental act unless it directly affects his private rights and here the complaint fails to show that plaintiffs were aggrieved in their personal rights in a manner differing from any other taxpayer.

3. Not only do plaintiffs lack legal capacity to sue, but their complaint fails to state a cause of action. Individual defendants offered to purchase the land and the building for $ 20,000, for the purpose of converting the building to apartment and commercial uses, and committed themselves to expend an estimated $ 500,000 therefor. A bank offered to purchase the land, clear of the building, for $ 122,100, for an undefined use. The cost of demolishing the building would have been $ 55,555.55 and the annual cost of maintaining it $ 35,000. In deciding to accept individual defendants' offer, the district's board of education considered, not only these factors but, inter alia, the potential tax revenues from the proposed conversion of the building. The sale did not require a referendum and was not a gift. It simply reflected the [***3] board's judgment of what was ultimately in the best interest of the taxpayers. As to individual defendants, there is no claim that they did not act in good faith or that any acts on their part were a cause of direct damage to plaintiffs which would give rise to a cause of action against them.

 

1701 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dolphin Lane Associates, Ltd. v. Southampton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


72 Misc. 2d 868; 339 N.Y.S.2d 966; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1000



December 29, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner filed an action against appellee town to declare unconstitutional certain ordinances enacted by the town. The town served an answer that basically denied the allegations of the complaint. The town, along with intervenor town trustees, asserted that they had superior title to certain portions of the real property claimed to be owned by the property owner in its action. This decision only dealt with questions of title.


OVERVIEW: The property owner field an action against the town to declare unconstitutional certain ordinances enacted by the town. The town denied the allegations of the complaint. The town and intervenors argued that they had superior title to certain portions of the real property claimed to be owned by the property owner. The court, dealing only with questions of title, found: 1) The proprietors received legal title under the acts of 1818 and 1831 and not merely a power to sell with an equitable interest in the proceeds from the sale of the common and undivided lands of the town. 2) The property owner had title to the beach lands on the south beach lying between the average high water line of the Atlantic Ocean and the southerly top edge of the sand dunes.3) The beaches were found to be subject to an easement in favor of the town. Said easement was found to be located between the dunes and the high water mark. 4) The town had title to the area known as Gull Island. 5) The town and intervenors had title to the land lying north of Dune Road, which was below the average high water line of the Shinnecock Bay.


OUTCOME: The court entered a decision that dealt only with questions of title to the land in dispute. The court found, among its five findings, that the property owner had title to the beach lands on the south beach lying between the average high water line of the Atlantic Ocean and the southerly top edge of the sand dunes.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Deeds -- conveyances -- plaintiff's predecessor in title had right to convey legal title to land in Southampton -- description of southern boundary as being on beach conveys title to average high water mark of Atlantic Ocean -- by law of 1818 town retained easement in land between dunes and Atlantic Ocean -- town owns island between ocean and mainland -- plaintiff's land north of road is bounded by high water mark of Shinnecock Bay.

1. Plaintiff acquired title to land in Southampton from grantors whose chain of title goes back to 1861. The grantors of 1861 had the right to convey legal title.

2. The deed described plaintiff's property as "bounded on the south by the beach". The word "beach" comprises the territory between high and low water over which the tide ebbs and flows. Plaintiff's deed carries title to the average high water mark of the Atlantic Ocean which is the southern boundary of plaintiff's lands.

3. A law of 1818 which enabled plaintiff's grantors of 1861 to sell prevented the sale of the productions of the waters. By such law the town retained an easement in favor of the public over the premises of plaintiff lying between the dunes and the high water mark.

 [***2] 4. A dispute arose between plaintiff's remote grantors and the Town of Southampton which was settled by the exchange of quitclaim deeds. Plaintiff's remote grantors conveyed to the town all their title "to the islands lying adjacent to the lands and beach on the south side of Shinnecock Bay so long as they shall remain islands". The island has always been an island and has never become part of the mainland". The town owns the island.

5. Plaintiff's land north of Dune Road, which bisects the premises east to west is bounded by the high water mark of the bay. The nature of the grasses growing indicates that, where one type grows and flourishes, there is daily tidal overflow. Lands where this type of grass flourishes are below the high water line. Above this is an area where this type of grass does not flourish. This is the area where the current average high water line is located and it is the boundary of plaintiff's land on the bay. The parties indicate that they would stipulate to a metes and bounds description of this area. Defendants have title to the land below the average water line of Shinnecock Bay.

 

1702 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Avant


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Albany County


69 Misc. 2d 445; 330 N.Y.S.2d 201; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1004



December 27, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Indictments dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant government contractors filed a motion to dismiss their indictments, which were obtained after the contractors had answered grand jury subpoenas. The grand jury was investigating the city and county's purchasing practices and sought testimony and documents from the contractors. The contractors claimed that the waivers they signed under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 103-b violated their right against self-incrimination.


OVERVIEW: The grand jury of the city and county issued a subpoena duces tecum and a subpoena ad testificandum to contractors during the grand jury's investigation into government purchasing practices. The attorney general told the contractors that, pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 103-b, they were required to waive immunity before responding to the subpoenas; otherwise, they would become disqualified for government contracts. The contractors signed waivers, responded to the subpoenas, and were then indicted based on the information they had disclosed. The contractors filed a motion to dismiss the indictments as violative of their constitutional right against self-incrimination. The court recited the rule that a prospective defendant could not be summoned before a grand jury to testify or to produce documents, because the privilege would then be violated. Re-indictment was possible but only when based on evidence entirely independent of what the defendant had produced to the grand jury. Section 103-b forced contractors to choose between criminal prosecution and their livelihoods. The court held that the statute was unconstitutional; the waiver was invalid; and the indictments were dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court held that the state law compelling a grand jury witness to waive immunity before responding to a subpoena violated the government contractors' rights against self-incrimination. The court dismissed the indictments.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- privilege against self incrimination -- defendants, as targets of investigation of Grand Jury, were called by Grand Jury and gave testimony signing waiver of immunity; defendant's privilege against self incrimination was violated; indictment against them is dismissed -- their waiver of privilege was not voluntary.

1. A Grand Jury investigating purchasing and contracting practices of the City and County of Albany subpoenaed defendants, who had entered into contracts with the City of Albany, to testify and to produce records. Defendants appeared before the Grand Jury and signed a limited waiver of immunity and upon being advised by their attorney that to be eligible to do work for the city, they must sign a waiver, they did. They gave their testimony and they were subsequently indicted. Since defendants were targets of the investigation, their privilege against self incrimination was violated. The indictments against them must be dismissed, and all the evidence adduced, records or testimony branded as invalid and not available for any further use against defendants.

2. Since section 103-b of the General Municipal Law gave defendants [***2] no choice if they sought to do business with the city, their waiver of the privilege against self incrimination was not voluntary but was forced and invalid.

 

1703 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Colgate-Palmolive Co. v. Erie County


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


68 Misc. 2d 704; 327 N.Y.S.2d 488; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1006; 3 ERC (BNA) 1673; 2 ELR 20168



December 27, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Colgate's petition for a temporary injunction is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company filed a petition seeking a temporary injunction to restrain defendant County of Erie from enforcing Erie County, N.Y., Local Laws No. 8 (1971) (Law) that prohibited the sale, in the county, of synthetic detergents containing phosphorous elements.


OVERVIEW: The county legislature enacted the Law based on its finding that significant pollution in and increased cultural eutrophication of the natural waters resulted from the release of phosphorus contained in detergents. The company asserted that it would have suffered irreparable harm if the Law's phosphate ban was not stayed. The county asserted that the health, safety, and welfare of the public would be adversely affected by a stay in the enforcement of the phosphate ban. The court denied the company's petition for injunctive relief. The court decided that the Law was not unconstitutional as an unreasonable burden on interstate commerce in violation of the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The court found that the only burden suffered by the company was to change its production facilities at their point of origin. The court followed federal precedent rejecting the argument that local environmental legislation was burdensome on interstate commerce. The court held that the Law was compatible with N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 17(7). The court held that the Law was not preempted by § 17(7) because the Law complied within the savings provision of § 17(7).


OUTCOME: The court denied the company's petition for a temporary injunction to restrain the county from enforcing the Law.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- environmental conservation -- control of phosphates -- preliminary injunction against Local Law of Erie County limiting phosphate content of detergents and then banning them is denied -- Local Law does not impose burden on interstate commerce -- Local Law has not been pre-empted by enactment of section 17 of Environmental Conservation Law.

1. Local Law No. 8 of the Local Laws of 1971 of the County of Erie limits to 8.7% by weight the content of phosphates in detergents to be sold in the county between April 30, 1971 and December 31, 1971 and after January 1, 1972, no detergent with phosphate may be sold in the county. A preliminary injunction against the enforcement of the Local Law is denied.

2. Such Local Law does not impose a burden upon interstate commerce in violation of the United States Constitution.

3. The Local Law has not been pre-empted by section 17 of the Environmental Conservation Law, which permits phosphates in detergents to the extent of 8.7% by weight between December 31, 1971 and May 31, 1973 and from June 1, 1973 no phosphate-based detergent may be [***2] sold in New York State. Section 17 provides that any regulation of a political subdivision in effect on June 1, 1971 shall not be affected by it. Since the Local Law was enacted on March 16, 1971 to take effect June 1, 1971, it was in effect on June 1, 1971 and has not been superseded by the State Law.

 

1704 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People by Lefkowitz v. Biochemical Procedures, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 753; 327 N.Y.S.2d 804; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1016



December 23, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney general sought a judgment to restrain respondent medical company from soliciting or accepting specimens from physicians for laboratory examination within the City of New York in the absence of a permit.


OVERVIEW: The attorney general instituted an action against the medical company for a judgment to restrain it from soliciting or accepting specimens from physicians for laboratory examination within the City of New York without a permit. The medical company contended that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 574 did not apply to it because its laboratories were outside the state. The court granted the attorney general's application. The court found that the medical company's activities violated § 574 because the statute did apply to laboratories outside the state. The court concluded that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 574 did not place an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court granted the attorney general's application for a judgment to restrain the medical company's activities.


HEADNOTES

Health -- laboratories -- clinical testing laboratory located in New Jersey cannot solicit or accept specimens from New York physicians (Public Health Law, § 574).

A clinical testing laboratory which accepts specimens from New York physicians must be subject to on-site inspections and control tests by New York inspectors and must have a New York permit. Therefore any such laboratory must be located in New York State. The New York State Attorney-General is granted judgment restraining a laboratory which is located in New Jersey from soliciting or accepting specimens from New York physicians (Public Health Law, §§ 570, 574; Executive Law, § 63, subd. 12). It cannot be said that our statutory requirements unduly and unconstitutionally burden interstate commerce.

 

1705 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kosches v. Nichols


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 795; 327 N.Y.S.2d 968; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1018; 10 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 147



December 22, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff creditors in separate matters brought ex parte applications for orders of seizure pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 7102, alleging that defendant debtors defaulted under retail installment contracts. The property in the first matter was household furniture, and in second matter was an automobile. In both cases the goods were purchased under retail installment sales agreements.


OVERVIEW: The creditors brought ex parte applications for an order of replevin under art. 7102, alleging that the debtors had defaulted in their payments under retail installment contracts. The court denied the applications, recommending that the creditors file notice of the action on the debtors, providing at least seven days notice prior to any hearing. The court held that there were no "extraordinary circumstances" shown in the two applications, which warranted such ex parte action without notice. Therefore, the court held that procedural due process required that the debtors be provided with notice and an opportunity to be heard prior to issuance of an order permitting seizure of property. The court held that such notice was particularly required because the property sought to be seized was the "necessities of life," including bedroom furniture and a car.


OUTCOME: The court denied the creditors' ex part applications for orders of seizure, and recommended that each creditor serve a copy of the required papers on each debtor, noticing the matter for a hearing in the court on at least seven days' notice. The court further held that if the creditors requested restraining orders or a shorter time period, such order to show cause might be brought.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Replevin -- ex parte seizures -- ex parte orders of seizure are denied as to necessities of life consisting of bedroom furniture and automobile; notice and hearing are required -- clauses in retail installment contracts giving seller right to enter debtor's home and seize goods without order are unconscionable.

1. Ex parte applications for orders of seizure under CPLR 7102 with respect to bedroom furniture and an automobile will be denied. These items are "necessaries of life" requiring the giving of notice and an opportunity to be heard and no "extraordinary circumstances" where notice and hearing can be dispensed with, have been shown in the instant applications.

2. Clauses in the retail installment contracts giving the seller the right to enter the debtor's residence and seize the goods without a court order are unconscionable. In one, a clause even gives the seller the right to enter and seize any other property without court order.

 

1706 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Farrigan v. Helsby


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


68 Misc. 2d 952; 327 N.Y.S.2d 909; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1037; 80 L.R.R.M. 2360



December 16, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teachers association sought review of an order from respondent Public Employment Relations Board of the State of New York, which was against the teachers association on its unfair labor practice claim that arose from a board of education's refusal to negotiate concerning an agency shop proposal.


OVERVIEW: The teachers association filed an unfair labor practice charge with the Board in which it claimed that the board of education had failed to bargain in good faith when it refused to negotiate concerning an agency shop. The Board concluded that the refusal to negotiate concerning the agency shop proposal could not be characterized as a failure to negotiate in good faith and issued an in favor of the board of education. The teachers association appealed. The court dismissed the petition for review of the Board's order. The court concluded that the Board did act in an arbitrary manner when it held that the board of education did not commit an unfair labor practice by refusing to negotiate concerning the agency shop proposal. The court found that the agency shop proposal that the teachers association submitted for negotiation with the board of education was clearly illegal. The court determined that the Board did not act in an arbitrary manner by failing to find that the board of education had committed an unfair labor practice by refusing to negotiate concerning an illegal item that was submitted for negotiations.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition for review of the Board's order against the teachers association on its unfair labor practice claim.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- "agency shop" -- schools -- school board properly refused to negotiate with teachers' association as to proposed "agency shop" whereby teachers who are not members of association must authorize board to deduct and pay over their association dues to association.

A so-called "agency shop" is illegal in public employment (State Finance Law, § 6-a; General Municipal Law, § 93-b; Civil Service Law, § 202; Education Law, § 3012, subd. 2). Accordingly, a school board, in its negotiations with a teachers' association, may refuse to negotiate concerning an "agency shop" proposal; that is, a proposal that any teacher must either be a member of the teachers' association or must sign a paper directing the school board to deduct from his salary the amount of the teachers' association dues and pay over such amount to the teachers' association. The Public Employment Relations Board correctly concluded that the board's refusal to negotiate as to such a proposal was not an unfair labor practice (Civil Service Law, § 209-a, subd. 1, par. [d]; § 201, subd. 4).

 

1707 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


General Electric Credit Corp. v. Fred Pistone, Jr., Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


68 Misc. 2d 475; 326 N.Y.S.2d 898; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1048



December 13, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Submit order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tractor owner filed a motion before special term to punish defendant purchaser for contempt for an alleged violation of and noncompliance with an order of seizure issued ex parte attempting to return machinery that the purchaser kept after the sale agreement was cancelled. The purchaser filed a motion to vacate the order of seizure.


OVERVIEW: The tractor owner filed an action to recover two chattels from the purchaser, and filed a motion to punish the purchaser for contempt for the alleged violation of and noncompliance with an order of seizure issued ex parte at the special term, and the purchaser filed a cross motion to vacate the order of seizure. The court found that the purchaser's motion to vacate the seizure was without merit and denied it. The court found that the purchaser had breached its contract, owed the tractor owner money, and was using the tractor owner's property, to which the purchaser had no vestige of a right of possession. The court found probable cause for the order of seizure based upon the averments in the affidavit upon which it was based, and the tractor owner's case had been bolstered even more by the unrefuted testimony of one of the witnesses that the purchaser's president was about to depart from the jurisdiction with the money to be received by the purchaser from the job upon which the purchaser was using the equipment of the tractor owner. The court also denied that part of the tractor owner's motion seeking to punish the president of the purchaser company for contempt.


OUTCOME: The court denied the purchaser's motion to vacate the order of seizure. The court also denied that part of the tractor owner's motion seeking to punish the president of the purchaser company for contempt.


HEADNOTES

Replevin -- secured transactions -- CPLR 7102 (as amd. by L. 1971, ch. 1051) is valid and constitutional; secured party may obtain order directing Sheriff to seize machinery from purchaser -- defendant purchaser's refusal to tell Sheriff where chattels were did not amount to contempt of court -- but said defendant shall submit to examination as to such whereabouts ( CPLR 7112).

1. A secured party may repossess the chattels -- machinery, in this case -- by filing a bond and obtaining an order of the court directing the Sheriff to seize the chattels from the purchaser ( CPLR 7102, as amd. by L. 1971, ch. 1051; cf. Uniform Commercial Code, § 9-503). In this case, the Sheriff served said defendant purchaser with the order, affidavit and bond, and with the summons and complaint. CPLR 7102 (as amd. by L. 1971, ch. 1051) is valid and constitutional.

2. However, the refusal by said purchaser-debtor and its president to tell the Sheriff where the chattels were did not violate any express provision of the court order and hence did not amount to a contempt of the court.

3. Nevertheless, said defendant and its president are ordered to submit to an examination as [***2] to the whereabouts of the chattels ( CPLR 7112).

 

1708 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kiger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 100; 326 N.Y.S.2d 591; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1058



December 8, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant Peter N. Kiger is found guilty and fined $ 10.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People brought an action charging defendant with violating N.Y. Comp. Codes R. and Regs. tit. 21, § 701.2(b).


OVERVIEW: A detective with the New York City Transit Authority testified that he saw defendant affix a small gummed-back sticker on the columns or pillars of the northbound platform at the West 4th Street Independent subway station. Defendant did not offer any defense, and moved for acquittal, among other things, on the ground that § 701.2(b) was unconstitutional in that it violated the rights of free speech guaranteed by the First and Fourteenth Amendments to the Constitution of the United States. Section 701.2 read: no person shall place, or cause, or procure to be placed upon or affixed to any part of the transit system any words, characters or device as a notice of, or reference to, any article, business, exhibition, profession, matter or event. Section 702.1 further provided that any violation of the rules of the New York City Transit Authority were punishable by a fine of not exceeding $ 25 or by imprisonment for not longer than 10 days, or both. The court found that § 701.2(b) being a reasonable regulation, narrowly drawn to serve a legitimate police power interest, was constitutional.


OUTCOME: Defendant was found guilty and fined $ 10.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- affixing notices in subway -- Rules and Regulations for Use of New York City Transit Authority (21 NYCRR 701.2 [b] and 702.1) which provide punishment for person affixing notices to any part of transit system are not unconstitutional -- Constitution does not deny right to enact laws in reasonable exercise of police power directly relevant to public safety.

The Rules and Regulations for Use of the New York City Transit Authority (21 NYCRR 701.2 [b] and 702.1) provide that no person shall affix any notices to any part of the transit system and that a violation of such rules would be punishable by fine or imprisonment. Defendant was found to have affixed a small sticker on the pillars in a subway station and on vending machines, candy machines and soda machines. The rule prohibiting such action is not unconstitutional. The affixing of political stickers which are difficult to remove would be imposing a great burden on the New York City Transit Authority. Such stickers might create a hazardous condition if pasted on the windows of subway cars and buses or in such manner as to interfere with the [***2] normal use and safe operation of the transit system. The Constitution does not deprive a State of its right to enact laws in reasonable exercise of its police power directly relevant to public safety.

 

1709 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Weston's Shoppers City, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Albany County


68 Misc. 2d 217; 326 N.Y.S.2d 685; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1060



December 8, 1971 



DISPOSITION: The judgments appealed from are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from a judgment of the Justice Court, Town of Colonie (New York), which dismissed two informations against defendant by sustaining a demurrer based on the grounds that the facts stated did not constitute crimes because N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 9 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The issue raised on appeal related to the constitutionality of § 9. Informations were filed against defendant alleging violation of the section by reason of alleged sales of a pair of earrings and a cartridge of film on Sunday. The lower court found that the section in question was unconstitutional on the ground that an exception to the prohibition of Sunday sales dealing with "souvenirs," found in § 9(4) was so vague as to be violative of the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. The import of the lower court's opinion was that the word "souvenir" was impossible of sufficiently precise definition to enable the court to determine what "souvenirs" consisted of. The court found that the word was not vague. The court warned that as long as § 9 was in effect, stores, large or small, selling general merchandise such as wearing apparel, hardware, mechanical devices, and jewelry, did so at their own peril.


OUTCOME: The judgment appealed from was reversed. The informations were remanded for disposition by trial or otherwise.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sales on Sunday -- section 9 of General Business Law prohibiting sales on Sunday is constitutional despite exception in favor of "souvenirs" -- defendant was entitled to test constitutionality of statute by demurrer.

1. Section 9 of the General Business Law prohibits all manner of public selling of property on Sunday except (subd. 4) "Prepared tobacco * * * soda-water * * * confectionery, souvenirs, newspapers, magazines". The word "souvenirs" in the context of the time when the statute was enacted in 1881 referred to small novelty and sundry shops which catered to people enjoying a day at a lake or a beach offering mementos of vacation areas and does not refer to general merchandise offered by present-day large mercantile establishments. The word is not vague and the statute is constitutional. The informations are remanded to the trial court for disposition.

2. Defendant was entitled to test the constitutionality of the statute by demurring to the informations.

 

1710 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Manhasset Park Dist.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


68 Misc. 2d 642; 327 N.Y.S.2d 30; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1067



December 7, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the petition to condemn is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner park district brought a proceeding before the court (New York) in eminent domain to acquire real property for public parking purposes.


OVERVIEW: The primary objections of the respondents, corporations and individuals, were are based upon the failure of the relevant town board to comply with town law in that it failed to make certain findings, and that the permission of the New York State Comptroller was not secured. Respondents also questioned the need for additional public parking. The court held that the town board was not required to comply with the relevant sections of the town law with respect to the proceeding to acquire additional property. Insofar as the two were in conflict, the Nassau County, N.Y., Civil Divisions Act, a special law, prevailed over the town law, a general law.The court also held that the contingent liability expressed in Nassau County, N.Y., Civil Divisions Act § 319.9 was but a recognition of the obligation of the town to pledge its faith and credit so that the district indebtedness would be met in the event that the district revenues were insufficient. This obligation of the town therefore was part and parcel of its governmental function and was a town purpose, which it could and had to meet, if the situation arose, by resort to its power of taxation.


OUTCOME: The petition to condemn was granted.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- park districts -- Manhasset Park District may condemn land for public parking purposes -- Town Board of Town of North Hempstead is not required, because of special law, to comply with Town Law as condition precedent -- need for additional public parking exists in district -- no unconstitutional imposition of costs of acquiring property on property not benefiitted occurred.

1. The Manhasset Park District of the Town of North Hempstead, Nassau County, is entitled to commence proceedings to acquire real property for public parking purposes. The Town Board has the power to create and establish public parking places in the Manhasset Park District (Nassau County Civil Divisions Act, § 319.3). The board is authorized to acquire real property for such purposes and is not limited to acquisitions for park purposes only.

2. The Town Board of the Town of North Hempstead is not required to comply with sections 194 and 202-e of the Town Law as a condition precedent because of the powers of the Manhasset Park District set forth in the Nassau County Civil Divisions Act (L. 1939, chs. 273, 710-717, as amd.) and the [***2] exceptions therein provided (§ 319.6). There is no requirement that the Town Board seek the State Comptroller's permission before acquiring and improving any additional property for the park district. If there is conflict, the Nassau County Civil Divisions Act, a special law, prevails over the Town Law, a general law.

3. The record demonstrates that there exists in the district a need for additional public parking.

4. The primary obligation to pay for the acquisition is upon the properties in the park district benefitted by the improvement, whose assessed valuations exceed sixty million dollars. There is no question of taxing, in the first instance, any property in the town outside of the district to pay for the district improvements. The contingent liability expressed in section 319.9 is recognition of the obligation of the town to pledge its faith and credit so that the district indebtedness will be met in the event that the district revenues are insufficient. Since the district is economically sound, there is no unconstitutional imposition of the ultimate costs on property not benefitted.
