People ex rel. Slofsky v. Agnew


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Clinton County


68 Misc. 2d 128; 326 N.Y.S.2d 477; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1077



December 2, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus against respondents, a director of a state mental hospital and the New York State Board of Parole, after petitioner was denied parole as a result of being confined to the state mental hospital.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of manslaughter in the first degree and was sentenced to a term of 10 to 20 years. Petitioner was denied parole four times by the Board because he was confined to the state mental hospital. Petitioner claimed that the Board's policy of denying parole to inmates of the state mental hospital was unconstitutional as violative of the Fourteenth Amendment. The court directed the Board to parole petitioner to a civilian mental hospital. The court held that the Board's sole reason for denying parole to petitioner was his confinement in the state mental hospital, that the Board's policy created classifications of inmates with mental illness and those without mental illness, and that the Board's policy had no rational basis. The court also held that the Board's policy deprived petitioner of his equal protection rights, that the Board's decision was invalid, and that petitioner was required to be paroled to a civilian mental hospital.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the Board's decision denying parole to petitioner was invalid and directed that petitioner be paroled to a civilian mental hospital.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parole -- hospital for criminal insane -- State Board of Parole should parole, to civilian mental hospital, convict who is now in Dannemora State Hospital and who, according to testimony of psychiatrist-director of Dannemora State Hospital, is now nonviolent and probably would recover from his mental illness if given civilian mental hospital treatment.

The court directs the State Board of Parole to take appropriate measures immediately to parole to a civil mental hospital an inmate of Dannemora State Hospital who has served 8 years of a 10-to-20-year sentence for manslaughter and who, according to the testimony of the psychiatrist-director of Dannemora State Hospital, has no assaultive or violent tendencies and probably would recover completely from his mental illness if given the benefit of civilian mental hospital treatment. (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.)

 

1712 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Chemical Bank v. Padilla


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 503; 326 N.Y.S.2d 950; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1091



November 30, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In all other respects plaintiff's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff payee and defendant, the maker of a note, both sought summary judgment in plaintiff's action to enforce a promissory note executed by defendant in plaintiff's favor. Plaintiff also moved to strike defendant's answer, and defendant moved for an order transforming the action into a class action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff and defendant both sought summary judgment in plaintiff's action to enforce a promissory note executed by defendant for the purchase of a car, the amount of which included pre-paid interest. After defendant made 15 monthly payments on the note within one year, the car was wrecked, and he sought to liquidate the debt by tendering the full balance on the note, before it was due. Plaintiff refused the tender, asserting that it was owed more, based upon its use of a different method of computing the amount of unearned interest that it was required to refund to defendant for early payment of the note. Defendant made no further payments, and plaintiff accelerated the balance and brought action. The court granted summary judgment for plaintiff, but only for the amount of defendant's tender. The court held that plaintiff's use of the "sum of the digits" method to compute defendant's rebate of interest was in violation of statute because it would have yielded to plaintiff a rate of interest which would be usurious if charged directly. Defendant's tender had the effect of stopping the debt, so plaintiff was not entitled to interest accrued after that date, or to costs.


OUTCOME: Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment was granted, but only for the amount tendered to it by defendant in pre-payment. Plaintiff's computation of the required refund to defendant for unearned interest on the note was erroneous where the amount it would have yielded to plaintiff was usurious. All other motions were denied.


HEADNOTES

Banks and banking -- personal loan prepaid -- pro rata rebate of unearned interest -- when personal installment loan is prepaid, unearned interest must be rebated pro rata -- bank's weighted method, called "sum of the digits", results in usury and is unlawful -- since borrower tendered correct pro rata amount due, bank is not entitled to costs or attorneys' fees.

1. In order to buy an automobile, defendant personally borrowed from plaintiff bank $ 3,451.84. For this, on June 13, 1969 he signed a promissory note to pay the bank $ 4,212 in 36 monthly installments of $ 117 commencing on July 21, 1969. That is, in addition to repaying the $ 3,451.84, he agreed to pay a $ 2 filing fee plus a total of $ 758.16 in 36 monthly installments. This $ 758.16 is the interest at 6% a year which $ 4,212 would earn if $ 4,212 were borrowed and were repaid at the end of three years. However, in 11 months, he paid $ 117 15 times, or a total of $ 1,755. Then, in May, 1970, he properly tendered to plaintiff bank $ 1,951.56 in full payment or prepayment. That is, $ 3,451.84 borrowed minus $ 1,755 paid = $ 1,696.84; and the interest which [***2] $ 4,212 would earn at 6% for one year = $ 252.72; and thus, $ 1,696.84 plus $ 252.72 plus $ 2 filing fee = $ 1,951.56.

2. The bank erroneously rejected this accurate tender, and erroneously asserted that it was entitled additionally to $ 134.44 unearned interest. That is, the bank contended that it was entitled to $ 387.16 as the interest on $ 4,212 for a period of less than one year. This figures the interest at more than 9% a year -- which rate is even more than the 7 1/2% maximum allowed even now by the Banking Law ( § 14-a, subd. 2, par. [a], cl. [1]; cf. § 108, subd. 4, pars. [b], [e]; cf. General Obligations Law, § 5-501).

3. Accordingly, the bank is granted summary judgment for only $ 1,951.56; and, since it refused a proper tender, this judgment is without costs or interest or attorneys' fees (Uniform Commercial Code, § 3-604, subd. [1]).t

4. In computing the refund of unearned interest, the bank unlawfully used a weighted method whereby it allocated unequal portions of interest to each installment due under the contract; that is, it allocated the largest proportion of interest to the first installment and the smallest proportion to the last, on the lender's [***3] theory that the lender's risk is at its peak at the time of the first installment and that the risk diminishes with each succeeding payment. The accountants call this widely used method the "sum of the digits". This method is contrary to the pro rata method required by the Banking Law ( § 108, subd. 4, par. [e], cl. [1]); and in this case, this method would give the bank a usurious rate of more than 9% a year, contrary to the Banking Law ( § 14-a).

 

1713 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hunt v. Board of Fire Comm'rs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


68 Misc. 2d 261; 327 N.Y.S.2d 36; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1096



November 29, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner volunteer fireman sought review of a decision of respondent, Board of Fire Commissioners of Massapequa Fire District (board) (New York), which suspended him from membership in the fire department under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 209-1. The fireman had violated a fire department rule that prohibited members to wear beards or sideburns below their ears.


OVERVIEW: The board duly adopted a rule that prohibited members of the fire department from wearing beards or sideburns that projected below their ears, and suspended the volunteer fireman from membership for violating the rule. The fireman claimed that the rule violated his constitutional rights and filed an action for reinstatement. The board claimed that the rule was necessary to create uniformity of appearance among firemen and in the interest of safety because facial hair could prevent a fireman's gas mask from adhering properly to his face. The court held that the rule was unconstitutional and ordered the fireman's reinstatement, finding first that the right to determine ones own personal appearance is a fundamental liberty. Since the right to determine one's own hairstyle is a matter of substantive due process, the court held that the burden was on the board to show substantial justification for its regulation. Although the board provided some evidence that long hair and sideburns might cause a safety risk, it did not show that in all cases they posed a risk and did not show that there was a triable issue as to the relationship between the rule and the public interest.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment, which declared unconstitutional the board's rule regulating the length of firemen's hair, and sideburns and prohibited them from wearing beards. The court ordered the fireman reinstated tome membership in the fire department.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- hair -- unreasonable regulations of Board of Fire Commissioners of fire district, prohibiting beards on firemen and limiting their mustaches and head hair and sideburns, declared unconstitutional -- board did not show any triable issue as to its contentions that any long hair prevents adequate sealing of respiratory masks and that long hair causes public disrespect -- court treats CPLR article 78 proceeding as action for declaratory judgment -- volunteer fireman's promise to abide by all regulations cannot apply to unconstitutional regulations -- fundamental liberties of citizens, including public employees, cannot be restrained unless substantial public benefit is proved.

1. Unreasonable departmental regulations as to the facial hair of members of a fire district -- that hair must not touch the collar, that sideburns must not extend below the ear, that mustaches must not extend beyond the lower lip, and that no beards are allowed -- are declared unconstitutional. A volunteer fireman who was suspended from his membership in the fire district for violating such regulations (cf. General Municipal Law, § 209-1) is granted summary judgment of reinstatement.

 [***2] 2. The Board of Fire Commissioners of the fire district did not show that there were any triable issues as to its contentions (a) that any long hair prevents an adequate sealing of a respiratory mask on the face and (b) that long hair on firemen causes the public to lose confidence in and respect for firemen.

3. Although this is a proceeding under article 78 of the CPLR, the court treats it as an action ( CPLR 103) in which the constitutionality of the regulation may be determined and in which a summary judgment may be rendered (cf. CPLR 7804, subd. [e]).

4. The fireman's promise, in his application to become a member of the fire district and in his oath of office, to abide by the district's rules, cannot apply to unconstitutional rules.

5. The weight of authority in this State and in other States and in the Federal courts is that a person, including a public employee, has the fundamental liberty and constitutional right to determine his own personal appearance. Cases upholding the constitutionality of regulations of hair by military and paramilitary organizations do not properly apply to firemen. A regulation which unreasonably limits a fundamental personal right of a public [***3] employee is invalid, not because there is a constitutional right to public employment, but because the constitutional rights of all citizens, public employees included, cannot be restrained unless it is shown that such restraint benefits the public interest so much as to outweigh the individual's constitutional right. Accordingly, as a matter of substantive due process, the burden is on the board to show substantial justification for its regulation.

 

1714 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re B.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Monroe County


68 Misc. 2d 95; 326 N.Y.S.2d 702; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1093



November 29, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Respondent's motion to declare section 712 (subd. [b]) of the Family Court Act unconstitutional as it affects 16- and 17-year-old females is herein granted. The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A petition was filed under the N.Y. Family Court Act § 712(b) alleging that respondent, a 17-year-old female, was a person in need of supervision (PINS). Responent filed a motion to declare § 712(b) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The Law Guardian contended that § 712(b) of the Family Court Act was unconstitutional as a violation of the equal protection clause of the Federal and State Constitutions, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, N.Y. Const., art. I, § 11, and violative of the Federal Civil Rights Act of 1964, 78 U.S. Stat. 241, in that it discriminated against 16- and 17-year-old females. The court held that people may need supervision and have inadequate homes regardless of sex and age. The fact that more males committed crimes than females was no reason for making females accountable for certain noncriminal acts when males the same age were not similarly accountable. In order to justify specialized handling of 16- and 17-year-old females from their male counterparts, it had to have been found that they tended to possess unique characteristics, were involved in different activities, and were viewed as a comparatively distinct entity by society. The court held that § 712(b) of the Family Court Act did violate the equal protection clause of the Federal and State Constitutions because the discrimination was not based upon any established unique characteristics of 16- and 17-year-old females.


OUTCOME: The motion to declare the relevant statutory section unconstitutional as it affected 16- and 17-year-old females was granted. The petition was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- persons in need of supervision -- section 712 (subd. [b]) of Family Court Act under which petition was brought alleging that 17-year-old female was person in need of supervision, unconstitutional -- discrimination not based on established unique characteristics of such females.

Section 712 (subd. [b]) of the Family Court Act under which a petition was brought alleging that respondent, a 17-year-old female, is a person in need of supervision is unconstitutional. An examination of the stated legislative rationale, as well as complementary legislation from the same Legislature and Federal statutes and Federal policy recommendations and directives indicates that this statute violates the equal protection clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions because the discrimination is not based upon any established unique characteristics of 16- and 17-year-old females.

 

1715 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cedar Rapids Engineering Co. v. Haenelt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Sullivan County


68 Misc. 2d 206; 326 N.Y.S.2d 653; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1102



November 24, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The replevin order is vacated. 5



5 In view of the determination herein, I did not reach the question of whether defendant was deprived of any rights guaranteed under the Fourth Amendment of the United States Constitution.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff creditor obtained an ex parte order of replevin against defendant debtor after the debtor defaulted under a security agreement for machinery and equipment. The debtor filed a motion to vacate or modify the order.


OVERVIEW: The debtor purchased machinery and equipment from the creditor and executed two promissory notes and a security agreement on behalf of the creditor. The security agreement provided that upon default, the creditor could declare the entire balance due and take possession of the machinery and equipment without notice. The debtor claimed that the county judge lacked authority to sign the order, that ex parte replevin orders were unconstitutional, and that he was not in default. The court vacated the replevin order. The court held that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Reg. tit. 22, § 862.2 permitted ex parte motions to be heard by a county judge, that the county judge had the power to issue the order, and that N.Y. C.P.L.R. art 71 was not unconstitutional on its face. The court held, however, that the machinery and equipment seized by the creditor were special property, that the creditor's allegation that the debtor would abscond with the property was insufficient, and that the creditor was not entitled to the order of replevin without affording the debtor notice and an opportunity to be heard.


OUTCOME: The court granted the debtor's motion to vacate the creditor's replevin order.


HEADNOTES

Replevin -- validity of ex parte replevin order -- County Judge in county without Trial or Special Term of Supreme Court had power to make ex parte order of replevin -- article 71 of CPLR by leaving to Justice question whether notice to debtor and opportunity to be heard shall be given him is constitutional -- chattels seized under replevin order were special property -- facts alleged in affidavit for replevin order were insufficient to sustain it; order is vacated.

1. During a period when no Trial or regularly designated Special Term is being held in a county, a County Judge, in an action in the Supreme Court whose venue is in that county, has jurisdiction of ex parte motions. Accordingly the County Judge acting as a Supreme Court Justice had power to make an order of replevin ex parte.

2. Article 71 of the CPLR as amended leaves to the Justice before whom an application for replevin is made the determination of whether the debtor shall be given [***2] notice and an opportunity to be heard "to conform to the due process of law requirements of the fourteenth amendment to the constitution of the United States". The procedures required by due process in replevin change in relation to several variables, some of which are the type of property, the character and extent of any creditor or governmental interest and the effect upon the debtor of the deprivation of the use of the property pending the outcome of the law suit. Since the Legislature could not outline the due process requirements for each replevin case, article 71 is not unconstitutional.

3. Since the seizure of the subject property, tools and equipment whereby defendant earns money, limits defendant's power to earn money, the chattels were special property.

4. Chattels constituting special property may be seized where there is a countervailing creditor or governmental interest and plaintiff's security interest is such a countervailing creditor interest. However the filing of perfunctory allegations by the creditor that the debtor was about to abscond with or fraudulently convey the property and that there was a valid debt are not constitutionally sufficient to justify the [***3] temporary deprivation of the use of special property. The papers contained in the application for the order of replevin were an insufficient foundation upon which to base an order authorizing replevin without affording defendant notice and an opportunity to be heard. The replevin order is vacated.

 

1716 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bergansky v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 251; 326 N.Y.S.2d 526; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1103



November 24, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly the petition is granted to the extent of remanding the proceeding to the New York State Liquor Authority for further consideration, including a fair hearing with a record adequate for review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to review respondent State Liquor Authority's determination disapproving the applicant's request for permission for part-time employment as a waiter in a licensed premises.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 102(2) required that one who had been convicted of a felony, and had not been pardoned, had to obtain written approval of the Liquor Authority permitting such employment. The applicant had been convicted of four felonies, including burglary, armed robbery and mail theft. He was on parole at the time of his application. The applicant filed the requisite petition for approval with the Liquor Authority. The applicant was given a brief interview but there was no hearing, no hearing officer, and no adversary proceedings. The applicant was not afforded an opportunity to introduce or cross-examine witnesses or to inspect documents or to offer evidence in explanation or rebuttal. The applicant was not permitted to present evidence as to the extent of his rehabilitation. The applicant claimed that § 102(2) was unconstitutional, at least as applied to him, in that it deprived him of due process of law. The court rejected the claim of unconstitutionality but found that the applicant should have been afforded an opportunity to call and to cross-examine witnesses, to inspect documents, and to offer evidence in explanation or rebuttal.


OUTCOME: The petition was granted to the extent of remanding the proceeding to the Liquor Authority for further consideration, including a fair hearing with a record adequate for review.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- restrictions on employment in licensed premises -- denial of approval of application by convicted felon for permission to work as part-time waiter in licensed premises was mechanistic; matter remanded for hearing and determination as to petitioner's rehabilitation.

Respondent Authority adopted undue rigidity and a mechanistic approach in denying to petitioner, who had been convicted of a felony, approval of his application for employment as a part-time waiter in licensed premises (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 102, subd. 2). There was no hearing, just a brief interview with an official, no hearing officer and no adversary proceedings. There was no consideration given to the extent of petitioner's rehabilitation. Even though the section here involved does not mandate a hearing, denial of one would violate due process. Accordingly, the petition is granted to the extent of remanding the proceeding to respondent for further consideration, including [***2] a fair hearing with a record adequate for review.

 

1717 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dominick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Erie County


68 Misc. 2d 425; 326 N.Y.S.2d 466; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1105



November 24, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's conviction is reversed on the law. The information below is dismissed and the restitution of the fine paid is hereby directed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the City Court of Buffalo (New York), which convicted him of a violation of the City of Buffalo, N.Y., Ordinances ch. IX, § 5(1).


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court considered whether: (1) the absence of a trial transcript denied defendant the right to full and fair appellate review; (2) the information filed by the police was defective as a matter of law; and (3) the certain sections of the City ordinances were unconstitutional as written or as applied. The court determined that defendant was convicted of a violation of a city ordinance, which was not a crime. Because of the absence of substantial portions of the trial record and its information, the court was unable to consider defendant's contention that the information was defective. The absence of a complete transcript prevented a full and fair appellate review, and thus, defendant's conviction had to be reversed. Finally, because City of Buffalo, N.Y., Ordinances ch. XXV, § 1703 suffered from numerous constitutional infirmities, the failure to comply with its provisions could not form the basis of a conviction and penal sanction. Thus, City of Buffalo, N.Y., Ordinances ch. IX, § 5(1), insofar as it was used to enforce City of Buffalo, N.Y., Ordinances ch. XXV, § 1703, was applied unconstitutionally.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and dismissed the information below.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- restraint on free speech -- use of sound equipment -- defendant's conviction for violating ordinance regulating use of sound equipment is reversed in absence of complete transcript -- ordinance imposes restraint on free speech and is unconstitutional; new trial not required.

1. Defendant's conviction for violation of an ordinance dealing with the use of sound equipment in public is reversed since the absence of a complete transcript of the trial prevents full appellate review.

2. The ordinance unconstitutionally imposes a restraint on free speech. Since defendant could not be convicted of violating this ordinance, a new trial is not required. The information is dismissed.

 

1718 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Hendrix


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 439; 326 N.Y.S.2d 646; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1121



November 18, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The father of an intestate decedent, who claimed to be the sole distributee of his son's estate, made application for payment of a part of his distributive share of the estate. The petition was opposed by the guardian ad litem of an infant purported to be the decedent's daughter.


OVERVIEW: A dispute arose over rights to the estate of an intestate decedent, between the decedent's father and an infant alleged by her guardian ad litem to be the decedent's daughter and the sole distributee of his estate. The decedent was not married to the infant's mother and no filiation proceeding had ever been instituted; the decedent had never acknowledged paternity. The court concluded that the New York statute requiring a reasonable substantiation of the claim of paternity, N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2(a)(2), did not impose an improper condition and did not result in a discrimination constituting a denial of equal protection of the law to an illegitimate child. The court found that the infant had not established that she was a distributee of decedent and, as a consequence, she lacked status to oppose the petition. The court ordered that the father's proceeding be consolidated with an accounting proceeding for determination of the solvency of the estate.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the infant lacked status to oppose the petition.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- discrimination against illegitimate infants -- EPTL 4-1.2 (subd. [a], par. [2]), which prevents illegitimate child from inheriting from father unless court, during father's lifetime, made order of filiation in proceeding instituted within two years of child's birth, does not discriminate against infant; infant whose paternity was not established is not distributee and lacks status to oppose petition for partial distribution of estate.

 EPTL 4-1.2 (subd. [a], par. [2]) provides that "An illegitimate child is the legitimate child of his father so that he and his issue inherit from his father if a court of competent jurisdiction has, during the lifetime of the father, made an order of filiation declaring paternity in a proceeding instituted during the pregnancy of the mother or within two years from the birth of the child." Such statute does not impose an improper condition and does not result in a discrimination constituting a denial of equal protection of the law to an illegitimate in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution. An infant whose mother was not married to decedent and did not in decedent's lifetime begin a paternity [***2] proceeding against him, decedent never having acknowledged her paternity, has not established that she is a distributee of decedent. She therefore lacks status to oppose a petition for partial distribution of the estate.

 

1719 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hochman v. State Tax Comm'n


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


68 Misc. 2d 781; 327 N.Y.S.2d 859; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1124



November 17, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer challenged a final determination of respondent State Tax Commission (New York), which denied petitioner a refund of a sales tax. Respondent cross-moved to dismiss the petition as a matter of law.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a nonresident, was the successful bidder for a horse that was injured at the claimant race and had to be destroyed. Petitioner filed an application for refund of the sales and use tax paid for the horse. Respondent denied the application for a refund. Petitioner sought review and annulment of respondent's decision. Respondent filed a cross-motion seeking the dismissal of the petition as a matter of law on the grounds that petition had failed to comply with the statutory requirements. The court granted respondent's motion and dismissed the petition. The undertaking which petitioner submitted had not been approved by a Justice of the Supreme Court and did not mention an amount sufficient to cover the costs of the prosecution of the proceeding. The undertaking therefore was not in compliance with N.Y. Tax Law § 1139(b). The court held that the statute did not violate the constitutional rights of petitioner.


OUTCOME: The court granted respondent's cross-motion and dismissed the petition seeking review of the denial of the sales tax refund.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- sales tax -- conditions precedent -- undertaking required by statute as condition precedent to maintenance of proceeding to obtain refund of sales tax does not comply with statute (Tax Law, § 1139, subd. [b]) -- statute is constitutional.

1. An undertaking, required by subdivision (b) of section 1139 of the Tax Law, to be filed as a condition precedent to the commencement of a proceeding to obtain a refund of a sales tax does not comply with said subdivision since it was not approved by a Justice of the Supreme Court and does not mention an amount sufficient to cover the costs of the prosecution of this proceeding.

2. Such subdivision does not violate petitioner's constitutional rights.

 

1720 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


North Bellmore Teachers Ass'n v. Bd. of Educ.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


68 Misc. 2d 238; 326 N.Y.S.2d 571; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1134; 78 L.R.R.M. 3117



November 15, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teachers' association sought a judgment prohibiting respondent board of education (board) from holding a hearing to fix a salary schedule and insurance coverage. The hearing was stayed pending the court's decision.


OVERVIEW: The collective bargaining agreement between the parties provided for the reopening and renegotiation of salary schedules and of insurance plans and contributions thereto and for the ultimate handling of grievances by arbitration. Following an impasse, a mediator was appointed and thereafter became the factfinder. At a meeting, the board rejected four of five of the factfinder's recommendations. The teachers' association received no notice that the recommendations were to be voted on at that meeting. The board ordered a public hearing to be held, which the court stayed. The court then vacated the stay and declared that the dispute between the parties was not arbitrable. The court held that the board did not violate N.Y. Civ. Serv. Laws § 209(3) and that § 209(3) was constitutional. The court found that fixation of salary and insurance levels under the reopening provision was not an arbitrable grievance under the agreement, which only required arbitration of disputes concerning its meaning, interpretation, or application. The court also found that § 209 permitted publicity of the ultimate results by any party at any time and mandated it by the factfinder within the five-day period.


OUTCOME: The court treated the teachers 'association's petition as an action for a declaratory judgment. The court vacated the stay, declared that the disputes was not arbitrable, held that the statute at issue was constitutional, and held that the board had not violated the statute.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- teachers -- in collective bargaining agreement, provision for final arbitration of "any dispute concerning the meaning, interpretation or application of this Agreement" does not apply to provision that salaries be renegotiated at end of first year -- board of education may validly and constitutionally resolve impasse between teachers' association and itself ( Civil Service Law, § 209, subd. 3, par. [e]) -- court treats CPLR article 78 proceeding as action for declaratory judgment ( CPLR 103).

1. A two-year collective bargaining agreement between a board of education and a teachers' association provided that the salary schedules should be renegotiated at the end of the first year. A further provision of the agreement, for final arbitration of grievances, "defined as any dispute concerning the meaning, interpretation or application of this Agreement", was not intended to require arbitration of the renegotiation.

2. The Public Employees' Fair Employment Act validly and constitutionally empowers a board of education to resolve an impasse between a teachers' association and the board itself by rejecting the recommendations of a mediator appointed by the Public Employment [***2] Relations Board and by rejecting the findings of a fact-finder appointed by the Public Employment Relations Board and by taking "such action as it [said board of education] deems to be in the public interest" ( Civil Service Law, § 209, subd. 3, par. [e]).

3. Although this is a proceeding under article 78 of the CPLR, the court treats this as an action ( CPLR 103) for a declaratory judgment. At the court's request, the Attorney-General has appeared ( Executive Law, § 71) in support of the constitutionality of the State legislative act involved.

 

1721 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Society of Plastics Industry, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 366; 326 N.Y.S.2d 788; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1141; 3 ERC (BNA) 1114; 1 ELR 20467



November 11, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, plastic container sellers and suppliers, sought a declaratory judgment determining that New York City, N.Y., Local Law No. 43 (1971) was unconstitutional and invalid. Plaintiffs also asked for permanent injunctive relief against its enforcement by defendant city.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed an action against the city in which they challenged the constitutionality of a city law that imposed a recycling tax upon plastic containers and not upon other similar items. Plaintiffs additionally requested injunctive relief to prevent the city from enforcing the law. The court granted a declaratory judgment in favor of plaintiffs and declared the city law unconstitutional. The court found the city law exceeded N.Y. Tax Law § 1201(f), which was the enabling statute that authorized it where § 1201(f) specifically fixed the taxable class and the city could not pick and choose from the types of materials enumerated in the class. The court reasoned that if the city chose to tax, the city needed to impose a tax on the entire taxable class. The court held the city law violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 because the discrimination against plastic containers was arbitrary and was not grounded upon any difference that had a substantial relation to the recycling tax. The court noted that it did not cost more to collect plastic containers than it did to collect paper, metal, or glass containers.


OUTCOME: The court granted a judgment in favor of plaintiffs upon declaring a city recycling tax law unconstitutional, invalid and void. The court permanently enjoined the city and its agents, attorneys, servants and employees from enforcing, carrying out, implementing or otherwise giving effect to the provisions of said local law.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- municipal tax for revenue and ecology -- State law authorizing city to tax "containers made in whole or in part of * * * paperboard, fibre, glass, metal, plastic or any combination of such materials" does not authorize city to choose to tax only containers "made in whole or in part of * * * plastic" -- Home Rule principles do not apply to constitutional questions of delegated tax powers -- evidence shows that city's discrimination against plastics industry and in favor of paperboard, fibre, glass, and metal industries contravenes State enabling act's declared purpose "to promote the recycling of containers and reduce the cost of solid waste disposal", and that such discrimination will damage or destroy businesses without legitimate reason -- moreover, "rigid or semi-rigid" is too vague -- however, no showing of undue burden on interstate commerce -- constitutionality may be attacked, not only by directly taxed corporations, but by corporations which supply industry; judicial trend is towards enlarging classes of persons aggrieved -- city's tax is unconstitutional; permanent injunction is issued against enforcement.

1. Section 1201 of the Tax Law authorizes [***2] cities of over a million inhabitants (New York City) to impose "any or all of the types of taxes set forth in the following subdivisions". Subdivision (f) of section 1201 authorizes taxes "on the sale of containers made in whole or in part of rigid or semi-rigid paperboard, fibre, glass, metal, plastic or any combination of such materials", in order "to promote the recycling of containers and reduce the cost of solid waste disposal" (L. 1971, ch. 399, adding subd. [f] to § 1201 of Tax Law). This subdivision does not enable the City of New York to pick and choose just one of those five materials. The city's tax is imposed only on containers "made in whole or in part of rigid or semi-rigid plastic" (Local Laws, 1971, No. 43 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 46, tit. F). This discriminates against the plastic container industry in favor of the paper, fibre, glass, and metal industries. Home Rule principles are inapplicable in determining whether the State has delegated any of its tax powers (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 1; art. XVI, § 1), and any constitutional doubt about such delegation must be resolved against the local tax. This tax by the City [***3] of New York is unauthorized and invalid, and a judgment of injunction is granted against enforcing it.

2. The local law, which was originated by the city's Environmental Protection Administration, states that its purposes are "raising revenue by imposing taxes on plastic containers and to promote the recycling of such containers and reduce the cost of solid waste disposal to the city"; and the local law further provides that it "shall be construed and enforced in conformity with" the State's enabling act -- which likewise states that it is intended "to promote the recycling of containers and reduce the cost of solid waste disposal". Yet the evidence demonstrates that neither recycling nor waste disposal, nor probably, even revenue, will be achieved by discriminating between plastics on the one hand and paperboard, fibre, glass and metals on the other hand. Such discrimination is arbitrary and without any reasonable relation to the objectives of the legislation, and thus the local law contravenes principles of equal protection of the laws (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 11; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.).

3. Furthermore, the evidence shows that the local law will operate to damage or destroy [***4] plaintiffs' industries and businesses, to the benefit of their competitors, and without any legitimate public reason therefor. Thus, the local law effectually deprives plaintiffs of their property without due process of law (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.).

4. Moreover, the local law does not state standards for defining "rigid or semi-rigid plastic", and thus it is so vague as to contravene constitutional principles of due process.

5. However, plaintiffs have failed to demonstrate that the local law imposes an undue burden on interstate commerce (U. S. Const., art. I, § 8) or that Congress has pre-empted the whole field of solid waste disposal.

6. The plaintiffs are (a) some 50 corporations which sell plastic containers to retailers or which supply raw materials or the dyes or machinery used in manufacturing plastic containers and (b) an incorporated association of the plastics industry. All of them have substantial interests in the controversy and thus they all have standing to sue. The current judicial trend in constitutional and important administrative controversies is not to limit the "persons aggrieved" to those who are directly taxed or regulated,  [***5] but to enlarge the litigant classes to include all substantially affected adversaries, in order that the important controversy may be fully presented to the courts.

7. Accordingly, New York City's Local Law No. 43 of 1971 is adjudged invalid, and the city is permanently enjoined from enforcing it.

 

1722 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lou Bern Broadway, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


68 Misc. 2d 112; 325 N.Y.S.2d 806; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1150



November 9, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant is convicted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Subsequent to a non jury trial before the court, in which he was convicted, defendant challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 245.10 and 245.11, which prohibited the display of sexual materials in a public thoroughfare or in a public place, on the grounds that the newly passed statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned and operated a theatre which showed adult movies. Defendant posted an advertisement, which consisted of a billboard depicting a nude female in prone position with her buttocks exposed and visible. Also visible from the street was a large billboard containing numerous smaller pictures of nude women. The state of New York had recently passed a statute consisting of two parts, which prohibited such a display in a public thoroughfare, even though the prohibited materials might not have been obscene under obscenity laws. Defendant was tried and convicted under this law and then sought to have the statute declared unconstitutional. The court declared the statute constitutional, as it was simply a time, place, and manner restriction, and it held that defendant was properly convicted under the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for violating a New York statute, which prohibited the display of sexual materials in a public thoroughfare or in a public place, and held that the statute was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- public display of offensive sexual material -- constitutional law -- statute making it misdemeanor to display, at any place easily visible from public thoroughfare, pictures or other representations which, though not obscene, appeal predominantly to prurient interest in sex (Penal Law, §§ 245.10, 245.11), is constitutional; theatre owner convicted.

Chapter 962 of the Laws of 1971, effective September 1, 1971, declares "that public displays of photographs, drawings and similar visual material depicting nudity, sexual conduct, and sado-masochistic activities and that appeal predominantly to prurient interest in sex are offensive to passersby when readily visible from public thoroughfares. Regardless whether such public displays are 'obscene' within the meaning of the penal law and constitutional law, they are not constitutionally protected, because they are thrust indiscriminately upon unwilling audiences of adults and children, and constitute assaults upon individual privacy. Accordingly, it is the objective of this Act to protect users of public thoroughfares from unwanted subjection to such displays." Accordingly, sections 245.10 [***2] and 245.11 are added to the Penal Law, making such displays a misdemeanor. These sections are valid and constitutional, and the owner-manager of a motion-picture theatre was convicted by the trial court of violating them.

People v. Carter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Oneida County


68 Misc. 2d 283; 325 N.Y.S.2d 772; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1152



November 9, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of conviction from the City Court of Utica (New York), which found him guilty of parking violations upon a plea of guilty and fined him a total of $ 900 or a jail term of 90 days.


OVERVIEW: Arrest warrants were issued for defendant's failure to pay parking tickets. The trial court found defendant guilty of parking violations upon his guilty plea and fined him a total of $ 900 or a jail term of 90 days. On appeal, defendant contended that the fine imposed was illegal and unconstitutional. The trial court's judgment of conviction was affirmed. The court held that the fine imposed upon defendant was not improper. Pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 372, the city had authority to impose a greater fine for a party's failure to respond promptly to traffic tickets as long as the fines were within the limits established as penalties for such offenses. Thus, the greater fine for failure to respond promptly to traffic tickets was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction against defendant for parking violations and fine of $ 900.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- parking tickets -- fine of $ 900 or 90 days in jail for recipient of number of parking tickets over two-year period is constitutional.

A judgment convicting defendant, whose automobile had been tagged with a number of parking tickets over a two-year period, on his plea of guilty and fining defendant a total of $ 900, or in lieu thereof, a jail term of 90 days, the Judge indicating a willingness to accept payment of the fine at $ 10 a week is affirmed. The fine is legal and constitutional.

People v. Fay's Drug Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Town of Camillus, Onondaga County


68 Misc. 2d 143; 326 N.Y.S.2d 311; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1160



November 5, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion for dismissal of the proceedings is granted. Case dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for dismissal of the proceedings, charging it with a violation of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law §§ 3, 9, the Sunday selling laws.


OVERVIEW: The crime involved defendant's alleged sale of a men's necktie on a Sunday. Defendant argued that N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law §§ 3, 9 were unconstitutionally vague and further that the prosecution of defendant was unconstitutionally discriminatory. The court concluded that N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 5 provided an exception so broad and so vague as to render the prohibition meaningless. While the vagueness of the statute could have been cured by amendment or complete re-enactment, the court held that the Sunday selling laws faced additional constitutional problems. The court found that because the laws forced individuals to give recognition to a religious observance which they would not have otherwise given, the laws had the effect of an advancement of religion in violation of U.S Const. amend. I. Additionally, the court held that the prosecution of defendant violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV because the statute in question has been discriminatory enforced against it.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion for dismissal and dismissed the case.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sale on Sunday -- sections of General Business Law prohibiting sales on Sunday violate First and Fourteenth Amendments of United States Constitution; charge against defendant is dismissed -- moreover, there is discriminatory enforcement of statute.

1. Sections 3, 5 and 9 of the General Business Law, prohibiting sales on Sunday with stated exceptions are so vague as to render the prohibition meaningless. Moreover the sections give support to the concept of religion and place a burden upon or create a practical difficulty for the nonreligious citizen. The sections violate the First and Fourteenth Amendments of the United States Constitution. The charge against defendant for violating the sections is dismissed.

2. Moreover, defendant has sustained the burden of proving that there has been discriminatory enforcement of the statutes against it.

Sawyer v. Vanadzin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rockland County


67 Misc. 2d 772; 325 N.Y.S.2d 684; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1166



November 4, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents sought review of a determination made by respondent Commissioner of the Rockland County Department of Health that their disabled child was not entitled for financial assistance to enable the child to partake in a speech therapy program because the child had not been a resident of the county for at least 10 months.


OVERVIEW: The parents' child suffered from a hearing deficit with resultant speech difficulties. The parents applied to the county health department for financial assistance to enable the child to partake in a six-month speech therapy program at the county center for the physically handicapped. The parents' application was denied on the ground that the child had not been a resident of the county for at least 10 months. On appeal, the court concluded that neither the Rockland County Legislature nor the Commissioner of the Rockland County Department of Health had the legal authority to impose the 10-month residency requirement. The court also held that the residency qualification was constitutionally defective. The court concluded that the 10-month residency requirement was illegal, unconstitutional, and void.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the Commissioner's determination and remitted the parents' application to the Commissioner for determination on the merits.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Health -- physically handicapped children -- residence requirement -- Rockland County Legislature was without authority to adopt resolution providing that "Health Department Physically Handicapped Children's Program" would be available to children who had been residents for 10 months and to children whose parents had been residents for 10 months -- moreover, such residency qualification is unconstitutional.

1. The Rockland County Legislature adopted a resolution providing, in part, that "the Health Department Physically Handicapped Children's Program will be available to children who will have been residents of the County of Rockland for a period of ten (10) months at the time of application and to children born of parents who shall have been residents of the County of Rockland for a period of ten (10) months at the time of application." The statutes indicate a strong State policy that facilities for treatment and rehabilitation be available to all children residing in this State who have suffered physical handicaps. Express authority for the imposition of a minimum residency requirement is not found in any of the statutes and there is no implied authority. Neither the Rockland [***2] County Legislature nor the Commissioner of the Rockland County Department of Health has the legal authority to impose the residency requirement.

2. Moreover, such residency qualification is unconstitutional.

 

1726 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Forlano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


67 Misc. 2d 996; 325 N.Y.S.2d 523; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1169



November 3, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to set aside the verdict is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of violating N.Y. Penal Law § 225.15(1). Pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.30, defendant moved to set aside the verdict upon the grounds that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The information charged defendant with the crime of Possession of Gambling Records in the Second Degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 225.15. Defendant was accused of possessing slips of paper containing gambling notations and reflecting bets on the outcome of sporting events. Defendant contended that this law was unconstitutional because it was void for vagueness and it prohibited legitimate activities in violation of the First, Fifth, and Fourteenth Amendments. The court held that, in determining the sufficiency of the notice to a defendant, the statute had to be examined in the light of the conduct with which he was charged. The constitutional requirement of definiteness was only violated by a criminal statute when it failed to give a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that his contemplated conduct was forbidden by the law. The court held that the articles which this defendant was found guilty of possessing were essential tools of the game. The court held that as applied to this defendant, the statute set forth sufficient ascertainable standards to give him fair notice that his conduct was forbidden.


OUTCOME: The motion to set aside the verdict was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- possession of gambling records -- Penal Law ( § 225.15, subd. 1), defining when person is guilty of possession of gambling records, not unconstitutional for vagueness -- as applied to this defendant, statute sets forth ascertainable standards -- statute not unconstitutional for prohibiting legitimate activities -- despite validity of statute, court questions its usefulness.

1. Subdivision 1 of section 225.15 of the Penal Law provides that: "A person is guilty of possession of gambling records in the second degree when, with knowledge of the contents thereof, he possesses any writing, paper, instrument or article: 1. Of a kind commonly used in the operation or promotion of a bookmaking scheme or enterprise." Defendant was found to have possessed slips which were records of lay off bets on baseball games. Defendant has not contended that the statute did not give him, or an ordinary person in similar circumstances, notice that his conduct violated the letter of the statute, but rather argues that the statute might be unconstitutionally applied to others under different circumstances. As applied to this defendant the [***2] statute sets forth sufficient ascertainable standards to give him fair notice that his conduct was forbidden, and it is not unconstitutional for vagueness.

2. The statute is not unconstitutional in that it prohibits legitimate activities. The State has the power to prohibit gambling and to declare such conduct criminal.

3. Despite the fact that the court finds the statute valid, the court questions the usefulness of the statute.

 

1727 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Artrol Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Village of Ocean Beach, Suffolk County


67 Misc. 2d 1087; 325 N.Y.S.2d 800; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1173



October 30, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff States filed an action against defendant restaurants for violations of the Ordinances of the Village of Ocean Beach art. II, § 4 (1967), dealing with the posting of signs.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance proscribed the size for signs within the village. Each of the restaurants conceded that on the date alleged in each information each of the respective properties had a sign or signs on top of, above, or on the roof, which exceeded four square feet in area and that some were illuminated, in violation of the ordinance. One restaurant conceded the existence of three illuminated signs on the side of the building and another restaurant conceded an illuminated sign was in the window of the premises. The restaurants contended that (1) the ordinance was unconstitutional because no public benefit was shown; (2) the ordinance deprived the restaurants of a property right in the signs; (3) there was no proof that the individual restaurants owned or maintained the signs in question; and (4) the ordinance was discriminatory. The court found that the ordinance was constitutional and that aestheticism was properly considered in enacting sign controls. Also, the two years' period for conformity in the ordinance was held to be reasonable and the ordinance therefore was a proper exercise of the police power enacted for a public benefit.


OUTCOME: Each restaurant was found guilty as charged.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- regulation of commercial signs -- village ordinance validly and constitutionally prohibits illuminated signs and limits size of other signs, and allows existing nonconforming signs to continue for not more than two years; defendants, who have maintained nonconforming signs for over two years, are found guilty -- individual defendants are criminally liable for their corporation's violations -- immaterial that other violators have not been prosecuted yet -- signs maintained by village in its governmental capacity are not subject to this ordinance.

1. A village ordinance validly and constitutionally provides: "Signs within the" village "shall not be illuminated and shall be limited in size to 1 square foot in area * * *. This size limitation shall not apply to existing commercial or business uses for which the signs may not exceed four square feet in area and may be placed only on the property on which the commercial or business use occurs. Nonconforming signs may continue such nonconformity * * * but the period of such nonconformity may not exceed 2 years from the date of the enactment of this ordinance." The defendants, who have maintained nonconforming [***2] signs for over two years since the ordinance took effect, are found guilty.

2. One defendant is a corporation which owns a restaurant. Another defendant is the president of that corporation. Another defendant is the sole stockholder and active manager of another incorporated restaurant. Another defendant is a partner in a company which owns a building and said building's nonconforming sign, and he is also a stockholder in the restaurant corporation located in that building. These individual defendants are criminally responsible (cf. Penal Law, § 20.25).

3. The ordinance is authorized by the Village Law ( § 89, subds. 47, 59). Furthermore, this village is within the Fire Island National Seashore and this ordinance is authorized by the regulations of the Secretary of the Interior (78 U.S. Stat. 928; Code of Fed. Reg., tit. 36, § 28.4, subds. [a], [b], [e]).

4. It is immaterial that other noncomplying persons and corporations have not been prosecuted as yet.

5. Signs which are maintained by the village itself in its governmental capacity are not subject to the village's ordinance.

 

1728 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Anderson v. Warden, New York City Correctional Institution for Men


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


68 Misc. 2d 463; 325 N.Y.S.2d 829; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1175



October 29, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The writs are dismissed without prejudice to proceedings by motion in the courts of conviction under article 440 of the CPL; or, in the alternative, upon application by defense counsel within five days after publication of this decision, the writs will be referred to the criminal motion parts in the courts of conviction to be treated as motions under article 440.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators filed petitions seeking writs of habeas corpus, and the court signed the writs. The court considered whether each relator's writ should be dismissed on the ground that a more appropriate remedy would be a motion to vacate judgment or set aside sentence under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 440.


OVERVIEW: The court dismissed relators' writs on the ground that the new motions to vacate judgment under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 440.10 and to set aside sentence under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 440.20 provided relators with an adequate remedy in their respective courts of conviction. The court determined that habeas corpus should be reserved for cases dictated by practicality and necessity and that habeas corpus was neither necessary nor practical in any case where the same grounds could be raised under art. 440. Therefore, where relators had remedies under both art. 440 and habeas corpus, art. 440 was to be preferred as being vastly more efficacious than habeas corpus writs. The court also determined that there was no constitutional difficulty with restricting contemporary grounds for habeas corpus. The court found that the state constitution's suspension of habeas corpus clause only applied to grounds for habeas corpus that existed when the clause was added to the constitution. The clause was added to the New York Constitution in 1821 when the only inquiry on habeas corpus was whether the court had jurisdiction to render the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed relators' writs without prejudice to proceedings on the ground that relators had an adequate remedy in their respective courts of conviction without resorting to the alternative remedy of habeas corpus.


HEADNOTES

Habeas corpus -- better remedy under CPL article 440 -- writs of habeas corpus, brought in Supreme Court in county of imprisonment, to vacate judgments or to set aside sentences, will be dismissed or will be referred to courts of conviction; motions in courts of conviction ( CPL art. 440) are speedier and more efficacious than habeas corpus proceedings in county of imprisonment -- CPL article 440 is constitutional.

Article 440 of the new Criminal Procedure Law validly and constitutionally provides for motions in the court of conviction to vacate judgments or to set aside sentences, in lieu of the former remedies of coram nobis and habeas corpus in such cases. The Supreme Court in the county of imprisonment still has jurisdiction of a writ of habeas corpus; but a motion under CPL article 440 provides a speedier [***2] and more efficacious remedy than the ancient form of proceeding by way of a writ of habeas corpus (cf. N. Y. Const., art. I, § 4; U.S. Const., art. I, § 9). Accordingly, these writs of habeas corpus in the Supreme Court by three different prisoners, each brought on different grounds, are dismissed, without prejudice to proceedings by motion under CPL article 440 in the respective courts of conviction; or, in the alternative, if defense counsel so request, these writs will be referred by this court to the respective courts of conviction, there to be treated as motions under CPL article 440.

 

1729 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Van Nostrand v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Seneca County


67 Misc. 2d 1096; 325 N.Y.S.2d 372; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1180



October 27, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff resident brought a proceeding for a declaration that a permanent plan of weighted voting was unconstitutional in view of the change in population of the county and the various towns as reflected by the 1970 census.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a board of supervisors and others, contended that the change of population was not significant enough to create any substantial disparity between the voting power of any legislator and the percentage of population of the county represented by him so as to require a further reapportionment. The court held that in passing upon the constitutionality of legislative apportionment, the court was required to, in light of the particular circumstances of the case, determine whether the county had made a good faith effort to achieve equality of representation and whether there was sufficient justification for any variance from absolute equality. The court held that whether or not the relevant plan, in view of the population change shown by the 1970 census, met this standard, could not be determined from the pleadings in the case, it could only be determined after hearing the opinions of experts based upon computer analyses. Accordingly, the court directed that a hearing be held.


OUTCOME: The court directed that a hearing be held before the court to decide the questions of fact in the matter.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- though Board of Supervisors of Seneca County was reapportioned on weighted voting basis in 1967, court will hear evidence to determine whether 1970 census figures require another reapportionment.

A weighted plan of voting for the members of the Board of Supervisors of Seneca County was approved by the court in 1967 based on the 1960 census. Now the 1970 census shows apparently important variations from the 1960 population distribution. The court will hold a hearing to determine whether a reapportionment is constitutionally necessary.

 

1730 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


County of Orange v. Metropolitan Transp. Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


71 Misc. 2d 691; 337 N.Y.S.2d 178; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1193



October 20, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] To sum up then, the motion for a preliminary injunction and for related relief is denied. The cross motion to dismiss the complaint and the individual causes of action contained therein is granted, but with leave to the plaintiff, if it be so advised, to replead the tenth, thirteenth and fourteenth causes of action in the event it has title to excepted Parcels "E", "F" or "G", within 10 days after service of a copy of the order to be entered hereon with notice of entry.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the state and the transportation authority, sought to acquire a large tract of land in connection with a plan for the development and expansion of an airport. Plaintiff county filed suit seeking to enjoin the authority from proceeding with the acquisition of the land located in the county after the defendant filed a map with the county clerk outlining the land being condemned. The authority filed a motion to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The authority developed a planned to expand an existing airport which required the condemnation of almost 9000 acres of land. The county objected to the plan and filed suit seeking to enjoin the authority from proceeding with the plan and alleging 14 different causes of action. The court concluded that the legislature's acts providing for the acquisition of the land were valid because the expansion of the airport was a public purpose and neither the absence of detailed plans for the use of the land nor the fact that some of the land would not be used until sometime in the future were sufficient grounds for invalidating the acquisition of the land. The court also rejected the county's argument that the authority had to obtain the county's consent before condemning the land because state statutes did not require such consent when the property was acquired for a transportation facility. The court held that the map submitted to the county clerk, which merely outlined the land being acquired, was not unconstitutionally vague. However, the court held that the description of certain lands excluded from the condemnation were not sufficient but the county lacked standing to object.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion of the state and transportation authority to dismiss the county's complaint, but granted the county leave to replead the objections to the descriptions of the excluded lands if it could establish that it had title to those lands. The court also denied the injunctive relief requested by the county.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- acquisition of airport -- in action to enjoin defendants from acquiring 9,000 acres of land to develop Stewart Airport, 1st and 3d causes of action alleging that appropriation was invalid because master plan had not been created therefor are dismissed as not stating causes of action -- failure to obtain consent of towns and villages does not invalidate appropriation -- cause of action charging that impact of acquisition on county's airport is unknown is dismissed -- section 1267-a of Public Authorities Law is not unconstitutional -- constitutional requirement that public corporations be created by special act of Legislature was not violated -- prior approval of county was not required  [***2] -- Legislature in act sufficiently located land to be acquired -- appropriation map showing perimeter of lands to be acquired was sufficient -- county's approval was not required where taking is for construction of airport.

1. In an action to enjoin defendants from acquiring approximately 9,000 acres of land for the expansion and development of Stewart Airport, the 1st and 3d causes of action allege that the appropriation was invalid because it was effected before defendants completed any detailed study or master plan therefor. The creation or expansion of an airport is a public purpose for which the power of eminent domain may be validly exercised, and the exercise of the power is in the Legislature or its instrumentality in which it reposes the power. They are the sole judges of the necessity of a taking. The allegations that defendants appropriated the land before completing a master plan for the expansion do not state a cause of action. Neither the absence of plans for use of the land nor that some of the land may not be used until sometime in the future provides a basis for invalidating the acquisition.

2. The failure of defendants to obtain the consent of the towns and [***3] villages in which the real property is located (Public Authorities Law, § 1267-a) does not invalidate the appropriation since such consent is not required for a transportation facility. The 2d cause of action is dismissed.

3. The 4th cause of action to the effect that the impact of the acquisition on plaintiff's county airport is not known fails to state a cause of action and is dismissed.

4. The 5th cause of action wherein plaintiff alleges that section 1267-a of the Public Authorities Law is unconstitutional because it authorizes the taking of property without due process of law fails to state a cause of action and is dismissed. Section 1267-a incorporates by reference section 30 of the Highway Law, whose procedures have been held to be constitutional and do not deprive owners of their due process rights. The absence of any requirement that notice be given the owners prior to the appropriation does not make the statute unconstitutional.

5. The 6th cause of action charging that the Stewart Airport Act (L. 1971, chs. 472, 473) violates section 5 of article X of the Constitution requiring that public corporations be created by special act of the Legislature is dismissed. Such [***4] act is a special act.

6. The 7th cause of action alleging that the Stewart Airport Act affects the property, affairs and government of plaintiff county and the failure to obtain the prior approval of the county executive and Legislature violated the home rule provisions of the Constitution is dismissed. The act involves matters of State concern and the home rule provisions are inapplicable.

7. The 8th cause of action alleging that the act, to the extent that it delegates to defendants powers of condemnation is vague as to the location of the land to be appropriated violating sections 6 and 7 of article I of the Constitution is dismissed. The Legislature sufficiently indicated the general location of the lands which respondent in its discretion may acquire.

8. The 10th, 13th and 14th causes of action charging that the appropriation map sets forth the perimeter of the land to be taken without a metes and bounds description of each parcel is dismissed. Prior to the determination of damages, if each property owner can from the map, determine that his property has been taken and to what extent, it is sufficient, and here each owner can do so, with the possible exception of owners [***5] who have title to some of the excepted parcels. Nothing in the causes of action indicates that the county is the owner of these excepted parcels. The county is permitted to replead if it has title to any of the excepted parcels.

9. The 11th and 12th causes of action are dismissed. The county's approval was not required where the land is appropriated for the construction of an airport.

Shapiro v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


67 Misc. 2d 1021; 325 N.Y.S.2d 787; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1201



October 19, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, plaintiff's complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorney, by order to show cause containing a stay against defendant city from enforcing collection of the unincorporated business income tax against self-employed practitioners, sought a declaration of unconstitutionality of New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 36 (1971).


OVERVIEW: The attorney sought a declaration that a city law that repealed the exemption enjoyed by self-employed doctors, lawyers, architects, and practitioners of any other profession from paying the city's unincorporated business tax was unconstitutional. The court found that the law was constitutional and dismissed the attorney's complaint. The attorney argued that the repealing statute discriminated against self-employed professionals in favor of salaried professionals, and that including professions within the classification of business created a double tax on the self-employed professionals; thereby denying the attorney and others the equal protection of the law and taking their property without due process of law. The court found no equal protection violation because the removal of the exemption for the professions was not purely arbitrary, based on fictions, or indicative of hostile or oppressive discrimination. There was no due process violation because the statute would not result in destroying the practice of the professions by self-employed members.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the attorney's complaint seeking a declaration that a law authorizing the collection of the unincorporated business income tax against self-employed practitioners was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- taxation -- New York City validly and constitutionally taxes professional incomes of self-employed doctors, dentists, lawyers, and other practitioners of professions.

New York City's unincorporated business income tax (Administrative Code of City of New York, § S46-1.0 et seq.; Local Laws, 1966, No. 22 of City of New York) now validly and constitutionally applies to self-employed lawyers, doctors, dentists, architects and other self-employed practitioners of professions (Local Laws, 1971, No. 36 of City of New York; Administrative Code, § S46-2.0, subd. [c] repealed; repealer authorized by L. 1971, ch. 412).

People v. Cerio


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Onondaga County


67 Misc. 2d 915; 325 N.Y.S.2d 43; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1214



October 13, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the above reasons, the motion is denied in its entirety, except for those applications and affidavits already furnished the defendants by the District Attorney.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action filed by the People of the State of New York, defendants filed a motion for an evidentiary hearing as to wiretaps and wiretap warrants, for disclosure of the evidence obtained through the wiretaps, and for suppression of the wiretap evidence. Defendants disputed the validity of the wiretap warrants. Defendants were charged with first and second-degree promotion of gambling and third and fourth-degree conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: A grandchild wiretap was established on a telephone number based on previous parent and child wiretaps. Specific wagers were given to one defendant at this number. Officers identified defendant's voice based on previous contact. The information from the grandfather tap resulted in defendants' indictments. Defendants argued that the informant behind the parent tap was a convicted burglar. The court denied the motion. The parent tap warrants were issued and before the informant was released from prison, the conviction did not necessarily render his information invalid, and the parent tap's validity had already been litigated. The grandchild tap warrant met the particularity requirement because it described defendants and the gambling-related conversations to be recorded. It was not necessary to amend the grandchild tap warrant to name defendants when they were named in the affidavits and warrant application. Because the court had previously found N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 814 et seq. (1968) constitutional, and because the police had complied with constitutional requirements and the New York statutes in effect at the time, the grandchild tap evidence was admissible.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion, except with regard to the affidavits and the applications that the district attorney had previously furnished to defendants.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- eavesdropping (wiretapping) affidavits and warrants which were issued in 1968 complied fully with Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 814 et seq.); defendants' motions in 1971 for inspection and suppression are denied.

Eavesdropping (wiretapping) affidavits, applications and warrants which were issued in 1968 complied fully with the Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 814 et seq.). Defendants' motions in 1971 for an order permitting them to inspect and examine all the intercepted conversations and for an order suppressing all those conversations and all leads obtained through those conversations, on the asserted ground that there was not adequate probable cause for the issuance of the warrants, are denied.
