Gorenberg v. Onondaga County Board of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Onondaga County; Oneida County


67 Misc. 2d 766; 325 N.Y.S.2d 262; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1224



October 12, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Upon all the memorandums submitted to this court, it is the holding of the court, that section 151 of the Election Law of the State of New York does not violate the Federal or State Constitution. Two, that section 1971 (subd. [a], par. [2], subpar. [A]) of title 42 of the United States Code does not render section 151 of the Election Law unconstitutional and that the petitioners' petition is in all respect denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner students brought a class action testing the constitutionality of N.Y. Elec. Law § 151 (1971) dealing with students living away from home and their ability to register and vote in elections. The students contended that 42 U.S.C.S. § 1971(a)(2)(A) pre-empted N.Y. Elec. Law § 151 (1971) and rendered it unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Most of the students were dormitory residents and were refused registration upon the grounds that they did not comply with N.Y. Elec. Law § 151 (1971) dealing with students living away from home. The students alleged that 42 U.S.C.S. § 1971(a)(2)(A) pre-empted the state statute and rendered it unconstitutional. The court disagreed and denied the students' petition in all respects. The court noted that the state statute required the use of certain criteria to determine if a student had, in fact, established a voting domicile and that the same criteria was applied to the other classification, those "employed in the service of the United States," identified in the statute. Just as no person acting under color of law could deprive a student qualified under state law or laws of his or her right to vote in any election, so, too, the students could not use "color of intent" to qualify to register and vote. The court found that the statute did not violate either the federal or state constitution. The court also found that the statute was not pre-empted or rendered unconstitutional by § 1971(a)(2)(A).


OUTCOME: The students' petition was denied in all respects.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- residence -- students -- Election Law ( § 151) providing that, for purpose of registering and voting, no person shall gain or lose residence by reason of his presence or absence while student in institution of learning, and directing use of certain criteria to determine whether student has established voting domicile, not unconstitutional.

Section 151 of the Election Law as amended by Legislature in 1971, providing, in part, that, for the purpose of registering and voting, no person shall be deemed to have gained or lost a residence by reason of his presence or absence while student of any institution of learning, and directing the use of certain criteria to determine whether a student has in fact established a voting domicle, does not violate [***2] the Federal or State Constitutions. Section 1971 (subd. [a], par. [2], subpar. [A]) of title 42 of the United States code does not render section 151 of the Election Law unconstitutional.

 

1734 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Perez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Onondaga County


67 Misc. 2d 911; 325 N.Y.S.2d 183; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1227



October 12, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action filed by the People of the State of New York, defendant filed a motion to dismiss the indictment that charged him under N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05 with possession of a dangerous weapon, contending that § 265.05 was unconstitutional when read with N.Y. Penal Law §§ 265.20, 400.00. Defendant alleged violations of due process, equal protection, the right to travel, and the Privileges and Immunities Clause.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was not from New York and did not have a New York handgun permit. In his home state, his handgun possession was legal. Defendant was arrested when traveling through New York after his handgun was found in his car. The court found that N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05 was constitutional. Restricting a traveler's gun possession did not unreasonably burden the right to travel given the government's power to protect its permit licensing requirements of N.Y. Penal Law § 400.00. Defendant's privileges and immunities argument failed because the danger to the public justified the discriminatory effect of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05. Defendant's substantive due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and U.S. Const. amend. V were not violated because it was not arbitrary or capricious for the State to take his handgun where the purpose of § 265.05 was only to allow those holding New York permits to possess handguns. Defendant's equal protection rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV were not violated because the classification based on protecting the public through the State's police power was not unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court held that the provision under which defendant was indicted was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- hand guns -- permits in New York for hand guns can ordinarily be issued only to New York residents; even if nonresident has gun permit from his own State, he cannot have his hand gun with him while traveling in or through our State; our statute is valid and constitutional (Penal Law, §§ 265.05, 265.20, 400.00); such defendant's motion to dismiss indictment is denied.

Defendant, a nonresident, asserts he has a valid permit from his State to possess a hand gun. However, he has no such permit in New York State; moreover, in New York State no such permit can usually be issued to a person who does not reside or work in New York State. Accordingly, while defendant was driving in or through New York State, assertedly in order to visit friends in yet another State, he was stopped by a policeman, and, when it was discovered that he had a hand gun, he was properly arrested and charged with unlawful possession of a dangerous weapon (Penal Law, §§ 265.05, 265.20, 400.00). Our statute is a valid exercise of our State's police power and does not contravene any provision of the United States Constitution. Defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment is denied.

 

1735 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Foley v. McKnealley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


67 Misc. 2d 889; 325 N.Y.S.2d 165; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1231



October 8, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxicab owner filed suit against defendant city for a declaratory judgment that City of Newburgh Ord. § 23-27, providing that taxicabs had to be covered by a higher public liability policy than state law mandated, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The city passed an ordinance requiring taxicabs wanting to operate in the city to be covered by higher public liability insurance policies than state law mandated. The taxicab owner filed suit against the city seeking a declaratory judgment that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court held that the ordinance was constitutional. The court found that the city had the authority to legislate in this area. The court also found that the regulation was not prohibited by state law. The State mandated a minimum of public liability insurance that taxicabs had to carry but this did not preclude cities from requiring taxicabs to carry more coverage. State law did not indicate that the legislature intended to pre-empt cities from legislating in this area. The ordinance was designed to accomplish a legitimate objective of the city, which was to protect those citizens who might have been injured in an accident with a taxicab. Furthermore, the ordinance was not discriminatory and was otherwise reasonable. The court found the ordinance to be reasonable because it was a reasonable exercise of a power implicit in the power to regulate taxi franchises and was nondiscriminatory.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of the city and found that the ordinance regulating taxicab public liability insurance was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor vehicles -- minimum insurance for taxicabs -- section 370 of Vehicle and Traffic Law requires minimum insurance liability for taxicabs of $ 10,000 and maximum of $ 20,000; section does not permit cities to establish by ordinance less than such minimum for taxicabs -- section does not prohibit cities from enacting local law requiring more than such minimum and ordinance of city doing so is valid and not discriminatory.

1. Section 370 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law requires carriers for hire to provide minimum insurance liability policies of $ 10,000 and a maximum liability of $ 20,000 and for property damage a maximum of $ 5,000. It was not the intention of such section to make uniform the public liability insurance coverage of all taxicabs throughout the State. Section 370 established a minimum public liability insurance coverage for taxicabs which would be a uniform minimum throughout the State, and to the extent that this minimum has been established and to that extent, cities are expressly prohibited from adopting any regulations which would permit less than the State-established minimum public liability coverage for taxicabs.

2. Section 370 does not forbid the [***2] enactment of a local ordinance by a city which requires taxicabs to provide greater public liability insurance coverage, and such ordinance is not in conflict with State law. A city's regulation of public liability insurance for taxicabs has no tendency to undermine the broad policy of the Vehicle and Traffic Law that uniformity should prevail throughout the State. Such an ordinance is valid and not discriminatory.

 

1736 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Taylor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Westchester County


68 Misc. 2d 93; 325 N.Y.S.2d 702; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1233



October 7, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons hereinabove set forth the motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion for an order permitting their counsel to accompany and be present with them during their individual testimony before a grand jury.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether defendants had a right to counsel from the inception of custodial interrogation prior to arrest, through all intermediate stages of a criminal trial, past sentence, and up to a hearing upon a parole revocation. The court denied defendants' motion. The court held that the sessions and hearings of a grand jury, so long as they were lawful and permissible, were not to be regarded as a judicial examination of the issue between parties. The grand jury inquiry was more in the nature of a preliminary examination and was not to be subverted and converted into a regular trial. There was no necessity for a formal trial or proving facts on one side and denying them on the other. A grand jury proceeding merely required an examination of the charges and an opportunity to make an explanation in reference to them. The court held that a grand jury witness was permitted to solicit the aid of his counsel outside the grand jury room subject to the proscriptions enunciated by the court overlooking the grand jury proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion.


HEADNOTES

Grand jury -- right to counsel -- witness before Grand Jury has right to solicit aid of his counsel outside Grand Jury room but is not entitled to have lawyer present in Grand Jury room -- section 190.25 of Criminal Procedure Law concerning persons permitted in Grand Jury room not unconstitutional.

1. A motion by defendants for an order permitting their counsel to accompany and be present with each of them during their individual testimony before the Grand Jury is denied. A witness before a Grand Jury has a right to consult with counsel but the lawyer is not entitled to be present in the Grand Jury room. The witness is entitled to solicit the aid of his counsel outside the Grand Jury room.

2. The court will not declare section 190.25 of the Criminal Procedure Law concerning persons permitted in the Grand Jury room to be unconstitutional.

 

1737 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan Transp. Authority v. Tuckahoe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


67 Misc. 2d 895; 325 N.Y.S.2d 718; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1235



October 7, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state agency initiated an action for injunctive relief against defendant mayor and defendant village that sought to enjoin the mayor and the village from interfering with the construction of a footbridge overpass in the village.


OVERVIEW: The state agency purchased self-propelled commuter cars to relieve traffic congestion. In order for the cars to run on the tracks already utilized by the subway trains, the floor level of the passenger stations had to be raised. The state agency was required to construct footbridge overpasses connecting the platforms. The state agency began constructing an overpass in the village. The construction was ceased when the village's mayor contended that public money was being improperly used for private purposes. The state agency filed a petition for injunctive relief that sought to enjoin the mayor and the village from interfering with the construction. The trial court granted the petition. The expenditure was constitutional because it was for the public benefit. The fact that a private contractor was doing the construction did not make the expenditure unconstitutional. The state agency was proceeding in the best interest of the general public.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state agency's petition for injunctive relief.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- expenditure of public money for private gain -- Metropolitan Transportation Authority, by entering into contract with commuter railroad whereby it agreed to pay for improvement to stations to accommodate new trains, was not expending public moneys for private gain but was acting in accordance with its powers; village is enjoined from interfering with construction.

The Metropolitan Transportation Authority (hereafter MTA) was established by the Legislature to develop and improve commuter transportation within the metropolitan transportation district including Westchester County. The Legislature appropriated 44.4 million dollars for the modernization of commuter railroad service and provided that no part of the appropriation be available for expenditure until the authority purchased or leased the assets of the railroad (the Penn Central) comprising the two commuter divisions. Thereafter the Legislature appropriated 7.5 million dollars for the alteration and improvement of passenger stations of the two divisions of the railroad. Subdivision 2 of section 1266 of the Public Authorities Law grants MTA power to repair a transportation facility by contract,  [***2] lease "or other arrangement." On February 3, 1971 the trustees of the railroad and MTA entered into a contract for the betterment of the stations to accommodate 80 new passenger cars whereby the railroad would use its own forces and equipment and that of others, the cost to be paid by MTA. The railroad entered into an agreement with a corporation for the construction of new platforms at the station. Defendant village ordered the work stopped because no building permit had been obtained. MTA is entitled to a temporary injunction against said village. Even though the MTA has not purchased or leased the railroad's facilities, MTA was authorized to expend public funds for a public benefit, the work to be performed by a private, independent contractor for ultimate benefit of the general public. The statute permits MTA to make an "other arrangement" for such construction and therefore its expenditure is not the expenditure of public funds for private gain and is constitutional.

 

1738 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Young v. Shuart


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


67 Misc. 2d 689; 325 N.Y.S.2d 113; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1247



October 4, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother brought an article 78 proceeding against respondent Commissioner of the Nassau County Department of Social Services (commissioner) challenging the commissioner's refusal of a special grant to the mother as an aid to dependent children recipient who claimed that she and the children were destitute because cash had been stolen from her.


OVERVIEW: The mother's petition alleged that she received public assistance in the category of Aid to Dependent Children, she received a check issued by the department of social services that she cashed, and she subsequently could not find $ 395 that she put in an envelope in her pocketbook. The department had a policy of refusing to give emergency aid or to duplicate stolen checks. The mother further alleged that without the issuance of a duplicate grant of $ 395, she and her children faced exhaustion of their food supply and eviction from their rented home. The court held that the commissioner was without discretion as a matter of policy and without regard to the facts of the particular case to refuse a special grant to an aid to dependent children recipient who claimed that she and her children were destitute because cash had been stolen from her. The court concluded that the article 78 proceeding could be maintained as a class action where the commissioner continued to litigate the issue despite decisions adverse to his policy; a class determination would bind the commissioner as to all members of the class and avoid multiple litigation of the issue common to all members of the class.


OUTCOME: The court directed that the commissioner pay to the mother $ 395 in emergency assistance subject, however, to the commissioner's right to reimburse himself for such payment out of future sums payable to the mother should it be determined by the commissioner that destitution had not been established.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social services -- emergency assistance -- County Commissioner of Social Services has no discretion to refuse, as matter of policy and without regard to facts of particular case, to issue special grant to public assistance recipient who alleges that her assistance cash has been lost or stolen and that emergency assistance is necessary to avoid destitution -- such recipient may maintain article 78 proceeding against Commissioner as class action -- under emergency circumstances, recipient was not bound to exhaust administrative remediesaby seeking fair hearing -- Commissioner's policy, and effort by State to define emergency assistance to exclude destitution due to loss, theft or diversion of grant, cannot be sustained -- court directs payment of emergency assistance subject to Commissioner's right to be reimbursed if, after consideration of facts, determination is made, and upheld, that destitution has not been established.

1. A County Commissioner of Social Services has no discretion to refuse, on the basis of a general policy uniformly applied to the entire public assistance caseload and without regard to the facts of the particular case, to issue a special grant to a public [***2] assistance recipient who alleges that her assistance cash has been lost or stolen and that emergency assistance is necessary to avoid destitution.

2. Such a recipient may maintain an article 78 proceeding against the Commissioner as a class action, since his refusal of the special grant as a matter of policy, and without consideration of the facts, involves a breach of duty which adversely affects in the same way the interest of every grant recipient whose cash is lost or stolen. Nothing in CPLR precludes bringing the proceeding as a class action and, as to an argument that, in the light of the doctrine of stare decisis, no benefit will be derived from a class action judgment in such a case, there are uncontroverted allegations that the problem is a continuing one and that the Commissioner continues to litigate the issue despite decisions adverse to his policy. A class determination will, unless reversed on appeal, bind him as to all members of the class and thus avoid multiple litigation of the issue common to all of them.

3. Petitioner in the present proceeding is the mother of four infant children who receives public assistance in the category of aid to dependent children [***3] and she alleges that, after she cashed her assistance check, she put the proceeds in an envelope in her pocketbook but later could not find the envelope or the money, and that, without the issuance of a duplicate grant, she and her children face exhaustion of their food supply and eviction for nonpayment of rent. Under these emergency circumstances, she was not bound to exhaust her administrative remedies by seeking a fair hearing.

4. The Commissioner's policies must conform to Federal standards even though wholly county funds are expended, since the Legislature has the authority, under the State Constitution, to mandate such expenditures upon the county and has declared that eligibility for public assistance must conform to the applicable Federal requirements, which do not contain any limitation on the availability of Federal aid in a case where the destitution results from the loss or theft of a grant. Viewed in this context, neither his stated policy, nor the State's effort to define "emergency assistance" (Social Services Law, § 350-j) so as to exclude "destitution * * * due to loss, theft or diversion of a grant already made" ( 18 NYCRR 372.2), can be sustained.

5. Since the [***4] facts concerning the claimed loss or theft are exclusively within petitioner's knowledge, summary judgment directing issuance of emergency assistance is not granted. The court directs that the Commissioner pay her the emergency assistance subject to his right to reimburse himself for such payment out of future sums payable to her if he determines, after consideration of the facts, and is affirmed on fair hearing and article 78 review, if sought, that destitution within the meaning of section 350-j of the Social Services Law and 18 NYCRR 372.2, as above limited, has not been established. The judgment will also define the class on whose behalf the proceeding has been brought and direct the Commissioner to consider on its facts each application for emergency assistance predicated on destitution resulting from theft or loss of a grant and to abandon his policy of denying such applications without regard to the facts.

 

1739 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Brandon Shores, Inc. v. Greenwood Lake


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


68 Misc. 2d 343; 325 N.Y.S.2d 957; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1259



September 30, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the instant motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff restaurant owner sought an order restraining and enjoining defendant village from enforcing Village of Greenwood Lake, N.Y., Local Laws No. 4 (1970), which prescribed the regulations of cabarets, dance halls, bars or lounges, and discotheques. Plaintiffs also sought an order preventing, restraining, and enjoining the village from preventing the restaurant from engaging professional topless dancers to perform and entertain its patrons.


OVERVIEW: The restaurant owner maintained and operated a restaurant, discotheque, and liquor bar. Prior to the enactment of Village of Greenwood Lake, N.Y., Local Laws No. 4 (1970), the owner employed topless dancers to perform for the entertainment of the patrons. Section three of the local law prohibited topless dancing in public establishments. The owner alleged that the local law was unconstitutional, that it violated the constitutional guarantees of freedom of speech, that it was void due to its vague and indefinite terms, and that it was not a proper exercise of the police powers of the village. The court held that the 1970 amendments to N.Y. Penal Law §§ 245.01, 245.02 clearly authorized the enactment by cities, towns, or villages of an "anti-topless" ordinance. The ordinance was merely an attempt by a local community to exercise its right to determine for itself those standards that it deemed offensive to its own general welfare, public safety, order, and morals. The ordinance clearly set forth the act to be prohibited, to whom it applied, the circumstances under which it applied, and the penalty therefor.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion of the restaurant owner and the injunction that would have prevented the enforcement of the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Theatres and shows -- topless dancers -- village local law banning performances by topless dancers is within police power (Penal Law, §§ 245.01, 245.02) -- local law is not vague -- presumption of constitutionality of local law was not overcome -- plaintiffs have failed to show such immediate harm as to warrant grant of preliminary injunction.

1. The enactment by defendant village of a local law banning performances by topless dancers was within the limits of its police power, granted by sections 245.01 and 245.02 of the Penal Law, which, while permitting topless dancers' performances, authorize villages by local law to prohibit such performances.

2. The local law is not vague and indefinite. It clearly sets forth the act to be prohibited, to whom it applies, the circumstances under which it applies, and the penalty.

3. Plaintiffs have failed to overcome the presumption that the statute is constitutional and have failed to show that the local law is arbitrary and capricious.

4. Moreover, plaintiffs have failed to show that they are immediately and irreparably harmed so as to warrant a preliminary injunction.  [***2] In waiting a year, plaintiffs have shown that they are not irreparably harmed.

 

1740 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Nattin Realty, Inc. v. Ludewig


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Dutchess County


67 Misc. 2d 828; 324 N.Y.S.2d 668; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1280; 3 ERC (BNA) 1121



September 23, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner realty company filed a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to set aside respondent municipality's denial of its application for a permit to erect multiple-family residential dwellings on its property.


OVERVIEW: The realty company previously applied to the municipality and was granted approval to erect multiple-family residential dwellings. Subsequently, but prior to issuing the building permit, the municipality passed an amendment to the zoning ordinance that placed the subject property in a one-family residential district. The realty company then filed a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, seeking to set aside the determination of the municipality denying its application for a building permit. The realty company additionally prayed for a declaration that the amendment to the zoning ordinance, which changed the zoning classification of the realty company's property, was unconstitutional as applied. The court denied the realty company's petition, holding that the zoning change enacted by the municipality was, under the circumstances, a valid exercise of its legislative power. The court held that the municipality presented sufficient evidence to warrant the rezoning of the realty company's property because it was prompted to do so by ecological considerations based not upon whim or fancy, but upon scientific findings.


OUTCOME: The court denied the realty company's petition to set aside the determination of the municipality that denied its application for a permit to construct multiple-family residential dwellings on its property.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- town's comprehensive ecological needs for central public systems of water supply and sewage disposal justify rezoning of tract from multiple-family dwellings zone to one-family dwellings zone.

A town zoning ordinance amendment, rezoning a tract of land from a multiple-family dwellings zone to a one-family dwellings zone, was validly and constitutionally justified by the town's comprehensive ecological needs for central public systems of water supply and sewage disposal. Nowadays such wide, long-range, ecological considerations are matters of public health, safety and welfare, and they outweigh a landowner's prospects of profit. Though the town's Planning Board had already approved this landowner's application to erect multiple dwellings on this tract, the Town Board properly held public hearings and enacted this rezoning. Accordingly, the landowner's article 78 proceeding is dismissed.

Friedland v. Diamond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term held in Ulster County for Sullivan County


67 Misc. 2d 642; 324 N.Y.S.2d 578; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1297



September 15, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The plaintiff's motion for summary judgment is denied. The defendants' cross motion to dismiss the complaint ( CPLR 3211) and for summary judgment ( CPLR 3212) is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, owner of a 30-acre tract of land in defendant village, applied for a permit to allow a tent camp on his property in the village, and after it was denied brought an action for declaratory judgment seeking to have a village ordinance prohibiting campsites declared invalid. The owner moved for summary judgment, and the village cross-moved both to dismiss the complaint and for summary judgment on undisputed facts.


OVERVIEW: The owner proposed a tent camping site, and alleged that a village ordinance which prohibited that use, Village of Wurtsboro, N.Y., Revised Trailer and Trailer Park Ordinance (July 28, 1970), was invalid. The court noted that even if proposed sanitary facilities, roadways, and access routes were constructed, such a tented community would have offended aesthetic sensibilities. Since the proposed campsite would house only transients, the village would have been forced to greatly enlarge its police and fire protection, to say nothing of its disposal facilities. Clearly the ordinance under attack, albeit prohibitory in nature, was not an irrational or arbitrary exercise of police power. Rather, it was a reasonable exercise of the municipal police power essential to the safety, health, peace, and good order of the village. The restriction imposed on the use of owner's property did not constitute a taking of his property in violation of the Due Process Clause of the United States and New York Constitutions. The owner was restrained in the use of his acreage not because the public was taking it for its use but because the proposed use was noxious and contrary to the public good.


OUTCOME: The owner's motion for summary judgment was denied. The village's cross motions to dismiss the complaint and for summary judgment were granted.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- tents -- village may prohibit any tent sites for transient public.

A village ordinance prohibiting any "campsite which is to be used as temporary residences for the transient public" is valid and constitutional. The statutory power to regulate "tourist camps or similar establishments" (Village Law, § 89, subd. 69) does not exclude the police power to prohibit tent sites.

 

1742 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Willmott


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Village of Ocean Beach, Suffolk County


67 Misc. 2d 709; 324 N.Y.S.2d 616; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1301



September 11, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to withdraw his plea of not guilty to loitering, a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(3). Defendant requested to file a demurrer alleging that his words were protected as free speech under U.S. Const. amend. I. Defendant requested a dismissal of the information on the basis that N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(3) was void for vagueness and for lack of due process in violation of U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The court treated the motion as one for dismissal of the information because the relevant law did not sanction a demurrer to an information. The court determined that N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(3) sufficiently informed a person of the criminal implications of loitering and pointed out the prohibited act. The court found that loitering itself was not prohibited, but rather loitering in a public place to engage, or soliciting another person to engage, in deviate sexual intercourse or other sexual behavior of a deviate nature was proscribed. The court concluded that the language of § 240.35(3) sufficiently informed a person of common understanding of what was forbidden. Thus, § 240.35(3) was not unconstitutional for vagueness and a lack of due process. The court decided that the free speech protections of U.S. Const. amend. I did not apply to lewd and obscene words. The court found that the words that defendant used comprised the essence of soliciting. The court ruled that the offense was completed when the solicitation occurred, and no consummation of the object of the solicitation was required. The court found that the proscribed solicitation enjoyed no constitutional protection.


OUTCOME: The court issued an order that denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- loitering -- Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 3), prohibiting loitering in public place for purpose of soliciting deviate sexual intercourse, is constitutional.

Subdivision 3 of section 240.35 of the Penal Law, which provides, that it is a violation when a person "Loiters or remains in a public place for the purpose of * * * soliciting another person to engage, in deviate sexual intercourse", is valid and constitutional. An information alleging the words whereby defendant solicited such intercourse is sufficient. Motion for dismissal is denied.

 

1743 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Marr


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Town of Colonie, Albany County


67 Misc. 2d 113; 324 N.Y.S.2d 608; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1302



September 10, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a 20-year-old man, was charged with disorderly conduct, resisting arrest, and third degree assault. Defendant's counsel made a timely written challenge to the panel of trial jurors pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 363. The ground for the challenge was that an examination of the jurors indicated, prima facie, that there was a systematic and deliberate exclusion of persons under the age 32 years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the selection process of the panel of trial jurors violated equal protection because persons under the age of 32 were systematically excluded from the jury venire. The testimony of the assistant jury commissioner indicted that the jurors were selected from voter enrollment books and from city and town directories. People listed in the directory as "students", however, were not selected as jurors because the jury commissioner determined that it would have been a hardship for them to serve. Defendant also offered statistical evidence that in all probability the list was not randomly selected. The court determined that young adults constituted a cognizable group for purposes of defendant's prima facie case and that defendant had introduced sufficient evidence for a prima facie case that the jury selection process violated equal protection. The court sustained defendant's challenge to the jury venire, holding that there was a systematic and intentional exclusion of young adults from the jury panel and that defendant was, therefore, deprived of a trial before a fair cross section of the community.


OUTCOME: The court sustained defendant's challenge to the jury panel and discharged the jury.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- challenge to jury panel -- systematic exclusion of students from jury panel adjudged unconstitutional by trial court; jury discharged.

After conducting a trial of defendant's challenge to the panel of petit jurors (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 361-368), the Justice Court of the Town of Colonie in Albany County determined that the County Commissioner of Jurors has been intentionally and systematically excluding all students, and that this exclusion has actually resulted in a panel which excludes most persons under the age of 30, and that such exclusion was unconstitutional. Accordingly, the court discharged the jury, insofar as the trial of the information against this defendant was concerned.

 

1744 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Weil v. Arthur Murray, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


67 Misc. 2d 417; 324 N.Y.S.2d 381; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1324



August 31, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff student brought suit against defendants, dance school and dance club, to recover the compensation she paid on contracts for dance lessons she entered into with a franchise holder of the dance school, claiming that the contracts were void under N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 394-b and that N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 394-c provided her a cause of action against defendants. The dance club defaulted.


OVERVIEW: The student paid the full amount due under the contracts on account. She brought suit to obtain a refund of the amounts she paid and the court granted judgment in her favor. It rejected the dance school's contention that N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 394-b was only intended to insulate students from liability for unpaid dance instructions and found that the student had the right to recover for paid, but unused dance instructions. In addition, to avoid the interdiction of § 394-b, the dance school was required to affirmatively show how much it earned under the contracts; however, the dance school failed to provide such proof. The court also found that the dance school could not be permitted to hide behind the franchisor-franchisee relationship when the form of the contract was the same as the previous contracts, with the exception of the name of the dance school being crossed out and the franchise holder's name inserted, and the studio still bore the name of the dance school. Finally, the dance school exercised more than the amount of control exerted in the normal franchisor-franchisee relationship, such that the franchise holder became the alter ego of the dance school.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment against the defendants in the student's suit to recover all amounts paid in advance for dance lessons.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Contracts -- dancing lessons -- section 394-b of General Business Law protects public from unconscionable contracts for instruction; three contracts signed by plaintiff with dance studio are contrary to public policy -- section 394-b may be used to support cause of action to recover value of unused lessons -- to avoid impact of section, defendant was required to show how much it had earned under contracts which it failed to do -- Arthur Murray, Inc. as franchisor exercised such control over franchisee as to render it, as well as franchisee, liable for unused value of dance lessons contracted for by plaintiff.

1. Plaintiff entered into three contracts with a corporation franchised by first defendant to teach dancing, one for $ 1,890, the second for $ 4,125 and the third for $ 1,442.70 and seeks to recover for instruction not received under section 394-b of the General Business Law. The section is designed to protect the public from unconscionable or exploitative contracts whereby the naive are made to pay exorbitant sums for lessons. The three contracts signed by plaintiff are contrary to public policy.

2. The section not only may be used when the signer is sued by the instructor [***2] but when the signer is a plaintiff to recover the "unused" instruction.

3. In order to avoid the section, defendant was required affirmatively to plead and adduce evidence to show how much it had earned under the contracts. The record is devoid of such proof.

4. In view of the fact that the name Arthur Murray School of Dancing was shown in the lobby of the franchisee's premises, and defendant franchisor exercised control over the operation, requiring weekly reports of all contracts with new students, gross intake and royalties computed thereon, coupled with the franchisee's transferability from one location to another, the franchisees became the alter egos of defendant Arthur Murray, Inc. It should not be permitted to hide behind the franchisor-franchisee relationship. Plaintiff is entitled to judgment against both defendants.

 

1745 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Alvaro v. Baar


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


67 Misc. 2d 489; 324 N.Y.S.2d 588; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1329



August 30, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is granted to the extent that the amendment to subdivision 1 of section 30 of the Election Law (L. 1971, ch. 389) is declared to be unconstitutional and the appointments made to the Board of Elections of the County of Albany on August 5, 1971, pursuant to such amended law, are null and void.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer filed a petition for an order prohibiting and restraining respondent county treasurer from expending funds for the payment of the salaries of two county election commissioners on the ground that those two commissioners were appointed pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 30(1), as amended by 1971 N.Y. Laws ch. 389, effective June 7, 1971, which allegedly violated N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 1(b).


OVERVIEW: The two commissioners were recommended for their posts pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 31. More than 30 days after the recommendation was made, the majority members of the county legislature having failed to appoint the recommended individuals, the minority members appointed the commissioners, and they took their oaths of office. The taxpayer filed suit, contending that N.Y. Elec. Law § 30(1), as amended by 1971 N.Y. Laws ch. 389, effective June 7, 1971, which authorized the minority of the county legislature to appoint the commissioners if the majority failed to do so within 30 days, was unconstitutional. The court agreed. N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 1(b) required that all local government officers be elected by the people of the local government, or some division thereof. N.Y. Elec. Law § 30(1), as amended by 1971 N.Y. Laws ch. 389, effective June 7, 1971 allowed an officer to be chosen by a minority of the county legislature, and therefore was not representative of the people of that county.


OUTCOME: The court granted the taxpayer's petition by declaring the amended law unconstitutional and by nullifying and voiding the two commissioners' appointments.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- appointment of members of Board of Elections -- amendment of Election Law ( § 30, subd. 1) which enables Republican members of Albany County Legislature to appoint Republican members of Board of Elections is unconstitutional.

An amendment (L. 1971, ch. 389) of subdivision 1 of section 30 of the Election Law which provides for a four-member Board of Elections for Albany County, two of whom shall be Republicans, whereby the designation of the two Republicans, recommended for appointment to the entire County Legislature in accordance with section 31, if not followed by the County Legislature, shall be made by the Republican members of the County Legislature, is in conflict with article IX (§ 1, subd. [b]) of the Constitution which provides for appointment of such members by "such officers of the local government as may be provided by law". The appointing [***2] officers are the entire County Legislature and appointment by a minority of members is unconstitutional.

 

1746 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Landing Estates, Inc. v. Jones


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


67 Misc. 2d 354; 324 N.Y.S.2d 255; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1385



August 4, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, owner of 35 acres of land, brought an action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for an order for the issuance of a certificate pursuant to N.Y. Town Law § 276(4) and seeking to annul and set aside the determination of respondent planning board. The planning board filed a motion to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: The landowner received a conditional approval from the planning board for a subdivision of 15 plots subject to the approval of the county health department. The approval provided for 13 plots instead of 15 plots. Thereafter the planning board held a public hearing where it approved the subdivision as to eight, not 13 plots. The eight permitted lots were about double the required square footage. The court held that the planning board was allowed to require more area per building plot than the standard, present certain additional facts. The decision of the board recited that the evidence at the hearing established a risk of pollution in a nearby pond because of the density sought by the proposed subdivision. The court held that in exercising its authority to grant or deny approval of a subdivision, the planning board could consider the impact of the proposed development on adjacent territory and property within its jurisdiction. N.Y. Town Law § 261 provided that the town board was empowered by ordinance to regulate and restrict the percentage of lot that could be occupied for the purpose of promoting the health, safety, or the general welfare of the community.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- approval of subdivision -- petitioner cannot contest decision of planning board on basis that it was not filed within 45 days of hearing -- petitioner received approval of plot on condition that 15-foot strip be dedicated but approval limited to 13 plots; after public hearing, approval was confined to 8 plots; planning board may require more area -- decision to approve only 8 plots was properly based on finding that danger of pollution of nearby pond existed.

1. Respondent town planning board granted conditional approval of petitioner's plot covering 35 acres by requiring the dedication of a 15-foot strip, the effect of which was to confine development to 7 1/2 acres and leaving the remaining 27 acres undeveloped, and approving 13 plots. After a public hearing, the planning board approved the subdivision but only for 8, not 13, plots. Petitioner cannot contest this decision on the basis of late filing (more than 45 days after the hearing) since there is nothing in the law concerning the filing of a decision within 45 days of the hearing.

2. The planning board may require more area per building plot than set by the town board. The planning board [***2] may increase the town board's minimal requirements.

3. The planning board properly based petitioner's giving up five plots by finding that the evidence established a serious risk of pollution of a nearby pond sustaining sea and wild life, even though the pond is beyond the confines of the subdivision. Such decision was neither arbitrary nor capricious.

Finkenberg Furniture Corp. v. Vasquez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, New York County


67 Misc. 2d 154; 324 N.Y.S.2d 840; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1393



August 2, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff furniture store sought to obtain a provisional remedy prior to the commencement of an action in replevin. It presented to the court a proposed order directing a marshal to seize, without prior notice, bedroom furniture in the possession of defendant purchasers.


OVERVIEW: The furniture store, in support of the proposed order, submitted a complaint and affidavit from which it appeared that the purchasers obtained, on an installment basis, a five-piece bedroom set, mattress and spring, and a TV set from the furniture store under two separate contracts. Payments had not been made, and the furniture store claimed that it was entitled to possession under the installment contracts. The court granted the application. The court held that the application of the furniture store clearly presented the possibility of the type of hardship envisioned by the exemption statute, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5205(a)(5), since the purchasers were apparently moving back to Puerto Rico and since the supporting papers, which demonstrated arithmetical miscalculations in favor of the furniture store and to the detriment of the purchasers, indicated that the purchasers may have been unable to comprehend their rights. Since, the furniture store had shown, prima facie, that it had not been paid, it was incumbent upon the purchasers to show why they had not paid.


OUTCOME: The court granted the furniture store's application for an order directing a marshal to seize, without prior notice, bedroom furniture in the possession of the purchasers.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Replevin -- ex parte seizure before commencing action -- furniture and TV set should not be ordered seized ex parte; court directs seller to serve purchasers with notice of hearing ( CPLR art. 71, as amd. by L. 1971, ch. 1051).

The unpaid installment seller of a TV set and a set of bedroom furniture has presented to this court, ex parte, an unserved summons and complaint, and an affidavit of nonpayment, and a proposed undertaking and order to authorize a City Marshal to enter the purchasers' home and to seize the furniture and TV set. The court denies such ex parte seizure ( CPLR art. 71, as amd. by L. 1971, ch. 1051; U.S. Const., 4th and 14th Amdts.). The court directs the proposed plaintiff to serve the proposed defendants with copies of this application and with notice of a hearing thereon; and meantime the court restrains the defendants from removing or selling or pledging the furniture and TV set ( CPLR 7102, subd. [d], par. 2).

 

1748 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cender


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


67 Misc. 2d 129; 323 N.Y.S.2d 807; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1404



July 29, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, petitioners' application is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, persons potentially subject to indictment by a grand jury, filed an application for a hearing on the manner of the selection of a New York county's grand juries and for the dismissal of any indictment found against them on the ground that the grand jury was selected in a manner violative of petitioners' constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were granted a hearing on the manner in which the grand jury was selected, and they were given the right to voir dire the panel. The grand jury was sworn in, subject to the condition that any indictment found against petitioners would be controlled by the results of the court's proceeding. Petitioners claimed that the selection procedures were discriminatory. The court conducted seven days of hearings and concluded that the manner of selection was not discriminatory. An individual was not entitled to a grand jury whose membership was approximately equal to the percentage of definable subgroups in a county population but was entitled only to a grand jury selected without deliberate, intentional, or systematic exclusion of any subgroup. The county's procedure for procuring the annual grand jury list was to use the names of the persons on the prior list, who had not served on a grand jury, augmented by qualified volunteers and invitees to make up the requisite 1,500 names. Petitioners failed to show that the selection process involved any deliberate, intentional, or systematic exclusion.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners' application.


HEADNOTES

Grand Jury -- selection -- Grand Jury which found indictment against defendants was not selected in violation of defendants' constitutional rights -- statutory scheme for selection of Grand Jury adheres to Federal constitutional standards -- selection process used does not involve deliberate exclusion of anyone -- insufficient evidence presented to find that practice of selecting women results in unconstitutional discrimination on Grand Jury list.

1. The Grand Jury which found an indictment against defendants was not selected in a manner that violated defendants' constitutional rights.

2. The manner in which a Grand Jury is selected is provided by a statutory scheme which adheres to Federal constitutional standards.

3. An individual is not entitled to a Grand Jury whose membership is approximately equal to the percentage of definable subgroups in the county population but is entitled only to a jury selected without deliberate, intentional or systematic exclusion of any such subgroup. Upon all of the evidence presented and upon a reading of the trial minutes and a review of all other evidence, defendants have failed to [***2] establish that the selection process as actually used involves any deliberate, intentional or systematic exclusion.

4. Under the circumstances there is insufficient evidence presented for the court to find that the practice relative to the subpoenaing of women for petit jury service results in an unconstitutional discriminatory factor with regard to the Grand Jury list.

 

1749 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Zeigler v. Riley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


67 Misc. 2d 82; 323 N.Y.S.2d 589; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1445



July 19, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an injunction action, plaintiff inmate sought a preliminary injunction restraining defendant superintendent of the penitentiary from cutting the inmate's long hair. The superintendent filed a motion to dismiss the action.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was sentenced to serve weekends at the penitentiary for a period of one year. While serving the sentence, the inmate filed an injunction action seeking to restrain the superintendent from cutting his long hair. The court denied the inmate's motion for a preliminary injunction and dismissed his complaint, with leave to amend. It held that the sanitary regulation relied upon by the superintendent was neither unreasonable nor arbitrary. Any constitutional right the inmate might have had to the aspects of his appearance was one which the State could limit during his lawful incarceration after conviction. The cutting of the inmate's long hair did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment proscribed by the Eighth Amendment to the United States Constitution. The fact that women prisoners were permitted to wear their hair long was far from the proof necessary to establish the purposeful and invidious discrimination proscribed by the Fourteenth Amendment. Moreover, the inmate's proof did not show a pattern of discrimination consciously practiced. It merely indicated some nonenforcement as to a few other than the inmate.


OUTCOME: The court denied the inmate's motion for a preliminary injunction to restrain the superintendent from cutting his long hair. The court also dismissed the inmate's complaint, with leave to amend.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Prisons and prisoners -- long hair -- prisoner's complaint seeking injunction against cutting of his long hair is dismissed, with leave to amend so as to set forth pattern of invidious discrimination.

Prisoners are reasonably required, for reasons of cleanliness and sanitation, to "be clean shaven; long hair should not be permitted" ( 7 NYCRR 5100.13 [e]). Accordingly, in this action by a person who has been sentenced to serve weekends in a county jail for one year, his application for a preliminary injunction restraining the cutting of his long hair and his beard is denied, and his complaint for a permanent injunction is dismissed; however, if so advised, he may amend his complaint within 20 days so as to set forth a pattern of invidious discrimination; and, meantime, the restraining provisions contained in his order to show cause shall continue for those 20 days.

 

1750 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Davis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Extraordinary Special and Trial Term, Ontario County


67 Misc. 2d 14; 322 N.Y.S.2d 927; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1456



July 9, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendants' motion is denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants moved to dismiss the indictments returned against them by a grand jury of the court on the grounds that the statutes, by virtue of which the court was created, violated separation of powers requirements and defendants' due process rights under the Constitution and statutory law of the United States.


OVERVIEW: A grand jury under the court's control returned indictments against defendants. Defendants sought dismissal of the indictments, claiming the statutes that authorized the governor to create the special term court and appoint its justice violated the separation of powers doctrine and due process protections under the federal Constitution. The court disagreed, holding that: (1) N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 27 and N.Y. Jud. Law § 149 granted authority to the governor to appoint an extraordinary term of the supreme court and to designate the time, place, and justice to hold the term; (2) this did not constitute an encroachment by the executive branch upon the functions of the judicial branch; (3) this did not raise the possibility that defendants' rights to fair trials before an impartial judiciary would be denied; (4) defendants were not deprived of due process merely because there existed a possibility that someday, sometime, a governor might appoint a judge to do the governor's bidding in violation of law; and (5) whether the legislative, executive, and judicial powers of a state were to be kept together or separate was a determination to be made by the state.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss the indictments returned against them by a grand jury.


HEADNOTES

Grand jury -- Extraordinary Term of Supreme Court -- Governor may appoint Extraordinary Term of Supreme Court and may appoint Justice to draw Grand Jury there; this does not contravene constitutional separation of executive and judicial powers; indictments handed up by such Grand Jury will not be dismissed.

The Governor may, when in his opinion the public interest so requires, appoint an Extraordinary Term of the Supreme Court and may designate a Justice to preside there and to draw a Grand Jury there, and may direct the Attorney-General to conduct proceedings there before such Grand Jury (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 27; Judiciary Law, § 149; Executive Law, § 63, subd. 2). These provisions do not violate constitutional principles of separation of executive and judicial powers or the due process of law required by the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution. Indictments handed up by such Grand Jury will not be dismissed.

 

1751 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Scher


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


67 Misc. 2d 345; 324 N.Y.S.2d 112; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1460



July 9, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the appeal is denied and the pro forma order of March 3, 1971 is confirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The executor appealed from the pro forma order of the court fixing the estate tax of the decedent. The order had been based upon a petition of the executor wherein an election was made to use values of five securities as of a date different from the date of death but the appraiser to whom the court referred the application to fix the estate tax valued the five securities as of date of death at a lesser sum, as requested by the executor.


OVERVIEW: In valuing the securities, the appraiser followed N.Y. Tax Law § 954(b), which directed that the New York gross estate shall be valued as of the time of the decedent's death except that if a Federal estate tax return be filed and the alternate valuation under the Internal Revenue Code was elected the New York gross estate shall be valued on the same basis. In this case, there was no Federal estate tax return required to be filed. In denying the appeal, the court held that the fact that there was a disparity of impact between one estate and another was not a sound argument against the valuation method prescribed in N.Y. Tax Law § 954. The distinction was neither arbitrary nor capricious. It invaded no constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied the appeal and the pro forma order was confirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- estate tax -- in estate under $ 60,000 gross, valuation as of date of death was proper; Tax Law ( § 954) allowing alternative valuation as to estate over $ 60,000 gross is not unconstitutional.

 Section 954 of the Tax Law which allows valuation of estate assets, in the alternative, as other than the date of death only in estates over $ 60,000 gross, and fixes date-of-death valuation in estates under $ 60,000, does not unconstitutionally discriminate against estates under $ 60,000 gross. The distinction is neither arbitrary nor capricious. Accordingly, the pro forma order fixing the estate tax on a valuation as of the date of death as to this estate under $ 60,000 gross is affirmed.

 

1752 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


66 Misc. 2d 907; 323 N.Y.S.2d 267; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1481



June 30, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petitions are dismissed with respect to both the allegations of trespass and of sexual abuse against both respondents.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent juveniles were charged as delinquents for acts that would constitute the adult crimes of criminal trespass and sexual abuse.


OVERVIEW: The two 15-year-old juveniles were found in the upstairs hall of a public school. The juveniles were not pupils in the school and had not been given permission to enter or remain in the building. The juveniles alleged that "trespass in a building" did not apply to public schools. The court held that trespass applied to school buildings, but that the juveniles had not committed criminal trespass. Petitioner teacher did not prove that there were anti-loitering signs posted in the school or that the juveniles were warned to stay out of or leave the school. There was no evidence that the juveniles knew that they were in the school illegally. The allegation of sexual abuse against the teacher was not proven beyond a reasonable doubt as to either juvenile.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petitions against both juveniles for acts that would constitute the adult crimes of criminal trespass and sexual abuse.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- trespass -- term "knowingly" in Penal Law definition of criminal trespass in third degree requires proof that individual have actual knowledge of illegality of his conduct -- fact that loitering in school is specifically made violation under another provision of Penal Law does not render provision as to trespass in "a building" inapplicable to public school -- school is not "open to the public" and order to leave it is not component of crime of trespass therein -- clause as to fencing or enclosure in trespass provision modifies only "real property", not "building".

1. The term "knowingly" in the provision of the Penal Law ( § 140.10) that a "person is guilty of criminal trespass in the third degree when he knowingly enters or remains unlawfully in a building or upon real property which is fenced or otherwise enclosed in a manner designed to exclude intruders" requires proof that the individual have actual knowledge of the illegality of his conduct. Since this element has not been proven beyond a reasonable doubt in juvenile delinquency proceedings [***2] charging two 15-year-old boys with acts which, if committed by an adult, would constitute the misdemeanor of trespass in the third degree, based upon their being in the upstairs hall of a public school although they were not pupils in the school and had not been given permission to enter or remain in it, the petitions are dismissed.

2. The fact that loitering in a school is specifically made a violation under another provision of the Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 5) does not render the provision as to trespass in "a building" inapplicable to a public school.

3. A school is not "open to the public" (Penal Law, § 140.00, subd. 5) and, accordingly, an order to leave a school building is not a component of the crime of trespass therein.

4. The clause as to fencing or enclosure in the trespass provision modifies only "real property", not "building".
