Hardy v. Commissioner of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


66 Misc. 2d 984; 322 N.Y.S.2d 918; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1520



June 21, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens brought a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to obtain judgment setting aside and annulling certain determinations of respondent commissioner of education. The commissioner brought a motion to dismiss the petition. Interested school board members brought a motion to dismiss for nonjoinder of necessary parties.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that the underlying actions concerned the call to remove certain school board members because they refused to sign a waiver of immunity before a grand jury investigating the board's activities. The commissioner determined that N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, which provided for the removal of public officers, could not have been employed as a basis for the removal in the underlying actions because a court determined that the subject state constitutional provision was violative of the U.S. Constitution. Therefore, the commissioner refused to remove the board members. The court found that the commissioner had the authority to interpret judicial decisions, which concerned themselves with matters of urgent educational concern. Accordingly, the court granted the commissioner's motion to dismiss. The court's resolution of the matter rendered it unnecessary to decide the motion to dismiss on the grounds of nonjoinder of necessary parties.


OUTCOME: The court granted the commissioner's motion to dismiss the petition.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- removal of members of board of education -- determinations of Commissioner of Education that members of board could not be removed because of decisions of Supreme Court of United States are not arbitrary and are beyond review by court.

Determinations of the Commissioner of Education that members of a board of education could not be removed for refusing to sign waivers of immunity before a grand jury investigating the board's activities, wherein he concluded that section 6 of article I of the Constitution could not be employed as a basis for removal in view of decisions of the Supreme Court of the United States holding such provision unconstitutional, are neither improper nor unusual nor arbitrary, and are beyond review by the court under section 310 of the Education Law. They do not involve error of law. The petition in this proceeding to review the determinations is dismissed.

 

1754 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Corning v. Donohue


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


67 Misc. 2d 276; 324 N.Y.S.2d 674; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1539



June 15, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application for a writ of prohibition pursuant to article 78 of the CPLR is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mayor sought a writ of prohibition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 as to enforcement of N.Y. Educ. Law § 2553, subd. 9.


OVERVIEW: The mayor sought a writ of prohibition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 as to the enforcement of N.Y. Educ. Law § 2553, subd. 9. The mayor contended it did not apply to Albany and that the provision was unconstitutional. The court found that § 2553, subd. 9 did not apply to Albany because the population of Albany fell below a set number. The court determined that the provision in and of itself was in violation of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 17 because it created a classification without a reasonable genesis for political advantage. The court found that the classification had no relation to the provisions of the law. The court granted the mayor's petition for a writ of prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court granted the mayor's petition for a writ of prohibition.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- appointive or elective Board of Education -- Special Term prohibited Board of Elections from accepting petitions purporting to nominate candidates for election as members of Board of Education of City of Albany (L. 1970, chs. 462, 492; Education Law, arts. 51, 52; N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17).

The final figures of the Federal census of 1970 showed, as contrasted with the Federal census of 1960, that the population of the City of Albany had fallen from above 125,000 to below 125,000 (cf. Education Law, arts. 51, 52). On May 8, 1970, chapter 492 of the Laws of 1970 became law, and it provided that, if a city which had a population of over 125,000 by the 1960 Federal census was found by the 1970 census to have less than 125,000, then its city school district should continue to be governed by article 52 of the Education Law until July 1, 1971. On May 5, 1970, chapter 462 of the Laws of 1970 had become law, and it provided that, if the people of the City of Albany so approved by a referendum at the general election of November, 1970, the Board of Education of the city should [***2] become elective, not appointive, and that the first such election of the members of said Board of Education should take place at the general election of November, 1971. The referendum did so approve. However, in this proceeding instituted by the Mayor of the City of Albany pursuant to CPLR article 78, Special Term of the Supreme Court held that chapters 462 and 492 of the Laws of 1970, read together, meant that chapter 462 became inapplicable to the City of Albany on and after July 1, 1971, and furthermore, that said chapter 462 of the Laws of 1970 insofar as it added subdivision 9 to section 2553 of the Education Law contravened section 17 of article III of the State Constitution; and accordingly Special Term enjoined and prohibited the Board of Elections from accepting petitions purporting to nominate candidates for election to the Board of Education of the City of Albany.

 

1755 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ferro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


66 Misc. 2d 752; 322 N.Y.S.2d 354; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1542



June 15, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Thirteen defendants filed motions to dismiss the informations that charged them in separate multi-count informations with contempt of a grand jury. Four of the defendants were tried and found guilty, and two such defendants also filed motions for judgment of acquittal.


OVERVIEW: The grand jury was empanelled to inquire into the commission of crimes to which grants of immunity from prosecution were applicable under N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 619-c. Even after the grand jury voted to confer immunity and the judge directed responses to the interrogations, defendants refused to answer under U.S. Const. amend. V. The court found that the statute accorded defendants transactional immunity and tendered at least as much as the state was indebted to pay for expropriation of their privilege of silence. The court also found that the questions posed in the trials that had been completed were relevant to the scope of the grand jury inquiry and defendants' refusal to respond was sufficient to support the charges of contempt. The court denied all of the motions to dismiss the informations, but denied without prejudice to renewal as to the untried defendants upon the possible grounds that the prosecutor may have verbally described an insufficient immunity and that the questions posed may not have been relevant to the inquiry. Based on the evidence, the court denied the motions for judgment of acquittal.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motions to dismiss the informations, but those filed by the untried defendants were dismissed without prejudice to renewal. The court also dismissed the motions for judgment of acquittal.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Witnesses -- transactional immunity -- section 619-c of Code of Criminal Procedure grants transactional and use immunity and is constitutional -- defendants are charged with contempt of Grand Jury; informations are sufficient -- as to four defendants who were tried, prosecutor's characterization of scope of immunity granted transactional and use immunity; said defendants' motions to dismiss informations are denied -- privilege against self incrimination is not privilege in civil actions -- defendants were obliged to testify upon grant of immunity -- defendants were not excused from testifying because they may be targets or because their attorneys were not permitted in Grand Jury room -- questions posed to defendants who were tried were relevant -- objections of tried defendants that they may not be convicted of more than one count of contempt will be resolved upon sentence.

1. The immunity conferred by section 619-c of the Code of Criminal Procedure is defined in subdivision 2 to constitute both transactional immunity from any prosecution, penalty or forfeiture on account of the transaction concerning which testimony or evidence was compelled, plus use immunity in any criminal [***2] proceeding against the witness, and such statute is constitutional.

2. Defendants, who were called before a Grand Jury impanelled to inquire into the commission of bribery of labor union representatives, violations of gambling and usury law and conspiracy to commit these crimes, have been charged with contempt of the Grand Jury in that each was interrogated and declined to answer. The informations are sufficient.

3. As to four defendants who were tried, the minutes show that the prosecutor's characterizations of the scope of the immunity constituted descriptions of transactional immunity or transactional coupled with use immunity. Their motions to dismiss the informations are denied, and as to the other defendants, their motions are denied without prejudice to renewal upon a record including the material extracts of the minutes.

4. The privilege against self incrimination has never constituted a privilege in civil actions for damages, including punitive damages.

5. Since the Fifth Amendment privilege was supplanted, defendants were obliged to testify as interrogated.

6. They were not excused from testifying because they may be targets or because their attorneys were not permitted [***3] in the Grand Jury room during the interrogation.

7. The questions posed to all defendants who were tried were relevant to the scope of the Grand Jury inquiry, and defendants' refusal to testify was sufficient to support the charge of contempt alleged in the information.

8. The objections of the defendants who have been tried that they may be prosecuted for or convicted of no more than one count of contempt will be resolved upon sentence by provision that no such defendant shall receive more punishment for conviction of plural counts than he would receive for conviction of only one count and also including a provision, in the event of imposition of terms of imprisonment, for concurrency thereof.

 

1756 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


St. Pierre v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Saratoga County


66 Misc. 2d 634; 321 N.Y.S.2d 995; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1551; 78 L.R.R.M. 2037



June 10, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teachers brought this article 78 proceeding seeking a judgment declaring that a penalty deduction from their salaries was void. The deduction was made by respondents, school authorities, pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210(1), (2), which prohibits strikes by public employees. The school authorities filed a cross-motion to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: The school authorities sent letters to the teachers stating that they had engaged in an illegal strike. Subsequently, school authorities withheld a portion of the teachers' pay for the days of the alleged strike. The teachers submitted affidavits denying that they had been on strike and stating that they had reported for work on the days indicated and had performed their normal duties. The school authorities found the affidavits insufficient to raise an issue of fact. The teachers brought this proceeding to reverse that decision and obtain the withheld pay. The court first noted that the school authorities final decision came in the letters sent to the teachers. Measured from this time, the withholding of pay was timely under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210(2)(g), which required it to occur within 90 days of the date of the determination. However, the court held that the withholding was improper because the teachers were denied a hearing on the matter. The affidavits submitted were a denial of any strike and were sufficient to raise an issue of fact that required a hearing. The court ordered that the withheld wages be paid to the teachers.


OUTCOME: The court held that the teachers' salaries were improperly withheld and directed the school authorities to pay the teachers the withheld amounts.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- strikes -- section 210 of Civil Service Law imposing penalties for strikes by civil servants should be strictly construed -- Superintendent of Schools made determination on October 29, 1970 that petitioner teachers engaged in illegal strike on September 8, 1970; deduction of penalties from salaries on January 8, 1971 was within 30- to 90-day period after determination -- affidavits of teachers raised issue of fact that no strike occurred; since hearing officer was not appointed to make determination on issue after hearing, petitioners are entitled to refund.

1. The Superintendent of Schools of respondent school district sent by certified mail on October 29, 1970 to each petitioner notice of a determination that each petitioner had engaged in an illegal strike on September 8, 1970 in violation of section 210 of the Civil Service Law. On November 17, 1970 all petitioners submitted affidavits that they had not engaged in an illegal strike on September 8, 1970. On December 9, 1970, the Superintendent sent a letter setting forth his findings that the affidavits submitted did not raise a question of fact. On the regular payday of January 8, 1971 respondents deducted [***2] from the earned salary of petitioners a sum equal to 2/200 of their annual salary for each day they were on strike. Subdivision 2 (par. [g]) of section 210 of the Civil Service Law which provides that payroll deductions are to be made not earlier than 30 nor later than 90 days following the date of the determination, should be strictly construed.

2. The determination required by the statute was not made until the Superintendent of Schools gave the notice of October 29, 1970. The financial penalties withheld on January 8, 1971 were within the statutory period.

3. Since the affidavits submitted by petitioners on November 17, 1970 were sufficient to raise an issue of fact, a hearing officer should have been appointed to determine if petitioners in fact violated the statute. (Civil Service Law, § 210, subd. 2, par. [h].) The statute does not require a hearing in every case in order to be constitutional. Petitioners are entitled to a refund of the moneys withheld.

 

1757 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Yevoli v. Cristenfeld


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 156; 322 N.Y.S.2d 22; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1550



June 10, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters brought an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondent county to prohibit the enforcement of an amendment of the democratic county committee.


OVERVIEW: The voters brought their N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to prevent the enforcement of an amendment of the democratic county committee. The court determined that the art. 78 proceeding action was not the proper form for the prosecution of the voters' action because the county committee was an unincorporated association. The court deemed the action one for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief. The court determined that the county committee had no authority to conduct a convention for the purpose of designating candidates by party for town office and that requiring candidates to execute a document as to party affiliation and to accept or reject their candidacy on that basis was improper. The court issued an injunction against the enforcement of the amendment and granted summary judgment in favor of the voters.


OUTCOME: The court enjoined enforcement of the by-law amendment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- inhibition against cross endorsements -- mandamus not maintainable against unincorporated association such as political county committee; matter treated as action -- amendment of county committee's by-laws to inhibit designees from accepting cross endorsement is void as violative of Election Law ( § 137, subd. 4) -- moreover, county committee cannot conduct convention for nominating candidates; in Nassau County it may be done only in primary election.

1. A mandamus-type proceeding under CPLR article 78 may not be maintained against a Democratic County Committee, an unincorporated association. This proceeding to declare a by-law of such committee unconstitutional will be treated as a plenary action for a declaratory judgment. Since no triable issues of fact are raised by the affidavits, the application will be treated as a motion for summary judgment in such action.

2. An amendment of the by-laws, to inhibit the seeking of "cross endorsements", which provides that designations or nominations shall be made only to persons who agree in writing to refuse to accept the designation or nomination of any other political party or independent party or body is void. It [***2] violates subdivision 4 of section 137 of the Election Law which permits the designation or nomination of a person as candidate who is not enrolled as a member of such party, and section 248 which prescribes the form of ballot to be used when the same person has been nominated for the same office by more than one party.

3. Moreover the papers reveal that respondents intend to conduct a county convention for the purpose of designating candidates for town office. Under the Election Law ( § 131, subd. 5) in Nassau County, the designation of candidates may be effected only at a primary election from Candidates who have filed valid designating petitions.
