Main Private Car Service, Inc. v. Mayor of Yonkers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


71 Misc. 2d 417; 336 N.Y.S.2d 207; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1563



June 7, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For all of the foregoing reasons, the court denies plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction and grants defendants' cross motion for summary judgment determining that the ordinance is valid and enforceable. The temporary restraining order contained in the order to show cause is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation moved for a preliminary injunction and defendant city cross-moved for summary judgment dismissing the complaint. The action was for a declaratory judgment determining that City of Yonkers, N.Y., General Ordinance No. 38-1970 was void and for an injunction restraining the city from enforcing the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance sought to regulate the private rental car industry within the city and required that such cars be inspected and licensed. The corporation alleged that the ordinance violated the Federal and State Constitutions, that the State pre-empted the field, that the ordinance constituted an unreasonable exercise of the city's police power, and that the State never delegated to the city the power to adopt the ordinance. The city submitted affidavits in support of the cross motion to dismiss, from which it appeared that during the past several years numerous complaints were received by the local police concerning the run-down condition of private rental cars. The court ruled that the corporation failed to offer proof sufficient to overcome the presumption of constitutionality. In addition, there was no merit to its contention that the State did not delegate power to the city to enact the ordinance under attack. The ordinance fell within the broad permissible area provided for under N.Y. Gen. City Law § 20(13). The ordinance was not unreasonable, arbitrary, or capricious. The court was satisfied that the ordinance did not prohibit anything expressly permitted under State law.


OUTCOME: The court denied the corporation's motion for a preliminary injunction and granted the city's cross motion for summary judgment determining that the ordinance was valid and enforceable. The temporary restraining order contained in the order to show cause was vacated.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- ordinance regulating private rental car industry -- city ordinance reasonably and validly requires inspection, maintenance and licensing of automobiles for rent.

An ordinance of the City of Yonkers, requiring that automobiles for rent within the city be inspected, equipped, maintained and licensed, is reasonable, valid and constitutional.

 

1759 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


State by Lefkowitz v. Parker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


67 Misc. 2d 36; 323 N.Y.S.2d 473; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1568



June 6, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the application by the Attorney-General is granted. The cross motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney general initiated a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Exec. Law § 63, subd. 12 to compel respondent landlords engaged in the rental of housing accommodations to place security deposits of their tenants in an interest-bearing bank account. The landlords filed a motion to dismiss the petition for legal insufficiency.


OVERVIEW: The attorney general contended that the amendment to N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law § 7-103 (1970) applied to security moneys received not only subsequent but prior to the effective date of the amendment. The landlords contended that the amendment did not apply to security deposits received prior to the effective date; that the retrospective application of the amendment would have represented an unconstitutional impairment of the landlords' contractual rights; and that the attorney general had no standing to bring a proceeding under N.Y. Exec. Law § 63, subd. 12. The court stated the contemplation and intention of the sponsors of the amendment in preparing it for the legislature was to place all security deposits in interest-bearing accounts. The court held that the amendment to N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law § 7-103 required the landlords to deposit rent security funds in an interest-bearing bank account, regardless of when acquired, beginning September 1, 1970. The court also held that the legislature enacted the amendment to § 7-103, the violation of which gave the attorney general standing because of illegal conduct. He was authorized to enjoin illegal acts in the carrying on of a business.


OUTCOME: The court granted the attorney general's application that the amendment to the statute applied to security moneys received not only subsequent but prior to the effective date of the amendment. The court denied the landlords' motion to dismiss the petition for legal insufficiency.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- security deposits -- prospective and retrospective statute -- landlords of houses of six or more apartments who, on September 1, 1970, were holding tenants' security deposits in noninterest-bearing accounts, must at once transfer such trust funds into bank accounts which pay interest at prevailing rate -- General Obligations Law ( § 7-103) applies both prospectively and retrospectively to moneys which landlords received from tenants both before and after September 1, 1970.

The General Obligations Law ( § 7-103, as amd. eff. Sept. 1, 1970) provides that landlords of houses of six or more apartments, with whom tenants deposit moneys as security for performance of leases or tenancies, must deposit such moneys in trust accounts in banks at the prevailing rates of interest. This amendment is not only prospective; it is also retrospective. The Attorney-General is granted judgment (Executive Law, § 63, subd. 12) directing landlords who, on September 1, 1970 were holding tenants' security deposits amounting to approximately $ 919,000 in noninterest-bearing accounts,  [***2] to transfer those trust moneys into bank accounts which pay interest at the prevailing rates.

 

1760 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York v. Wyman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


66 Misc. 2d 402; 321 N.Y.S.2d 695; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1617



May 18, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, City of New York, public hospitals corporation, and indigent pregnant women, filed an action against respondents, Commissioner and Department of Social Services, seeking a judgment annulling a determination limiting Medicaid-reimbursable abortions to those "medically indicated," directing the Commissioner to continue to reimburse abortions under N.Y. Penal Law § 125.05, and declaring N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 365-a(2) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner issued a determination limiting Medicaid reimbursements for abortions to those that were "medically indicated." The City of New York and other petitioners filed an action under New York City Charter § 394(c), seeking to annul the Commissioner's determination, a judgment directing the Commissioner to continue to provide reimbursement for abortions authorized pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 125.05, and a declaration that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 365-a was unconstitutional. The court granted the petition, holding the directive unconstitutional and directing the Commissioner to continue to provide reimbursement for abortions. The court held that the directive violated the right of indigent women to equal protection because the right to terminate a pregnancy was meaningless as a practical matter unless the necessary services were Medicaid-reimbursable. The directive tended to defeat the objective of N.Y. Penal Law § 125.05 which was to ameliorate the conditions prevailing when abortion was permitted only when deemed necessary to save the life of the mother. However, the court held that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 365-a was constitutional because it was neither vague nor indefinite.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition, holding the Commissioner's directive unconstitutional and directing the Commissioner to continue to provide reimbursement for abortions authorized pursuant to the New York Penal Law. The court upheld the constitutionality of the challenged provision of the New York Social Services Law as not vague or indefinite.


HEADNOTES

Social services -- elective abortions -- State Commissioner of Social Services' directive, prohibiting medicaid reimbursement for elective abortions, is unconstitutional -- city and city's health and hospitals corporation have standing to sue under CPLR article 78.

1. A letter or directive by the State Commissioner of Social Services dated April 8, 1971 purported to constitute merely a "Clarification of Policies Relating to Reimbursement for Abortions", and to restrict any medicaid reimbursement for abortions to those abortions which are "medically indicated", and to eliminate "elective induced abortions" from the physicians' allowable fee schedules (18 NYCRR 533.6). The Commissioner's "Clarification" is not justified by the Rules of the State Board of Social Welfare (18 NYCRR 90.1). In actual effect, the Commissioner's "Clarification" would amend the State and Federal statutes governing abortions and medicaid (Penal Law, § 125.05, subd. 3; Social Services Law, §§ 363, 363-a, 365-a, subd. 2; Social Security Law [U. S. Code, tit. 42, ch. 7, subchap. XIX, §§ 1396, 1396a et seq.]). The Commissioner's directive is unconstitutional (U. S. Const.,  [***2] 9th and 14th Amdts.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 11) and is annulled.

2. Since medicaid reimbursements are made to city hospitals and others, and are not paid directly to medicaid-eligible individuals, the city and its health and hospitals corporation have standing to maintain this proceeding under CPLR article 78.

 

1761 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kim v. Orangetown


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rockland County


66 Misc. 2d 364; 321 N.Y.S.2d 724; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1620



May 17, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, since plaintiff has established a clear legal right to the relief sought and has demonstrated that he will be subjected to irreparable injury if a restraining order is not obtained, the motion for a preliminary injunction is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In his action for declaratory judgment, plaintiff doctor filed a motion for preliminary injunction, seeking to restrain defendant municipality and its officers, agents, and employees from enforcing a municipal regulation regarding abortion facilities.


OVERVIEW: The municipal ordinance prohibited justifiable abortional acts in any building, including a doctor's office, except a hospital, a facility administered by a hospital, or a facility having a hospital agreement. Violators were subject to imprisonment and a fine. The doctor performed many justifiable abortional acts in his office, which was located in the municipality. The court granted the doctor's motion for preliminary injunction, finding that the ordinance was void on its face as being inconsistent with N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 309(1)(f), limiting the penalty that could be imposed for violation of a public health regulation. Furthermore, the court found even after severing the penal portion of the ordinance, the ordinance was unconstitutional in that it was in conflict with the state penal law, which specifically exempted from the criminal category all justifiable abortional acts, whether performed in a hospital or a doctor's office. By enacting legislation covering all abortional acts, the state had clearly preempted the area of legislation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the doctor's motion for a preliminary injunction against the municipality which would restrain the municipality from enforcing its regulation of abortion facilities.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- local law limiting abortions -- physician is granted preliminary injunction against enforcement of town local law purporting to prohibit abortions except in hospitals; subject matter is pre-empted by State laws.

A town ordinance or local law, prohibiting physicians, under penalty of 15 days' imprisonment and $ 1,000 fine, from performing lawfully justifiable abortions except in a hospital, is an unconstitutional interference with State legislation governing the same subject (Penal Law, § 125.05, subd. 3; Education Law, § 6514, subd. 2, par. [e]; Public Health Law, § 308, subd. [d], and § 309, subd. 1, par. [f]) and is not authorized by the home rule article of the State Constitution (art. IX, §§ 2, 3) or by the Municipal Home Rule Law ( § 10, subd. 1, par. a, subpar. [12]) or by the Town Law ( § 130, subd. 15; § 136). Plaintiff physician, upon filing a $ 1,000 bond, is granted a preliminary [***2] injunction against enforcement of the local law.

 

1762 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Doherty v. Meisser


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 550; 321 N.Y.S.2d 32; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1632



May 12, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a committeeman interim-appointed democratic committeeman, brought an action against defendant Commissioners of the Board of Elections of Nassau County (commissioner) after the commissioner declared that the committeeman would not be able to vote in the democratic primary because he was under 21 years old.


OVERVIEW: The court held that voters between the ages of 18 and 21 otherwise qualified to vote were permitted to vote for the positions of county committeeman in the democratic primary election. The court held that it had jurisdiction because the committeeman brought his claim before the court, and the commissioner requested dismissal and sought substantive relief from the court. The court held that county committeemen were not federal, state or local officers, or public officers. The court reviewed the four basic pertinences of membership on the county committee to elections, which included selection of candidates, support of candidates, central power of the county committee, and direct powers of the county committee over nomination of candidates. The court found numerous benefits of membership. The court found that the county committeemen were pivotal in the electoral process and to federal, state, and local elections. The court held that the democratic county committee was a substantial component of federal elections; thus, the federal voting statute, which set the voting age at 18, was controlling on voting for those county committeemen who manifestly pertain to federal elections.


OUTCOME: The court held that voters between the ages of 18 and 21 otherwise qualified to vote were permitted to vote for the positions of county committeeman in the democratic primary election.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- 18-year-old vote -- primary election for county committeemen -- in action for judgment declaring that voters over 18 but under 21 are entitled to vote in Democratic primary for election of county committeemen, New York Secretary of State is not necessary party -- United States Voting Rights Act Extension of 1970 has been held to apply to Federal elections by Oregon v. Mitchell (400 U.S. 112); even though construction of Federal statute is involved, State courts have jurisdiction of action -- functions and duties of county committeemen pertain to all elections, Federal, State and local; primary to elect county committeemen therefore pertains to Federal election; Federal voting statute applies and 18-year-olds must be accorded right to vote in Democratic primary election for county committeemen.

1. In an action for a declaration that infant plaintiff, over 18 but under 21, is entitled to vote in the Democratic primary in Nassau County wherein enrolled voters will vote for county committeemen, and certain State and local public offices, the New York Secretary of State is not a necessary party. He has no function with regard to the primary election of Democratic county [***2] committeemen.

2. The United States Voting Rights Act Extension of 1970 has been held to apply to Federal elections by Oregon v. Mitchell (400 U.S 112). Even though construction of the Federal statute and cases may be involved plaintiff has the right to commence the action in the courts of New York. Unless otherwise prescribed by Federal statute, a plaintiff has the right to sue in either State or Federal court where appropriate bases of jurisdiction exist. There is no statute forbidding the commencement of the action in the State court. Moreover, defendants elected this forum by moving to dismiss and to seek substantive relief from this court.

3. The functions and duties of county committeemen pertain to all elections, Federal, State and local. They select candidates, support the chosen candidates, carry the central power of the county committee, and have direct power to nominate candidates in certain instances, whether for Federal, State or local office. A primary in which county committeemen are elected necessarily pertains to Federal, State and local elections. Since the Democratic county committee is a substantial component of Federal elections, the Federal voting [***3] statute controls on voting for these county committeemen. Accordingly, 18-year-olds must be accorded, under the United States Voting Rights Act Extension of 1970 and Oregon v. Mitchell (supra), the right to vote for county committeemen.

 

1763 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Robin v. Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 482; 321 N.Y.S.2d 20; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1641



May 10, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the Village's applications for such relief are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff doctor filed an action against defendant village to have a village ordinance, Village of Hempstead, New York, Ordinances ch. 7, art. VI, declared invalid. The village filed a cross-motion to enjoin violation of the ordinance and also filed a third party action against third party defendant corporation to enjoin violation of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance limited the performance of "justifiable abortional acts" as defined in N.Y. Penal Law 125.05(3) to a hospital duly licensed and accredited. The corporation owned the premises at which the doctor performed abortions. The court held that the ordinance was not in conflict with the Penal Law because the ordinance only regulated the place for performing abortions, it did not prohibit them. The village also had the power to regulate where justifiable abortional acts were performed. The court noted that under the N.Y. Village Law § 89(59) the village's police powers extended to protecting public health and safety. The government had a valid interest in protecting human life after conception, as well as regulating the disposal of human remains, including fetuses. Thus, the doctor failed to meet his burden of proving that the ordinance was unconstitutional or to show that he was entitled to a temporary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court, in the doctor's action challenging the village's ordinance regulating the permitted location for performing abortions, denied the doctor's request to enjoin enforcement of the ordinance. Because the court found the ordinance constitutional, it was unnecessary to grant the village's request to enjoin the doctor and the corporation from violating the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- local law limiting abortions -- village ordinance may require that any abortions be performed in hospital.

A village ordinance, prohibiting any "justifiable abortional acts" ( Penal Law, § 125.05, subd. 3) to be performed within the village unless performed in a State-licensed hospital, is presumptively valid and constitutional (cf. Village Law, § 89, subd. 59). Accordingly, plaintiff physician is denied a preliminary injunction against the village, and the village may enforce its ordinance against an unlicensed establishment.

 

1764 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York State Environmental Facilities Corp. v. Young


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


66 Misc. 2d 299; 320 N.Y.S.2d 821; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1646



May 5, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff environmental corporation filed a motion for a preliminary injunction seeking to enjoin defendant real property owners from taking any steps to prevent the environmental corporation or its representatives from taking soil borings upon the owners' properties for the purpose of furnishing a municipality with a study and plan for a solid waste disposal facility.


OVERVIEW: The environmental corporation attempted to make soil borings upon the owners' property to carry out a contract with a municipality to furnish a study for a solid waste disposal facility. After the real property owners refused to allow the environmental corporation to enter the property, the environmental corporation filed a motion for a preliminary injunction to restrain the property owners from taking any steps to prevent it from taking soil borings upon their property. The court granted the motion, finding that pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1284(1), the environmental corporation had a right to enter upon the property since it was assisting a municipality in the planning of a solid waste disposal facility pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1283(1). The court determined that since the entry was temporary and the infliction of damage would be incidental, there was no constitutional violation. The court noted that a tenant occupying the property was not a necessary party to the action. The court concluded that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2512 did not exempt the environmental corporation from filing an undertaking, since it was a public benefit corporation pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Corp. Law § 3(4).


OUTCOME: The court granted the environmental corporation's motion for a preliminary injunction to restrain the property owner from taking any steps to prevent the environmental corporation or its representatives from taking soil borings on the owner's property pending the final determination of the action.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- New York State Environmental Facilities Corporation -- New York State Environmental Facilities Corporation is validly and constitutionally empowered to enter on private lands in order to make soil borings -- it is public benefit corporation and hence it must file bond for preliminary injunction ( CPLR 2512; 6312, subd. [b]; General Corporation Law, § 3, subd. 4).

1. New York State Environmental Facilities Corporation is validly and constitutionally empowered by the Public Authorities Law (art. 5, tit. 12, § 1284, subd. 10) "To enter upon such lands, waters, or premises as in the judgment of the corporation may be necessary, convenient or desirable for the purpose of making surveys, soundings, borings and examinations to accomplish any purpose authorized by this title, the corporation being liable for actual damage done". Defendant property owners are preliminarily enjoined and restrained from interfering.

2. However, plaintiff corporation must first file a bond ( CPLR 6312, subd. [b]) and the court fixes the amount at $ 10,000. Plaintiff corporation is a public benefit corporation (General Corporation Law, § 3, subd. 4) and [***2] hence CPLR 2512 does not exempt it from filing a bond.

 

1765 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Parrino v. Lindsay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


66 Misc. 2d 342; 321 N.Y.S.2d 311; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1649



May 4, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Under all the undisputed factual circumstances, including the strong presumption of the constitutionality of legislative enactments ( Lincoln Bldg. Assoc. v. Barr, 1 N Y 2d 413; Defiance Milk Prods. Co. v. Du Mond, 309 N. Y. 537) as well as the recent cases in this field cited herein, the plaintiffs' motion is denied and the cross motion is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlords filed a motion for summary judgment in their action seeking a declaration that the Senior Citizen Rent Increase Exemption Law (law), New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § Y51-5.0, subd. n (1970), was unconstitutional and an order enjoining the law's enforcement. Defendants, the City of New York, N.Y. and its mayor, filed a cross-motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint as a matter of law.


OVERVIEW: The City passed a law that exempted a certain class of low-income elderly persons from the rent increases permitted for rent-controlled apartments. The law was of short term and was due to expire when a new, computerized system of rent control became effective. The court disagreed with the landlords' contention that the law was unconstitutional, and dismissed the complaint. The court noted that the City's council, in passing the law, considered the critical state of housing for low-income elderly residents and the limited means available to these people to effectively increase their income to meet higher rentals for housing. The court observed that a legislature's creation of a class was not, in itself, unconstitutional provided that the distinction was based upon reasonable grounds. In this regard, the court found that the undisputed facts before the City's council concerning the plight faced by elderly persons were clearly a sufficient and reasonable basis for the action it took in passing the law. Under the circumstances, including the strong presumption of constitutionality for legislative enactments, the court concluded that dismissal of the landlords' action was warranted.


OUTCOME: The landlords' motion was denied and the cross-motion of the City and its mayor to dismiss the complaint was granted.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- rent control -- temporary exemption of low-income persons over age 62 from some allowable rent increases, constitutional.

The so-called Senior Citizen Rent Increase Exemption Law (Local Laws, 1970, No. 31 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-5.0, subd. n), temporarily exempting a limited class of low-income persons over age 62 from some of the rent increases which are permitted for rent-controlled apartments by Local Law No. 31 of 1970 (New York City Rent and Rehabilitation Law, as amd.; Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-5.0), is a valid and constitutional exercise of the legislative police power.

 

1766 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Schatz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Bronx County


66 Misc. 2d 381; 321 N.Y.S.2d 186; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1650



May 4, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to suppress will be granted as to the alleged admission by defendant in his response to the officer's question, "Where is it?", and otherwise denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action filed by the People of the State of New York, defendant filed a motion to suppress an admission and drug evidence resulting from the search and seizure of mailed packages. Defendant was charged with criminal possession of contraband.


OVERVIEW: Packages arrived from Afghanistan addressed to an individual in care of defendant. Based on past experience, customs agents opened them and found hashish. A sting was later executed at the packages' address. Defendant told officers that he threw the packages out the window. Defendant was arrested. The court granted defendant's motion as to his admission. Reasonable and probable cause requirements were not applicable to mail from foreign countries. 19 C.F.R. § 9.5 and postal regulations allowed customs agents to open sealed packages. Because the packages were not designated as first class mail they were not restricted under 39 U.S.C.S. § 4251 as mail "closed against postal inspection," and 39 U.S.C.S. § 4057, which required a search warrant, was inapplicable. A search warrant for the address was not required because the officers only intended to arrest defendant and only entered because he would not open the door. Defendant lacked dominion over the packages once he threw them away. However, his admission that he threw them away was suppressible because the officer announced his authority and defendant did not waive his Miranda rights.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress with regard to his admission.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- foreign parcels may be opened by United States customs inspectors; contraband narcotic drugs were thus found -- uniformed postal employee delivered said parcels to addressee, who opened apartment door, whereupon police entered apartment and announced themselves; defendant said he had thrown parcels out of window; police found said abandoned parcels in yard below window and properly seized them -- since defendant was already in custody, his answering words are suppressed; but his motion to suppress parcels from evidence is denied.

1. Two parcels from Afghanistan, Asia, arrived in New York by foreign mail, addressed to an individual, "c/o" the defendant. United States customs inspectors, aware of smuggled drugs from Afghanistan, properly caused these foreign parcels to be opened. Such opening of foreign parcels, particularly those foreign parcels which are not "first class mail", is authorized by Federal statutes and by United States Customs Regulations and [***2] United States Postal Regulations and by numerous Federal court decisions. In these parcels were contraband narcotic drugs called Hashish. Some three weeks thereafter, a uniformed post office employee, carrying the two parcels, knocked on the door of the apartment of the addressees, while a policeman and a United States customs agent remained downstairs. A woman answered the knock and said that the named individual no longer lived there and that the defendant was not home; and thereupon she was given the parcels and she closed the door. Again the postal employee knocked and asked for the signed receipt; and thereupon the receipt, signed with defendant's name, was handed to the postal employee through the peep hole of the door. A few minutes later, the postal employee, with an officer at each side of him, knocked again. This time the defendant himself opened the door, and thereupon the officers entered, and the police officer announced that he was a police officer and said, "The parcel delivered to you contained narcotics, where is it?" The defendant answered "I don't know what was in the parcel * * * I threw it out the window", and pointed to the window. The police found the [***3] abandoned parcels in the yard below the window and properly seized them.

2. Defendant was already in custody and, since the officers did not warn him of his constitutional rights, his answering words will be suppressed. But defendant's motion to suppress from evidence the parcels, which he abandoned and which the officers seized, is denied. The purpose of the knocking on the door and the entry was to make an arrest for which there was adequate "notice" (Code Crim. Pro., § 178) and reasonable and probable cause. No warrant was necessary.

 

1767 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Finger Lakes Racing Ass'n v. New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Comm'n


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Albany County


65 Misc. 2d 946; 320 N.Y.S.2d 801; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1656



May 3, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendants' and defendants-intervenors' motions for summary judgment are granted, and the motion of plaintiffs for the same relief is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff licensee race track operators commenced actions against defendants, the New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Commission, New York State Racing Commission, New York State Harness Racing Commission, a city, and a county, seeking a declaratory judgment that 1970 N.Y. Laws 143, 144 were unconstitutional and for an injunction restraining the implementation and enforcement of these statutes.


OVERVIEW: The licensee race track operators sought a declaratory judgment that the New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Law and the New York City Off-Track Betting Corporation Law were unconstitutional. The licensee race track operators argued that the conducting of off-track pari-mutuel betting would deprive the tracks of property without due process and without just compensation; that the statutes impaired their charters and franchises; and that the system of off-track betting was an unconstitutional and excessive exercise of the police power of the state. The court found that the statutes in question were constitutional. The court held that the passage of the off-track betting laws was a continuation of a valid exercise of police power of the state and a furtherance of the power and authority given to the legislature over pari-mutuel betting on horse races by N.Y. Const. art. I, § 9. The use of space and facilities at the tracks was within the scope of the object and purpose of the legislation and the statute provided for an adequate opportunity to be heard on the subject of compensation.


OUTCOME: The court found in favor of defendants and found the off-track betting laws to be constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Horse racing -- off-track pari-mutuel betting -- New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Law (L. 1970, ch. 143) and New York City Off-Track Betting Corporation Law (L. 1970, ch. 144) are constitutional, and were validly enacted on Governor's message of immediate necessity.

1. The New York State Constitution (art. I, § 9) legalizes such "pari-mutuel betting on horse races as may be prescribed by the legislature and from which the state shall derive a reasonable revenue for the support of government". Pursuant to this provision and the State's police power, the Legislature has validly and constitutionally enacted the New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Law (L. 1970, ch. 143) and the New York City Off-Track Betting Corporation Law (L. 1970, ch. 144). The provisions (in L. 1970, ch. 143) for allocating part of the revenues to the participating municipalities are valid and constitutional. The provisions for compensation to the licensed pari-mutuel race tracks appear to be just; in any case, any issue as [***2] to the constitutionality of the compensation is premature at this time.

2. "No bill shall be passed * * * unless it shall have been * * * upon the desks of the members, in its final form, at least three calendar legislative days prior to its final passage, unless the governor * * * shall have certified * * * the facts which in his opinion necessitate an immediate vote thereon" (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 14). The courts cannot review the adequacy or the substantive merits of the Governor's message.

 

1768 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re B.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Kings County


66 Misc. 2d 279; 320 N.Y.S.2d 813; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1658



April 30, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a delinquency proceeding, respondent, a juvenile, was charged with possession of a loaded .22 caliber home made gun, containing one live round of ammunition, an act which, if done by an adult, would have constituted the crime of possession of a loaded weapon, a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05. The court rendered a judgment.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile was charged with possession of a loaded home made gun, an act which violated § N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05(4), (9) if committed by an adult. The court concluded that the gun was not a firearm because it was incapable of being discharging a bullet. There was no evidence that the juvenile had any experience with firearms and no evidence that he was capable of repairing the gun so as making it able to fire. There was no evidence to show that the juvenile intended to use the imitation pistol unlawfully, therefore the court dismissed as to possession of an imitation pistol, a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05(9). The court rejected the juvenile's contention that N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05(4) was unconstitutional as a violation of equal protection because it made possession of certain instruments a crime for a juvenile and not an adult. The court could not say that age was not a reasonable classification in the area of dangerous weapons and that a zip gun or live ammunition was not more dangerous in the possession of a juvenile than it was in the hands of an adult. The court adjudged the juvenile a delinquent because he did have in his possession a live round of ammunition.


OUTCOME: The court adjudged the juvenile to be a juvenile delinquent.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- juvenile delinquency -- boy who had homemade, inoperable pistol loaded with one live round of ammunition was adjudged juvenile delinquent (Penal Law, § 265.05, subd. 4; Family Ct. Act, § 712) -- his counsel's failure to object to allegation that he was under age 16 amounted to admission.

1. A boy had a homemade .22 calibre pistol loaded with a live round of ammunition. The pistol was incapable of firing, and there was no evidence that the boy knew how to make it operable or that he had any intent to use it (cf. Penal Law, § 265.05, subd. 9; § 265.15, subd. 4). Nevertheless, he is adjudged a juvenile delinquent (Family Ct. Act, § 712). The Penal Law (§ 265.05, subd. 4) validly and constitutionally provides that "Any person under the age of sixteen years who has in his possession * * * any instrument * * * in or upon which any loaded * * * cartridges may be used, or any loaded * * * cartridges or ammunition therefor, * * * shall be adjudged a juvenile delinquent." (Italics supplied.)

2. Although there was no evidence that the boy was under 16, his counsel's failure to raise any objection to the allegation of the petition that the boy was under 16 amounted [***2] to an admission of that fact.

 

1769 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. L.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Criminal Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 191; 320 N.Y.S.2d 456; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1670



April 27, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the court finds the statute constitutional and the motion to dismiss is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged as a youthful offender with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 240.36, which provided that a person was guilty of loitering when he loitered or remained in any place with one or more persons for the purpose of unlawfully using or possessing a dangerous drug. He moved to dismiss, claiming that "any place" meant any public place but the acts occurred on private property and that the law was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and five other boys were sitting at a table in the backyard of a private residence. They had instruments and were playing music, which disturbed the neighbors, who called police. When the police arrived, they found the boys talking and saw a pipe on the table, which contained a small amount of hashish. Defendant and the others were arrested and charged with a violation of § 240.36. In a case of first impression, the court rejected the argument that the statute was unconstitutional and held that if the crime of loitering was proven, it was no less a crime because it was committed on private property. The court construed the words to mean exactly what they said: any place. However, the gravamen of the crime was in the words "for the purpose of" using or possessing a dangerous drug. Here, the mere fact that a pipe was on the table was insufficient evidence on which to conclude beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant was loitering with others for the purpose of using or possessing a dangerous drug. A pipe on the table did not create a presumption of purpose attributable to all. The totality of the circumstances led to the conclusion that the parties gathered to make music.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss but found him not guilty.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering for purpose of using drug -- section 240.36 of Penal Law which renders person guilty of loitering if he loiters in "any place" with others "for purpose of" using dangerous drug is constitutional; "any place" refers to private as well as "public" place -- defendant was playing music with others in backyard of private home; police's finding of lit pipe containing hashish on table did not render defendant guilty since purpose was to play music.

1. Section 240.36 of the Penal Law, which provides that a person is guilty of loitering when he loiters in "any place" with one or more persons "for the purpose of" unlawfully using or possessing a dangerous drug is constitutional. The phrase "any place" does not mean a public place but includes private property.

2. Defendant, along with others, was in a backyard, playing music. Neighbors called the police who found a lit pipe with a spot of hashish in it on the table. The proof is not convincing that defendant gathered for the purpose of using a dangerous drug. Rather his purpose was to play music. That a pipe was [***2] on the table is insufficient evidence upon which to conclude beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant was loitering with others for the purpose of using a dangerous drug. A pipe on the table does not create a presumption of purpose attributable to all. Defendant is not guilty.

 

1770 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Strauss


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Criminal Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 268; 320 N.Y.S.2d 628; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1673



April 26, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, this court finds that the statute is constitutional and further determines that the defendant is guilty of having violated the same.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charge of loitering against him on the grounds that N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6) was violative of both the United States and New York State Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested after a policeman saw him running from the policeman's backyard and getting into a car. When stopped, defendant stated that he was in the residential area because he had to go to the bathroom. Defendant had a Florida driver's license and did not indicate why he was in New York. Defendant was charged with loitering under N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6). At the close of the State's case against him, defendant sought dismissal by claiming that the statute was violative of the United States and New York Constitutions. The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss, holding that the statute was constitutional and that defendant was guilty of violating it. Because the statute had been found to be constitutional as to public railroad stations, there was little question that it was constitutional when applied to the circumstances of a quiet, dark, strictly residential-type neighborhood where the unlawful conduct might certainly be a great deal more relevant.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the charge of loitering and found him guilty.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 6) is constitutional and defendant is found guilty of loitering.

The Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 6) validly and constitutionally provides that "A person is guilty of loitering when he: * * * 6. Loiters, remains or wanders in or about a place without apparent reason and under circumstances which justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime, and, upon inquiry by a peace officer, refuses to identify himself or fails to give a reasonably credible account of his conduct and purposes". Defendant, who had a Florida driver's license and who offered no explanation of his suspicious conduct at 3:30 a.m. in a residential area of Lynbrook, New York, is found guilty.

 

1771 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York Trap Rock Corp. v. Roslyn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 334; 320 N.Y.S.2d 584; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1675



April 26, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trucking companies brought an action challenging the validity of two sections of an ordinance enacted by defendant village. The trucking companies contended that the provisions were unconstitutional and beyond the authority delegated to the village to regulate vehicular traffic. The matter was submitted to the court upon a stipulation of what evidence would be adduced at trial.


OVERVIEW: One provision challenged by the truckers prohibited the spilling of certain materials from trucks onto the village's streets and sidewalks. The other section required that trucks be covered. The trucking companies owned approximately 250 trailer and dump trucks and regularly transported garbage and building materials through the village's streets. Regulation of the use of streets and highways was vested in the state and the village had only such authority as was delegated to it by the legislature. The legislature had specifically provided villages with the authority to regulate and prevent the deposit of ashes, garbage, rubbish, and filth upon the streets or public places. The legislature's enactment of an anti-littering statute did not pre-empt the field of anti-littering legislation. However, the village had no authority to regulate truck equipment. Equipment standards were set exclusively by the legislature because of the need for uniformity and to prevent an undue burden on intrastate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the section prohibiting the spilling of materials onto village streets was valid but that the section requiring that trucks be covered was not.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- anti-littering ordinances -- anti-littering ordinance is valid, but not ordinance requiring trucks which carry sand or gravel to use expensive close-fitting covers of metal or canvas.

1. A village ordinance validly provides that "No person being the owner, driver, helper * * * of any * * * vehicle used in the * * * transport of * * * gravel, sand, * * * or any material used for building purposes * * * shall allow any of the materials * * * to fall or blow from such vehicle upon any of the streets, highways, sidewalks or public places in the village" (cf. Village Law, § 89, subd. 44, and Vehicle and Traffic Law, §§ 1220, 1604, 1640).

2. But a further ordinance requiring any such vehicle while in the village to be covered with a closely fitting metal or canvas cover -- plaintiff corporation claims that a metal cover would cost $ 4,000 per truck and a canvas cover $ 500 per truck -- is unauthorized and invalid (cf. Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 375).

 

1772 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Taggart


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Suffolk County


66 Misc. 2d 344; 320 N.Y.S.2d 671; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1679



April 23, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the information is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an information that charged him with loitering in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the loitering statute on the ground of vagueness. The court found the statute constitutional, observing that it required proof of suspicious behavior and a failure to give an explanation concerning the reason for being present in a place. The court held that the statute had a reasonable connection with the prevention of crime and the preservation of public order. The court found that § 240.35(6) punished overt acts or antisocial behavior and not mere status or condition, in that the loitering was such as to justify suspicion of criminal activity.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- peace officer has valid and constitutional power to arrest and search person whom he justifiably suspects of criminal activity and who refuses to identify himself or to give reasonably credible account of his conduct (Penal Law, § 240.35, subd. 6).

The Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 6), read together with the Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 177, subd. 1; § 154-a), validity and constitutionally empowers a peace officer, without a warrant, to arrest, and thereupon to search, a person who "Loiters, remains or wanders in or about a place without apparent reason and under circumstances which justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime, and, upon inquiry by a peace officer, refuses to identify himself or fails to give a reasonably credible account of his conduct and purposes".

 

1773 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Yonkers Raceway, Inc. v. Yonkers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


66 Misc. 2d 589; 322 N.Y.S.2d 77; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1690



April 19, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer brought an action for a declaratory judgment and for a permanent injunction restraining defendant city from taking any actions to impose a retroactive admissions tax on parking revenues. The taxpayer moved for a preliminary injunction and to quash a subpoena served by the city comptroller, and the city cross-moved to dismiss the complaint and to compel compliance with the subpoena.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer paid an admissions tax for over 18 years, when the city claimed that parking revenues were taxable by the city by reason of 1954 State and local enactments and demanded that records for a 16-year period be preserved and made available. The taxpayer based its action upon the proposition that the attempt to tax parking revenues was illegal and unconstitutional as the State enabling statutes did not empower the city to exact such taxes. The court held that no useful purpose would be served, and certainly the taxpayer would be seriously prejudiced, by subjecting it to a lengthy and costly administrative proceeding before the city comptroller as a predicate to any court review, especially where the comptroller was seeking old records and the deposit of a large sum of money to be held by the city without payment of interest thereon as a condition to commencement of a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78. The court held that an amendment to 1952 N.Y. Laws ch. 148 clearly excluded taxation of parking revenues. There was no ambiguity present. The interpretation proposed by the city was illogical and outside the orbit of the taxing statute, and the city was not empowered to tax such receipts.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment, declaring that the revenues derived by the taxpayer from parking were not taxable by the city and that there was no authority in the comptroller or city to levy or seek to levy a local tax on that form of income. The subpoena duces tecum was quashed, and the city's motion to dismiss was denied.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- tax on "admissions" -- Raceway is granted summary judgment declaring that City of Yonkers has no power to impose admissions tax on parking revenues earned by Raceway -- Raceway is not required to exhaust its administrative remedies under city's law imposing tax as condition precedent to commencement of action -- 1954 amendment to enabling act granted city power to tax "admissions" paid for admission to clubhouse or other special facilities within race meeting grounds; parking is outside grounds; enabling act excludes taxation of parking revenues.

1. In an action for a declaratory judgment that defendant City of Yonkers has no power to impose an admissions tax on parking revenues earned by plaintiff Raceway, plaintiff is entitled to summary judgment declaring that revenues derived from parking are not taxable by said city.

2. By an enabling act of 1952, defendant city was authorized to enact a law taxing "general admissions" to harness horse race meetings held in such city. The enabling act was amended in 1954 so as to delete the term "general admissions" and to substitute the term "admissions which was defined to mean "the [***2] admission charge required to be paid * * * for admission * * * including any charge required to be paid * * * for admission to the clubhouse or other special facilities within the race meeting grounds or enclosure at which the harness race meeting is conducted". Parking for patrons was provided outside the enclosed grounds. In 1970, the city claimed that parking revenues were taxable and demanded that records for the 16-year period from 1954 to 1970 be produced on demand. Plaintiff is not required to exhaust its administrative remedy before prosecuting this action. It claims that the attempt to tax parking revenues is illegal and unconstitutional. No useful purpose would be served, and plaintiff would be prejudiced, by subjecting it to a costly administrative proceeding. An action for a declaratory judgment is proper despite the exclusivity section of the taxing statute.

3. The amendment to the enabling act in 1954 excludes taxation of parking revenues.

 

1774 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rosenblatt v. Common Sense Newspaper, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Dutchess County


66 Misc. 2d 59; 320 N.Y.S.2d 83; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1697



April 13, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner district attorney filed an action for injunctive relief to enjoin respondents, newspaper and individuals, from further distribution of obscene material to minors.


OVERVIEW: Respondents distributed a newspaper to students at a high school. Interspersed between articles on ecology, poverty, and other articles of a sociological nature was an article on homosexuality that included a photograph depicting two nude males in a homosexual embrace and a cartoon depicting a young child doing gymnastics on a man's erect penis drawn to exaggerated scale. The district attorney argued that the publication was obscene as to minors within the meaning of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 22-a and N.Y. Penal Law § 235. Respondents argued that § 235 was inapplicable to a civil proceeding. The court granted the injunction in favor of the district attorney, holding that the definitions in the statutes on the meaning of "obscene" and "harmful to minors" were applicable to the civil proceeding for injunction. The court further found that the material was obscene as to minors and was harmful to minors. Additionally, the court held that surrounding the obscene material with socially redeeming material could not have served to rescue the newspaper from the finding of obscenity.


OUTCOME: The court granted the injunction in favor of the district attorney.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- obscenity -- civil action for injunction -- after trial, court enjoins publishers of magazine which is harmful to minors under age 17 from distributing it to such minors (Code Crim. Pro., § 22-a; Penal Law, § 235.20, subds. 1, 6) -- defendants disclaim knowledge of whereabouts of undistributed copies, hence court will not further order them to surrender all copies to Sheriff.

1. After trial by the court, the publishers of a certain issue of a magazine are enjoined from distributing it to minors under the age of 17 and within the buildings or grounds of any public grade school or high school (Code Crim. Pro., § 22-a, subds. 1, 2; Penal Law, § 235.20, subds. 1, 6). The fact that section 22-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure mentions section 235.00 of the Penal Law does not mean that the court cannot also apply section 235.20 of the Penal Law. The court finds that the contents of the magazine -- particularly an article on homosexuality, and a photograph of two nude males in a homosexual embrace, and an exaggerated phallic cartoon -- are obscene and harmful to minors, even though the magazine also contains articles on ecology, poverty, and women's liberation.

 [***2] 2. Subdivision 3 of section 22-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure provides that the injunction "shall" also direct the defendants to surrender "any" of the obscene materials to the Sheriff for destruction. However, the defendants disclaim any knowledge of the whereabouts of the thousand or more copies of the magazine which remain undistributed, and therefore the court will not direct the defendants to surrender all undistributed copies to the Sheriff.

 

1775 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Massry


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Rensselaer County


66 Misc. 2d 222; 320 N.Y.S.2d 617; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1701



April 7, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to inspect the Grand Jury minutes is granted and the Supreme Court Grand Jury stenographer is directed to furnish a copy of the Grand Jury minutes respecting indictments C-3835(1970) and C-3836(1970) to the retained attorney of movant-defendant Mitchell.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed motions for an order granting the right to inspect grand jury minutes.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that secrecy of grand jury minutes was not absolute and determination of whether disclosure was to be permitted rested in the court's discretion. Before exercising its discretion the court was required to balance the competing interests of the one seeking disclosure and the People in opposition. None of the most frequently used reasons for secrecy had application. Defendants were not about to escape and the identities of all those who could have testified before the grand jury were publicly known. The grand jury that indicted defendants felt compelled to issue a report condemning the manner in which the public hearings that led to the indictments were conducted. It followed that the testimony relating to defendants was possibly of such minimal quality as not to meet the standards required by N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 249, 251. The equities of interest preponderated in favor of defendants.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motions to inspect the grand jury minutes.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- minutes of Grand Jury -- Supreme Court has concurrent jurisdiction with County Court to order inspection of minutes of Supreme Court Grand Jury -- constitutionality of law creating State Investigation Commission need not be determined -- upon stated considerations, each defendant is entitled to inspection of Grand Jury minutes.

1. With respect to motions to inspect Grand Jury minutes where the indictments were handed up by a Supreme Court rather than a County Court Grand Jury, the Supreme Court has concurrent jurisdiction with the County Court even though the indictments had been moved for trial to the County Court.

2. The ruling in Jenkins v. McKeithen (395 U.S. 411) does not compel the finding that the New York enactment creating the State Investigation Commission (SIC) and giving it its powers (L. 1958, ch. 989) is unconstitutional. The dissimilarities in the Louisiana statute in Jenkins  [***2] v. McKeithen and the New York statute, together with the consideration that would have to be given to the legislative right to create special bodies within constitutionally approved State Departments to inquire into those aspects of both private and public conduct, inimical to the interests of citizens, make such a holding hazardous. Moreover, a review of the constitutionality of the law creating SIC is unnecessary in deciding the motion for inspection of Grand Jury minutes.

3. Determination of whether there should be disclosure of the Grand Jury minutes rests in the Trial Judge's discretion. The first defendant has an interest in protecting his reputation as a businessman in the community. The reasons for secrecy of Grand Jury minutes are not applicable. Defendant is not about to escape and the identity of all those who could have testified before the Grand Jury with respect to tax assessment reductions is publicly known. Moreover, the Grand Jury that indicted defendant issued a report condemning the manner in which SIC conducted its hearings and attacking the quality of the testimony. The testimony relating to defendant may have been of such minimal quality as not to [***3] meet the standards required by sections 249 and 251 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. Considering the above with the fact that movant was continuously represented by counsel at both the private and public hearings and that fact was known to the District Attorney who, nevertheless, subpoenaed defendant and had him sign a waiver of immunity without consulting his known attorney, it becomes clear that the equities of interest clearly preponderate in favor of defendant. The motion to inspect the Grand Jury minutes is granted.

4. The second defendant is entitled to disclosure of the Grand Jury minutes in the interests of justice.

 

1776 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Richmond County


66 Misc. 2d 683; 322 N.Y.S.2d 170; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1713



April 5, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a factfinding hearing was held where it was determined that one of respondent parents' children was neglected, the court addressed whether there was sufficient proof of child abuse and neglect with regard to all three children.


OVERVIEW: The issue of abuse and neglect by parents was raised after a previous disposition in the neglect case. Parents' attorney objected to the introduction into evidence of the previous child abuse case, reduced to neglect, as severely prejudicial to his clients and a severe violation of due process and their constitutional rights. The court rejected the parents' constitutional challenge holding that the preponderance of evidence rule was sufficient to satisfy due process under the Fam. Ct. Act § 1046(d). Further, there was no violation of parents' rights of privilege against self incrimination. They were not required to take the stand and testify in child abuse or neglect proceedings because the constraint upon them to give testimony arose from the force of circumstances and not from any form of compulsion forbidden by the Constitution. The court concluded that there was ample proof in the record that both parents were ignorant of proper child care and supervision, and serious injuries had occurred to the child as a direct result of their acts of omission and commission. That evidence was sufficient to remove the other two children from the home as well.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the findings from the hearing and held that the evidence established that the parents abused and neglected their children.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and child -- abused children -- record in prior child abuse case involving same infant is admissible on subsequent child abuse case -- child abuse case is governed by preponderance of evidence rule -- presumption in statute that injuries to child not ordinarily found except by reason of acts of parents does not violate parents' privilege against self incrimination -- in child abuse case trial by jury is not part of parents' right of constitutional due process -- child abuse found as to girl -- neglect found as to boy -- court finds neglect as to newly born infant to prevent injuries to her.

1. The record in a prior child abuse case, involving the same child, the charges having been reduced to neglect, is admissible in a second child abuse case. Due process of law requires careful scrutiny of the medical history of the child to determine if a pattern of child abuse or neglect is involved in the two instances.

2. A child abuse proceeding under article 10 of the Family Court Act is governed, not by proof beyond a reasonable doubt, but by the preponderance of evidence rule. Article 10 does not involve a crime or criminal proceeding, even though a criminal court has, or [***2] may be exercising jurisdiction over the facts alleged in the petition. Due process is not violated by application of the preponderance of evidence rule. Section 1046 (subd. [b], par. [i]) of the Family Court Act specifically provides that in a fact-finding hearing, any determination that the child is an abused or neglected child must be based on a proponderance of evidence.

3. Section 1046 (subd. [a], par. [ii]) of the Family Court Act provides that proof of injuries sustained by a child of such a nature as would ordinarily not be sustained except by reason of acts or omissions of the parent shall be prima facie evidence of child abuse or neglect on the part of the parent. Such presumption does not violate the privilege against self incrimination. The parents are not required to take the stand. Upon proof of injuries to a child as described in the statute, petitioner is deemed to have established a prima facie case and the burden of going forward shifts to the parents. Because the parents do not wish to assume the burden does not make the statutory presumption unconstitutional.

4. Even though the charges against respondents can form the basis of a felony prosecution,  [***3] they are not entitled to trial by jury in the Family Court as a matter of constitutional due process.

5. The court finds that there is ample proof of child abuse as to an older girl.

6. As to a boy, the court finds that he is a neglected child.

7. As to a newly born female infant, the court finds neglect in the exercise of discretion to avoid an imminent risk to the child's life or health.

 

1777 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Kleinerman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Kings County


66 Misc. 2d 563; 319 N.Y.S.2d 898; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1722



March 31, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A widow filed a notice of election under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts § 5-1.1(b) (1966) (the statute) directed against Totten trust accounts set up by her husband, prior to his death, for his two daughters from an earlier marriage.


OVERVIEW: Prior to the statute at issue, the husband opened Totten trust accounts for the benefit of his two daughters from an earlier marriage. After the statute took effect, the husband transferred the accounts to another bank, without making any changes to the amounts involved or the beneficiaries. After the husband's death, his widow filed a notice of election directed against the account and alleged that under the statute, the accounts had been become testamentary substitutes when they were transferred to the second bank and as a result, she was entitled to a shares of the accounts. The court found that the commission, which enacted the statute, went beyond constitutional and statutory limitations when it provided that a testator who executed a will on or before the statute's effective date was free thereafter to effect inter vivos transactions long into the future while that will remained unrevoked. The court held that the decedent did not convert an exempt inter vivos transaction into a testamentary substitute when he transferred the Totten trust accounts in issue without change. The court held that the accounts retained their character as exempt inter vivos transactions.


OUTCOME: The court held that decedent did not convert an exempt inter vivos transaction into a "testamentary substitute" by transferring the Totten trust accounts at issue without change from one bank to another, after the statute took effect.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Descent and distribution -- election to take intestate share -- "exempt" Totten trust savings acounts created by decedent prior to August 31, 1966 did not become "testamentary substitutes" subject to widow's right of election ( EPTL 5-1.1, subd. [b]) by decedent's transfer after August 31, 1966 of such accounts to another bank paying higher interest where form and amount of account remained same -- interest on exempt savings account paid after August 31, 1966 is not testamentary substitute -- additional moneys deposited after August 31, 1966 in such accounts and interest earned thereon are testamentary substitutes.

1. EPTL 5-1.1 (subd. [b]) grants to a surviving spouse a right of election against "testamentary substitutes" which include gifts causa mortis, Totten trust accounts, joint savings accounts, other joint tenancies and certain revocable transfers in trust or otherwise. Where a will is involved (1) the will must have been executed after August 31, 1966, (2) the transactions must have been effected during the marriage, and (3) after August 31, 1966. All three conditions must be present or the inter vivos transaction becomes an "exempt" transaction against [***2] which there is no right to elect. Where there is intestacy, (1) the person must have died after August 31, 1966, (2) the transactions were effected during the marriage and (3) after August 31, 1966. All three conditions must be present or the transaction becomes "exempt" not subject to the right of election. Here, decedent died intestate in 1970 leaving a widow, and two children of an earlier marriage. The widow was granted letters and will receive her intestate share. She has filed a notice of election against two Totten trust accounts, created in 1960 for each of decedent's two daughters. On April 11, 1968, after EPTL 5-1.1 became effective, he transferred these accounts from one bank to another which paid higher interest. In form and amount the accounts remained the same. The formerly exempt accounts did not become testamentary substitutes by such transfer. Such accounts, created on or before August 31, 1966 retain their character as exempt inter vivos transactions when transferred from one bank to another without increase in amount or change of beneficiary.

2. Interest or dividends earned after August 31, 1966 on an exempt savings account are not testamentary substitutes.

 [***3] 3. Additional moneys deposited after August 31, 1966 in Totten trust or joint savings accounts and interest or dividends earned thereon, become, under the express terms of the statute, testamentary substitutes.

 

1778 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lustig v. Congregation B'Nai Israel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


65 Misc. 2d 1052; 319 N.Y.S.2d 994; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1731



March 29, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion of the fourth-party defendant to dismiss the complaint is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Fourth-party defendant, party host, brought a motion to dismiss the complaint of fourth-party plaintiff, party caterer, which sought indemnification from fourth-party defendant in a personal injury action brought by a guest who slipped and fell at the party.


OVERVIEW: Fourth-party defendant, party host, hired fourth-party plaintiff, party caterer, to cater a Bar Mitzvah for fourth-party defendant's son at his congregation's reception hall. A provision in the contract purported to indemnify the caterer for any damages of any type that might arise from the event. Plaintiff, party guest, fell on the slippery floor and brought suit against defendant congregation. Fourth-party defendant filed a motion to dismiss the complaint of fourth-party plaintiff. The court found that the legislature clearly declared that the standard caterer indemnification clause in the contract was against public policy when it enacted N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law § 5-322. Although the law was enacted four months after the date of the contract, but one year before the contract was to be performed, the court decided that it would not enforce the unconscionable contract. The court granted the motion to dismiss fourth-party plaintiff's claim.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion of fourth-party defendant, party host, to dismiss the complaint of fourth-party plaintiff, party caterer, because the blanket indemnification provision in the contract between them was unenforceable as against public policy.


HEADNOTES

Indemnity -- indemnification of caterer -- provision in contract with caterer, indemnifying caterer against negligent injury of guests, is unconscionable and contrary to public policy and void -- even though catering contract was signed before section 5-322 of General Obligations Law was enacted, it was to be performed and was performed after said declaration of public policy was enacted.

 Section 5-322 of the General Obligations Law (enacted and effective June 14, 1966) declares that "Every covenant * * * in connection with or collateral to any contract entered into with any caterer * * * exempting the said caterer * * * from liability for damages caused by or resulting from the negligence of the caterer * * * at the affair contracted therefore, shall be deemed to be void as against public policy and wholly unenforceable." This declaration of public policy applies to such an unconscionable provision in a catering contract which was signed on February 7, 1966 for an affair which was to take place over a year later on May 21, 1967 and whereby the caterer was purportedly indemnified by the person hiring [***2] him against liability for injuries to guests caused by the caterer's negligence. Accordingly, in this negligence action by a guest who attended the affair and slipped and fell on an over-waxed floor, the caterer's complaint over against the person who hired him is dismissed.

 

1779 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York v. Colchester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


66 Misc. 2d 83; 320 N.Y.S.2d 156; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1739



March 25, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city filed an action against defendant town seeking a declaration that 1960 N.Y. Law ch. 944 was unconstitutional and an order relieving it of any obligation to remove snow from highways that it had rerouted. The town filed a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The city built reservoirs to augment its water supply. As a result, it rerouted certain roads. The state adopted ch. 944, which required the city to repair and maintain the additional highways and to remove snow and sand the highway surfaces whenever necessary. The city sought a declaration that ch. 944 was unconstitutional. The court entered judgment in favor of the town. It held that ch. 944 was constitutional and a reasonable exercise of the police power. The city had to meet its obligations of sanding and snow removal. Chapter 944 was not violative of the home rule provisions of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2. It was a matter of state concern to assure the maintenance of the roads. The state mandated maintenance of the roads as a condition to condemnation of the property and construction of the reservoirs. 1960 N.Y. Law ch. 944 affected the health and safety of the residents of the up-state communities in a more significant manner than the residents of the city. No discriminatory tax burden was imposed.


OUTCOME: The court found in favor of the town in the city's action seeking a declaration that the statute was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- New York City Water Supply Act -- Administrative Code of City of New York (§ K51-37.0, subd. a, as amd. in 1960) validly and constitutionally requires City of New York to maintain highways and bridges around its reservoirs in Delaware, Ulster and Sullivan Counties, and "to remove the snow and to sand the highway surfaces whenever necessary" -- motion to dismiss action for failure to prosecute it must be denied if movant has omitted to serve 45-day demand first ( CPLR 3216).

1. Pursuant to the Water Supply Act (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 51, tit. K), the City of New York constructed reservoirs in Delaware, Ulster and Sullivan Counties, in order to augment the city's water supply. As a result of the construction, certain roads in those counties had to be discontinued and rerouted. The act provided that the city "repair and forever maintain such additional highways"; and this was amended in 1960 so as to require the city to "repair and forever maintain such additional highways and bridges and to remove the snow and to sand the highway surfaces whenever necessary, except such as shall be part of any state route" (Administrative Code of [***2] City of New York, § K51-37.0, subd. a). The amendment is valid and constitutional (N. Y. Const., art. I, §§ 6, 11; art. IX, § 1, subd. [e]; art. IX, § 2; art. XVI, §§ 1, 2; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.).

2. A motion to dismiss an action for failure to prosecute it must be denied if the movant has not first served a demand that plaintiff file a note of issue within 45 days ( CPLR 3216).
