People v. Shaughnessy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Third District, Trial Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 19; 319 N.Y.S.2d 626; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1759



March 16, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating North Hills, N.Y., Ordinance Prohibiting Entry Upon Private Property in the Incorporated Village of North Hills, § 1. At the conclusion of defendant's trial, defendant submitted a motion to dismiss on the grounds that the ordinance was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a passenger in a vehicle that entered onto private property without permission. Defendant was charged with violating North Hills, N.Y., Ordinance Prohibiting Entry Upon Private Property in the Incorporated Village of North Hills, § 1. At the conclusion of defendant's trial, she sought to dismiss the charge against her. The court held that the People failed to establish defendant's guilt because she was merely a passenger in a vehicle and thus, an essential element in criminal responsibility, an overt voluntary act or omission to act, was missing.


OUTCOME: Without addressing the constitutionality of the challenged ordinance, the court found defendant not guilty of the charged offense.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- mental culpability -- unknowing passenger in automobile which trespassed on private realty is not guilty (cf. Penal Law, § 15.10).

A young driver of an automobile apparently trespassed into privately owned realty, in violation of a local ordinance, and the police arrested the passenger. But she understood that they were headed for a public park across the road from the privately owned realty. She is not guilty (cf. Penal Law, § 15.10).

 

1781 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Watchtower Bible & Tract Soc. v. Altman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


65 Misc. 2d 891; 319 N.Y.S.2d 345; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1774



March 11, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application is accordingly denied and the petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nonprofit organization sought review of the determination of respondent New York City Commissioner of Rent and Housing Maintenance, which denied the nonprofit organization's protest and affirmed the order of the New York City District Rent Director, which denied certificates of eviction for its rent-controlled apartments.


OVERVIEW: The nonprofit organization bought an apartment building and several years later sought to evict the tenants so that its own members and staff could use the apartments. The district rent director denied the application for certificates of eviction until the nonprofit organization complied with the relocation requirements of New York City, N.Y., Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation Regulations § 59(b). The legislature then passed a new regulation, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § Y51-6.0(j)(2) (1970), which prohibited the issuance of certificates of eviction to nonprofit organizations that sought to evict tenants to withdraw the apartments from the housing market. On review, the court denied the application of the nonprofit organization and dismissed its petition. The court found that the regulation was constitutional and that the nonprofit organization had not carried its burden of proving that the section was enacted without a reasonable basis or that it was reasonably related to a manifest evil. The court found that regulation in effect at the time of the commissioner's final determination was controlling and therefore the new regulation was applicable.


OUTCOME: The court denied the nonprofit organization's application for certificates of eviction and dismissed its petition for review of the commissioner's decision.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- regulation permitting organization which is exempt from Federal taxation to evict tenants in order to obtain apartments for use of its staff was, in effect, validly and constitutionally revoked by Local Laws, 1970, No. 30 of City of New York, in view of housing shortage -- petitioner could obtain certificates of eviction if it provided suitable relocations for tenants whom it sought to dispossess (N. Y. City Rent, Eviction and Rehabalitation Regulations, § 59, subd. a, par. [3]; § 59, subd. c; § 54, subd. e); however, District Rent Director validly and constitutionally issued interim order staying petitioner's relocation efforts -- finally, Commissioner of Rent and Housing Maintenance properly denied petitioner's application for certificates of eviction -- law which is in effect when determination is made governs -- petitioner's civil and constitutional rights not violated.

1. Petitioner is a nonprofit membership corporation organized for charitable and religious purposes, commonly known as Jehovah's Witnesses. In 1964 it acquired a large rent-controlled apartment [***2] house in Brooklyn, New York City. In 1969 it filed application with the city's Department of Rent and Housing Maintenance for leave to dispossess the remaining tenants of the 26 rent-controlled apartments, on the ground "that it is an organization exempt from taxation under the Federal Internal Revenue Code, and that it seeks in good faith to recover possession of the housing accommodations for immediate and personal use and occupancy as housing accommodations by members of its staff" (N. Y. City Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation Regulations, § 55, subd. e). However, in view of the housing shortage, this regulation was, in effect, validly and constitutionally revoked in July, 1970 (Local Laws, 1970, No. 30 of City of New York, amending Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-6.0, subd. j, par. [2], last sentence).

2. Petitioner stated a further ground for its application, to wit, that it "seeks in good faith permanently to withdraw occupied housing accommodations from the * * * rental markets" and that it "requires the housing accommodations * * * for its own immediate use in connection with its charitable or educational purposes" (N. Y. City Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation [***3] Regulations, § 59, subd. a, par. [3]), that is, for the purpose of housing its ministers in accordance with its religious beliefs and its chartered purposes. However, the cited section further provides (Regulations, § 59, subd. c) that, in such case, a certificate of eviction "may only be issued on condition that the landlord comply with the requirements of paragraphs e and f of Section 54 of these Regulations" -- that is, the landlord must provide suitable relocations, to be approved by the Commissioner of Rent and Housing Maintenance, for the tenants who are to be dispossessed. While petitioner was still attempting to make such relocations, the District Rent Director of the Department of Rent and Housing Maintenance issued an interim order staying petitioner's application and relocation efforts. In view of the housing shortage, such stay was valid and constitutional. Then, finally, the District Rent Director denied petitioner's application for certificates of eviction, and such denial was properly affirmed by the Commissioner of Rent and Housing Maintenance.

3. There is no showing that the Commissioner made his final determination before Local Law No. 30 of 1970 was enacted [***4] (headnote par. 1). The law which is in effect when the determination is made governs.

4. There is no showing that any of petitioner's civil or constitutional rights have been violated.

 

1782 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Amendola v. Bronstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


66 Misc. 2d 50; 320 N.Y.S.2d 373; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1793



March 3, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] On the foregoing findings of this court, the application is denied and the petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, police sergeants who took a promotional exam for the position of lieutenant, sought relief from the Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County, to revive an examination list that was amended. The petition was opposed by respondents, the Department of Personnel, Civil Service Commission of the City of New York and the city police commissioner, and intervenor-respondents, sergeants who took a subsequent promotional exam.


OVERVIEW: A list was compiled based on the scores from a promotional exam from sergeant to lieutenant. The list was to expire two years after it was established. The list was reranked and revised to include additional persons who had failed the exam, successfully challenged the results, and attained passing scores. Pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, the police sergeants filed a petition seeking to revive the list and to extend its duration for two years from the date it was revised. They alleged that the time period for promotional opportunities for those on the revised list was less than the two years afforded to those on the original list and that this was in conflict with the uniformity of treatment and competitiveness guaranteed by N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6 and violated the terms of the notice of examination. The court denied relief and dismissed the petition. The court held that the corrective procedures, with respect to revision of the list and order of promotions, taken by Personnel were legal, reasonable, and constitutionally sound. There was no violation of the notice of examination because Personnel complied with requirements regarding the duration of an eligible list.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition and denied the application for relief.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- examinations -- rerating -- promotion examination expressly provided that resulting eligible list should expire two years after such list is established; list was certified on May 6, 1968, and appointments were made up to No. 256; then Special Term directed ( CPLR art. 78) that certain other answers should have been accepted; accordingly, revised list was issued on November 21, 1969, and No. 256 who had already been appointed became No. 492; Special Term directed that all contestants up to revised No. 491 should be certified for appointment even after May 6, 1970; this did not unconstitutionally discriminate against contestants who failed to attain No. 491's rating.

The announcement for a New York City Police Department competitive promotion examination stated that "The eligible list resulting from this examination will expire two years from the date it is established." (Civil Service Law, § 56; Rules and Regulations of N. Y. City Civil Service Commission, rule 4.6.6.) An eligible list of 401 names was established on May 6, 1968, and appointments were made up [***2] to and including No. 256 on the list. Then, in proceedings under CPLR article 78, Special Term of the Supreme Court decided that certain of the questions on the examination could have been answered as well by certain answers other than those which had been accepted by the city's Department of Personnel; and thereupon the Department of Personnel rerated all of the contestants accordingly, and issued a revised list on November 21, 1969. The result was that No. 256 on the original list, with a rating of 78.625%, became No. 492 on the revised list, with a revised rating of 78.525%. Special Term further ordered that all those contestants whose rerating equaled or exceeded 78.525% should be certified as eligible for promotion even after the list expired on May 6, 1970. Such order did not unconstitutionally discriminate against those contestants who failed to attain 78.525% on the revised ratings (cf. N. Y. Const., art. V, § 6). As to them, the list expired on May 6, 1970. Their judicial review proceeding ( CPLR art. 78) is dismissed.

 

1783 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Milonas v. Schwalb


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


65 Misc. 2d 1042; 319 N.Y.S.2d 327; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1835



February 18, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the petition is accordingly denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner exhibitors sought a writ of prohibition, seeking to restrain respondent criminal court judge from proceeding with a proposed hearing for a warrant authorizing seizure of an allegedly obscene film which the exhibitors claimed confronted them with the imminent and irreparable deprivation of their constitutional rights. The criminal court judge filed a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: A criminal charge was brought against the exhibitors for showing an allegedly obscene motion picture. An assistant district attorney invited the judge to view the movie and after seeing the movie, the judge concluded it was obscene, and warrants were issued for the arrest of the exhibitors. The judge set the matter down for an "adversary hearing" prior to issuing a warrant of seizure of the film pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 791 et seq. The exhibitors sought a writ of prohibition against the proposed proceeding. The court held that seizure on probable cause prior to a final judicial determination of a question of obscenity would have been a severe restriction on the exercise of First Amendment rights because it did not allow an impartial tribunal for hearing; the proposed procedure permitted no participation by the real parties in interest; there was a limited right to present evidence; there was only a limited, inadequate and contingent right of appeal; and suppression was permitted on an inadequate burden of proof. The court granted the writ of prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the petition for a writ of prohibition. The court issued judgment in favor of the exhibitors and prohibited the criminal court from proceeding with the hearing on issuance of a warrant of seizure.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- motion pictures -- seizure -- Special Term of Supreme Court prohibits Judge of New York City Criminal Court, who has attended motion picture showing and has had theatre employees arrested for obscenity, from conducting hearing as to "circumstances tending to establish * * * probable cause" for issuing warrant to seize film (Code Crim. Pro., § 795) -- such standard of proof unconstitutional (U. S. Const., 1st Amdt.); nor should accuser be Judge; nor is proceeding against mere employees really adversary hearing -- proper procedure is action in Supreme Court for injunction, or action in Supreme Court for injunction and seizure (under Code Crim. Pro., § 22-a), against owners and distributors of film and any other really interested parties.

1. One of the 98 Judges of the New York City Criminal Court attended a showing of a motion picture at a theatre and concluded that the film was obscene. He issued warrants to arrest, on charges of obscenity (Penal Law, § 235.05), not the owner of the theatre or the owner or distributor of the film, but some minor employees of the theatre, such as the ticket [***2] taker and the theatre supervisor. Also, he set a date for a so-called adversary hearing prior to issuing a warrant to seize the film (Code Crim. Pro., § 791 et seq.); and he announced that the only issue would be whether the film had any redeeming social value. In this proceeding instituted under CPLR article 78 by the defendants, Special Term of the Supreme Court prohibits any such "adversary" hearing, on the ground that it would irreparably invade and impair First Amendment and other constitutional rights.

2. Under section 795 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, the Judge may issue the warrant of seizure without proof by a fair preponderance of the evidence or beyond a reasonable doubt, but merely on evidence of "circumstances tending to establish * * * probable cause for believing that * * * grounds exist". Moreover, at the hearing the so-called "adversaries" are mere minor employees, and the court may or may not permit the really interested parties to intervene. Furthermore, the Judge's temptation to vindicate his own prior determination might become overwhelming. Then, after decision of the hearing, months might elapse before the obscenity charges are tried. Eventually,  [***3] these minor employees might be acquitted for lack of scienter. But, even then, the Judge would not be required to order the film returned, since the warrant was supported by "probable cause" (Code Crim. Pro., § 809). So the owners and distributors of the film would have to institute and win a plenary replevin suit to regain their film. Or, if the employees were convicted, the seizure of the film could be reviewed only by appealing from the judgment of conviction -- which might take many more months. During all this time the film could not be exhibited and distributed and might lose its currency and its market.

3. A proper procedure might be an action in the Supreme Court under section 22-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure to enjoin the owners and distributors from distributing or selling an obscene "photograph, figure, image", etc. Said section 22-a directs that there be a trial on the day after joinder of issue, and a decision within two days after trial, and no seizure until judgment. The standard of proof would apparently be a fair preponderance of the evidence. Appeal could follow immediately. This statute has been called a "model" ( Freedman v. Maryland, [***4] 380 U.S. 51, 60). Another statute (Penal Law, § 410.00) provides for a proceeding and trial in our Supreme Court, against the owners of seized equipment which was used for filming or projecting pornographic still or motion pictures, to forfeit such equipment. Said section 410.00 does not state the standard of evidence, but it certainly would not be mere probable cause. And, of course, another proper and constitutional procedure would be a plenary action in our Supreme Court for an injunction against distribution and exhibition.

 

1784 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Orrell v. Planning Bd., Pound Ridge


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


66 Misc. 2d 843; 322 N.Y.S.2d 444; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1841



February 17, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, town planning board, sought dismissal of the claims of petitioner developers, brought under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, which attacked the board's adoption of a zoning ordinance that provided for cluster zoning as authorized by N.Y. Town Law § 281.


OVERVIEW: The town adopted an ordinance that subjected the developer's residential land to minimum two-acre and three-acre bulk requirements. The developers applied for approval of a subdivision plan that set out plots of one and a half acres each. The board accepted the plan, but the town revised it to comply with its ordinance and sent it back to the board. The board approved the revised plan. In their complaint, the developers alleged that the town had improperly abdicated its zoning power to the board, which was out of reach of the electorate, in violation of constitutional restraints. The court found that the town had followed the directive of the state legislature in § 281 in delegating its zoning approval power to the board, and it had properly imposed additional restrictions on the board's authority. In the achievement of the goal of average density control, only minor decisions were left to the discretion of the board because the guidelines were firmly established by § 281. The dismissal of the developer's petition was proper. The court distinguished case law relied upon by the developers because N.Y. Town Law § 281 had undergone amendments since the case had been determined.


OUTCOME: The court granted the board's motion for an order dismissing the developer's petition that challenged the validity of a zoning ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- when Planning Board applied principles of cluster zoning to land development plan, Town Board did not delegate legislative power to Planning Board since section 281 of Town Law sets guidelines for Planning Board's exercise of discretion -- decision of Planning Board was final -- Planning Board has not illegally rezoned, nor contradicted master plan.

1. Respondent Planning Board applied the principles of cluster zoning to a parcel of land which it approved for development in accordance with a plan. There was no delegation of the legislative power of zoning thereby to the Planning Board. Section 281 of the Town Law gives the Planning Board discretion subject to the conditions of the statute. Only the details were left to the Planning Board within the guidelines of the statute. Section 261 et seq. of the Town Law which grant legislative authority to zone exclusively to Town Boards cannot be related to section 281. Section 281 is a further expression of the policy and theory of zoning, manifesting an intent to control average density and to facilitate the adequate and economical provision of streets and utilities and to preserve the [***2] natural and scenic qualities of open lands. Section 281 is not inconsistent with section 261 et seq., but is the logical and consistent development of the policies set forth in the earlier sections. Matter of Hiscox v. Levine (31 Misc 2d 15), decided in 1961, does not determine the issue of delegation of power because section 281 was substantially amended since that case was decided. The law which was in effect in 1970, at the time of the proceedings of which petitioners complain, contained conditions, guidelines and limitations upon the exercise of administrative power which the law that was applied in Hiscox did not contain.

2. The decision of the Planning Board was not advisory, and the Town Board was not authorized to reserve to itself the power to review. The decision of the Planning Board was final and conclusive under section 281.

3. The Planning Board has not illegally rezoned, nor has it contradicted a still-to-be-completed master plan. The power of the Town Board to grant limited powers to the Planning Board under section 281 exists independently by authority of the Legislature and would continue to exist no matter what master plan were adopted. No [***3] Town Board has the power to repeal it.

 

1785 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cooks of New York, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Onondaga County


65 Misc. 2d 790; 318 N.Y.S.2d 960; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1846



February 16, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The decision appealed from is, therefore, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the County Court of New York, Onondaga County, which found it guilty of a violation of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law §§ 2 and 9.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a retail corporation, was found guilty of a violation of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law §§ 2 and 9, which prohibited the doing on Sunday of certain acts. The court held that defendant's equal protection rights were not violated because no evidence of class prosecution, personal animosity, or any other illegitimate reasons was introduced at trial. Thus, defendant did not prove intentional discrimination against it by the sheriff's department. Further, reading N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 4 with N.Y. Penal Law § 55.10(3), the trial court correctly determined that breaking Sabbath was a violation, not a misdemeanor. Thus, defendant was not entitled to a jury trial. Moreover, it was not error to require defendant's attorney to proceed to trial without defendant's representative because the trial court was lenient in permitting the defense adequate time to come to trial. Additionally, if the charged offense was a misdemeanor, a trial could be had in the absence of defendant, pursuant to N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 356. Finally, there was strong presumption that a duly enacted statute was constitutional, and defendant failed to meet its burden of proving that the Sabbath laws were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sabbath breaking -- corporation was properly convicted, without jury, of Sabbath breaking -- General Business Law ( §§ 2, 3, 9) constitutional -- violation triable without jury -- defendant corporation was represented by competent counsel throughout; absence of defendant at first day of trial not prejudical (Code Crim. Pro., § 356).

1. Judgment of a town Court of Special Sessions, without a jury, convicting a corporation of conducting its store on Sunday (General Business Law, § 9), is affirmed by the County Court.

2. The Sabbath laws (General Business Law, §§ 2, 3, 9) are constitutional, even if not uniformly enforced. There is no evidence that this defendant was invidiously discriminated against.

3. Trial for this "violation" need not be by jury (General Business Law, §§ 4, 12; Penal Law, § 55.10, subd. 3, par. [a]; Baldwin v. New York, 399 U.S. 66, followed).

4. Defendant was represented by competent counsel, and at his request the trial was adjourned many times between December, 1969 and April, 1970. The trial finally began on April 10, 1970, in the corporation's absence, but with its attorney [***2] present, and was adjourned to April 24, at which time a representative of the corporation was present, as well as the attorney, and was concluded on May 1, 1970, at which time said representative of the corporation and said attorney were present. No right of the corporation was prejudiced (Code Crim. Pro., § 356; UJCA, §§ 2001, 2006).

 

1786 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Pratt v. Tofany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 172; 320 N.Y.S.2d 555; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1853



February 10, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against respondent, the New York Commissioner of Motor Vehicles, petitioner, a junior operator's licensee, sought removal from the license of the restriction imposed by N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law §501(1)(b), which prohibited him from operating a motor vehicle within Nassau County, New York except for the purpose of driving to and from a state approved cooperative work-study educational program.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law §501(1)(b) prohibited junior operators from driving within Nassau County, New York except for the purpose of driving to and from a state approved cooperative work-study educational program. This restriction applied to New York City without the work-study exception, but did not apply in any other county in the state. Pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, the licensee filed an action to remove the restriction. The court initially noted that the proper proceeding was a declaratory judgment action and treated it as such. The court held that the statute did not violate the home rule section of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(b)(2) because it was not a special law. However, the court declared the statute unconstitutional because it violated the equal protection provisions of U.S. Const. amend XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. The court held that the basis for the statute, which was traffic safety and that junior operators were a greater traffic hazard in Nassau County and New York City, was not reasonably related to the prohibition. There were no statistics that showed conditions in Nassau County were substantially different from those in other counties.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the challenged statutory prohibition on junior operator's licenses violated the federal and state constitutional guarantees of equal protection.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor vehicles -- junior operator's license -- restriction, that junior operator's license cannot be used in Nassau County except to drive to and from State-approved co-operative work-study program, is unconstitutional.

Under the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 501, subd. 1, par. b), a holder of a junior operator's license cannot use it in Nassau County except to drive to and from a State-approved co-operative work-study educational program. This restriction in Nassau County is not justified by present conditions and it therefore contravenes the constitutional guarantees of the equal protection of the laws, even though apparently the Legislature could reasonably and constitutionally prohibit all persons under the age of 18 from driving anywhere at all in the State. Declaratory judgment is granted accordingly, even though in form this is an article 78 CPLR proceeding.

 

1787 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Villaneuva


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Long Beach, Nassau County


65 Misc. 2d 484; 318 N.Y.S.2d 167; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1855



February 10, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of these holdings, the motion to dismiss the information is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an information that charged him with loitering in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6) on the ground that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with violating § 240.35(6). Section 240.35(6) made it a crime to loiter in or about a place without apparent reason and under circumstances that justified suspicion that a person might be engaged or about to engage in crime, and, upon inquiry by a peace officer, refused to identify himself or failed to give a reasonably credible account of his conduct and purposes. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information on the ground that § 240.35(6) was unconstitutional. The court granted the motion and held that § 240.35(6) was unconstitutional because it was vague and indefinite. The court ruled that the information was a bare statement of § 240.35(6) without any explanation or description of any acts, conduct, or suspicious actions of defendant that caused the police officer to make the arrest. The court held that the omission of such explanation or description was indeed fatal. The court ruled that a statute that provided that a person could be arrested by reason of circumstances that justified suspicion of the commission of a crime was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 6), authorizing arrest of person who "loiters * * * under circumstances which justify suspicion * * * and * * * fails to give a reasonably credible account of his conduct and purposes", is unconstitutional; information dismissed.

The Penal Law ( § 240.35) makes loitering a violation, and provides, in part, that "A person is guilty of loitering when he: * * * 6. Loiters, remains or wanders in or about a place without apparent reason and under circumstances which justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime, and, upon inquiry by a peace officer, refuses to identify himself or fails to give a reasonably credible account of his conduct and purposes". This subdivision is unconstitutional, and an information couched in the words of this subdivision is dismissed.

 

1788 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Coleman v. Ginsberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


66 Misc. 2d 46; 319 N.Y.S.2d 338; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1870



February 4, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the request to rescind the penalty of a fine imposed on the one petitioner whose section 75 hearing was held and to permanently bar the postponed section 75 hearings of the other petitioners, is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, five civil service employees (employees), brought a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to declare unconstitutional certain disciplinary procedures invoked against the employees by respondent Administrator of Human Resources Administration of the City of New York, to repay to one of the employees the fine thereby imposed upon him, and to bar the Administrator from conducting further proceedings against the employees.


OVERVIEW: The employees were charged with misconduct in participating in an unauthorized meeting of employees during regular working hours. The employees were given a choice between accepting a fine or requesting a hearing pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 75. All five petitioners chose the § 75 procedure. The employees then brought an action challenging the disciplinary procedure invoked against them. The court denied the petition, holding that: (1) the employees did not have standing to challenge the constitutionality of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 1103-1.0(2) because the ordinance was not applicable to permanent competitive civil service employees and they were not aggrieved persons since none of the five employees accepted the offer of a fine under the ordinance; and (2) although the employees had standing to challenge the implementation of § 1103-1.0(2) by N.Y. Dep't of Soc. Serv. Exec. Order No. 427, the employees failed to show that the implementation of the provision was unconstitutional because the procedure caused no detriment to the employees if they elected to have a N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 75 hearing.


OUTCOME: The court denied the employees' petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- disciplinary proceedings -- allegedly delinquent employees who declined to accept fine (under Administrative Code of City of New York, § 1103-1.0, subd. 2, and Executive Order No. 427 of city's Department of Social Services) cannot have stay of disciplinary proceedings under section 75 of Civil Service Law.

1. The Civil Service Law ( § 75) governs disciplinary proceedings against all civil service employees in the competitive class. This includes dismissals, suspensions, demotions, and fines, after full testimonial hearings with counsel. The Administrative Code of the City of New York (§ 1103-1.0, subd. 2) provides: "Except as otherwise provided by law" -- and therefore, except as to employees in the competitive class -- "every head of an agency is empowered * * * 2. In his discretion, to cause deductions to be made from the salaries, compensation or wages of subordinates of such agency, not exceeding thirty days' pay, as a fine for delinquency [***2] or misconduct."

2. Executive Order No. 427 of the city's Department of Social Services provides that the department may inform an employee of his alleged misconduct and let him submit an answering statement and give him an interview with the city's Bureau of Personnel Administration, and then, within three days, let him elect whether he should accept the penalty of a fine under said section 1103-1.0 with the right to appeal to the city's Commissioner of Social Services as to the excessiveness of the fine, or whether he should require that section 75 of the Civil Service Law should govern. In addition, in practice, the Department of Social Services advises the employee that he has the right to be represented at the interview by a union representative or attorney.

3. Five employees of the department were thus charged, under the Administrative Code (§ 1103-1.0) and Executive Order No. 427, with misconduct in that they participated in an unauthorized meeting of employees which was held during regular working hours in a welfare center of the city. All five chose to be governed by section 75 of the Civil Service Law. Under these circumstances, there is no merit to their contention [***3] that a section 75 hearing would be tainted by the department's having already decided that they are guilty. Their application ( CPLR art. 78) to restrain any proceedings under section 75 is denied.

 

1789 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Mercado v. Walsh


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


65 Misc. 2d 616; 318 N.Y.S.2d 390; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1888



February 1, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The cross motion of the defendants to dismiss the plaintiff's complaint is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenant, individually and on behalf of other tenants similarly situated, filed a motion by order to show cause in his declaratory judgment action for a temporary injunction to restrain the defendant city agencies from informing and directing the tenants to pay a rent increase. The city agencies filed a cross motion to dismiss the action.


OVERVIEW: The tenant challenged the effective date of the rent control statute, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § Y51-5.0(a)(2), which authorized specific increased monthly payments. The tenant claimed that he and other tenants in rent controlled apartments were being deprived of their property without due process because of the requirement that the flat payments were retroactive based on the statute's effective date. The city agencies sought dismissal of the tenant's complaint on grounds that no justiciable issue was presented. Dismissing the complaint, the court found that the motion for temporary injunction was without merit. The court held that the tenant's request that the court change the effective date was wholly without merit because the court could not substitute itself for the city legislature. The question of the wisdom of the effective date established by the legislature was not for the courts to decide.


OUTCOME: The court granted the cross motion by the city agencies to dismiss the action.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- retroactive increase in rent -- enactment of amendment to rent control law so as to require certain tenants to pay, retroactively, for three months, rent increase of $ 15 per month was within Enabling Act and complaint for judgment directing defendants not to advise tenants to pay increase is dismissed -- fixing of retroactive date is legislative matter and cannot be changed by court.

1. The enactment of a local law amending the rent control law so as to require tenants of rent controlled apartments of more than three rooms, whose maximum rent is less than $ 60 per month, to pay retroactively for the months of August, September and October, 1970 $ 15 more per months was within the Enabling Act and the complaint in this action for judgment directing defendants not to advise tenants to pay such increase is dismissed.

2. The local law provided for the effective date albeit retroactively. The court may not change such date and may not substitute itself for the city legislature.

Amanuensis, Ltd. v. Brown


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, New York County


65 Misc. 2d 15; 318 N.Y.S.2d 11; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1889



January 29, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner building owner filed suit against respondent tenants for warrants of eviction for nonpayment of rent.


OVERVIEW: There were rent-impairing violations, and over 90 other violations, that had the cumulative effect of significantly reducing the habitability of the apartments. The court held that: (1) residential tenants were permitted to raise defenses to nonpayment eviction proceedings in three cases: where the landlord had not made a good faith effort to comply with the law, where there were substantial violations and code enforcement remedies were ineffective, and where the continuance of substantial violations was part of a purposeful and illegal effort to force the tenants to abandon their apartments; (2) the N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 302-a(3)(c) requirement that a tenant deposit unpaid rent into an account prior to a hearing was wholly arbitrary and a violation of procedural due process; (3) the tenants' defense based on a breach of the warranty of habitability was sustained; (4) the owner was denied all claimed rent but damages in excess of the unpaid rent, based violations of a warranty of quiet enjoyment and fitness for use, were not proved; and (5) a separate trial was appropriate for the owner's alleged responsibility for criminal acts that occurred in the building.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the owner's petitions for warrants of eviction and assessed costs to the tenants.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and tenant -- nonpayment of rent -- in summary proceedings for nonpayment of rent, unabated violations of Multiple Dwelling Law and Housing Code permit tenants to raise defenses against payment -- use by landlord of continued violations as part of plan to effect removal of all tenants precludes recovery of rent -- section 302-a of Multiple Dwelling Law provides that landlord cannot recover rent if rent impairing violations have been placed against multiple dwellings and not canceled within six months, but tenant cannot raise section as defense unless he pays rent to clerk of court; condition violates procedural due process -- counterclaim by tenants for damages for violation of warranties of quiet enjoyment and fitness for use should be resolved in separate action.

1. In summary proceedings for nonpayment of rent, violations of the Multiple Dwelling Law and the Housing Code, that have continued unabated for more than six months permit tenants to raise defenses against payment where landlord has not made a good faith effort to comply with the law and the violations are so substantial as to affect the habitability of the premises, where remedies to correct the violations [***2] have been pursued without effect, and where the continuance of the violations is part of a purposeful and illegal effort to force tenants to abandon their apartments ( Davar Holdings v. Cohen, 255 App. Div. 445, affd. 280 N. Y. 828, distinguished).

2. The use by landlord of the continued vi olations as part of its plan to remove all tenants is illegal and precludes recovery of the rent.

3. Section 302-a of the Multiple Dwelling Law provides that if a rent impairing violation has been placed against a multiple dwelling and not canceled within six months, the owner cannot recover the rent, but to raise such defense, tenant must deposit the rent with the clerk of the court. The provision requiring tenant to deposit the rent with the clerk as a condition to permitting him to raise the defense provided by section 302-a violates procedural due process in that no hearing is provided for. Accordingly, the defense is available to all tenants including those who did and those who did not pay the rent to the clerk.

4. A counterclaim by tenants for damages for violation of the warranties of quiet enjoyment and fitness for use should be resolved in a separate trial on the basis of new [***3] pleadings.

 

1791 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


De Hond v. Nyquist


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


65 Misc. 2d 526; 318 N.Y.S.2d 650; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1892



January 29, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is denied in all respects and the proceeding is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Board of Education member sought an order prohibiting the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York from determining an appeal by respondent city residents that challenged the member's right to serve on the Board of Education of the City School District of the City of Rochester.


OVERVIEW: The residents sought to have the Commissioner declare the member ineligible to serve on the Board because he was not a city resident for three years, as required by N.Y. Educ. Law § 2553(1). The member claimed the statute was unconstitutional. The court found that the Commissioner had jurisdiction to determine the appeal, denied the member's petition, and dismissed the proceeding. Although the court doubted the wisdom of § 2553(1), it held that the statute was constitutional. The statute was not an unconstitutional exercise of legislative power. It did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the federal Constitution because there was a reasonable relationship between the residency requirement and the legislative objective to obtain public officials who were knowledgeable about and concerned with the affairs of the governmental unit they sought to serve. A resident had a stake in community life and was more apt to be familiar with its problems and, therefore, better qualified to perform the duties of his office. The statute did not violate N.Y. Const. art. XIII, § 1 because it was not an additional test for public office.


OUTCOME: The court denied the member's petition and dismissed the proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- members of school board -- person who was elected as member of Board of Education of City of Rochester is ineligible to serve if he was not resident of city for at least three years (Education Law, § 2553, subd. 1); such requirement is constitutional.

The Education Law ( § 2553, subd. 1) validly and constitutionally provides, in part, that no one is eligible to be elected as a member of the Board of Education of the City School District of the City of Rochester who "has not been a resident of the city school district * * * for a period of at least three years immediately preceding the date of his election" (cf. N. Y. Const., art. XIII, § 1; art. I, §§ 1, 11; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.). The Education Law ( § 310, subd. 7) further validly provides that the State Commissioner of Education shall determine this appeal, in which certain residents of the city ask said Commissioner to declare that such elected member is not a three-year resident and is therefore ineligible to serve.

 

1792 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Commercial Lawyers Conference v. Grant


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


65 Misc. 2d 897; 318 N.Y.S.2d 966; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1907



January 25, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The cross motions are accordingly granted and the complaints dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff collection associations sought relief to declare unconstitutional and enjoin the enforcement of the Consumer Protection Law of 1969 of the City of New York and Regulation 4 thereof. Defendant public officials filed cross-motions to dismiss the complaints.


OVERVIEW: The city of New York enacted laws and the city Commissioner of Consumer Affairs created regulations pursuant thereto that made any pre-judgment communications between a collection agency and a debtor's employer an unconscionable trade practice in the collection of a debt arising from the sale of consumer goods or services or the extension of consumer credit. The collection associations filed suit to declare these enactments unconstitutional and for an injunction. The public officials filed motions to dismiss, which the court granted. The court held that the law and regulation did not conflict with any other debt practice collection laws. The court granted the cross-motions of the public officials and dismissed the collection associations' complaint to have certain debt collection laws and regulations declared unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the cross-motions of the public officials and dismissed the collection associations' complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- New York City Consumer Protection Law -- Regulation 4 of New York City Commissioner of Consumer Affairs, prohibiting creditors of consumers and their collection agencies and attorneys from communicating or threatening to communicate with such alleged debtor's employers before obtaining judgment "except as specifically permitted by statute", is valid and constitutional.

The New York City Consumer Protection Law of 1969 defines an "Unconscionable trade practice" as "Any act or practice in connection with the sale, lease, rental or loan or in connection with the offering for sale, lease, rental or loan of any consumer goods or services, or in the extension of consumer credit, or in the collection of consumer debts which unfairly takes advantage of the lack of knowledge, ability, experience or capacity of a consumer"; and it authorizes the Commissioner of Consumer Affairs to adopt regulations which "may supplement but shall not be inconsistent with the rules, regulations and decisions of the Federal Trade Commission and the Federal courts in interpreting * * * 15 U. S. C. 45(a)  [***2] (1), or the decisions of the courts interpreting General Business Law § 350 and Uniform Commercial Code § 2-302." (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 2203d-2.0, subd. b; § 2203d-3.0). The Commissioner's Regulation 4 provides that "It shall be an unconscionable trade practice in the collection of a debt arising from the sale of consumer goods or services or the extension of consumer credit, for a creditor or alleged creditor or employee or agent thereof (including a collection agency or collection attorney to whom an account has been referred) to communicate or threaten to communicate with an alleged debtor's employer prior to obtaining a final judgment against the debtor, except as specifically permitted by statute." The regulation is valid (cf. U. S. Code, tit. 15, § 1610) and constitutional.

 

1793 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Weston's Shopper's City, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Town of Colonie, Albany County


64 Misc. 2d 987; 317 N.Y.S.2d 812; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1908



January 25, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Because of this court's decision that section 9 of the General Business Law is unconstitutionally vague in violation of the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, the informations are dismissed and the defendant is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant demurred to the State's charges of violating a Sunday blue law, N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 9, by selling or permitting to be sold a pair of earrings and a cartridge of film on grounds that the facts stated did not constitute a crime and that § 9 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued the items sold fell under the souvenir exception in N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 9(4) and, alternatively, that the word souvenir was unconstitutionally vague. The court held that § 9 was unconstitutionally vague in violation of the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, which provided that no state shall deprive any person of life, liberty, or property without due process of law. The court rejected defendant's first argument that the items were souvenirs because it concluded that the word "souvenir" could not be objectively interpreted. The souvenir exception to the statute was so vague and uncertain that men of common intelligence had to guess at its meaning. The vagueness deficiency rendered the statute unconstitutional. The court went beyond arguments in the briefs and suggested that the punishment aspects of the Sunday blue law were cruel and unusual, in violation of the Eighth Amendment, but left that determination to the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court found the Sunday blue law unconstitutional, dismissed the informations, and discharged defendant.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- sales on Sunday -- "souvenirs" which may be sold on Sunday under exception of section 9 of General Business Law is not word capable of objective determination and therefore earrings and film which defendant sold on Sunday cannot be construed as "souvenirs" -- word "souvenir" is unconstitutionally vague; informations dismissed and defendant discharged.

1. "Souvenirs" which may be sold on Sunday as an exception under section 9 of the General Business Law, which prohibits the sale of all merchandise on Sunday with certain enumerated exceptions, is not a word which may be objectively interpreted and therefore earrings and film which defendant sold on Sunday cannot be construed to be "souvenirs".

2. Since what is a souvenir cannot be objectively determined, section 9 is so vague, indefinite and uncertain that its meaning cannot be determined by reasonable men and the statute is unconstitutional as violative of the due process [***2] clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution. The informations are dismissed and defendant discharged.

 

1794 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lawrence Towers Associates v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


67 Misc. 2d 217; 323 N.Y.S.2d 812; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1911



January 21, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff grantee filed a motion for an order striking defendant city's answer and directing summary judgment in the grantee's favor. The grantee challenged the propriety of a real estate transfer tax on property that it acquired from the federal government.


OVERVIEW: The grantee acquired property from the federal government. The real property transfer tax return that was filed by the grantee and the grantor stated that the transaction was exempt and that no tax was due. However, the exemption that was afforded the federal government under N.Y. Tax Law §§ 1201(b), 1230 and under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § II46-6.0(a)(2) only applied to transfers to a governmental body. The court found that the real property transfer tax as it applied to the grantee was constitutional. There was no basis for holding it unconstitutional on the ground that it was imposed upon grantees. It did not inflict double taxation on the grantee because it, as a subsequent grantor, would be primarily liable for the tax when it sold the property. A different transaction would be taxed, and the grantee could provide for the payment of the tax by a future grantee through an increase in the sales price or otherwise. The court denied the grantee's motion to strike the city's answer.


OUTCOME: The court denied the grantee's motion.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- real property transfer tax -- transfer tax upon Federal Government's deed to plaintiff was properly imposed under Tax Law and Administrative Code of City of New York since exemption is afforded "to" any governmental body; tax is constitutional -- no double taxation occurs when plaintiff sells it at later time.

1. On a deed to plaintiff executed by the Federal Housing Authority as grantor, a real property transfer tax and penalty was properly imposed on plaintiff under sections 1201 (subd. [b]) and 1230 of the Tax Law and title II of chapter 46 of the Administrative Code of City of New York, since exemption is afforded "to any governmental body". The tax is imposed upon the grantee and it cannot be held unconstitutional on that basis. No basis for relieving plaintiff of the penalty has been advanced.

2. Nor does the statute inflict double taxation on plaintiff because it, as grantor, would be primarily liable for the tax when and if it sells the property. A different transaction would then be taxed.

 

1795 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Mario


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


65 Misc. 2d 708; 317 N.Y.S.2d 659; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1922



January 19, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding under the N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act, respondent minor child was adjudicated "a person in need of supervision." Petitioner state requested that the minor child be placed in a state training school.


OVERVIEW: In a proceeding under the N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act, the minor child was adjudicated a person in need of supervision (PINS). The state recommended that the minor child be placed in a state training school. The minor child argued that the restraint imposed by training school placement of a PINS violated the guarantees of Due Process and Equal Protection of law. The court determined that the minor child's placement in the state training school for boys would promote his welfare and his psychological and social development. The court held that the restraint imposed on the minor child was constitutional, because reasonable rehabilitative measures were justified based on the minor child's truancy, and required by the compulsory education law. The restraint of a parentally uncontrollable school truant by a training school was constitutional, where the court reasonably found that the restraint would benefit the minor child.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the minor child's placement in the state training school for boys would promote his welfare and his psychological and social development.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- persons in need of supervision -- long-time habitual truant, now 13 1/2 years old and unacceptable to foster homes, shall be placed preliminarily in Warwick State Training School for Boys, but shall not be forced to stay there if he leaves -- beneficial and rehabilitative restraints of State training school programs do not infringe on truant's constitutional rights.

1. The boy involved in this case has been adjudged a "person in need of supervision" (Family Ct. Act, § 712). He is now 13 1/2 years old, and he lives with his mother and siblings on a public welfare budget in a slum neighborhood, and he shares a room with an 18-year-old brother who has been out of school and out of work for over a year and who is actively homosexual. The mother is rather passive and unable to control or motivate her children. She tries in vain to have the 13 1/2-year-old attend school, but he remains a long-time habitual truant (cf. Education Law, §§ 3213, 3214, 3232). The special individualized school maintained by the Office of Probation for truants was willing to accept him, and an expert probation officer tried in vain to persuade him to try it. On most nights he remains out [***2] of his home until 2 or 3 a.m., apparently "running the streets with older boys"; but he will not say where or when; and he usually sleeps until 3 or 4 p.m. Foster homes which the Office of Probation has asked to accept him have refused. At this stage of his life, he probably would not change his behavior if he were living in a friendly foster home; but possibly he is not too old to change psychologically and socially in the more structured surroundings of the Warwick State Training School for Boys (cf. Social Services Law, § 425; § 427, subd. 2). The Family Court directs that he be placed in that school preliminarily for nine months, subject, however, to two conditions: (a) he shall not be forced to remain for any prolonged period in the special dormitory in the locked Administration Building there, and (b) if he leaves the school without permission, he shall not be forced to return. In those circumstances, force would only militate against his possible rehabilitation. The court requests the school to report on his progress in the first six weeks.

2. Even though this boy has committed no crime, his commitment will not infringe his constitutional rights, since the normal restraints [***3] and rehabilitative programs of the State Training School will probably benefit him.

 

1796 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pobliner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 209; 320 N.Y.S.2d 257; 1971 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1936



January 12, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, following his murder conviction and the denial of a new trial, sought a certificate of reasonable doubt and admission to bail pending an appeal of his conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and given a 25-year to life prison term. Defendant's motion for a new trial was denied and defendant sought a certificate of reasonable doubt and admission to bail pending defendant's appeal. The State opposed the granting of the certificate to because pursuant to N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 555 defendant was ineligible for bail because he had been convicted of a class A felony. Defendant argued that § 555 was unconstitutional because it denied equal protection by denying him a right accorded to all other first offenders pending their appeal from a conviction for a crime not punishable by death. The court found that defendant was in the same position as any other defendant convicted of a class A felony in that no one convicted of a class A felony was entitled to bail pending an appeal, that the prohibition against excessive bail did not grant defendant an absolute right to bail, that a right to appeal did not automatically confer a right to bail on defendant, and that there was rational basis for distinguishing in N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 552 and 555 between bail for offenders merely accused of a crime and those who had been tried and convicted.


OUTCOME: Defendant was denied a certificate of reasonable doubt and bail.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Bail -- right to bail -- defendant convicted of murder is not entitled to certificate of reasonable doubt or admission to bail -- section 555 of Code of Criminal Procedure denies to person convicted of class A felony right to bail; statute constitutional; immaterial that defendant is first offender or that admission to bail is granted to persons accused of crimes.

1. On defendant's conviction for murder, although defendant would be entitled to a certificate of reasonable doubt, since he is not eligible for bail, defendant's motion for a certificate of reasonable doubt and for admission to bail is denied.

2. Section 555 of the Code of Criminal Procedure denies to a person convicted of a class A felony the right to bail. Such section is not unconstitutional as a denial of equal protection. Bail is denied by statute to other first offenders convicted of other class A felonies. Defendant's being a first offender does not entitle him to any greater rights. In view of the nature of the crimes classified as class A felonies, the Legislature had a rational basis for making a distinction, for the purpose of bail, as between first offenders convicted of a class A felony and first [***2] offenders convicted of other crimes. There is also a basis for distinguishing between persons who have been accused of crimes but not tried and convicted and those who have been tried and convicted.

People v. Rodman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


65 Misc. 2d 123; 316 N.Y.S.2d 887; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1012



December 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] I am therefore constrained to grant the motion to dismiss. 11



11 When I acknowledge such constraint, I do so almost apologetically, for my personal inclination and desire are to uphold the acts of the law enforcement officers in respect of engulfing ecological problems, especially of air pollution in our city. But my functions as a Judge preclude subjective desires. I must decide on the law as it is and I cannot stultify myself in that capacity simply because I should prefer a different result.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought to dismiss a summons and complaint alleging violation of New York City, N.Y., Air Pollution Control Code § 9.03(a)(1) (1964), by emission of dense smoke from a smokestack. He claimed that the summons did not indicate whether the alleged act was a misdemeanor or a violation, that it was not properly sworn to, and that it was insufficient as a matter of law.


OVERVIEW: The court found that because it imposed penalties of more than 15 days but less than one year of imprisonment, New York City, N.Y., Air Pollution Control Code § 9.03(a)(1) (1964) was a misdemeanor statute and did not describe a violation, that § 9.03(a)(1) was penal in intent, and that it was intended to protect the city's inhabitants from harmful air pollution. The court found that the complaint was properly sworn to, that the court had jurisdiction over the subject matter and defendant when he consented to appear by counsel, and that the specific wording of the complaint was far too general to inform defendant of the alleged infraction. The court found that the simplified form of "universal" summons used did not apprise defendant of what was forbidden and the severity of his alleged offense. The court granted the motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the summons and complaint.


HEADNOTES

Health -- air pollution control -- charge that defendant committed offense of "dense smoke from incinerator stack * * * in violation of section 9.03 subd. A(1) * * * Air Pollution Control Code" is misdemeanor -- complaint was properly sworn to -- complaint fails to state facts of charge and is dismissed.

1. A charge contained in a complaint that defendant committed "offense * * * dense smoke from incinerator stack * * * in violation of section 9.03 subd. A(1) * * * Air Pollution Control Code" is a misdemeanor since the law imposes as a penalty more than 15 days' imprisonment.

2. The complaint was properly sworn to by an inspector from the Department of Air Pollution Control.

 [***2] 3. The complaint fails adequately to state the facts of the charge against defendant so as to enable him to defend himself against it. It is dismissed.

 

1798 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re "S"


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


64 Misc. 2d 1002; 317 N.Y.S.2d 180; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1038



December 18, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This matter was before the court on a motion demanding jury trials in delinquency proceedings for respondent juvenile, who was accused of committing acts that would constitute class B felonies if he was over the age of 16.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile, a 15-year-old boy, was accused of committing acts that would constitute first degree rape and kidnapping, both with the aid of deadly weapons, if he was over the age of 16. A jury trial was demanded and the court determined that the juvenile was entitled either to jury trials or to advance rulings that he was in jeopardy of incarceration in an adult prison for longer than six months. It recognized a recent decision holding that jury trials were not required in juvenile delinquency trials, but distinguished it from the instant case because the juvenile in this case was is in jeopardy of being contained within an adult institution pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 758, while the juvenile in the prior case was protected from incarceration in an adult institution by a negotiated plea agreement. Further, it was the possibility of more than six months' incarceration, not the actual sentence, which determined whether a jury was required. It then made an advance ruling, based on the concurrence of counsel for both sides, that the juvenile could not be incarcerated in a non-juvenile institution for more than six months nor in a state training school for more than three years.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the juvenile was entitled either to a jury trial or a ruling he could not be incarcerated in an adult penal institution for more than six months and, on the concurrence of counsel for both sides, the court entered an advance ruling that the juvenile could not be incarcerated in an adult institution for more than six months nor in a state training school for more than three years.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- juvenile delinquents -- juvenile who committed acts which if done by one over 16 years of age would be class B felonies is entitled to determination in advance that he cannot be incarcerated in nonjuvenile institution more than six months nor in training school more than three years.

A 15-year-old boy who is accused of committing acts which, if he were over 16 years of age, would constitute class B felonies for which he could be committed to a prison for longer than six months, i.e., three consecutive three-year terms in Elmira Reception Center, is entitled to a determination in advance of trial that he cannot be incarcerated in a nonjuvenile institution for more than six months, nor in a New York State Training School for more than three years. The court so interprets section 758 of the Family Court Act as to rule out the possibility of more than six months in Elmira Reception Center or more than three years in the New York State Training School for Boys and as to save the constitutionality of the section in the light of recent decisions of the highest courts of the State and country.

 

1799 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fhagen v. Miller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


65 Misc. 2d 163; 317 N.Y.S.2d 128; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1070



December 9, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, based upon all of the foregoing, the plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment is denied and defendants' cross motion is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, allegedly mentally ill persons, filed an action against defendant state mental health commissioner in the Supreme Court of New York, New York County (New York) in which they challenged the constitutionality of the emergency involuntary commitment procedures of N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law §§ 72 and 78. The parties filed cross motions for summary judgment. The court issued it findings and opinion.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs contended that § 78, which authorized temporary confinement of an individual without prior notice or hearing, must be held to contravene the requirements of due process unless the sole standard for determining "immediacy" of care was that the patient constituted an immediate physical threat to himself or society. The court granted summary judgment for the commissioner. The court held that §§ 72 and 78 did not violate plaintiffs' due process rights. The court observed that after plaintiffs filed their lawsuit, the commissioner promulgated additional rules under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 14, § 62.2, which provided additional safeguards for patients who were involuntary committed in mental health hospitals. Regarding the standard for commitment challenged by plaintiffs, the court stated that it deemed it unwise and unnecessary to limit the basis for emergency involuntary admission under § 78 to dangerous conduct. The court said that an adequate workable standard was that the admission procedure would be authorized where it was found that immediate action was necessary for the protection of society and the welfare of the allegedly mentally ill person.


OUTCOME: In plaintiffs' constitutional challenge to the involuntary emergency mental health commitment procedures, the court granted the commissioner's motion for summary judgment and denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Mental health -- person in need of immediate observation -- procedures for temporary or emergency admission to State hospital of "any person alleged to be in need of immediate observation, care or treatment", and for subsequent retention of such patient if necessary ( Mental Hygiene Law, § 78; 14 NYCRR 62.2), are valid and constitutional -- such patient need not be "dangerously" mentally ill -- civil standards of proof apply -- patient may "correspond without restriction" and "once by telephone" ( Mental Hygiene Law, § 15; § 70, subd. 6; 14 NYCRR 62.2); these provisions are constitutional.

1. Section 78 of the Mental Hygiene Law provides (subd. 1) that "any person alleged to be in need of immediate observation, care or treatment for mental illness" may be involuntarily admitted to a State hospital or State-approved institution, apparently even upon an application by a lay person, and (subd. 3) that "Any peace officer * * * may take into custody any person who appears to be mentally ill and is conducting himself in a manner which [***2] in a sane person would be disorderly, and may * * * remove him to any hospital specified in subdivision one of this section", and (subd. 2) that any patient who is admitted for such immediate observation may be retained for more than 30 days if the need for additional care and treatment is certified by two examining physicians, and, further, that "the admission and retention of any such patient shall be subject to all of the provisions for notice, hearing, review and judicial approval * * * as provided in sections" 72, 73 and 74 of the Mental Hygiene Law. The regulations of the State Commissioner of Mental Hygiene ( 14 NYCRR 62.2) limit such emergency admission to 15 days, and provide for prompt notice to the State Mental Health Information Service, and further provide that the patient's demand for release shall be treated as a demand for habeas corpus and shall be communicated forthwith to the State Mental Health Information Service (cf. Mental Hygiene Law, §§ 88, 426). These provisions of the statute and regulations (cf. Mental Hygiene Law, § 2, subd. 8; § 7, subd. 5; § 9, subd. 3; §§ 11, 12) are valid and constitutional.

2. In view of the due process provided by the statute [***3] and regulations, it is not constitutionally necessary that the "person alleged to be in need of immediate observation" be "dangerously mentally ill" (cf. Mental Hygiene Law, §§ 85, 76, 74).

3. If a peace officer takes such a person into custody (under Mental Hygiene Law, § 78, subd. 3), the standard of "disorderly" conduct should be the same as in the Penal Law ( § 240.20), except, however, that such disorderly conduct need not be proved beyond a reasonable doubt since this is not a criminal action.

4. None of these procedures (under Mental Hygiene Law, § 78, subds. 1, 2, 3) are criminal, and hence the need for immediate observation and/or subsequent retention and treatment need only be shown by civil standards of proof.

5. The Mental Hygiene Law ( § 15; § 70, subd. 6) and the regulation ( 14 NYCRR 62.2), read together, provide that the patient shall be allowed to "correspond without restriction" with any public official and with the Mental Health Information Service, and that the patient, upon admission, shall "be given the opportunity to communicate once by telephone or, if not possible, by the next most expeditious method with any person within the State." It cannot be said [***4] that these are unreasonable and unconstitutional restrictions.

 

1800 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Trief


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


65 Misc. 2d 272; 317 N.Y.S.2d 525; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1072



December 9, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence obtained by interception of telephonic communications, wiretapping, and eavesdropping, bugging. Defendant also filed a motion to dismiss the indictment for failure to prosecute in the State's prosecution of defendant under former N.Y. Penal Law for the crimes of conspiracy and accepting unlawful fees.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a former police officer who was charged with crimes of conspiracy and accepting unlawful fees during a period when the department was conducting an extensive organized unlawful gambling investigation. Defendant filed motions to suppress evidence by interception of telephonic communications and eavesdropping, and a motion to dismiss for want of prosecution. The court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained by wiretapping but denied the motion to suppress evidence obtained by bugging. The motion to dismiss for failure to prosecute was denied. The court held that the evidence obtained as a result of the wiretapping was suppressed because the wiretapping was conducted pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 605, which was not repealed as claimed by the State and that the evidence was wrongfully obtained. The court that the judicial documents authorizing the bugging would have to be provided to the court before a determination could be made, and that delays in prosecution were primarily the result of defendant's motions.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained by wiretapping but denied the motion to suppress evidence obtained by bugging. The motion to dismiss for failure to prosecute was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- wiretapping and bugging -- evidence which police obtained, pursuant to court orders, by tapping telephone wires, is inadmissible and is ordered suppressed -- but evidence which police obtained by electronic "bugging" in 1960 to 1965 will not be suppressed if affidavits and ex parte orders met standards of that time -- 2 1/2 years' delay without trial, thus far, has been occasioned by defendant's pretrial motions, hence defendant's motion to dismiss indictment is denied.

1. Pursuant to ex parte court orders under former section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure, the police intercepted telephonic communications. Such interception was and is illegal under section 605 of the Federal Communications Act of 1934. Evidence thus illegally obtained will be inadmissible in a trial in our State courts after June 17, 1968 ( Lee v. Florida, 392 U.S. 378, and Fuller v. Alaska, 393 U.S. 80, followed). Said section 605 (U. S. Code, tit. 47, § 605) was amended, not repealed, by the Omnibus Crime Control and Safe Streets Act of 1968 (82 U.S. Stat. 197 to 239). Accordingly, defendant's motion to suppress such evidence is granted.

2. However, evidence which [***2] the police obtained, not by intercepting telephonic communications, but by means of other electronic "eavesdropping" or "bugging", will not be ordered suppressed, unless the court now finds that the affidavits and ex parte orders of authorization did not meet the standards which were reasonably believed to be applicable at the time when those orders were obtained ( People v. Morhouse, 21 N Y 2d 66, 77, followed).

3. It cannot be said that defendant's right to a speedy trial has been violated. Although some 2 1/2 years have elapsed thus far, the delay has been occasioned to a substantial degree by pretrial motions made by said defendant and by other defendants to suppress evidence; and therefore, defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment is denied.
